Con: This would only be the case in a scenario where offer was inelastic \(either because goods are too expensive to produce, thus unenforceable, or already at peak production, because all the land and resources are already destined to other more important products, making opportunity cost too high\), otherwise it would respond to the expansion in the consumer market by producing more, effectively offsetting inflation
1.2.1.3.6. Con: This would only be the case in a scenario where supply was inelastic \(either because goods are too expensive to produce, or already at peak production, because all the land and resources are already used for other more important products, making opportunity cost too high\), otherwise producers would respond to the expansion in the consumer market by producing more, effectively offsetting inflation
1.2.1.3.6.1. Con: Not all goods are elastic, e.g. land. Therefore, the inflation would only be offset in some sectors of the economy and not others.
1.2.1.3.7. Pro: Segmented inflation realistically might occur since more people will be buying low-income goods/services that previously could not be afforded by so many people, such as basic foodstuffs, low-cost housing, cheap cars, etc.
1.2.1.3.8. Con: The hand that provides the resources for UBI will be the same hand that taxes the economy to generate the revenue. UBI is redistributive in its principle, so what purchasing power you take from one you given to another. Ignoring the intricacies of exactly how consumer goods markets will adjust, the net impact on inflation is likely to be neutral. Furthermore, successful implementation of UBI will require coordination between fiscal and monetary policy authorities.
1.2.1.4. Con: Luxuries may be marginally priced out, but necessities wouldn't be, as UBI is additive, not redistributive.
1.2.1.4.1. Con: Wealthier people save more as a percentage of total assets. Any change to that would increase the total cash in economy and therefore inflationary pressures.
1.2.1.4.2. Con: Since a UBI must be funded by increased taxation, it is effectively redistributive.
1.2.1.5. Con: The current economic challenges are [too little inflation, not too much](http://www.investopedia.com/news/us-inflation-hits-fouryear-high/?ad=dirN&qo=investopediaSiteSearch&qsrc=0&o=40186).
1.2.1.5.1. Pro: Inflation can be countered through monetary policy. Interest rates are currently too low, as is inflation. Generating inflation would be good for the country, and interest rates could be raised to counter any risk of hyperinflation.
1.2.1.6. Con: Inflation would only occur if the money supply was increased to pay for a UBI.
1.2.1.6.1. Pro: Because a UBI is a redistribution plan, the amount of money in existence will, in aggregate, be the same. Therefore, consumption will not increase.
1.2.1.6.1.1. Con: [Redistributing money](https://www.theguardian.com/business/2015/jun/04/better-economic-growth-when-wealth-distributed-to-poor-instead-of-rich) to the poor will likely increase goods sales as the poor spend proportionately more of their income on consumables than on savings.
1.2.1.6.1.1.1. Con: This is not because poor people consume more, but because they earn less. If they would earn more, it is likely that they would save more of their income as well.
1.2.1.6.2. Pro: The [Quantity Theory of Money](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantity_theory_of_money) states that inflation will only happen if money supply increases. With UBI, aggregate money supply in the economy does not increase, it just redistributes money from one part of the economy \(the rich, specific industries, specific economic activities, etc., depending on how the UBI was funded\) to the public.
1.2.1.6.3. Con: In an era of accelerating technology, we should see negative pressure on inflation, meaning UBI could be funded by central banks printing money, a kind of QE for UBI.
1.2.1.6.4. Con: Besides the quantity of money \(which affects general inflation\) the relocation of money creates localised inflation too.
1.2.1.6.4.1. Pro: In the USA, as more money was made available for university costs \(loans, etc.\) the costs then soared far beyond that of general inflation. A UBI would create localised inflation rendering the income payments useless.
1.2.1.6.4.2. Pro: An equal influx of money to all the population would create severe localised infaltion in consumer goods such as food, transport, housing, etc. as the purchasing power of everyone is shifted upwards.
1.2.1.6.4.3. Con: An UBI would indeed cause some localised inflation, but it's impossible to make sweeping statements on whether they would offset the costs.
1.2.1.6.4.4. Con: There's a critical difference between a UBI and the money made available for higher education: the latter is specifically purposed \(which logically increases the risk of inflation\) and the former is not. It allows people to spend the money on a large variety of things, greatly lowering the risk/amount of inflation.
1.2.1.6.5. Con: Inflation for individual products will increase faster for products generally bought by UBI recipients. Overall across all products inflation may remain the same.
1.2.1.7. Con: Real cases suggest that a UBI would not lead to greater inflation.
1.2.1.7.1. Pro: In [a study in Mexico giving out cash actually had a lesser effect on the market than giving out necessary goods](https://www.vox.com/policy-and-politics/2017/9/20/16256240/mexico-cash-transfer-inflation-basic-income) - in this case, food.
1.2.1.7.2. Pro: Both [Alaska and Kuwait](https://medium.com/basic-income/wouldnt-unconditional-basic-income-just-cause-massive-inflation-fe71d69f15e7) experienced decreases in inflation after versions of UBI were implemented.
1.2.1.8. Con: Inflation is not inherently harmful as long as real wages and purchasing power remain constant.
1.2.2. Con: -> See 1.1.5.3.1.3.
1.2.3. Con: UBIs would increase the productivity of individuals.
1.2.3.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.5.3.1.3.6.4.1.
1.2.3.2. Pro: Many people work jobs they are not great at because they need those jobs to get through the month. At the same time, many willing and productive people are sitting at home, searching for a job. If UBI were implemented, the first type of person could leave a job they are not happy with knowing they will be financially secure, while the latter type of person could take their post. This would boost productivity.
1.2.3.3. Con: UBIs tend to incentivize more value-driven activities \(like sports or the arts\), creating inefficiencies in how people spend their time and money.
1.2.3.3.1. Con: We have the ability to do controlled experiments of both size and scale to see how a UBI would affect different communities.
1.2.3.3.1.1. Pro: [Finland](http://www.independent.co.uk/news/business/news/finland-universal-basic-income-experiment-lesson-tips-ubi-poverty-unemployment-a7871441.html), among other countries, is experimenting with a UBI under controlled conditions to rigorously determine its effects.
1.2.3.3.2. Con: There is more to life than "productivity" and "value". If there is no time to use and enjoy the utility we create, it is valueless.
1.2.3.3.2.1. Pro: Factors other than mere productivity and GDP including [well-being](https://www.activesustainability.com/sustainable-development/alternatives-to-gdp-for-measuring-well-being/) are considerations, in particular in the western world.
1.2.3.4. Pro: [Evidence](http://behavioralscientist.org/wisdom-universal-basic-income/) suggests that a UBI would be beneficial from a psychological well-being and productivity perspective.
1.2.3.4.1. Pro: Current welfare systems induce a psychology of fear of ['earning too much'](http://reason.com/reasontv/2016/03/03/the-human-cost-of-welfare-how-the-system), which can hinder the potential for self-reliance and economic growth. A UBI would remove this fear, as it is not dependent on one's earnings.
1.2.4. Con: Poverty is very [expensive](https://www.economist.com/news/united-states/21663262-why-low-income-americans-often-have-pay-more-its-expensive-be-poor) for a country. Therefore, not having a UBI is more expensive than a UBI.
1.2.4.1. Pro: The increase in economic contribution from those lifted out of poverty by UBI could be greater in many cases than the cost of providing those individuals a UBI.
1.2.5. Pro: The financial burden that countries would have to bear in order to implement a UBI that guaranteed that people at least reach the poverty line would have serious economic consequences.
1.2.5.1. Con: If a country's economy can create millionaires and even billionaires, it can afford to give everybody [enough to eat](https://www.weforum.org/agenda/2016/07/the-world-produces-enough-food-to-feed-everyone-so-why-do-people-go-hungry). A society should be organized so that everyone's basic needs are fulfilled before allowing excessive wealth for a few people.
1.2.5.1.1. Pro: Ensuring the life and health of our fellow man is one of our first moral obligations as humans.
1.2.5.1.1.1. Con: The moral obligations of humans are not the moral obligations of governments.
1.2.5.1.1.1.1. Con: Democratic governments are not only responsible for the people under their care, but also ideologically should be representative of them. Therefore, they should attempt to carry out their collective moral obligations, without descending into tyranny.
1.2.5.1.1.1.2. Con: Governments are made up of humans. Those men who are part of governments choose what rights should be defended by the law, so governments have many more moral obligations than normal citizens.
1.2.5.1.1.2. Con: We are morally obligated to ensure *opportunity,* not outcome—especially if people simply choose not to work. Traditional welfare can target those who are temporarily unfortunate \(e.g. family breadwinner dies\) or long-term disadvantaged \(e.g. disabled\), but a UBI provides a basic income to all people, regardless of their actual need.
1.2.5.1.1.3. Con: Although we are morally obligated to help others, this does not mean we should be legally compelled to do so, especially considering \(for example\) that what the government considers "need" and what is actually "need" may be 2 different things. Similarly, if the government already taxes its citizens without fixing the problems, it should be hesitant for blaming/shaming the rich for not providing even more.
Ultimately, the parent is missing a crucial link between moral principle and legal policy.
1.2.5.1.1.3.1. Con: Many people do not carry out moral obligations they do not immediately see/feel or are not aware of; legal mechanisms ensure moral obligations are fulfilled.
1.2.5.1.2. Con: An economy can have the potential to create a large amount of wealth, but still be unable to fairly allocate/distribute or produce enough of a certain resource, such as food.
1.2.5.1.2.1. Con: Many of the countries in question produce enough food to comfortably feed more there population without any type of intervention.
1.2.5.1.2.2. Pro: Whilst this is theoretically possible, the issue with millionaires in an economy where people are starving is that they represent a concentration of clout at the expense of others. When one person has a greater income in a day than another does in a year, it is not that the first is working harder: it is that they are profiting from the others work at their expense.
1.2.5.1.2.2.1. Pro: Investing does not directly produce any labour or products.
1.2.5.1.2.2.2. Con: Remuneration can be for work but for the most part in modern societies with free markets people are remunerated for their contribution to society. Those with the greatest contributions tend to be those who have produced innovation or improved efficiency. Thus much wealth transfer is a result of society "voting" for the contributions they value most by purchasing them in markets.
1.2.5.1.3. Con: Governments are capable of providing welfare, but the costs of providing "universal" basic income at a level which "meets the poverty line" are \(likely\) far greater than traditional, basic welfare for the needy.
1.2.5.1.4. Pro: In wealthier countries, very few people actually face even temporary starvation in any given year.
1.2.5.1.4.1. Pro: In the U.S., for example,[less than 1% of the population](https://mises.org/library/america-struggling-hunger) faces hunger in a given year and [less than 4,000 people](http://www.worldlifeexpectancy.com/cause-of-death/malnutrition/by-country/) die from starvation or malnutrition. Estimates of how much a UBI would cost [vary](http://basicincome.org/news/2017/05/much-ubi-cost/) from 595 billion to over 4 trillion. Giving 1% of the population $150 a month for food to cover both starvation and food insecurity would only cost $5.9 billion.
1.2.5.1.4.1.1. Con: The costs to maintain people above the poverty line are far more than just "providing enough money to prevent starvation", as most industrialized countries are already monetarily capable of doing that.
1.2.5.1.4.1.2. Con: If only 1% of the population receives the money, then it is no longer a "Universal" Basic Income. Therefore, such a proposal cannot be used as an example cost for UBI.
1.2.5.2. Con: A UBI would enable other forms of revenue collection that could help offset its cost. Hence, it would be partially self-funded, so it can be affordable.
1.2.5.2.1. Pro: A UBI offsets its cost by creating wealth and increasing the amount of economic activity that states can tax.
1.2.5.2.1.1. Con: The degree to which it might "create wealth" to offset its costs is likely to be low. Overall, this does not suggest that UBIs would actually be affordable.
1.2.5.2.1.2. Pro: The size of an economy is determined by two main factors: first, the total amount of money available. But, more importantly: the speed of monetary circulation between actors \(a factor which is commonly overlooked\). Again in a very simplified model, everyone can get rich on just $1 along as it is transferred very rapidly in exchange for goods. In fact, just handing money to people to buy things may be the best stimulus for the economy imaginable \(but cause inflation, over stimulus\).
1.2.5.2.2. Pro: -> See 1.2.1.3.2.2.1.11.
1.2.5.3. Pro: Since this would have to be funded by taxes, as more recipients began to rely on it as a source of income there would be increasingly less funds to pay for it setting up an inevitable unfunded deficit.
1.2.5.3.1. Con: Experiments and proto trials suggest that governments could afford to implement a UBI.
1.2.5.3.1.1. Pro: Alaska has had a UBI-type programme [for many years](https://www.opendemocracy.net/en/opendemocracyuk/alaska-model-citizens-income-in-practice/), and yet can still afford it and [grow its GDP](https://www.bea.gov/news/2018/gross-domestic-product-state-2nd-quarter-2018).
1.2.5.3.2. Pro: Most countries are already in debt; a situation that the additional spending required for a UBI will only worsen.
1.2.5.3.2.1. Con: According to International Monetary Fund data released in 2017, the general level of government debt globally is projected to fall, on average, in the years up to 2022. [\(Source: IMF projections\)](http://bit.ly/2hMuYLM)
1.2.5.3.2.2. Con: A straight UBI cuts the costs associated with running a complicated welfare system, allowing more budget allocation to go directly to the people receiving welfare instead of it being sunk into administration.
1.2.5.3.2.2.1. Con: It is unrealistic to suggest that a decrease in administration cost for welfare systems will make a substantial impact on the total cost of the system.
1.2.5.3.2.3. Con: 1. Government debt itself is not inherently bad assuming that the debt is issued in currency it controls and that the increased money supply does not create excessive inflation. 2. Debt may, in fact, have a multiplier effect on the economy by creating a chain reaction of economic activity. In general, when money supply increases at the bottom or through direct spending \(like infrastructure\) the multiplier is greater because the money is spent faster than by tax cuts to the top.
1.2.5.3.3. Pro: A UBI could not be successfully funded as employed taxpayers make up the base, therefore publicly funded programs will become ripe for private take over.
1.2.5.3.4. Con: This is not the case if the increasing gains from automation were also taxed.
1.2.5.3.4.1. Con: Taxing automation would discourage automation, which UBI fundamentally relies on.
1.2.5.3.4.1.1. Con: UBI is in no way built on or relying on automation. It was proposed way before industrialization even: [en.wikipedia.org](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Basic_income#History)
1.2.5.3.5. Pro: If everyone got a paycheck whether they worked or not, there would be little incentive to pay the taxes required to disburse a UBI.
1.2.5.3.6. Pro: UBI requires funds to enact, and those funds must be generated before they're dispersed. If everyone is on a UBI, then no one is required to generate those funds, and it becomes a 'chicken or the egg' paradox. If only some are on a UBI, then non-UBI people will be forced to hand over a chunk of their generated income to fund UBI. There is no free meal ticket.
1.2.5.4. Con: A UBI need not be set at the poverty line to be an improvement over the status quo.
1.2.5.4.1. Con: Even if the UBI did not meet the poverty line—such as if it only met half of the poverty line—it would likely still be extraordinarily expensive while not even solving the issues at hand.
1.2.5.4.1.1. Con: Many countries are currently paying welfare to the unemployed and poor. In a first step, a UBI can be designed to cost the same amount of money as the existing social system. Wealthier people pay a share equivalent to their UBI \(=\> no change in total\), and unemployed people get as much welfare as before. This would bring many of the advantages of UBI \(psychology, less bureaucracy, only motivated people in retraining courses instead of forced attendance\) at no cost.
1.2.5.4.2. Pro: Many Americans work two or three jobs. Simply being able to quit one of these jobs would mean the world to millions of single working parents across this country.
1.2.5.4.2.1. Pro: Approximately [13 million](https://www.census.gov/library/stories/2019/06/about-thirteen-million-united-states-workers-have-more-than-one-job.html) Americans work more than one job, with a small percentage working two or more.
1.2.5.4.3. Pro: Even though an affordable UBI may not be sufficient to prevent poverty by Western standards, it should still provide enough to avoid extreme stress and inequality. With additional income or provision possible under a UBI low-paid employment will become both feasible and attractive as a form of self-provision.
1.2.5.5. Con: While there may not be any country that can afford a UBI today, it does not mean a particularly wealthy country could never afford one in future.
1.2.5.5.1. Pro: Increasing reliance on automation and artificial intelligence will provide levels of surplus that make a UBI more readily affordable.
1.2.5.5.1.1. Pro: Increases in technology will eventually result in an excess of productivity. Government issued UBI is necessary to distribute the returns on this productivity equitably.
1.2.5.5.1.1.1. Pro: When [productivity reaches incredibly high rates](https://www.technologyreview.com/s/519016/stop-saying-robots-are-destroying-jobs-they-arent/), there will be no need to have as many people working. Instead, we could afford to distribute a UBI to the increasingly large number of people who would no longer work.
1.2.5.5.1.1.1.1. Con: The excess productivity could be incentive to layoff the amount of workers it would displace and maintain all other operations as normal. This would create even more profit for corporations due to no longer needing to pay wages and supplemental benefits.
1.2.5.5.1.1.1.1.1. Pro: Corporations look after themselves before their employees. If it is beneficial to them to be rid of employees, there will definitely be cuts.
1.2.5.5.1.1.1.1.2. Con: Those profits would be taxed at a high rate and distributed as UBI. By distributing buying power to laid-off workers, UBI allows the capitalist market system to survive under conditions where many people are not working most of the time.
1.2.5.5.1.1.1.2. Con: Productivity growth[has been stagnant or in decline](https://www.oecd.org/std/productivity-stats/) in most OECD nations. The [data shows no relationship between productivity growth and net unemployment](https://www.technologyreview.com/s/519016/stop-saying-robots-are-destroying-jobs-they-arent/).
1.2.5.5.1.1.2. Con: The returns on this productivity could equally be distributed by lowering tax rates for low/middle income earners or increasing public funding for existing welfare, education, or healthcare systems.
1.2.5.5.1.1.2.1. Con: The chain of command of a public administration has fixed costs, plus the variable one related to corruption. The use of unneeded intermediaries decreases efficiency and raises costs.
1.2.5.5.1.1.3. Pro: A UBI is necessary and plausible if AI is to take a majority of jobs on the market. It is necessary as a majority of people who do work now will no longer be able to find jobs. It is plausible as AI will reduce the cost of many products and services available to us, thus we will be able to enjoy more with less, in which case the total living cost will be reduced, thus the amount of UBI necessary will be reduced \(discounting inflation etc\).
1.2.5.5.1.1.4. Pro: UBI allows us to continue with technological advances without the worry of losing jobs. In fact, because of technology and the ability it has to reduce the work needed by humans, UBI is necessary. Imagine a farmer in the 19th century and the amount of work needed to sustain a farm compared to today. The same amount of time, effort, and work could now feed hundreds if not thousands and all families should reap the rewards of this advancement!
1.2.5.5.1.1.5. Pro: UBI is a necessity for the economy in the future. Due to increases in automation, there will be a shortage of suppy for the workforce. In contrast to this, the people at the top of the business will earn huge amounts of money; creating an income gap like we’ve never seen before. It might not be time for UBI right now, but it’s definitely a solution to future problems.
1.2.5.5.1.1.6. Pro: Global wealth is not a fixed quantity: as technology improves, the world economy sees an aggregate benefit without requiring anyone else's wealth to decrease commensurately. UBI is an appropriate response to the aggregate increase of global wealth because of technology.
1.2.5.5.1.2. Pro: A society in a position to implement a UBI presupposes automation and AI. With mechanized efficiency and without the cost of human labor it is necessary to reevaluate the costs of living in a post-scarcity society when determining the economic viability of a UBI.
1.2.5.5.1.3. Con: This is just another way to describe heavier taxation of industry, which eventually damages an economy's competitiveness and tax revenues.
1.2.5.5.2. Pro: The affordability problem can be avoided by implementing a UBI only when an economy is mature enough that it is beneficial. A society requiring only ~70% of its citizens to contribute to the labor force is by definition producing enough excess that it can fund some minimal standard of living for the remaining 30%. Finding the relevant line is important, but this is a problem regarding execution, not the concept itself.
1.2.5.5.2.1. Con: This argument begs the question \(or is tautological\), at least as written; it is basically saying "UBIs could be beneficial when/where the implementation would be beneficial." It does not support the claim that any \(realistic\) economy will benefit from a UBI \(as opposed to other, means-tested welfare\).
1.2.5.5.2.2. Con: If the debate is made just about the ideal concept and ignores flawed execution, this makes [many "poor execution" arguments](https://chicagounbound.uchicago.edu/cgi/viewcontent.cgi?article=1387&context=public_law_and_legal_theory) against means-tested welfare irrelevant. In other words, you can't just compare the ideal UBI to a realistic \(imperfect\) means-tested welfare system; you have to compare ideal to ideal or realistic to realistic.
1.2.5.5.3. Con: The question seems to imply whether a UBI should be implemented today as part of the discussion around how to socially adjust to AI. To say 'we are not there yet' still argues that this shouldn't be allowed as we are incapable of implementing the idea. If it is impossible to implement, then we shouldn't consider it.
1.2.5.5.4. Con: UBI depends on an unsustainable level of global market wealth. If every economy achieved the kind of market success - that would be required to pay its citizens a UBI - we would need far more global growth. The Limits to Growth analysis \(see - [youtu.be](https://youtu.be/U7Z6h-U4CmI)\) clearly tells us this would require several more planets.
1.2.5.5.5. Con: It is also possible that if/when governments can eventually afford UBIs they will also have better or more means-tested welfare, making UBIs still inferior. In other words, in the future it might be better to just stick with means-tested welfare which has been increased/improved.
1.2.5.5.6. Con: Such a hypothetical future where the economy has massive surplus potentially may not even be struggling with extreme poverty. Unless the claim/we identify what this hypothetical/theoretical future society looks like, it is difficult to evaluate how practical/beneficial a UBI would be in the society.
1.2.5.6. Con: A UBI prevents a major rise in poverty in times of financial crisis, so the government can save money to where a UBI is affordable.
1.2.5.6.1. Pro: Universal income will protect people from the next inevitable banking crash, so governments do not spend money they cannot afford to help citizens out financially.
1.2.5.7. Con: Government legislative actions can restrict markets that impact the cost of living to keep prices regulated, which would decrease the amount of UBI needed to curb poverty.
1.2.5.8. Pro: A state that is losing its financial leeway cannot effectively pursue economic policies.
1.2.5.8.1. Pro: Providing citizens with a UBI may result in losing [20-30% of a nation's GDP](https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2019/may/06/universal-basic-income-public-realm-poverty-inequality).
1.2.5.8.2. Pro: Without sufficient funds, governments cannot help the economy in times of economic recession by launching stimulus plans.
1.2.5.8.3. Pro: Major investments in education or infrastructure can no longer be sustainably financed.
1.2.5.9. Pro: If government spending is significantly higher than revenue, the bonds issued by these governments may be downgraded by [rating agencies](https://www.nytimes.com/2010/03/16/business/global/16rating.html), making it harder and more expensive for them to raise funds on the capital markets.
1.2.5.9.1. Pro: [Higher interest rates](https://www.nytimes.com/2010/03/16/business/global/16rating.html) on debt add to the country’s overall debt burden and can force the government to reduce spending, increase taxes or both.
1.2.5.10. Con: Simulations and statistical analysis show that many governments could actually afford to fully implement a UBI.
1.2.5.10.1. Pro: Microsimulation from Barcelona University study shows that [only 3.4% of Spanish GDP](http://www.redrentabasica.org/rb/la-renta-basica-incondicional-y-como-se-puede-financiar-comentarios-a-los-amigos-y-enemigos-de-la-propuesta/) is needed to finance a sufficient UBI. This is without removing any funding for the army, health or education.
1.2.5.10.1.1. Con: Microsimulations cannot credibly simulate the effects of a nationwide UBI. Criticisms of UBI tend to center around its macroeconomic effects, which microsimulations do not address.
1.2.5.10.1.1.1. Con: Macroeconomic study shows that UBI would grow the economy. [rooseveltinstitute.org](http://rooseveltinstitute.org/modeling-macroeconomic-effects-ubi/)
1.2.5.10.1.1.1.1. Con: The study assumes that inflation will not significantly be affected by this change in the economy, even though it just seems that more people are spending, rather than the money being used more efficiently.
1.2.5.10.1.1.1.2. Con: The study does not seem to account for decreased-incentive effects. That is to say, the study proposes a policy of higher taxes on the rich, but does not account for whether this might discourage the rich from working as much, offering as many investments/loans, and/or expanding their business.
1.2.5.10.1.1.1.3. Con: The study seems to improperly estimate investment and loan effects. That is to say: the study claims overall economic activity would increase because the proposal would be funded largely by taxes on the higher-income population, which the study states tend to save a higher proportion of their money. However, a decrease in savings decreases the ability to invest/acquire loans \(contrary to its assumption that "Generally, investment is determined by the level of economic activity"\).
1.2.5.10.1.2. Con: Given Spain's GDP of [$1.2 Trillion \(USD\)](https://tradingeconomics.com/spain/gdp) and a population of 46.4 million, 3.4% of GDP equates to a UBI of only $879 per person.
1.2.5.10.1.2.1. Pro: Even assuming that $879/year in Spain equates to roughly $1160/year in the USA—since the cost of living in Spain is roughly 32% lower \(as suggested by [this site](http://www.mylifeelsewhere.com/cost-of-living/united-states/spain)\)—this is clearly an insufficient sum for a UBI.
1.2.5.10.1.2.2. Con: Not every single person in Spain would receive a UBI; such as non-citizens or children.
1.2.5.10.1.3. Con: Expressing costs in terms of GDP is misleading, because GDP generally does not link well with government taxation/spending. For example, in the US, federal and total government revenue in 2016 [was estimated to be $3.27 and $6.13 trillion](https://www.usgovernmentrevenue.com/federal_revenue) \(respectively\). US GDP in 2016, on the other hand, was multiple times that: [nearly $18.6 trillion](https://www.bea.gov/newsreleases/national/gdp/2017/pdf/gdp4q16_adv.pdf). Ultimately, many government programs can be made to look small when expressed in percentages of GDP, but that is misleading when talking about government spending.
1.2.5.10.1.4. Con: This 3.4% will have to be ploughed out of some other thing it has been already allocated for, and possibly allocated year on year. That departments and services that would be would suffer as a result.
1.2.5.10.1.4.1. Con: Most UBI implementations will make other benefits obsoleete. Like unemployment benefits, or multiple child monetary support.
1.2.5.10.2. Con: In the U.S. providing a UBI of $10,000 \(just below the poverty level\) to 300 million Americans would cost over $3 trillion dollars, easily [eclipsing current levels of tax revenue](http://www.cbpp.org/poverty-and-opportunity/commentary-universal-basic-income-may-sound-attractive-but-if-it-occurred).
1.2.5.10.2.1. Con: The average personal income tax rate in the USA is [above 35%](https://tradingeconomics.com/united-states/personal-income-tax-rate); assumed the UBI will be added to the taxable amount, a significant part would come back in taxes.
1.2.5.10.2.2. Con: This would also contain nearly all the welfare hand-outs so that some is not all new cost.
1.2.5.10.2.3. Con: This [ignores the fact](https://www.weforum.org/agenda/2017/01/why-we-should-all-have-a-basic-income/) that a UBI would also be implemented as a net transfer system: "In the same way it does not cost $20 to give someone $20 in exchange for $10, it does not cost $3 trillion to give every adult citizen $12,000 and every child $4,000, when every household will be paying varying amounts of taxes in exchange for their UBI. Instead it will cost around 30% of that, or about $900 billion."
1.2.5.10.2.3.1. Pro: $900 billion would be relatively easily accommodated through tiny tax rates increases \(5% for an extra $300b\) while also eliminating 25% of defence spending \($215b\), wage subsidies \($72b\), the home mortgage interest deduction \($70b\), and social security \($220b\).
1.2.5.10.2.3.1.1. Con: It is highly unlikely that there would be a 25% cut to military spending anytime soon.
1.2.5.10.2.3.1.1.1. Con: There are already people who are significantly dissatisfied with the amount of military spending, and advocate for a reduction in it.
1.2.5.10.2.3.1.1.1.1. Con: It does not matter what a few people think; people have always wanted to cut the military spending. In the end, a 25% reduction seems highly unrealistic.
1.2.5.10.2.3.1.2. Con: 2017 tax revenues are $3.3 trillion, $300 billion is 9% of that, tax rate would have to be increased by 9%, which is nearly double the "tiny" 5% suggested, the math is off. Also, I'm not sure where these numbers came from defense spending is $590B, and Social Security is $939B, 25% of each is $148B and $235B respectively, $383B total, compared to the $435B for those two that's specified, this is off by more than $50B! Please link a source...[Budget Data](https://www.gpo.gov/fdsys/pkg/BUDGET-2019-BUD/pdf/BUDGET-2019-BUD-24.pdf)
1.2.5.10.2.3.2. Con: "Net transfer" systems lose efficiency when the people who create value \(and subsequently earn money\) are taxed to cover for those who do not or cannot. Otherwise, deep/extreme socialism could solve all of our inequity and inefficiency problems. Traditional, means-tested welfare systems, while imperfect, can strike the proper balance of redistribution.
1.2.5.10.2.3.3. Con: This ignores the fact that taxes would still have to be raised \(or other programs cut\) in order to afford this program. Many people might end up getting more money than they have to pay in new taxes, but overall taxation would have to increase by at least however much money is provided through the UBI.
1.2.5.10.2.3.3.1. Con: It doesn't matter if the taxes are higher, when you are given the money to pay those higher taxes up-front.
1.2.5.10.2.3.4. Con: The US population is estimated at 252 million adults, and 74 million persons under age 18. At the levels you suggest that would require tax revenues of $3.3 trillion, even if you then redistribute that. Current US revenues are about $3.3 trillion, so you're talking about doubling the current US tax rates.[Federal Budget](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_federal_budget#/media/File:CBO_Infographic_2017.png) [Population Data](https://www.census.gov/quickfacts/fact/table/US/PST045217)
1.2.5.10.2.3.5. Con: While it doesn't cost $20 to give someone $20 in exchange for $10, you still have to have the $20 to start with. You'd still have to collect the full $3.3 trillion. If you're arguing for negative income tax, I suggest you tack on to one of the already existing claims for that.
1.2.5.10.2.3.6. Pro: If we assume UBI level is $12000, and it's a negative income tax, and if you make more than $12000, you get nothing. Then the whole program would require extra revenues in the US of about $600B. [Income Data](https://www2.census.gov/programs-surveys/cps/tables/pinc-01/2018/pinc01_1_1_1.xls)
1.2.5.10.2.3.6.1. Con: $12000, is well below the US poverty line of $22500. At that level it would cost $1.6 trillion, and this argument is under the claim, "UBI's that meet the proverty line are too expensive for governments to afford". At some point, yes you can lower the number enough that some\(not all\) people won't argue with you about the funding anymore, at that point would you even be making things enough better to worry about providing the benefit.
1.2.5.10.2.4. Con: Many UBI programs do not pay out to children. There are only about 250 million adults in the USA. Some programs only pay out to adults over 21 years of age, thereby reducing the number of benefactors to 230 million.
1.2.5.10.2.4.1. Con: In the end, this is only a small difference from the grandparent. If the UBI were $10,000 annually and there were roughly 200 million benefactors, expenses would be roughly $2 trillion—half of [current federal spending overall.](https://www.thebalance.com/current-u-s-federal-government-spending-3305763)
1.2.5.10.2.5. Con: [Here is an example](https://works.bepress.com/widerquist/75/) where it would cost $539 billion. It was made by a leading figure of the Basic Income Earth Network, based on data from the U.S. Census Bureau.
1.2.5.10.2.5.1. Con: The cited [source](http://basicincome.org/news/2017/05/much-ubi-cost/) is unreliable—even dubious.
1.2.5.10.2.5.1.1. Pro: The source makes an extremely dubious/misleading comparison between UBI and current entitlement spending. Specifically, the source says that the gross cost of a UBI program is "often-mentioned but not-very-meaningful," but in the very next part of the sentence, it compares the *net cost* of a UBI to the *gross cost* of current entitlement programs. If the source says that gross costs of a UBI aren't important, it shouldn't compare UBI's net costs to gross costs of entitlement programs.
1.2.5.10.2.5.1.2. Pro: The source itself describes its estimates as "back-of-the-envelope calculations," rather than even halfway rigorous economic models.
1.2.5.10.2.6. Con: This implies that the current taxation structure is the ideal structure. UBI allows the tax free threshold to be lowered, increasing revenue from the bottom, and a change to the tax structure for the top end of town more in line with the [New Deal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Deal) could very well allow it to be sustainable.
1.2.5.10.2.6.1. Con: If the tax free threshold is lowered \(i.e. if poorer people could be taxed\) this would undermine the value of the UBI.
1.2.5.10.2.6.2. Con: If the tax free threshold were lowered, this might discourage poorer people from increasing their income \(e.g. by working\) in the same way that traditional welfare does \(to an extent\).
1.2.5.10.2.6.3. Con: This merely assumes that increasing taxes on a poorer segment of society will noticeably offset the spending increases. On the contrary, it seems unlikely that this will have significant positive effects on revenue.
1.2.5.10.2.7. Con: With a UBI, the pressure on other social welfare programs is drastically decreased. In the US, the costs of social security, unemployment benefits, medicare and health rebates, and food entitlements [are approximately $2.2 trillion dollars](https://www.nationalpriorities.org/budget-basics/federal-budget-101/spending/). Cutting these programs would become feasible under a UBI, where it would replace most entitlement systems.
1.2.5.10.2.7.1. Pro: Even moderate reductions would provide feasibly close to one trillion dollars of freed government revenue.
1.2.5.10.2.7.1.1. Con: This would not be "new" government revenue; the "reductions" in traditional welfare program would be eclipsed by increases in spending on UBI.
1.2.5.10.2.7.2. Con: Without medicare, many low-income families would burn through their UBI very quickly, unless there's a medical system such as Canada has in place.
1.2.5.10.2.7.3. Con: If a $10,000/year UBI were enacted in the US, the total costs would be roughly around $2.5-3.5 trillion dollars \(depending on who receives benefits\). Thus, even if the traditional welfare programs were somehow fully cut, this would still represent an increase in overall spending by hundreds of billions of dollars.
1.2.5.10.2.7.3.1. Con: -> See 1.2.5.10.2.3.
1.2.5.10.2.7.4. Pro: In fact one of the very goals of a UBI is to obviate the need for any other social welfare programs. The saving it hopes to exploit is not just the money given to the needy in such a program but the entire overhead of delivering them \(including staff, real estate, and infrastructure\). All these savings go toward making a livable UBI feasible and attractive as an alternative to not addition to all other existing welfare programs. [Guaranteed Income as a Replacement for the Welfare State](http://www.fljs.org/files/publications/Murray.pdf)
1.2.5.10.2.8. Con: Providing a UBI, particularly when targeted at the consumer sector of the economy \(which traditionally has a very high multiplier effect on fiscal injections\) can also drastically improve economic performance to pay for increases in government spending.
1.2.5.10.2.8.1. Con: A UBI is not targeted toward the consumer sector of the economy; it is given to everyone equally.
1.2.5.10.2.8.1.1. Con: The vast majority of citizens are from the "consumer sector," so the program still has the highest impact on this sector.
1.2.5.10.2.8.2. Pro: Households in the US have very low rates of savings, and thus very high rates of spending. Even assuming a moderately high propensity to save, of 0.25 \(25% of every dollar of income saved\), the multiplier effect of additional money to households would sit around 3-4. The US currently collects around one quarter of GDP in the form of tax revenue, meaning that the UBI would come close to paying for itself in the form of tax revenue increases.
1.2.5.10.2.8.2.1. Con: Treating the UBI as a stimulus assumes that the poor are not already spending at similar rates through the status quo means-tested system, and any gains in pretax income given to the status quo middle class must surely be offset by any increases in the marginal tax rate that would result in this.
1.2.5.10.2.8.2.2. Con: Any discussion of consumption multipliers must factor for how savings are [multiplied](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/m/multipliereffect.asp) by the fractional reserve system of banking. Decreasing savings to increase consumption very reasonably may have no net-multiplier effect \(possibly even a negative multiplier effect\), and/or also may reasonably decrease the efficiency of combined consumption + investment.
1.2.5.10.2.8.3. Pro: Even when not directly claimed by tax revenue, increases to national wealth that come from increased economic activity justify economic policies to increase government wealth, such as international borrowing or printing new money. When these policies are implemented as the result of increased economic performance, negative side effects such as inflationary pressures and loss of credit worthiness can be avoided.
1.2.5.10.2.8.3.1. Con: This assumes that a UBI increases spending among low socio-economic status people over the current means-tested welfare system, which already provides substantial capital to this group to spend on necessities like food and shelter.
1.2.5.10.2.9. Con: The United States has already gone through many years of tax reductions, as well as reductions in government spending. Congruently with this the income disparity has skyrocketed, and many already live below the poverty line. Therefore the United States may be a bad example to evaluate the feasibility of implementing a UBI.
1.2.5.10.3. Con: In Sweden \(one of the largest welfare states\) if each citizen were given a basic liveable income of 12,000€/year, the annual cost of this policy alone would be as much as the [government spends in total](http://www.government.se/articles/2016/09/the-2017-budget-in-five-minutes/). This seems unaffordable.
1.2.5.10.3.1. Con: The government could just come up with a different tax structure.
1.2.5.10.3.1.1. Con: Tax cannot be higher than GDP \(that would mean 100% tax\). Taxation in many develop countries is close to \(or even above\) 50% of GDP. So taxes could never be more than doubled. [List of tax revenue to GDP ratio](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_countries_by_tax_revenue_to_GDP_ratio).
1.2.5.10.3.1.2. Pro: UBI doesn't have to be a new institution. It can be built into tax graduation or a "negative tax" for those who make less then UBI.
1.2.5.10.3.2. Con: It is in the largest welfare states that a UBI would be the most affordable because it would replace the already-expensive set of social services.
1.2.5.10.3.3. Pro: In Stockholm that income doesn't even cover the cost of a one bed flat. A typical small radhaus \(terrace\) costs over 20,000 euros a year in rent.
1.2.5.10.3.3.1. Con: While Stockholm is the most expensive place to live in Sweden, someone living on welfare in the existing system \(income ~€12000\) can't affort that anyway, so the point is moot.
1.2.5.10.3.4. Pro: In the best case scenario, the government would have to scrap many major welfare schemes in order to afford the cost of a UBI.
1.2.5.10.3.4.1. Con: That's the point. The idea is to replace means-tested benefits with a universal income that a person can always rely on having.
1.2.5.10.3.5. Con: The total cost of UBI is not equal to the amount of money paid to individuals - rather, it is the differential increase or decrease in the wealth of the nation: [scottsantens.com](http://www.scottsantens.com/the-cost-of-universal-basic-income-is-the-net-transfer-amount-not-the-gross-price-tag).
1.2.5.10.3.5.1. Con: Regardless of how you account for the overall net cost, it is valid to point out that multi-trillion dollar government program would require the government to spend that many trillions of dollars. Therefore, the money has to come from somewhere, and not only is it necessary to account for the costs, this demand for money can cause problems such as if the government has to raise taxes or change tax structures in order to pay for it.
1.2.5.10.3.6. Con: It could be set as a percentage of GDP and that people could choose if they want UBI, or not \(for the sake of others\). For instance - people with high income from their work could reject their UBI for government budget, charity, or someone else, or to make UBI higher \(could be done only once per year to lower costs of administration\). This could teach people solidarity.
1.2.5.10.3.6.1. Con: This would be a very ineffective at reducing costs, because of course most people would still opt in \(given that it is basically a free $10,000 payment\).
1.2.6. Con: A UBI would allow governments to foster liberal economic policies without throwing people into economic and social insecurity.
1.2.6.1. Pro: If high enough, a UBI could allow the removal of the minimum wage since it provides sufficient bargaining power to prevent exploitation.
1.2.6.1.1. Pro: Removing the minimum wage would allow companies to hire lower-skilled, less experienced, or less productive workers at appropriate rates.
1.2.6.1.1.1. Con: This could bring down the wages of skilled workers as well.
1.2.6.1.1.1.1. Con: With the UBI, skilled workers might still end up making about as much as they were before.
1.2.6.1.1.1.1.1. Pro: Companies would still have to give decent wages as an incentive for skilled workers. People may be less willing to work if they're already provided for, they'd work to receive extra comfort such as being able to afford their own home or hobbies.
1.2.6.1.2. Pro: Without a minimum wage, companies [should be able to](https://www.ecnmy.org/learn/your-government/regulations/are-minimum-wages-good/) hire more people.
1.2.6.1.3. Pro: Without a minimum wage, prices would generally go down on products made in that country, which could help the economy.
1.2.6.2. Pro: Liberal economic policies are worth pursuing.
1.2.6.2.1. Pro: By virtue of increasing economic liberalization across the world, liberal economic policies are useful for countries to develop ties with other countries.
1.2.6.3. Pro: This is of primary importance in the age of AI and robotics.
1.2.7. Pro: A UBI might require higher taxes, which would hurt the economy.
1.2.7.1. Con: Technological advances in wealthy countries have created a [surplus of wealth](https://data.worldbank.org/indicator/NY.GDP.MKTP.CD) that can be shared directly as income to citizens rather than indirectly through tax spending.
1.2.7.2. Pro: In the short run, tax increases have a [negative impact on demand](https://www.economicshelp.org/blog/145810/economics/the-impact-of-taxation/). As households and companies have less available income and capital due to higher taxes, they will spend and invest less.
1.2.7.2.1. Pro: Higher taxes will mean many have less disposable income, which means they will spend less in the economy, decreasing aggregate demand.
1.2.7.2.2. Con: A UBI as additional form of income mitigates this negative effect, especially for those with lower incomes.
1.2.7.2.3. Con: However they will have paid a fair tax rate that benefits all citizens. Instead of low taxes lining the pockets of investors and private companies delivering the universal services a government should provide its people.
1.2.7.3. Pro: In the long run [high marginal tax rates](http://www.taxpolicycenter.org/briefing-book/how-do-taxes-affect-economy-long-run) have a negative impact on the supply by discouraging work, saving, investment, and innovation.
1.2.7.3.1. Con: The IMF released a report claiming that higher taxes would [not negatively affect growth](https://www.theguardian.com/business/2017/oct/11/imf-higher-taxes-rich-inequality-jeremy-corbyn-labour-donald-trump).
1.2.7.3.1.1. Con: Higher taxes, particularly among the rich, would likely lead to an increase in tax evasion, thereby decreasing the total amount of money the government receives.
1.2.7.3.1.2. Pro: The bulk of tax increases could be levied upon the super-wealthy. In the US in particular this would only marginally shift that top tax rate [back to its historic norm of 50%+](http://www.ctj.org/pdf/regcg.pdf) which did not seem to overly constrain growth between 1917 and 1987.
1.2.7.3.1.2.1. Pro: There already are initiatives founded by [millionaires](https://www.theguardian.com/us-news/2016/mar/21/40-millionaires-ask-new-york-raise-taxes-wealthy-1-percent-plan-fairness) to raise the tax for the rich. This shows their willingness to contribute more to society.
1.2.7.3.1.2.1.1. Con: There several offshore tax schemes - such as those discovered by the [Panama Papers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Panama_Papers) - that prove wealthy individuals are willing to try to hide their assets to contribute less.
1.2.7.3.1.2.2. Con: As the very richest are able to [shape tax policy](https://www.nytimes.com/2015/12/30/business/economy/for-the-wealthiest-private-tax-system-saves-them-billions.html?_r=0), it is unlikely they would accept extensive tax increases.
1.2.7.3.1.2.2.1. Con: That doesn't mean that it can't happen in a democracy.
1.2.7.3.1.2.3. Pro: A UBI would have a much greater positive impact on the economy that tax cuts, as giving $1 to those with lower incomes has three times the growth effects on GDP as compared to giving the same $1 to high income individuals, due to the economic multiplier effect. [huffingtonpost.com](https://www.huffingtonpost.com/scott-santens/the-economist-just-came-o_b_7447312.html)
1.2.7.3.1.2.4. Pro: There would be enough wealth if it were to be distributed down from [billionaires](http://time.com/money/5112462/billionaires-made-so-much-money-last-year-they-could-end-extreme-poverty-seven-times/). No one needs that much money \([apparently](https://qz.com/1211957/how-much-money-do-people-need-to-be-happy/) money doesnt increase happiness after $105,000 per year\). \(There should be a cut off point; if you make more than 500,000 a year \(and slowly decrease it to 105,000 to reduce shock\), you only keep 200,000, the rest goes to the government, who can make much better use of it.\)
1.2.7.3.1.2.4.1. Con: There would be difficulties implementing this, as many wealthy people would feel robbed of the fruits of "their" hard work, ignoring or overlooking the advantages of their privilege.
1.2.7.3.1.3. Pro: Misinformation by government that lower taxes are beneficial to society and the citizenry is a fallacy perpetrated to ensure votes at the ballot box.
1.2.7.4. Con: A UBI would not have to be paid for by taxes. As with other forms of government spending, a UBI could be paid for by fiat spending \(i.e. 'printing money'\).
1.2.7.4.1. Con: Issuing enough fiat currency to fund a UBI must be balanced against the compounding [effect](https://www.economicshelp.org/blog/634/economics/the-problem-with-printing-money/) this would have on the economy and inflation since there is little point in providing a universal income if, as result, goods are priced above the budget it would allow.
1.2.7.4.1.1. Con: The inflationary constraint upon fiat currency is not some arbitrary number, but instead the real productive capacity of the economy in question. Given the profound levels of current waste and untapped potential, we are nowhere close to maxing out our real productive capacity. The best way to avoid critical inflation from fiat spending is thus to spend it on supporting real productive capacity, rather than upon speculation or rent-seeking \(title-income, interest, etc.\).
1.2.7.4.2. Pro: The US dollar is a sovereign fiat currency. Congress creates it by decree with spending and can afford anything for sale in dollars with no structural revenue constraints or the threat of involuntary default on any obligation denominated in dollars. The only constraint on a fiat currency is inflation, not the ability to "fund" anything with taxation. Taxes are not "revenue" for fiat system, as the issuer of currency has no need to "get" currency to spend. Affordability is not on the table.
1.2.7.4.3. Pro: An economy using a fiat currency is not revenue constrained. The issuer of the currency doesn't need to "get" currency from anyone to spend. Taxes only serve to drive a currency's demand and to draw down currency to prevent inflation. Only currencies pinned to a commodity \(gold\) or fixed exchange rate are dependent upon revenue.
1.2.7.4.4. Pro: The US dollar is a sovereign fiat currency that we can never run out of and we can never involuntarily fail to pay any obligation denominated in it. This takes "affordability" off the table.[youtube.com](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=i35uBVeNp6c&t=169s) [neweconomicperspectives.org](http://neweconomicperspectives.org/2012/03/where-did-the-federal-reserve-get-all-that-money.html)
1.2.7.4.5. Pro: In the future, countries will afford the UBI, as part of a [macro economic process](http://rooseveltinstitute.org/modeling-macroeconomic-effects-ubi/) when unemployment becomes too high and the economy is near collapse and/or major deflation. Modern monetary theory itself can prove this affordability, especially when a country has sovereignty over its' own currency. Please don't forget new currencies and international influence in regards to the UBI that will help other countries afford this, if the need arises. [rooseveltinstitute.org](http://rooseveltinstitute.org/modeling-macroeconomic-effects-ubi/)
1.2.7.5. Pro: A UBI would take money away from the rich, which would greatly decrease job creation and economic growth.
1.2.7.5.1. Con: Rich entrepeneurs may prefer investing in automated process to save on labor costs. This would mean that employment may not necessarily increase if entrepeneurs are given greater freedom.
1.2.7.5.1.1. Pro: [Automation](http://www.forbes.com/sites/eriksherman/2016/12/17/automation-has-created-more-jobs-in-the-past-but-will-it-now/#55efab0627fd) will not create new jobs like other technologies did.
1.2.7.5.1.1.1. Pro: The loss of jobs due to automation will dramatically reduce social mobility as most individuals' skills will be rendered obsolete — particularly those who perform menial work.
1.2.7.5.1.1.1.1. Pro: [Automation](http://www.businessinsider.de/stephen-hawking-ai-automation-middle-class-jobs-most-dangerous-moment-humanity-2016-12?r=UK&IR=T) will accelerate the already widening economic inequality around the world as very small groups of individuals can make enormous profits while employing very few people.
1.2.7.5.1.1.1.1.1. Con: Blaming technological change for inequality absolves the elites of their responsibility. Technology transforms production, but [power and politics determine](https://www.theguardian.com/technology/2016/may/16/universal-basic-income-equality-tech-silicon-valley) how the dividends are distributed.
1.2.7.5.1.1.1.1.2. Pro: A storybook "[rags-to-riches](http://www.businessinsider.com/rags-to-riches-stories-2011-11)" career will become next to impossible to achieve.
1.2.7.5.1.1.1.1.3. Pro: As automation replaces human workers, it's likely something like this will be used to create a new serf class, and a new land owner nobility, essentially repeating the middle ages. In other words, as more people are unemployed, the government will use UBI to prevent uprising and civil unrest against the economic elite. This is very likely, but it is not at all a good thing.
1.2.7.5.1.1.1.1.4. Pro: The exponentially accelerating progress in robotics and AI will inevitably result \(as we're starting to see already\) in the elimination of essentially all jobs and corporate shareholders reaping all the benefits. There are only two possible outcomes:  either we tax the corporate profits resulting from automation to provide a UBI, or there will be blood in the streets.
1.2.7.5.1.1.1.1.5. Pro: An UBI can unleash the creative forces once the need for basic survival imperatives and creature comforts are removed. An infrastructure must be created to capitalize and support on creative, innovative surge in new paradigms. Industrial monopolies would represent a danger to this system.
1.2.7.5.1.1.1.2. Con: Having a universal basic income will help give those whose skills are rendered obsolete through automation the time, energy, and financial support to learn new skills or retire.
1.2.7.5.1.1.1.3. Pro: This is due to the production of digital technologies that [eliminate the need](https://www.technologyreview.com/s/531726/technology-and-inequality/) for many middle-class jobs.
1.2.7.5.1.1.2. Pro: Experts predict a "[jobless future](http://www.economist.com/news/special-report/21700758-will-smarter-machines-cause-mass-unemployment-automation-and-anxiety)" as most jobs can be broken down into a series of routine tasks, more and more of which can be done by machines.
1.2.7.5.1.1.2.1. Pro: According to [Stephen Hawking](http://www.bbc.com/news/technology-30290540), full artificial intelligence could supplant the human race.
1.2.7.5.1.1.2.2. Con: The fact that a computer can do something better than a human being doesn’t mean that the computer will replace the human being. The market will determine whether it is economical to do so, given the costs and perceived benefits.
1.2.7.5.1.1.2.3. Con: When machines replace one kind of human capability,[new human experiences and capabilities emerge.](https://www.forbes.com/sites/stevedenning/2015/06/04/the-robots-are-not-coming/#574dc6131913) These might be mostly higher value and offer more interesting work than the experiences and capabilities that have been replaced by machines.
1.2.7.5.1.1.3. Pro: The idea of automation and [artificial intelligence](http://www.forbes.com/sites/eriksherman/2016/12/17/automation-has-created-more-jobs-in-the-past-but-will-it-now/2/#33a8b61b23ae) is different than other technological inventions before as it merges the existing ideas of machinery, electricity and computing to something entirely new.
1.2.7.5.1.1.4. Pro: General artificial intelligence will be able to learn at a faster rate than humans.
1.2.7.5.1.1.5. Con: Experts suggest that automation and AI will create more jobs than it will replace. [www.cnbc.com](https://www.cnbc.com/2017/06/16/ai-robots-jobs-alphabet-eric-schmidt.html)
1.2.7.5.1.2. Pro: In the current age of information technology, a very small amount of people can gather a huge amount of money without any need to significantly increase the employee base of a company. Any major website company is a perfect example of that.
1.2.7.5.2. Con: The rich can only benefit from a wealthier middle and working class. With more money being spent businesses will have higher profits, in turn giving the rich more money.
1.2.7.5.3. Con: It is estimated that having a UBI for 8 years will grow the USA's GDP by [2.5 trillion dollars.](https://www.cnbc.com/2017/08/31/1000-per-month-cash-handout-would-grow-the-economy-by-2-point-5-trillion.html)
1.2.7.5.3.1. Con: An increase in the GDP is not in itself a good thing, especially when it just seems to be "more money is spent" \(as the study seems to suggest\); one must link to how quality of life is affected by a change in the economy.
1.2.7.5.3.2. Con: The linked study makes multiple questionable/disputed assumptions.
1.2.7.5.4. Pro: The rich are the biggest [job creators](https://www.forbes.com/sites/johntamny/2013/12/11/sorry-henry-blodget-but-the-rich-do-create-nearly-every-job/) in the economy.
1.2.7.5.5. Pro: The rich are disproportionately likely to [invest their wealth](https://www.mercatus.org/bridge/commentary/economic-effects-ultra-rich-wealth-tax) compared to other classes.
1.2.7.5.6. Con: The rich are not needed to create jobs.
1.2.7.5.6.1. Pro: Job creation is driven by demand of goods and services, not the amount of wealth the rich have. UBI would increase such demand, thereby creating jobs.
1.2.7.5.6.1.1. Con: Only the "rich" have the capital to start such enterprises as to produce these new products and services you are suggesting. Starting business isn't a cheap thing, you need tens of thousands to hundreds of thousands of dollars of liquid capital for a bank to even consider giving you a loan to help you get started.
1.2.7.6. Pro: The reduced incentive to work for those who are guaranteed an income will reduce government tax revenue.
1.2.7.6.1. Con: Income taxation is not the only source of government revenue. Money is also made from exports, property taxes, the sale of bonds, and government investments.
1.2.7.6.2. Con: Simultaneously, the revenue from sales taxes is likely to increase as a UBI provides a secure source of income for citizens that can be used to consume goods and services.
1.2.7.6.3. Pro: A UBI would enable people to work less or even leave the workforce permanently.
1.2.7.6.3.1. Pro: It is in the best interest of society to heavily incentivize all people to participate in the workforce.
1.2.7.6.3.1.1. Pro: Increased labor participation is [important for economic development.](http://www.worldwatch.org/increase-labor-force-can-be-engine-development-0)
1.2.7.6.3.1.1.1. Con: In times of increasing automation, 3D printing, and intelligent software; labor participation has become less important.
1.2.7.6.3.1.1.1.1. Pro: As our society becomes more and more automated and a society that will function with less human workers becomes possible, it may be time to reexamine our assumptions about what actually makes a society "wealthy".
1.2.7.6.3.1.1.1.1.1. Pro: If people are too busy earning money to enjoy the benefits of it, there is no point to their actions.
1.2.7.6.3.1.1.1.1.1.1. Pro: Furthermore, depending on what form this capital takes and what actions are taken to increase it, their actions may in fact be actively harmful and destructive, either to other humans or to animals, land, and the environment.
1.2.7.6.3.1.1.1.2. Con: We are [very far from a world without need for human labor](http://secondmachineage.com/). There are many problems that can only be addressed by humans in healthcare, childcare, eldercare, cleaning the environment, etc.
1.2.7.6.3.1.1.2. Pro: If our productivity levels stay the same, then the fewer people work, the smaller general welfare will grow.
1.2.7.6.3.1.2. Con: Societal wealth is a matter of total working hour productivity. More working-hours means more produced, and more material wealth. It is a function of society to decide how much work \(and thus wealth\) for which it is worth sacrificing free time for.
1.2.7.6.3.1.2.1. Con: Social wealth is not a matter of total working hours as claimed. Labour productivity depends much more on the amount of capital present in a country than on total working hours, as shown by the difference in productivity between the US and a country like India. If a UBI removes the incentive to build capital or to maintain existing capital, it will decrease social wealth, regardless of total hours worked.
1.2.7.6.3.1.2.1.1. Con: Given an amount of "capital"—or, more correctly, a given level of technical progress—the amount of labor exercised correlates linearly to the wealth produced until the limits of technology to scale are reached. New technology raises the carry capacity by allowing further scaling, and reduces the invested labor per unit of productive output.
1.2.7.6.3.1.2.1.2. Con: "Capital" is just an abstraction of labor—labor is invested to produce and maintain capital—and generally describes technology, either knowledge of processes \("human capital"\) or tools \(e.g. machines\). It is more-correct to say productivity is determined by technical progress.
1.2.7.6.3.1.2.1.3. Con: Humans economize: they will expend the minimum means to produce the maximum ends. If a UBI were to reduce the total labor, labor would be organized to maximize productivity—to maintain the highest level of technical progress so as to maximize the unit production per labor hour.
1.2.7.6.3.1.2.1.3.1. Con: The fact that society would economize is irrelevant because it does not imply society would be better off. If half of your wealth is destroyed, you economize, exactly because you are worse off than before. But the fact that you economize does not mean it is a good idea to destroy wealth. If UBI removes incentive to build capital or maintain existing capital, you surely economize but you are also worse off.
1.2.7.6.3.1.2.1.3.1.1. Con: The capital you would lose in this scenario would be a waste. Building a factory which cannot be staffed is a use of labor which could otherwise produce actual things and thus wealth; building said factory would be a destruction of wealth. Building or maintaining the capital you claim would go unbuilt or unmaintained would decrease wealth.
1.2.7.6.3.1.2.1.4. Con: This is internally-inconsistent, as it implies that the amount of capital required is fixed and does not grow or shrink with the amount of use to which that capital is put. The is implied by reasoning that an economy cannot scale down to any significant degree, in turn implied by reasoning that less labor means the capital used by that labor goes neglected and decays—rather than that some of the capital becomes excess as production scales down.
1.2.7.6.3.1.2.2. Con: The sentiment is true, but working more \(over employment\) has been known to cause economic crashes. Certain levels of 'productivity' just aren't sustainable due to phenomena [such as the business cycle \(see chapters 9-12\)](http://www.academia.edu/24045849/Macroeconomics_Third_Edition_by_Charles_I._Jones_W.W._Norton_2014).
1.2.7.6.3.1.2.2.1. Pro: Overwork also has health implications, which increases healthcare costs as well as decreasing productivity.
1.2.7.6.3.1.3. Con: A society exists for far greater purposes than providing meaningless work for factory fodder. People who are free from mindless tasks \(that machines can do\) are free to pursue more creative and fulfilling tasks.
1.2.7.6.3.1.3.1. Pro: If someone can get by on UBI while they chase their dreams, great things can happen. History is littered with geniuses in their fields who have died young/been forced to abandon their work through poverty.
1.2.7.6.3.1.3.2. Pro: It is in the best interest of society to have a happy and healthy workforce. It is detrimental to society and individuals to have participation in the workforce so heavily incentivized that it leads to mental and physical illness.
1.2.7.6.3.1.4. Con: As automation and machines eventually displace more and more humans from the workforce, having people work less may actually have a positive effect.
1.2.7.6.3.1.4.1. Pro: Systematic unemployment due to lack of available jobs is a future threat to human welfare even in the current economy, and that threat is [likely to increase in the future](https://www.weforum.org/agenda/2016/01/what-is-the-future-of-work/).
1.2.7.6.3.1.4.1.1. Con: We should be trying to [create more jobs](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=_n6ivGgb9RY) and not finding ways to get people out of the workforce.
1.2.7.6.3.1.4.1.1.1. Pro: Job creation increases economic well-being.  Like it did in the [Reagan era](https://www.forbes.com/sites/kylesmith/2014/09/11/sorry-obama-fans-reagan-did-better-on-jobs-and-growth/#549b9fab55e1)!
1.2.7.6.3.1.4.1.1.1.1. Con: It is doubtful that all relevant factors are the same as they were in the Reagan era.
1.2.7.6.3.1.4.1.1.2. Pro: More jobs means a better economy because more jobs means more people with money.  More people with money means more people buying things which means more profits for companies which means even more jobs!
1.2.7.6.3.1.4.1.1.2.1. Con: Given [that over the last few decades real wages have barely budged](http://www.pewresearch.org/fact-tank/2014/10/09/for-most-workers-real-wages-have-barely-budged-for-decades/), this calculus clearly no longer holds. Any growth in jobs and the economy has not lead to increases in consumer spending power; because costs are instead rising in terms of basic expenses such as food and healthcare.
1.2.7.6.3.1.4.1.1.3. Con: Creating jobs is nice and even if we 'should' try, it is likely that will be [impossible in the future](https://www.weforum.org/agenda/2016/01/what-is-the-future-of-work/), at least that 100% employment is impossible.
1.2.7.6.3.1.4.1.1.4. Con: This implies \(or avoids saying\) we should avoid UBI in order to drive job creation. Individuals benefit from UBI and corporations create jobs. Supporting individuals when corporations fail to create jobs makes sense. Whether or not corporations should or shouldn't create jobs is irrelevant.
1.2.7.6.3.1.4.1.1.5. Con: UBI is not a way of getting people out of the workforce; it is a better way of supporting those who are between jobs, those who are working in jobs that don't suit them, and those who can't find a job they are able to do. UBI could allow them the opportunity to find a better fit for their talents and abilities, or allow them the freedom to learn more in order to do so.
1.2.7.6.3.1.4.1.2. Pro: The argument strengthens if humanity rejects its current apocalyptic drive for consumption. To the extent unemployment is unavoidable and not a result of personal inadequacy or market down-cycles, then UBI because a necessary protection insurance against the societal costs of widespread crime and poverty.
1.2.7.6.3.1.4.1.3. Pro: When employment is working at a task better done my machines and only preserved as human labor for the sake of creating jobs, 100% unemployment should be a goal not something to fear.
1.2.7.6.3.1.5. Con: Participation in the work force has external costs.
1.2.7.6.3.1.6. Pro: The government's priority should always be to encourage employment instead of giving people the hope that they can subsist on subsidies, or innovation and production will stagnate.
1.2.7.6.3.1.7. Con: Currently, people work out of social and economical obligation. A UBI diminishes this obligation, enhancing people's sense of freedom and agency.
1.2.7.6.3.1.7.1. Con: People are free to choose not to work in capitalist systems. The fact that there are consequences to that choice doesn't make it economic coercion.
1.2.7.6.3.1.7.1.1. Con: By logical extension: when pointing a gun to an individual's head and asking them to do something, said individual is free not to do said thing. The fact that there are likely going to be consequences to that choice is exactly what makes it coercive.
1.2.7.6.3.1.7.2. Con: It is not the job of government to unconditionally support individual's 'freedom' if they are not willing to engage in the economic exchange of labor.
1.2.7.6.3.1.7.3. Pro: For some people, who are genuinely not fit for the workforce, this is a good thing. These people can now live much more enjoyable lives.
1.2.7.6.3.2. Con: People working less actually increases employment as, assuming demand remains constant, other workers will be brought in to make up for it. Lower average hours worked with sustained demand will lead to higher employment rate until employment reaches a maximum.
1.2.7.6.3.3. Pro: Increases in population combine with increases in productivity to ensure that there will always be less work needed.
1.2.7.6.3.4. Con: Existing welfare schemes reduce people's incentive to work because earning more money reduces the number of benefits they accrue. A UBI fixes this and actually provides [a higher marginal return to earning an income](https://surlysubgroup.com/2016/06/13/universal-bassic-income-and-marginal-rates/).
1.2.7.6.3.4.1. Pro: In the US, the effective marginal tax rates on the poor [are often as high as 109% of their income](https://surlysubgroup.com/2016/06/13/universal-bassic-income-and-marginal-rates/) because of the corresponding reduction in welfare benefits as they earn more.
1.2.7.6.3.4.2. Pro: UBI does not punish those of lower classes who begin to earn more money, but instead keeps the incentive to move up in income to the point where UBI is no longer necessary for the individual. Therefore this saves the government more money over time in welfare spending.
1.2.7.6.3.4.3. Con: Even if the existing welfare schemes are \(supposedly\) worse than UBIs, welfare schemes could be improved so that the disincentive to increase income is not as steep as the [\(1999\) source](https://www.epionline.org/wp-content/studies/shaviro_02-1999.pdf) of the [parent's source](https://surlysubgroup.com/2016/06/13/universal-bassic-income-and-marginal-rates/) illustrates. In other words, traditional welfare programs could be fixed rather than eliminated and replaced with UBI.
1.2.7.6.3.4.3.1. Con: This argument is a status quo bias. It adresses only one of the current wellfare system's shortcomings but maintains that it should be preserved to avert change.
1.2.7.6.3.4.3.2. Pro: If the main problem with current welfare is the disincentive to work, a reworked version of [workfare](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Workfare) can solve that problem without the need for the high cost of a UBI. Requiring some level of employment before social benefits supplement that income \(for those physically and mentally able\) incentivizes employment and reduces overall welfare costs because the government is only supplementing, not providing the income.
1.2.7.6.3.4.3.2.1. Con: Invisible disabilities, or those who have difficulty proving their disability \(or do not have full information about the availability of schemes\), could easily slip through the cracks. A UBI allows no cracks by including every individual.
1.2.7.6.3.4.4. Pro: A UBI is a fairer alternative to most welfare programs because everyone gets it — not just the people who need it the most which could be interpreted as rewarding incompetence/laziness.
1.2.7.6.3.4.4.1. Con: It really makes no difference to the rich though. It only helps those who aren't being taxed already, since those with enough tax to outweigh the UBI will not really get any benefit. If anything, a UBI, if it is more desirable to live off of than a taxable income, may cause some of the rich to stop working.
1.2.7.6.3.4.4.1.1. Con: Much of the money 'earned' by the wealthy is earned through investment, not work, which is still taxable income. Also, as the wealthy tend to make significantly more than would be provided via UBI, the idea that they would 'stop working' to get that free money instead is fairly unlikely.
1.2.7.6.3.4.5. Pro: UBI would correct the unintended consequence of broken families and encourage marriage and stable family structure. Current welfare discourages marriage as evidenced by the rise in single mother households whereas UBI would not penalize a single mother for the added income of a spouse but instead encourage the combining of incomes through marriage.
1.2.7.6.3.4.5.1. Con: Providing for the people through government is destructive of families, as it directly competes with or purports to replace [the responsibility](https://www.lds.org/scriptures/ot/gen/3.19) of [fathers to provide](https://www.lds.org/topics/family-proclamation?lang=eng&old=true) for their families.
There is [no more economical way](https://www.forbes.com/sites/aparnamathur/2015/10/30/the-family-foundations-of-economic-growth/#3a60007a2a9b) to live than in intact families as the fundamental provident unit of society.
1.2.7.6.3.4.5.2. Con: Traditional welfare systems can exist which are neutral or even incentivize marriage.
1.2.7.6.3.4.5.2.1. Pro: In the UK it was historically very beneficial to be married, although it is now rather neutral, [due to](https://www.money.co.uk/guides/how-will-getting-married-affect-your-finances.htm) married couples/marriage allowance, tax bracket sharing, and tax free gifts.
1.2.7.6.3.4.5.2.2. Pro: Many countries, [like Germany](https://www.expattax.de/marriage-tax-classes-germany/), have joint taxation. This benefits married couples who can choose to submit taxes together, meaning they can often share unused tax credits.
1.2.7.6.3.4.5.2.3. Pro: Proposed changes to the Swiss marriage tax model make getting married [more financially beneficial](http://www.loc.gov/law/foreign-news/article/switzerland-abolition-of-marriage-tax-penalty-proposed/) than being an unmarried couple.  It also adds financial incentive for couples with children to get married.
1.2.7.6.3.4.5.3. Con: Much of any marriage penalty comes from tax changes, rather than welfare, because most couples are not on welfare. 

UBI would not affect the taxation system, and therefore would not have as much of an impact on marriage incentives as a tax reform world.
1.2.7.6.3.4.5.4. Con: Increasing numbers of single mother households just suggests that relationships are not lasting as long.

If a correlation was to be drawn between current welfare systems and lack of marriage, we should look at numbers of unmarried couples, not single mothers.
1.2.7.6.3.4.5.5. Con: A UBI would not encourage combining of incomes through marriage, as any family would get the same benefits even if they stayed as an unmarried couple.
1.2.7.6.3.4.6. Pro: In the current system, if one gets a job, they get reduced benefits, resulting in less stability and netting less capital for subsistence. With UBI of 1k a month, working merely increases the capital for subsistence and [promotes working](http://fortune.com/2017/09/03/universal-basic-income-economy-study/), even with a minimum wage job, to thrust an impoverished household into the lower middle class.
1.2.7.6.3.4.6.1. Pro: The small increase in income from getting a minimum wage job, at the cost of hours of free time, often does not motivate unemployed people to find one. A UBI, retained when entering the workforce, provides better incentive.
1.2.7.6.3.5. Con: A UBI will enable unhappy workers who detract from work productivity to leave their jobs, so happy and productive workers can take their place.
1.2.7.6.3.5.1. Con: Those who detract from productivity will automatically be replaced through market forces.
1.2.7.6.3.5.1.1. Pro: Employees who fail to be productive will be let go and replaced by new employees who can do a better job.
1.2.7.6.3.5.2. Pro: Most people go to work today merely because they will not be able to feed themselves or their family otherwise. A UBI will remove that obligation.
1.2.7.6.3.5.2.1. Con: [Choice](https://www.psychologytoday.com/blog/ulterior-motives/201404/why-having-the-choice-do-nothing-is-so-important) has been found to be a powerful motivator, as a positive relationship has been found between one's ability to choose not to work and one's commitment to work.
1.2.7.6.3.5.2.1.1. Con: The illusion of choice can also be harmful when it comes to reinforcing harmful social norms. For example, women may be more likely to 'choose' not to work under a UBI, but this choice may be heavily influenced by social messaging and gender norms.
1.2.7.6.3.5.2.1.1.1. Con: Approximately [40%](http://www.nytimes.com/2013/05/30/business/economy/women-as-family-breadwinner-on-the-rise-study-says.html) of American mothers are the breadwinners in their household.
