This number increases when non-married couples are taken in consideration. Therefore, it is probable that even with a UBI, the representation of women in the workforce will continue to grow.
1.2.7.6.3.5.2.1.1.2. Con: With a UBI, women currently dependent on their partner's salary will now have their own money.
1.2.7.6.3.5.2.1.2. Con: This commitment needs to be evaluated relative to the net loss of workers who choose to opt out. Though the quality of workers may marginally increase, the quantity decreases to a more significant and harmful extent.
1.2.7.6.3.5.2.1.2.1. Con: [An experiment](https://public.econ.duke.edu/~erw/197/forget-cea%20\(2\).pdf) with a UBI in Canada resulted in households reducing their workloads by about [13%](https://qz.com/765902/ubi-wouldnt-mean-everyone-quits-working/).
1.2.7.6.3.5.2.1.2.1.1. Con: The main reason fewer hours were worked was that male students stayed in school longer – they no longer had to help support their family – and mothers stayed at home longer with newborns. These were both responses that benefit society overall.
1.2.7.6.3.5.2.1.2.1.2. Con: The experiment in question only ran for four years, and was known by participants to have a fixed term. The need for future employment is likely a major skewing factor compared to a permanent UBI program.
1.2.7.6.3.5.2.1.2.1.3. Con: The experiment ran short term, which could have skewed the results: the rise in unemployment may have been temporary. Learning different skill sets, switching careers, starting a business, or even de-stressing \(so they can pursue a career without mental or physical health problems\) takes time. It's possible that a portion of the 13% did just that.
1.2.7.6.3.5.2.2. Con: Existing welfare systems in some countries already allow people to not work while still managing to feed their families \(at least as much as a UBI would\).
1.2.7.6.3.5.2.2.1. Pro: The German "[Hartz IV](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hartz_concept#Hartz_IV)" offers recipients, usually long-term unemployed, social benefits and a monthly payment for them and their families to maintain a basic standard of living.
1.2.7.6.3.5.2.3. Con: People work for reasons other than income.
1.2.7.6.3.5.2.3.1. Pro: Labor is often very meaningful to a lot of people, since it provides some sense of dignity.
1.2.7.6.3.5.2.3.1.1. Con: There are other ways people experience dignity. Being respected and acknowledged for social, familial, and political contributions also provides dignity and meaning to a person's life.
1.2.7.6.3.5.2.3.1.1.1. Pro: Contributions in the form of personal development and financially aiding the family are among the greatest of these ways.
1.2.7.6.3.5.2.3.1.2. Pro: Solely living on the UBI could make people feel useless.
1.2.7.6.3.5.2.3.1.2.1. Con: Nothing is stopping people who want to work from working.
1.2.7.6.3.5.2.3.1.2.2. Con: Or it could give them the freedom to do things they actually want to do, that would feel meaningful to them.
1.2.7.6.3.5.2.3.1.3. Con: Current societal and cultural shaming for "not contributing" \(despite there being no universally recognized higher cause to which someone should be contributing\) is what is depriving people of dignity in the first place, which they then attempt to regain by working.
1.2.7.6.3.5.2.3.1.4. Con: This does not represent everybody and is not an excuse to force labour onto those who would rather not engage in it, unless there is a genuine need for that labour.
1.2.7.6.3.5.2.3.2. Con: A UBI allows people to work for these reasons unrelated to income at more genuine and efficient levels by reducing financial stress and coercive incentives.
1.2.7.6.3.5.2.3.3. Con: Then their work could become an activity they choose to engage in, and UBI provides more choices and freedom of choice overall - including that one.
1.2.7.6.3.5.2.4. Pro: In wealthy countries people who work only for survival would be able to stop working.
1.2.7.6.3.5.3. Con: This assumes there are a surplus of happy workers to take necessary jobs.
1.2.7.6.3.5.3.1. Con: There is an excess of all workers at the moment, and while it is possible none of them will be more agreeable with the working conditions, it is probable a good number will be.
1.2.7.6.3.5.3.1.1. Con: There is only an excess of available workers because of the requirement for every human being to work. UBI eliminates that imperative.
1.2.7.6.3.5.4. Con: This assumes happy workers are more productive, which is [false](http://www.hiringsmart.com/articles/608/Happy_Employees_are_not_Necessarily_Productive_Employees/).
1.2.7.6.3.5.4.1. Con: The linked article states that disengaged workers are not productive - so enabling disengaged workers to leave a job they are not engaged with and find a job or other activities which are more engaging and satisfying to them would produce a similar benefit.
1.2.7.6.3.5.5. Pro: Happy workers are [more productive](http://fortune.com/2015/10/29/happy-productivity-work/).
1.2.7.6.3.5.5.1. Con: Workers are not 'happy' if their work is not significantly higher valued than "non-work" in terms of spending capacity and available income; the UBI devalues the perception of basic work.
1.2.7.6.3.5.5.1.1. Pro: Prior to a UBI, a worker had a certain available income, and a non-worker had no income. Post UBI implementation a non-worker would have available income. Individuals earning more than [50.000 USD](https://docs.google.com/spreadsheets/d/1ZH_i4nqHE1OKmapiHqusnXF8LpXJfGZgU0nNIcKN13Y/edit?usp=sharing) would have to pay more into the UBI tax than they get back in UBI payments; they can afford less than what they had before. The worker's comparative marginal gains over a non-worker pre vs. post UBI would show their work is less valuable.
1.2.7.6.3.5.5.1.1.1. Con: The amount someone makes does [not reflect the value of their labor](https://theweek.com/articles/451285/1-percent-dont-work-harder-money--work-less), earnings from capital gains and inheritance are not made from hard work.
1.2.7.6.3.5.5.1.1.1.1. Con: In the case of inheritance, it doesn't reflect the work of the person receiving the inheritance, but it reflects the work of the person providing the inheritance. That money has to come from somewhere.
1.2.7.6.3.5.5.1.1.1.2. Con: In the case of capital gains, these are produced by the investment of already existing capital. The previously existing capital that is needed to invest is the result of work. Then researching and choosing an investment, and taking the risk associated is the work that goes into the investment itself.
1.2.7.6.3.5.5.1.1.2. Con: Of note, the 50,000 mark is only accurate under certain taxation/payment schemes. There is, inevitably, a point at which someone must be paying in more than they are giving out, but exactly where that point is, depends on the particular UBI plan enacted.
1.2.7.6.3.5.5.1.1.3. Con: Not all income is work related. Some is investment related. And the higher up the income ladder you go, the larger the percentage of income 'earned' is actually based on investments, thus, while it may 'reduce the value' of some people's work, it does so at a lower and lower rate, at the higher points of the economy.
1.2.7.6.3.5.5.1.1.4. Con: Reduction of the value of work at higher levels in order to maintain a minimum basic living standard across the board is a reasonable cost.
1.2.7.6.3.6. Con: This makes an assumption that UBI will be set at an enormously high level - a level that would certainly not be practical or feasible given the economic realities and the budgets of state and federal governments.
1.2.7.6.3.6.1. Pro: UBI will most likely be set at levels that allow for the assurance of fresh food, water, electricity, and housing. Done right, UBI can be set at levels low enough.
1.2.7.6.3.7. Pro: UBI will increase depression and suicide rates as people lose purpose derived from work.
1.2.7.6.3.7.1. Con: Many suicides are correlated with [poverty](https://www.ajpmonline.org/article/S0749-3797\(16\)30461-5/fulltext), harsh living circumstances, work stress, and hatred of one's job.
1.2.7.6.3.7.1.1. Con: There can be different reasons for suicide - this does not invalidate the fact that many people derive purpose and order from their work.
1.2.7.6.3.8. Pro: A fundamental economic principle is to add value through work. When income is given for free without work this does not add value to the economy.
1.2.7.6.3.8.1. Con: There are no things like "fundamental economic principles", they are observations that have been valid in the past. They are traditions in the sense that they have been repeated so often people take them for laws. E.g. there could be so many opportunity costs that are avoided that the net turnover will be positive \(think of health, less pollution, etc.\)
1.2.7.6.3.9. Pro: The UBI may result in fewer citizens being forced to provide for an increasing number of unproductive individuals in society.
1.2.7.6.3.10. Pro: A UBI, by eliminating the need to work, would strongly decrease the incentives that promote innovation.
1.2.7.6.3.10.1. Pro: UBI will negatively impact incentives for technological and economic progress. Be it fame, fortune, or philanthropy, we have all the great things in our life readily available because the brains behind them had something motivating them.
1.2.7.6.3.10.1.1. Con: Innovation requires the ability to pursue avenues that might include risk. With no reasonable safety net, these risks may be avoided instead of pursued.
1.2.7.6.3.10.1.2. Con: Motivations can be more than money. Warren Buffett is one of the richest people in the world, and still working at an age many people have retired.[g.co](https://g.co/kgs/Cwy8m9)
1.2.7.6.3.10.1.3. Con: Money is not the motivator behind innovation. The great innovators in history were all driven by a will to help people, or by their own passion for their subject.
1.2.7.6.3.10.1.3.1. Con: The belief that all innovators in history were not driven by money may be hard to back up. If someone asked people like Thomas Edison, Steve Jobs, Elon Musk, Henry Ford or the Wright Brothers about their drive to go from a neat idea to a scale that could benefit society, money would likely be a leading factor. Useful innovation needs scale and scale requires a lot of work and money. Payoff must be greater than cost for innovation. Here is a good article about driving innovation [sciencedirect.com](http://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S004873331730104X)
1.2.7.6.3.10.1.3.1.1. Con: Thomas Edison claimed credit \(and eventually copyright\) out of greed for work that Nikola Tesla did in order to benefit humanity.
1.2.7.6.3.10.1.3.1.1.1. Pro: Nicola Tesla did work for the good of mankind, as can be seen in his plans for [universal energy](https://www.theguardian.com/science/2003/jul/03/research.science1), something that would be almost impossible to commercialize.
1.2.7.6.3.10.1.3.2. Pro: -> See 1.2.7.6.3.10.1.3.1.1.1.
1.2.7.6.3.10.1.4. Con: Much of the greatest technological innovation has been made possible through general research - research that was subsidized through public funds with no direct expectation of profit. The motivator here is not something that a UBI would destroy: human curiosity and the possibility of changing the world.
1.2.7.6.3.10.1.4.1. Pro: Those motivations don't magically disappear because we get a little bit of money.
1.2.7.6.3.10.1.4.1.1. Pro: Very few people who achieve fame and fortune cease pursuing those goals once they achieve a moderate amount of wealth. This shows that, to an extent at least, incentives to achieve those forms of success go above and beyond just getting rich.
1.2.7.6.3.10.1.4.1.2. Con: -> See 1.1.5.3.1.1.3.1.
1.2.7.6.3.10.1.5. Con: -> See 1.1.5.3.1.1.4.
1.2.7.6.3.10.1.6. Con: Many mathematicians and scientists of the past were born into wealthy and affluent positions affording them the freedom to peruse their true interests benefiting society and not what keeps bread on their table.
1.2.7.6.3.10.1.7. Pro: -> See 1.2.1.3.2.2.1.4.3.
1.2.7.6.3.10.1.8. Con: [The idea that money is a motivator is untrue,](https://youtu.be/dgKKPQiRRag) and studies have proven that money only motivates up until a living wage, after which competence is a greater motivator.
1.2.7.6.3.10.2. Con: A UBI would help inventors \(who tend to be terrible at making money\) continue to invent. Thus incentivising some of the greatest minds in society to use their mind instead of work to survive.
1.2.7.6.3.11. Con: This would be highly beneficial for those with moderately severe chronic illness or disability or those who otherwise cannot qualify for disability assistance programs.
1.2.7.6.3.12. Con: Evidence suggests that social security systems like UBI do not lead people to stop working.
1.2.7.6.3.12.1. Pro: Germany has a very sophisticated [social state](https://www.internations.org/germany-expats/guide/15984-social-security-taxation/social-security-in-germany-15970), with a variety of public payments and benefits that enable citizens to live at least a humble life without work. Nevertheless, the [unemployment rate](http://www.tradingeconomics.com/germany/unemployment-rate) is very low.
1.2.7.6.3.12.2. Pro: The average amount given for social security in the US is [not enough](https://www.cnbc.com/2020/01/13/why-its-hard-to-for-retirees-to-get-by-on-social-security-benefits.html) for many to live on without other streams of income.
1.2.7.6.3.12.3. Pro: Brazil has a program called "Bolsa Família" \(Family Allowance\), which is basically UBI but only for poor people. Although it is not exactly UBI, there were enough [studies](http://www.scielo.br/pdf/ecos/v19n3/08.pdf) in Brazil to prove that the described social program did not make, with some exceptions, people lazy. In a lot of cases, the exact opposite was observed: people worked harder to pursue their dreams.
1.2.7.6.3.12.4. Con: A negative income tax \(a variant of UBI\) [was already tried](https://www.heritage.org/poverty-and-inequality/commentary/universal-basic-income-has-been-tried-it-didnt-work) in six U.S. states between 1968 and 1980. It reduced people desire to work and lead to them having longer lengths of unemployment.
1.2.7.6.3.12.4.1. Con: The cited article is a questionable interpretation of the broad range of data collected in several interesting experiments. The author of the article focuses solely on the reduced productivity of those involved, and even that is examined only in a decrease in hours of work. No consideration is made to how much they started at, their quality of life, or long term investments/improvements.
1.2.7.6.3.12.4.2. Con: The article explicitly says that the programs analyzed were negative income taxes, which are not universal basic income \(since negative income taxes decrease pay out as one makes more money\).
1.2.7.6.3.12.4.3. Con: These programs took place while traditional welfare was still in place, weakening the study's applicability for a UBI which replaces traditional welfare.
1.2.7.6.3.12.4.4. Con: It is not clear what the recipients did with their extra time; some of them may have chosen to do productive/positive activities that just aren't technically "work," such as pursuing education, taking care of sick family members, volunteering, that just aren't "work." Without knowing these details, we cannot reliably determine the extent/harm of this effect.
1.2.7.6.3.12.4.5. Con: Negative tax structure and UBI are not even remotely comparable: NTS decreases the more you earn so essentially working to maintain the same quality of life. This is incentive not to work. Not comparable to UBI which is no strings attached and the more you work, the more quality of life improves.
1.2.7.6.3.12.5. Pro: UBI experiments and trials show that people do not tend to work less when given an unconditional income.
1.2.7.6.3.12.5.1. Con: -> See 1.2.7.6.3.5.2.1.2.1.
1.2.7.6.3.12.5.2. Pro: In 2011 Iran established a [program](https://theoutline.com/post/1613/iran-introduces-basic-income) that guaranteed citizens cash payments of equivalent to third of the nation's median income. A report found no evidence for the hypothesis this unconditional income reduced the willingness to work.
1.2.7.6.3.12.5.2.1. Pro: People engaged in the Iranian [service industry](https://theoutline.com/post/1613/iran-introduces-basic-income) worked more than before the implementation of the payment program.
1.2.7.6.3.12.5.2.2. Pro: Iranian business people started to expand their [businesses](https://theoutline.com/post/1613/iran-introduces-basic-income).
1.2.7.6.3.12.5.2.3. Pro: There is [evidence](https://theoutline.com/post/1613/iran-introduces-basic-income) that citizens started to pursue more satisfying kind of work instead of leaving the labor market for good.
1.2.7.6.3.12.5.3. Pro: A UBI does not undermine the incentive to work, as evidenced by this [London-based study](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=aIL_Y9g7Tg0).
1.2.7.6.3.12.5.4. Pro: [A UBI experiment](http://www.developmentpathways.co.uk/resources/wp-content/uploads/2016/04/Indias-Basic-Income-Experiment-PP21.pdf) in India showed that overall employment increased with the implementation of a UBI and there was a sharp increase in more independent labor.
1.2.7.6.3.12.5.4.1. Con: The experiment did not actually test a UBI, because benefits were temporary, which significantly alters the incentive of beneficiaries towards making something lasting out of it. If they thought this income would become the norm instead of being a financial windfall, they would not be as motivated to reduce debt, for example.
1.2.7.6.3.12.5.4.2. Con: The experiment did not actually test a UBI, because the benefits were not paid from taxes collected in the villages, but injected from outside. Instead of UBI, which is a zero-sum game within the economic zone in which it is implemented, they brought in purchasing power from outside the "UBI" zone, which of course makes the villages better off.
1.2.7.6.3.12.5.4.2.1. Con: While this experiment may not be an accurate representation of the macroeconomic consequences, it still shows the effects of a UBI on individual behavior: increased school attendance, reduced debts, and a shift in occupations from wage laborer to farmer.
1.2.7.6.3.12.5.4.2.2. Con: Even if purchasing power is redistributed rather than raised \(on net\) a zero-sum change can still improve net welfare as the benefit of the marginal dollar to the poor is much higher than the to rich.
1.2.7.6.3.12.5.4.2.2.1. Pro: Also in any small scale view, this is not a sum zero game. A poor area \(such as this village\) will net benefit, and a rich area \(which could have been a wealthy town nearby\) will net loose.
1.2.7.6.3.12.5.4.3. Con: The experiment did not actually test a UBI as it is usually proposed, because the benefits were designed to be just enough to make a difference, but not enough to live off. It was a supplemental income that still required the villagers to work for their livelihood, as opposed to an income that can buy at least food, clothing and shelter.
1.2.7.6.3.12.5.5. Pro: A UBI experiment in Finland showed that those who received UBI [did not work less](https://www.newscientist.com/article/2193136-universal-income-study-finds-money-for-nothing-wont-make-us-work-less/) than a control group of those who received traditional benefits.
1.2.7.6.3.13. Con: People will not drop out of work due to cultural reasons.
1.2.7.6.3.13.1. Pro: The [Protestant work ethic](https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC1501011/) is a strong element of many western societies that causes moral pressure to work and is still a dominant spirit of the modern era. \([Castells, Himanen, p. 19](https://archive.org/details/TheHackerEthicAndTheSpiritOfTheInformationAge)\).
1.2.7.6.3.13.1.1. Pro: For Protestants, the ['psychic harm'](https://hbr.org/daily-stat/2013/08/there-really-is-such-a-thing-a.html) from unemployment is 40% more than for other social groups, due to hard work being viewed as a 'calling' rather than a job.
1.2.7.6.3.13.2. Con: Societies and its values are subjects of constant change and progress. Triggered by the UBI, working less or even not working at all could become the social norm and the source for individuals' identity and self-esteem becomes a different one.
1.2.7.6.3.13.2.1. Con: Just as societal change can alter the working habits toward working less, they can also form around working more efficiently or productively.
1.2.7.6.3.13.2.1.1. Pro: [Financial stress](https://www.shrm.org/resourcesandtools/hr-topics/benefits/pages/employees-financial-issues-affect-their-job-performance.aspx) can affect health to the point of decreasing productivity. A UBI can alleviate this stress, thus indirectly increasing productivity.
1.2.7.6.3.13.2.1.1.1. Pro: Increasing amounts of physical illnesses are being found to be stress-related, as stress weakens the immune system and negatively affects the physical body in other ways.
1.2.7.6.3.13.2.1.1.2. Pro: UBI reduces levels of stress and anxiety that result from an insecurity in wages and allows a person to build psychological resources that enhance possibility for upward mobility.
1.2.7.6.3.13.2.1.1.3. Pro: Focus and attention are limited resources; when you have more mental space because some of the stress to take care of the basic needs is relieved, it allows you the ability to explore more options in life. In this regard, money \(UBI\) is a non-linear in its effects, once people get over the inertia of the first $500-1000 per month it expands their possibilities, which means a nation could operate at a higher level of productivity.
1.2.7.6.3.13.2.1.2. Pro: -> See 1.1.5.3.1.3.6.4.1.
1.2.7.6.3.13.2.2. Con: Whilst being productive is important, a move away from financial status as the primary source of one's pride should actually be a favourable development for societies.
1.2.7.6.3.13.3. Pro: In many cultures, especially in East Asia \(e.g. [South Korea](http://edition.cnn.com/2015/07/23/asia/south-korea-work-culture/)\), there is huge societal pressure to work.
1.2.7.6.3.13.3.1. Pro: The societal pressure to work in South Korea is deeply rooted, as it begins in the [classroom](http://www.npr.org/sections/parallels/2015/04/15/393939759/the-all-work-no-play-culture-of-south-korean-education).
1.2.7.6.3.14. Con: People will not drop out of work due to personal values.
1.2.7.6.3.14.1. Pro: Even if provided with a UBI, people would continue to work in order to ensure better education and resources for their children.
1.2.7.6.3.14.2. Pro: -> See 1.2.7.6.3.5.2.3.1.
1.2.7.6.3.14.3. Pro: People want to improve their social status.
1.2.7.6.3.14.3.1. Con: There are other ways to improve social status, such as through [education](https://www.equalitytrust.org.uk/social-mobility-and-education), [marriage](https://www.demographic-research.org/volumes/vol26/8/26-8.pdf) or friendship.
1.2.7.6.3.14.3.2. Pro: Improving social status will allow people to enjoy the kind of lifestyle they envision for themselves, thus, improving their happiness.
1.2.7.6.3.14.4. Pro: For many people work is an important source of [personal identity](https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC1501011/) and self-esteem.
1.2.7.6.3.14.4.1. Pro: [55%](http://www.gallup.com/poll/175400/workers-sense-identity-job.aspx) of US workers report getting their sense of identity from their job.
1.2.7.6.3.14.4.2. Con: [Work](http://www.huffingtonpost.com/joe-robinson/self-identity_b_1128731.html) as a major source of personal identity can be detrimental to a person's sense of self-worth, as it is tied to a dynamic external construct and daily performance instead of internal values and attributes. Even if people leave the workforce, it will - under this premise - lead to a more positive and sustainable sense of self-identity.
1.2.7.6.3.14.5. Pro: Some people work because they are driven by a personal mission.
1.2.7.6.3.14.5.1. Pro: A UBI could restore pride in kinds of work that are culturally significant, but otherwise are not usually fiscally responsible undertakings.
1.2.7.6.3.14.5.2. Pro: Some famous and successful [CEOs](https://www.fastcompany.com/3026791/dialed/personal-mission-statements-of-5-famous-ceos-and-why-you-should-write-one-too) have a clear personal mission.
1.2.7.6.3.14.6. Con: Parents may struggle to find a healthy work-home balance. These individuals may believe it makes more sense to leave the workplace in order to focus on their childrens' welfare or care.
1.2.7.7. Con: Taxes other than income taxes could be raised in order to lessen the economic impact on individuals.
1.2.7.7.1. Pro: UBI through a tax on [energy resources](https://www.canadadrives.ca/blog/news/carbon-taxes-and-carbon-tax-rebates-in-canada-explained)(Canada’s carbon tax has two parts: a carbon levy on fuel purchases and a big emitters program for industrial facilities. To explain how the carbon levy affects the average Canadian, this article will focus more on the carbon levy and less on the big industrial emitters.\n...\nFor the provincial governments that refused to adopt the carbon tax, Trudeau wasn’t about to reward them with billions in new tax revenue. That’s why the feds created the Climate Action Incentive \(CAI\) – to make sure taxpayers are on the receiving end of the carbon tax revenue – not uncooperative premiers. It also softens the blow for those cringing at increased power bills and pump prices.) and financial transactions \(such as those in [Canada and Switzerland](https://en.unesco.org/inclusivepolicylab/analytics/greening-basic-income)(Carbon price-and-dividend schemes are in place in Canada and Switzerland, with dozens of other jurisdictions implementing forms of carbon pricing.)\) makes an increase on the income tax unnecessary, while making society more equal.
1.2.7.7.1.1. Con: Canada claimed that [80%](https://torontosun.com/opinion/columnists/goldstein-trudeaus-2022-climate-plan-more-carbon-taxes)(While Ottawa has claimed 80% of households in these provinces end up better off financially because of the rebates) of families would be financially better off. In 2021, Parliamentary budget officer reports that though most households are financially better off it is [60%](https://torontosun.com/opinion/columnists/goldstein-trudeaus-2022-climate-plan-more-carbon-taxes)(...while most households... end up better off financially because of rebates, 40% of Ontario households, not 20%, are now paying more in carbon taxes than they receive in rebates.) of families.
1.2.7.7.2. Con: The costs of those forms of taxes are all ultimately met by Citizens; even if indirectly.
1.2.7.7.3. Pro: If higher tax revenue is required, a [Land Value Tax](https://www.kialo.com/should-a-land-value-tax-be-introduced-to-replace-other-taxes-8742) could be used. Such a tax does not harm the economy and has other localized benefits beyond simply raising revenue.
1.2.7.8. Con: A UBI would improve peoples heath, thus creating major savings in the healthcare sector, which is publicly funded in many developed countries.
1.2.7.8.1. Pro: Basic income has been shown to have a [positive affect](https://www.researchgate.net/publication/313385410_Reducing_health_inequities_Is_universal_basic_income_the_way_forward) on birth outcomes in Alaska.
1.2.7.8.2. Pro: Income transfers are some of the most effective means of [improving health outcomes](https://c.ymcdn.com/sites/alphaweb.site-ym.com/resource/collection/86D31666-E7EA-42F1-BDA1-A03ECA0B4E3D/OnPhysicians_Letter_BIG_170815.pdf) and being healthy is a fundamental precursor to almost all other rights and freedoms.
1.2.7.8.2.1. Con: Government regulates levels of societal freedom more than health. Take two individuals, one very healthy and one with a terminal disease; both can live in the same totalitarian society a with equally little freedoms.
1.2.7.8.2.1.1. Con: The person with the poorer health will still have fewer freedoms. \(E.g. limited access to public services, time consuming treatments, ...\)
1.2.7.8.2.1.2. Con: There will always be some limitations for what you can do, the freedom given by UBI is that people get power \(monetary power\) and can choose what to do with it — without the threat and fear of not being able to survive.
1.2.7.8.3. Pro: A UBI reduces mental health complications \([p. 4](https://academic.oup.com/jpubhealth/article-pdf/40/1/3/24372631/fdx006.pdf)(In an unconditional cash transfer experiment in Malawi, schoolgirls\nwere ~38% less likely to suffer psychological distress than the\ncontrol group. A 1996 longitudinal study conducted on a tribal reservation in the USA examined the psychiatric outcomes following the opening of a casino, which lead to a rise of income in the reservation. The study results showed that drug dealing in adolescents declined, high school graduation rates increased and the youngest of the family were less likely to experience any psychiatric disorder. Research studies in Kenya present a multitude of mental health benefits from income supplements: youth experienced 24% less depressive\nsymptoms, psychological well-being increased and the benefits played a buffering effect for the mental health of orphaned children. Young men reported feeling healthier and being more hopeful. In short, income security appears to be a key factor in protecting mental health.)\).
1.2.7.8.3.1. Pro: Unresolved, compounded [stress](https://www.nimh.nih.gov/health/publications/stress/index.shtml) can lead to mental disorders like depression and anxiety. With money being a [major stressor](http://www.apa.org/news/press/releases/2015/02/money-stress.aspx) of 64% of Americans-particularly in the lower income bracket-a UBI can decrease this stress by increasing the amount of money contributed to a household.
1.2.7.8.3.2. Pro: Stress has been affecting the lives of those living in the cities [so much](https://www.stress.org/daily-life/), mainly work, money and the future, that it is literally [changing the pathways in our brains](https://www.nature.com/articles/nature10190) and there is [data proof](http://www.gwern.net/docs/nature/2010-peen.pdf) that living in the cities or urban areas in general has a direct impact on our mental health and how prone we are to disorders, in developed countries.
1.2.7.8.3.2.1. Pro: That is [debilitating to society](http://psycnet.apa.org/buy/2004-15935-004) as a whole, no matter who you are as [stress is infectious in and of itself](https://www.healthline.com/health-news/stress-cues-from-others#2) AND can make you more [susceptible to other diseases](https://www.clinicaleducation.org/resources/reviews/chronic-stress-increases-infection-susceptibility/).
1.2.7.8.4. Pro: India trialed a [UBI in 2010](https://www.worldcrunch.com/culture-society/universal-basic-income-5-experiments-from-around-the-world-1): The trial resulted in a sharp increase in food sufficiency and a drop in illness in villages that received the funds compared to those that did not."
1.2.7.8.5. Pro: Minorities and the poor have real problems that endanger their health and sometimes their lives, like living in very poor conditions, being malnourished, not having access to health care and education, etc. A UBI can afford them better health, education, and housing.
1.2.7.8.5.1. Con: There are [government public assistance programs](https://www.usa.gov/benefits) available that already provide benefits to improving health care access, education, proper nutrition, and housing.
1.2.7.8.5.2. Con: UBI is another wealth re-distribution scheme that ultimately reduces the choices of consumers, the quality of goods & services.  [Problems with UBI Schemes](https://mises.org/blog/dangers-universal-basic-income)
1.2.7.8.5.3. Pro: A UBI will reduce unclaimed medical costs caused by malnurishment, dehydration and various homeless injuries. In turn, this will cause an overall increase in national health.
1.2.7.8.5.4. Pro: Eliminating these problems should be the absolute priority, even if this does not also solve discrimination and inequality.
1.2.7.8.5.5. Con: A UBI is unlikely to provide people with enough income to significantly expand their freedom of choice. People who are poor will still be significantly less well off than the rich.
1.2.7.8.5.5.1. Con: People who currently must take a job or starve will have the freedom to keep looking for something better. People are less likely to feel trapped under a UBI.
1.2.7.8.5.5.2. Con: Low-income people often feel trapped by the location of the social programs they currently receive. With a UBI that people can take with them when they migrate within a country, people will have better opportunities to relocate.
1.2.7.8.5.5.3. Con: The greatest marginal value of UBI funds would be to the least mobile and skilled in society. The assurance of a consistent revenue stream, regardless of where a person lives, opens up myriad possible life changes which would simply be inaccessible for someone caught in the crushing wheel of poverty. UBI increases personal freedom and mobility.
1.2.7.8.5.5.4. Con: There will always be someone who is relatively better off. However, financial security does allow more room for freedom of choice.
1.2.7.8.5.5.4.1. Pro: The point of a UBI is to help give everybody the same set of starting conditions. After that, meritocracy can play out fairly.
1.2.7.8.6. Pro: A UBI would help decrease hospitalisation rates.
1.2.7.8.6.1. Pro: A UBI can provide the additional funds needed to purchase healthier foods, which can result in better overall health.
1.2.7.8.6.1.1. Pro: [Chronic diseases](https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC5876976/) such as diabetes and heart disease often result in multiple hospitalizations. These conditions can be helped and prevented through [proper nutrition](https://www.who.int/dietphysicalactivity/publications/trs916/summary/en/); a resource UBI can help provide.
1.2.7.8.6.2. Pro: There was an 8.5% reduction in hospitalization observed in the Dauphin, [Manitoba experiment](http://Embrace future branch https://www.kialo.com/ubi-creates-well-defined-groups-of-recipients-and-donors-according-to-net-incomeoutcome-its-easier-to-be-a-recipient-so-1634.8910?path=1634.0~1634.1_1634.8912-1634.8910&active=-1634.8910&action=comments crime branch https://www.kialo.com/one-of-the-us-constitutions-preambles-intent-is-to-provide-for-the-general-welfare-and-article-181-says-congress-1634.8723?path=1634.0~1634.1_1634.1131_1634.8723).
1.2.7.8.6.2.1. Con: Better health while in poverty will mean more women in poverty will choose to have children out of wedlock, thus increasing the population of government dependants and the social problems associated with single parent families.
1.2.7.8.6.2.1.1. Con: Better health while in poverty doesn't imply more children out of wedlock. For better health to exist there has to be reproductive health services which will in turn address this issue. This correlation does not seem to happen.
1.2.7.8.6.2.1.2. Con: Reduction of poverty means that women - in or out of wedlock - are better able to access things such as contraception, and thus better able to avoid unwanted children. This is likely to balance any increase in the number of those who choose to have children that they may not want to have if poorer - but may still have anyway.
1.2.7.8.6.2.1.3. Con: The same logic could be used to attack any initiative that would increase the health of people in poverty. This is not sufficient reason to avoid improving the health and living conditions of those living in poverty.
1.2.7.8.6.2.2. Pro: In medicine poverty is considered a Risk Factor for a lot of diseases. [en.wikipedia.org](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diseases_of_poverty)
1.2.7.8.7. Pro: A UBI helps disabled people.
1.2.7.8.7.1. Pro: A UBI would allow for disability to be dealt with in a non-exploitative or demeaning way.
1.2.7.8.7.2. Pro: Disabled people's medical bills would be unlikely to pile up and force them to change their lifestyles if they had a UBI.
1.2.8. Pro: A UBI could decrease the incentive for workers to take jobs that are unappealing and/or uncomfortable, even where those jobs are vital to society and the economy.
1.2.8.1. Pro: If incentives for workers in menial jobs are not adjusted via supply and demand the government will have to intervene with incentives which will counteract the benefits of UBI via larger taxes and hidden costs.
1.2.8.1.1. Pro: One possible solution to making these jobs more attractive are tax incentives that result in lower public revenues.
1.2.8.2. Con: If those low-skill jobs are fundamental, but increasingly attract fewer workers, the jobs will then require higher wages until their attractiveness reaches an equilibrium.
1.2.8.2.1. Pro: A UBI will decrease the supply of low-skilled workers. This dynamic will be counteracted by rising wages \(in low-skilled jobs\) and a surge in Automation \(to replace lost workforce\). A UBI therefore acts almost like a pigouvian tax on low-skilled work \(similar to a minimum-wage\).
1.2.8.2.2. Pro: Many factories and warehouses pay double federal minimum wage for entry level. It isn't about the pay, it's about the culture of people not willing to work with their hands and get them dirty. It's about the culture that only office work is good work, and that actual labor is beneath the common man.
1.2.8.2.3. Con: The equilibrium you talk about is bankruptcy for many small businesses that can no longer afford a waiter, a cleaner, or a dishwasher. This simply plays into the hand of well established businesses and makes the barrier to entry much higher for anyone looking to start something new.
1.2.8.3. Con: Many of these jobs no longer require labour as they have become automated.
1.2.8.3.1. Con: Some jobs are not currently possible to automate. Others are not economically feasible to.
1.2.8.4. Pro: If there are any menial jobs that cannot be automated, it will be difficult to arrange to have them done. Menial labor, if not motivated by necessity or by the hope for better opportunities, doesn't have what little appeal it had in the first place.
1.2.8.4.1. Con: There are many perverse factors and market failures that currently prevent the free market from working effectively as it should.
1.2.8.5. Con: A UBI does not need to be set so high that everyone is satisfied, only enough that their needs are met at a minimal reasonable level.  Anything beyond that would still require a job.
1.2.9. Con: A UBI gives everyone money to spend on goods and services, therefore increasing aggregate demand and output.
1.2.9.1. Con: Redistribution is zero-sum: everything given to unproductive people has to be taken from productive people, either directly through taxes, or indirectly through borrowing or inflation.
1.2.9.1.1. Pro: Even if the transferred money drives up sales for productive people \(who offer goods or services\), because the money used to purchase the goods and services had been first taken from them, they essentially had to provide them for free.
1.2.9.2. Pro: A UBI offers the same benefits as raising the minimum wage, but without increasing the cost of labor. This keeps prices down as incomes go up, resulting in better purchasing power.
1.2.9.2.1. Con: The government may increase taxes over products and services, which in turn inflates the cost of goods and reduces the purchasing power.
1.2.9.3. Pro: -> See 1.2.1.3.2.2.1.4.1.
1.2.9.4. Pro: A UBI would likely have a growth effect on the market by putting money in the hands of the impoverished, who studies have proven spend a [greater percentage](https://www.huffpost.com/entry/why-should-we-support-the_b_7630162)("All those dollars low-wage workers spend create an economic ripple effect. Every extra dollar going into the pockets of low-wage workers, standard economic multiplier models tell us, adds about $1.21 to the national economy. Every extra dollar going into the pockets of a high-income American, by contrast, only adds about 39 cents to the GDP.") of their income than wealthier individuals.
1.2.9.4.1. Pro: As some companies cannot afford to pay high salaries, a UBI works as a subsidy. It helps the affected companies, e.g. start-ups, to develop and expand.
1.2.9.4.1.1. Con: On the other hand, a UBI can be misused by employers as an excuse for low salaries.
1.2.9.4.2. Pro: Black Americans have low [saving rates](http://thepowerisnow.com/savings-rate-of-african-americans/). Thus, money received would be channeled into the economy directly.
1.2.9.4.2.1. Con: As the [source](http://thepowerisnow.com/savings-rate-of-african-americans/) states, the most likely reason is that Black Americans do not earn enough to save. The money paid out to them might give them a chance to save.
1.2.9.4.2.1.1. Con: Much of the money received may have to go to [debt settlement and other expenses](https://www.theatlantic.com/business/archive/2015/10/debt-black-families/409756/), with very little going into savings.
1.2.9.4.2.1.1.1. Con: Even just clearing existing debts is a starting point for savings - when income is no longer eaten up by interest fees on debt, individuals can begin to save.
1.2.9.4.3. Con: Increased spending means decreased saving, and therefore less capital construction. This makes society less wealthy in the long run.
1.2.9.4.3.1. Con: More capital is constructed rather by re-investing than by keeping saved money-based capital. Saved capital is out of the market for all the time it stays saved.
1.2.9.4.3.1.1. Con: Money that is saved in a bank or other financial institution is not out of the market, but instead is now accessible to most anyone who wants to borrow. Money saved "under the mattress" does not contribute to economic growth, but is a negligible portion of all savings. It also plays an important role in the security of low income households \(which are most likely to practice it\).
1.2.9.4.4. Con: It doesn't help the economy to reward low-paid jobs, but [higher paid jobs](http://www.nytimes.com/1990/01/17/opinion/the-reagan-boom-greatest-ever.html). Higher paid jobs require ambition, motivation, and hard work. It's only worth it if there are incentives to do it.
1.2.9.4.4.1. Con: People would still receive an incentive by the extra income generated from their job.
1.2.9.4.4.1.1. Pro: A UBI is unlikely to inhibit someone like Steve Jobs or Bill Gates from their passions. If there are creative people out there, a UBI would give them time to work on what is important to them so they are free to be more creative, instead of working an unrewarding job.
1.2.9.4.4.2. Con: Higher paying jobs still reward people more then lower paying ones.
1.2.9.4.4.3. Con: Motivations are worthless if you don't have the ability to act on them. You can't start a business without an office you can't afford; you can't work hours of manual labor if you are starving; you can't get a job as a teacher if you can't afford to learn the knowledge you wish to impart.
1.2.9.4.4.3.1. Con: Although opportunity is not perfectly equal, a degree of equality is offered through the [ability to receive investment/loans](https://www.inc.com/guides/2010/07/how-to-finance-your-business.html). Even if someone is very poor, if they have a very good business idea, they will likely be able to receive some startup money.
1.2.9.4.4.3.1.1. Con: It seems like every head of a successful business has a story about how they were poor, and destitute, and someone gave them a shot. But these businesses are far from the total of the story as most people, even with great ideas, don't get those loans and their stories are buried by survival bias.
1.2.9.4.4.4. Con: This argument mirrors almost perfectly the [gospel of property](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prosperity_theology), [which tells people with cancer should seek treatment by giving money to a rich person who merely talks about how rich he is, instead of seeking the medical attention they need](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=7y1xJAVZxXg). Both put people with wealth on a pedestal, and lie to the poor and desperate that if they just give to these 'purer' people \(in this case, by working for them\) they can be better and more successful people.
1.2.9.4.4.4.1. Con: This misses the point of the grandparent, which partially emphasizes that higher-paying jobs tend to be more valuable to + lacked by society, since the price signals of the market \(as established in economics 101\) indicate what society wants more or less of.
1.2.9.4.4.5. Con: Money does not measure the worth of a person, their contribution to the society does. An individual's monetary worth measures only their talent in amassing a resource and is a solitary act benefiting only one individual. An act that in turn deprives others of access to that  resource and impedes contribution by the many to society. If money is the only measure of personal worth, then one's life is a meaningless pursuit of coin and paper that ceases to have value upon one's death.
1.2.9.4.5. Pro: A fairer distribution of wealth has been shown to boost [economic growth](https://www.theguardian.com/business/2015/jun/04/better-economic-growth-when-wealth-distributed-to-poor-instead-of-rich) by increasing domestic spending.
1.2.9.5. Pro: A UBI will stimulate the economy, as wealth scarcity reduces cash flow. A UBI will improve cash flow and thus encourage people to spend more freely, especially those who work and educate for extra wealth privileges.
1.2.9.6. Pro: People tend to [spend less and save more](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paradox_of_thrift) when there is no security net. The economy would be better off having people circulating their money around to a greater extent.
1.2.9.7. Con: Generating income from paid work also drives demand by increasing supply, thereby balancing demand.
1.2.9.7.1. Con: There might not be enough work to sustain a basic standard of living for most people when automation and the use of AI ramp up.
1.2.9.8. Con: Taking money from net producers, through tax, and giving it to net consumers is a drain on the net producers, thereby reducing the amount of production that can be invested in economic growth.
1.2.10. Pro: Those who have [multiple citizenship](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Multiple_citizenship), could receive a UBI in a wealthy nation, then spend it in a poor country with a low cost of life. That would be a leak of capital and labour from the rich country to the poor one.
1.2.10.1. Con: In order to receive a UBI, it may be required to live in the country where it is offered and to have a permanent residence for 6-8 months.
1.2.10.2. Con: While this is a slight economic bad from the rich country's perspective, this seems net-good from an overall-happiness perspective. That money is likely to go further in improving quality-of-life and provisioning for the citizen in the poorer country, and may also meaningfully boost quality of life for the people who sold him things.
1.2.10.2.1. Con: If too many people move from affluent countries to developing countries, it could increase the overall cost of living and is therefore negative for the local populations.
1.2.10.3. Con: This case \(and the related case of "double-citizenship double-UBI income"\) can likely be handled via government agreements about what benefits and taxes apply to multiple-citizenship persons, and what multiple-citizenships are permitted.
1.2.10.4. Pro: Many people do something similar when they retire abroad. They chose to go to live in countries with a cheap cost of living and there they can have luxury with their pension which would be small compared to the cost of living in their home country.
1.2.10.5. Con: It is unlikely that those that can afford to travel internationally would receive a significant portion of their income as UBI.
1.2.10.5.1. Pro: [In 2017, the average US outbound traveler](https://travel.trade.gov/outreachpages/download_data_table/2017%20Profile%20of%20US%20Residents%20Visiting%20Overseas.xlsx) spent $1,476 outside of the US as part of a trip for business or pleasure \(cell C402\). The average length of stay abroad was over 16 days \(cell C206\). The average household income of international travelers was $127k \(cell C477\).
1.2.11. Con: A UBI allows labour to be more effectively allocated across entire economies.
1.2.11.1. Pro: A UBI will allow for a freer form of working where people can take jobs and pursue projects more directly related to their skills, while maintaining a stable standard of living while they wait on the fruit of their collective labors.
1.2.11.1.1. Con: A lack of need may inhibit innovation by diminishing people's motivation to create new things.
1.2.11.1.1.1. Con: Inventors tend to come from [rich families](https://qz.com/1167332/data-shows-us-inventors-arent-just-good-at-science-they-come-from-rich-families/)(If you want to be an American inventor, you’ll need two things: to excel at math and science, and to have a rich family.\n\nFive economists tracked over 1 million inventors from birth, and discovered several startling things. Children of parents from the top 1% of income are 10 times more likely to file a patent than the bottom 50%. White children, across all family incomes, are three times more likely to invent than black children. Only 18% of all the inventors are women.) and privleged backgrounds, not poor ones.
1.2.11.1.1.2. Con: The necessity there referenced is a task that is undoable, or needs to be done better, not a lack of means in general. For example, projecting ones voice thousands of miles almost instantaneously was impossible before the invention of the telephone, and being able to eat won't make working in the dark easier, but a light-bulb will.
1.2.11.1.1.3. Con: Or it may create the free time for a motivation or inspiration which would otherwise be repressed and ignored until it fades \(due to higher - or higher-seeming - priorities\) to instead be pursued.
1.2.11.1.1.4. Con: The need for food and shelter is only one need that might spur innovation. Other needs will arise when this most basic need is removed and hence encourage further innovation.
1.2.11.1.1.4.1. Pro: Climate change is an existential problem. The necessity to solve this problem may spur innovation regardless of the economic conditions of those who try to innovate.
1.2.11.1.1.5. Con: Innovation is not necessarily an inherent good.
1.2.11.1.2. Con: This outcome can be achieved with a more traditional unemployment insurance scheme.
1.2.11.1.2.1. Con: Not all types of work are covered by these schemes. Non-traditional pursuits in music, writing and art do not have a quantifiable average income which can be addressed.
1.2.11.1.2.2. Con: Traditional unemployment insurance schemes lose many people through the cracks and are easily exploitable.
1.2.11.1.2.2.1. Pro: It only saves people from the stress of poverty after poverty is already present. Economic stress produces compiled anxiety in a household due to education costs and requirement for more loans and credit.
1.2.11.1.3. Pro: A UBI affords people the time and financial freedom to diversify their skill sets.
1.2.11.1.3.1. Pro: UBI allows young incoming workers to the economy the time and money to first figure out what they truly love to do and are gifted at so, when they do enter the workforce, they are happier and much more productive over their 40-plus years of employment.
1.2.11.1.3.2. Con: Some people will not use this time to diversify their skill set.
1.2.11.1.3.2.1. Con: It is up to each individual to choose, whether or not they want to work more, educate themselves or to do other things. It is the same today: some people work, some study, and \(like it or not\) some stay home on a welfare program.
1.2.11.1.3.2.2. Con: Some people may not use this time to diversify their skill-set, however, it is unclear whether it will be a significant percentage, enough to negate the benefits of those who would.
1.2.11.1.3.3. Con: Such extra skills will be worthless if people do not apply them, for example, on a job.
1.2.11.1.3.3.1. Con: This assumes that the only things that add worth to society are jobs. Volunteers in many fields seem to disprove this.
1.2.11.1.3.3.1.1. Con: Volunteers may do a lot of good, but jobs do the most for economic growth.
1.2.11.1.3.3.1.1.1. Con: Our economy is driven by people working: people can't afford not too. Thus while jobs are the engine of the current economy, that doesn't speak to the possibility of an alternate economic system in which volunteer work forms the basis of work.
1.2.11.1.3.3.2. Con: A "skill" that provides recreation has the benefit of increasing morale regardless of whether it provides an economic benefit or not.
1.2.11.1.3.3.3. Con: In a time of exponential technology growth, it is unpredictable what jobs will exist in the future.  As people explore their previously-untapped skills \(such as creativity\), they could very well invent a job that's needed, rather than waiting for a government or corporation to discover that need.
1.2.11.1.3.4. Pro: -> See 1.1.5.3.1.1.7.
1.2.11.1.4. Pro: Without the burden of basic material survival, individuals have more time and energy to consider the wider issues relating to their jobs. This tends to lead to innovation, as well new possibilities. Freedom from material want allows space for perspectives to change in such a way that benefits society as a whole.
1.2.11.1.4.1. Pro: UBI would foster innovation by freeing people from toiling to meet basic needs.
1.2.11.1.4.2. Pro: One of the first thing large companies do for desirable employees is take money off the table by providing them with an income that allows them to be free from worry in order to pursue the objectives of their jobs. By expanding this to the general public, and taking away the worry of basic housing and nutrition, it allows for creativity and realization of potential. Most people will get bored of sitting around and doing nothing.
1.2.11.1.4.2.1. Pro: Universal Basic Income merely is Capitalism that doesn't start at zero dollars. It starts with food, shelter, and health.
1.2.11.1.5. Pro: Put another way it's [Seneca's barbell](https://plasubtil.wordpress.com/2014/07/23/antifragile-chapter-ten-seneca/) strategy. UBI's the cost of an option that protects from the downside of extreme harm \(the absorbing barrier of destitution\) and so affords the freedom \(survival and time\) to engage in activities that may bring upside to do more than merely survive. Conceived this way, you set a level that allows not only for the basics of healthcare, shelter, and food; but is additionally structured for the skills acquisition that upside depends on. Redundancy? Absolutely.
1.2.11.1.6. Pro: A UBI would promote a more motivated, productive workforce in employees free to choose work according to their talents, interest, or prospective benefits rather than taking something just to survive.
1.2.11.2. Con: The UBI would decrease the incentive to stay on top of changing market conditions and develop the skills and personal savings needed to stay productive and self-sufficient because the guaranteed income provides a fallback option in the event that one becomes unemployed and financially depleted.
1.2.11.2.1. Con: This is false for individuals who are intrinsically motivated to work in a sector, which the economy values above UBI.
1.2.11.2.2. Con: This claim is only true if people are happy to take a hit in their standards of living due to not earning additional money.
1.2.12. Con: Many top economists support introducing a UBI.
1.2.12.1. Pro: Widely respected economists, such as free market capitalist [Milton Friedman](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=xtpgkX588nM&t=50s), were in favor of a basic income guarantee in the form of a [negative income tax](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milton_Friedman#Social_security.2C_welfare_programs.2C_and_negative_income_tax) and saw it as having positive effects on the national economy.
1.2.12.2. Pro: Winners of the economics Nobel Prize have [endorsed](https://www.forbes.com/sites/francescoppola/2017/08/31/top-economists-endorse-universal-basic-income/#5647098715ae) the implementation of a UBI.
1.3. Pro: A UBI is the most effective and efficient wealth redistribution system.
1.3.1. Con: Current welfare systems are already highly efficient.
1.3.1.1. Pro: [Compared to current welfare systems](https://thespinoff.co.nz/featured/31-03-2016/i-love-the-idea-of-a-universal-basic-income-but-heres-the-problem/), introducing a UBI either means cutting the benefits of current recipients by more than the amount of the UBI, or adding the UBI payment to non-recipients to the total cost.
1.3.1.1.1. Pro: For the UBI to not leave the most needy welfare recipients worse-off it would need to cover the costs of overlapping entitlements, which would cost far above the poverty line — [up to $22,000 in the case of New Zealand](https://thespinoff.co.nz/featured/31-03-2016/i-love-the-idea-of-a-universal-basic-income-but-heres-the-problem/).
1.3.1.1.2. Pro: An 'affordable' UBI that does not fully replace the existing targeted means of welfare distribution \(i.e. one that is supplemented by other targeted schemes\) retains many of the existing issues with poverty traps, inefficient distribution and high costs.
1.3.1.2. Pro: Empirical evidence shows that at [least 90%, if not 99%, of the capital used](http://www.cbpp.org/research/romneys-charge-that-most-federal-low-income-spending-goes-for-overhead-and-bureaucrats-is?fa=view&id=3655) for American welfare systems goes towards the beneficiaries.
1.3.1.2.1. Con: In 2015 13.5% of the U.S. population lived in poverty \([U.S. Census](http://www2.census.gov/programs-surveys/demo/tables/p60/256/pov_table3.xls)\). This shows that currently existing welfare programs, while efficient, are not doing enough.
1.3.1.2.1.1. Con: Welfare programs are not intended to provide more than basic needs.
1.3.1.2.1.2. Con: The fact that current welfare programs are \(supposedly\) inefficient does not mean that a UBI would be more efficient.
1.3.1.2.1.3. Con: The fact that poverty still exists does not mean that existing welfare programs are ineffective. This is somewhat similar to saying that laws against murder "are not doing enough" since some murder still occurs.
1.3.1.2.1.4. Con: Poverty rates were declining decade over decade before the expansion of social welfare spending. Since the creation of the welfare state and its associated safety nets, poverty rates have ceased to decline in the US. This suggests that welfare has a harmful effect on poverty rates, and the answer is to provide less of it, not double down with a UBI. [Poverty rates - 1950 to 2015](http://granitegrok.com/wp-content/uploads/2012/07/failed-war-on-poverty.jpg)
1.3.1.2.1.5. Pro: Universal Basic Income is an important step towards eradicating World Poverty. People are poor not by choice. For example, people born into poverty, unable to find jobs, disabled & unable to work, etc., who therefore have no income or means of survival. On the other hand, there are those born with silver spoons in their mouths living in opulence without the need to work for an income. This is obviously "fated" and beyond the individual's control; "fate" has obviously not been fair. Needs action.
1.3.1.2.2. Con: While the efficiencies of the current welfare systems are already high, that doesn't mean they couldn't be more efficient.
1.3.1.2.2.1. Con: With that high level of efficiency it is possibly less risky and costly to try something else than to further upgrade current system.
1.3.1.2.2.2. Con: The main issue addressed by the [grandparent claim](https://www.kialo.com/argument/1634.1730) is the misconception that traditional welfare programs are highly inefficient. It does not need to prove that traditional welfare is more efficient than UBIs; rather, the parent here needs to suggest that UBIs are more efficient than traditional welfare—and quantify that improvement, to the extent possible.
1.3.1.2.3. Con: Even if the majority of the capital goes towards the beneficiaries, the money may not be being spent very effectively.
1.3.1.2.3.1. Con: [The Politico article](https://www.politico.com/magazine/story/2017/07/20/how-san-francisco-turned-its-tenements-into-treasures-215391) seems to suggest that the public-private partnership system was a successful case of targeted/means-tested housing subsidies/benefits.
1.3.1.2.3.2. Pro: Technically speaking most of the money spent on [public housing](https://www.politico.com/magazine/story/2017/07/20/how-san-francisco-turned-its-tenements-into-treasures-215391) goes towards the beneficiaries, but results in very poor quality housing compared to housing offered by the private sector for the same cost.
1.3.1.3. Con: Existing welfare systems have burdensome sets of conditions and application processes. A UBI would remove these burdens.
1.3.1.3.1. Pro: '[Workfare](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Workfare)' programs require people to participate in cheap labour or volunteer work. This is a form of modern day [slavery](https://www.huffingtonpost.co.uk/dr-simon-duffy/workfare-is-modernised-slavery_b_2773051.html).
1.3.1.3.2. Pro: These conditions can include [urine drug testing to access welfare checks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Drug_testing_welfare_recipients) or weekly [unscheduled visits](https://www.gov.uk/dwp-visit) at the homes of welfare recipients.
1.3.1.3.2.1. Con: No explanation has been offered as to why this is "unfair" or "unreasonable"; on the contrary, many people would consider it reasonable \(at least on the surface\) that one must undergo periodic drug tests in order to receive government support.
1.3.1.3.3. Con: Targeted welfare programs are superior to a UBI.
1.3.1.3.3.1. Con: A UBI affords choice and agency to individuals to make economic decisions that targeted welfare programs cannot give them.
1.3.1.3.3.1.1. Con: Choices can be facilitated in a variety of other ways. For instance, [education](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/School_voucher) and [housing](http://www.cbpp.org/research/housing/policy-basics-the-housing-choice-voucher-program) vouchers can be used to increase the choices between government services for poor individuals.
1.3.1.3.3.1.1.1. Con: Vouchers still limit choices to government approved options and dictates about how much people should spend on specific things. A UBI provides a higher level of agency.
1.3.1.3.3.1.1.2. Con: The most direct and efficient method of facilitating choices in a society is through a common currency exchanged for goods and services within a free market environment.
1.3.1.3.3.1.2. Con: Government has greater access to research institutions and therefore macro-level information. This can be used to manage inter-generational dynamics better than an individual is able to.
1.3.1.3.3.1.2.1. Pro: For instance, moving young children into better areas has little effect on their parents, but massively improves their [expected earnings](http://scholar.harvard.edu/hendren/publications/effects-exposure-better-neighborhoods-children-new-evidence-moving-opportunity). Thus parents may be reluctant to move without information about how their futures will be affected, which governments are better placed to access.
1.3.1.3.3.1.2.2. Con: Macro-level information is useful for setting macro-level policy that impacts numerous groups. Less so for micro-level decision making where individual circumstance and chance play a massive role in resource distribution.
1.3.1.3.3.1.2.3. Con: Macro-level information is costly to collect and often goes out of date soon after it is made available.
1.3.1.3.3.1.3. Con: It is disingenuous to claim that money is equivalent to choices. Behavioural evidence shows that people tend to be risk loving, discount expected losses, and are [unable to conceptualise distant future benefits](http://blogs.lse.ac.uk/politicsandpolicy/book-review-poor-economics/). Often this results in people wasting money on unwise purchases.
1.3.1.3.3.1.3.1. Con: Money is equivalent to choice, as in many cases people can purchase goods or services that actually influence their own future choices towards wise outcomes. For example, the wealthy spend a lot of money purchasing [commitment devices](http://messymatters.com/akrasia/) that enable or incentivise self-control, such as medical insurance or a personal trainer.
1.3.1.3.3.1.3.2. Pro: People treat losses as less likely to affect them adversely than they actually are. This is why people drive drunk and choose not to wear seatbelts, when the rational choice is to do neither of these things.
1.3.1.3.3.1.3.2.1. Con: This is highly subjective. There is no actual evidence suggesting most people would take those risks.
1.3.1.3.3.1.3.3. Con: This is more likely to happen in situations where people are encouraged to think short term, and have no stability. People are more likely to consider long term impacts when they know their basic needs will be provided for as long as they make wise decisions.
1.3.1.3.3.1.3.4. Con: The behavioral evidence cited above has been contradicted numerous times, including in the science study referenced in [this](http://www.theatlantic.com/business/archive/2013/11/your-brain-on-poverty-why-poor-people-seem-to-make-bad-decisions/281780/) article, which notes that poverty and the lack of stability causes bad decision making rather than it being an innate characteristic.
1.3.1.3.3.1.3.5. Con: Money enables individuals to invest in education. Education extends occupational possibilities and choices.
1.3.1.3.3.1.4. Pro: Allowing recipients to select their own providers can save money on contracting and oversight for the companies selected to provide traditional welfare services.
1.3.1.3.3.1.4.1. Pro: It also allows for competition in service quality even when offering services to the less wealthy.
1.3.1.3.3.1.5. Pro: Welfare benefits are often tied to a specific situation, which means that getting out of that situation results in the loss of those benefits. This can trap people in their situations. Conversely, a UBI allows for any change in circumstances without threatening such a loss.
1.3.1.3.3.1.6. Pro: In order for a state to provide housing, education and food for everyone, there would be needed a partly socialistic system, which althrough the history has been proven to not work. On the other hand, a state that supports private industries to help people with food, housing and education would be more viable.
1.3.1.3.3.1.7. Con: A UBI will dismantle not-for-profit essential welfare infrastructure like public food storage & distribution. This will lead to conflict and strife during times of shortage and waste in times of plenty.
1.3.1.3.3.1.7.1. Con: People freed from the requirement of performing paid work \(or provided with additional income\) by the existence of a UBI may - and many likely will - choose to devote their time and/or spare finances to altruistic or charitable projects, bolstering not-for-profit services.
1.3.1.3.3.1.8. Pro: Targeted programs are inherently paternalistic, insofar as they assume that the government is better suited than the disadvantaged themselves at making decisions benefiting their well-being.
1.3.1.3.3.1.8.1. Pro: People are best at making economic decisions for themselves, as they know their own needs and circumstances and can thus better account for where to direct money.
1.3.1.3.3.1.8.1.1. Con: People tend to underestimate their future needs and often struggle to plan ahead. This is especially true when it comes to saving for future health problems because people often do not take seriously the possibility that they will be affected.
1.3.1.3.3.1.8.1.1.1. Con: National health care will still exist under a UBI model.
1.3.1.3.3.1.8.1.2. Con: Dominant members of households can misuse UBI payments to further their own needs instead of providing for their families.
1.3.1.3.3.1.8.1.2.1. Con: This problem can apply to any family with capital of any kind.
1.3.1.3.3.1.8.1.2.1.1. Pro: The same is possible with welfare checks.
1.3.1.3.3.1.8.1.2.2. Con: Considering everyone would be receiving the same UBI, it would be difficult for any individual in a family to totally abuse their power and waste money in a way that wouldn't be obvious to other members of their family.
1.3.1.3.3.1.8.1.2.2.1. Pro: With a UBI, housewives will have their own money and no longer be completely dependent on their partner's salary.
1.3.1.3.3.1.8.1.2.3. Pro: There is less accountability for how one uses income comparted to conditional grants or particular items.
1.3.1.3.3.1.8.1.2.4. Pro: [Analysis of Canadian expenditure surveys](http://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/10.1111/1468-0335.00148/abstract) found that the gender of the recipient of a marginal increase in income predicted whether or not that income would be used to finance child care. This suggests that women should be targeted with grants to more efficiently improve social welfare, rather than everyone receiving equal incomes.
1.3.1.3.3.1.8.1.2.4.1. Con: The analysis is made with data from 1992, it is possible those gendered divisions have changed after more than 20 years.
1.3.1.3.3.1.8.1.2.4.2. Pro: In the National Rural Employment Guarantee Scheme \(NREGS\) in India female [NREGS](http://www.usc.es/economet/journals1/aeid/aeid14213.pdf) income goes towards children, while male income does not \(at least in the proposed categories, i.e. clothing and footwear\).
1.3.1.3.3.1.8.1.2.5. Pro: Often income would go into a shared bank account for a couple living together. Targeted welfare can often more easily reach women or children directly.
1.3.1.3.3.1.8.1.3. Pro: The needs of people are not static and there are unexpected costs that occur \(e.g. fees for a child's school trip\). Allowing people themselves to account for these changes is better.
1.3.1.3.3.1.8.1.4. Con: Targeted welfare, such as providing conditional or item-specific benefits, is not mutually exclusive to the poor also receiving cash grants.
1.3.1.3.3.1.8.1.4.1. Con: Some cash grants are not equivalent to a complete unconditional cash grant that offers full agency to those making decisions and not just partial agency.
1.3.1.3.3.1.8.1.5. Con: It is very easy for governments to analyse consumption patterns and account for seasonal changes.
1.3.1.3.3.1.8.1.5.1. Con: This kind of research is useful for analyzing broad trends, but it is inadequate for addressing different families with different circumstances and challenges.
1.3.1.3.3.1.8.2. Con: Most targeted programs only make minimal assumptions about the needs and desires of the disadvantaged: they provide things such as money, food, and childcare that are of universal value.
1.3.1.3.3.1.8.3. Pro: Welfare programs provide benefits designed for the average person who uses welfare. Most people do not reflect the average and have diverse needs, which can be better met by individuals making their own choices.
1.3.1.3.3.1.8.3.1. Con: Most welfare programs, as a baseline, provide money to those in need. Money can be used by people to purchase what they need, regardless of what those needs are.
1.3.1.3.3.1.8.3.1.1. Con: -> See 1.3.1.3.3.1.8.1.4.1.
1.3.1.3.3.1.8.3.2. Pro: A UBI helps decentralise the management of funds by allowing more local involvement in the process.
1.3.1.3.3.1.9. Pro: A UBI shifts purchasing power from the government to individuals, thereby incentivizing what people to purchase what they actually want rather than what the majority wants them to want.
1.3.1.3.3.1.9.1. Pro: Targeted welfare programs empower government busybodies to tell poor people how to live their lives.
1.3.1.3.3.1.10. Con: A UBI may be misused by the lower income population due to a lack of expertise in household financial management.
1.3.1.3.3.1.11. Con: This also affords UBI recipients the ability to exhaust their entire income on addictive substances \(sugary foods, alcohol, drugs, etc.\) which is why no one supports a full replacement of all social programs with UBI. There is no evidence that I am aware of that suggests that human beings make rational decisions when their incomes rise.
1.3.1.3.3.2. Pro: In some countries, governments guarantee a percentage of their salary to people who have been fired from their job. Often, that is a rate that would far exceed that of a UBI. This gives unemployed people of all incomes enough of a buffer to handle jobless periods.
1.3.1.3.3.2.1. Con: The duration of these guaranteed salaries is limited. A UBI would provide a source of income indefinitely.
1.3.1.3.3.2.1.1. Pro: Most targeted unemployment benefits have time limitations. Across Europe, only Belgium guarantees unlimited unemployment benefits. In other countries, the benefits last from 3 to 24 months for a worker with 22 years of contributions \([European Commission, pg. 5](http://ec.europa.eu/europe2020/pdf/themes/2015/unemployment_benefits_20151126.pdf)\).
1.3.1.3.3.2.2. Pro: [In Germany](https://www.angloinfo.com/how-to/germany/working/unemployment/unemployment-benefits)(If the claimant has no children, they receive 60 percent of their previous net earnings. If caring for children under 18, this rises to 67 percent.), the government guarantees those eligible for unemployment benefit at least 60% of their previous salary.
1.3.1.3.3.2.2.1. Con: However the high percentage of the former salary is only provided for some months. Afterwards the affected individuals experience a significant downgrade in payments.
1.3.1.3.3.2.3. Pro: Unemployment [programs](http://ec.europa.eu/europe2020/pdf/themes/2015/unemployment_benefits_20151126.pdf) in some European countries offer around [75%](http://www.iamexpat.nl/expat-page/official-issues/benefits/unemployment-benefit/requirements) of their last earned income rate for 1 to 2 years.
1.3.1.3.3.2.4. Con: These programs operate like an insurance scheme - people must have been earning a salary in the first place, and thus contributing to taxation.
1.3.1.3.3.2.5. Con: This is only true for people who meet certain criteria, such as having officially worked at least 6 months out of the last 12 or having worked at their last job for at least 3 years. Also: these benefits are usually cut off the second this person accepts a job - any job, even one that pays 10 times less than the "poverty line".
1.3.1.3.3.3. Con: Targeted welfare programs incentivize people to be able to qualify for the program \(e.g. not working\). This kind of incentive is generally dysfunctional, as are countermeasures to address the dysfunction. UBI addresses this problem by eliminating all qualification criteria, thereby making it neutral with regard to dysfunctional incentive.
1.3.1.3.3.4. Pro: Welfare programs have the advantage that they can direct welfare spending in order to promote positive choices by the recipients. Because a UBI provides direct cash payments this is not possible.
1.3.1.3.3.4.1. Con: [The vast majority](http://blogs.worldbank.org/impactevaluations/do-poor-waste-transfers-booze-and-cigarettes-no) of welfare recipients spend the money they receive responsibly.
1.3.1.3.3.4.2. Pro: Food vouchers can be used to direct the spending of the poor towards improving their diet and away from harmful choices such as drugs or alcohol.
1.3.1.3.3.4.2.1. Con: Even targeted assistance programs only place soft limits on spending. Any assistance that can be used to purchase goods \(ie food stamps\) [can be easily sold on a black market](http://www.washingtontimes.com/news/2013/aug/19/food-stamp-black-market-rise/).
1.3.1.3.3.4.2.2. Pro: At-risk families will tend towards addiction and spend a UBI on marijuana, drugs, alcohol and/or cigarettes. This would make these type of families more dependent on Social Services, costing the government even more. Funding like food stamps for all, is better as it restricts purchases to certain socially agreed tradable items. However recipients can still work and so earn money to purchase luxury items.
1.3.1.3.3.4.2.2.1. Con: There is no factual base for such a claim. [evidence](https://www.thedailybeast.com/the-myth-of-welfare-and-drug-use) exists to [support](http://ifyouonlynews.com/politics/nine-myths-the-right-loves-to-believe-about-welfare/) that [claims like this are myths](https://groundswell.org/7-lies-about-welfare-that-many-people-believe-are-fact/)
1.3.1.3.3.4.2.2.2. Con: Replacing various government assistance programs like SNAP with UBI allows for greater efficiency in distributing money. Instead of having to get government housing assistance in one place, food purchasing assistance in another, unemployment assistance in another, etc, one simple system would address basic income needs.
1.3.1.3.3.4.2.2.3. Con: To draw specific attention to something mentioned elsewhere on here: research by the federal government tells us that the percentage of people on welfare who are on drugs is about the same as in the U.S. as a whole. While many people imagine that people on welfare are more likely to be on drugs, that does not bear out in the research.
1.3.1.3.3.4.2.3. Con: Non-perishable foods can be sold or bartered away, although typically at a loss relative to the store price. Thus, the effect that food vouchers have on poor people who would buy drugs or alcohol over food is that they spend even less on food and even more on drugs than if they'd just had money to spend how they wanted.
1.3.1.3.3.4.3. Pro: Welfare programs often offer direct rebates for purchases that aid in securing employment, such as buying professional clothes or repairing a car need for a commute.
1.3.1.3.3.4.4. Con: Unlike targeted welfare, the point of UBI is precisely to empower people financially and spend such money freely, not being forced to follow a specific worldview as to which spending is positive and which is negative.
1.3.1.3.3.4.4.1. Pro: It is not the role of the government in a free society to direct the life choices of its citizens, so long as those life choices do not infringe on the rights of other citizens. The point of UBI is that the citizens themselves, and not the government, are better at deciding how their aid money can be best spent to most efficiently help their lives.
1.3.1.3.3.4.5. Pro: Cash disbursements have a tendency to be misused. It would be better to address societal problems more directly to make sure the money is used for positive outcomes.
1.3.1.3.3.4.5.1. Con: This claim contains no proof that its suggested methods would be any more effective than the provision of a UBI.
1.3.1.3.3.4.5.2. Con: Providing a UBI does not prevent any of these things from being implemented, and may in fact allow more time for individuals to provide labour to such causes.
1.3.1.3.3.4.5.3. Pro: People's basic needs can be addressed directly by providing soup kitchens, night shelters, used clothing distribution, free schooling and free healthcare.
1.3.1.3.3.4.6. Con: The government can still influence the spending by taxing undesirable items, such as cigarettes or alcohol, less favourably.
1.3.1.3.3.4.7. Pro: Society has the principled right to build paternalistic incentives into its provision of welfare, since welfare is funded through taking away money from the rest of the taxpayers and giving it to a particular person. As such, taxpayers should be able to impose conditions on the receipt of that money.
1.3.1.3.3.4.7.1. Pro: In Australia, parents who refuse to [vaccinate](http://www.cnn.com/2015/04/13/asia/australia-anti-vaccination-welfare-cut/) their children can lose up to $11,000 in benefits a year since this refusal is not supported by taxpayers or public policy.
1.3.1.3.3.4.7.1.1. Con: There is no reason why a UBI couldn't have basic requirements attached to them, such as vaccinations, schooling, and other public health and safety needs.
1.3.1.3.3.4.7.1.1.1. Con: Making UBI contingent on people obeying government mandates, no matter how reasonable, compromises the universal part: there's a real risk of it becoming the government paying people to make certain choices and not others.
