1.3.1.3.3.4.7.1.2. Con: People refuse vaccines, use drugs, or contribute to various causes for reasons other than welfare. To punish those individuals by leaving them outside of the welfare system is a violation of their basic dignity.
1.3.1.3.3.4.7.2. Con: Foremost society has a duty to help underprivileged citizens according to the basic human rights in the constitution, instead of a right to demand certain behaviors.
1.3.1.3.3.4.7.3. Con: The structure of those programs is decided by people who have enough power to get a majority of the legislature to vote in their favor — and that is rarely the powerless. There's no reason to expect that such conditions, imposed by the powerful on the weak — would have the effect of protecting the weak from the powerful.
1.3.1.3.3.4.7.4. Con: A UBI would not necessarily have to be funded by taxes. It could be funded by a sovereign wealth fund that is sustained with the proceeds of the use \(or abuse\) of our natural resources, eg mineral leases, public land forestry, fines on polluters. This money is our birthright, literally the “commonwealth” the founders envisioned.
1.3.1.3.3.4.7.5. Pro: When the rest of society grants money, charitably, to certain individuals, it is perfectly reasonable to design the conditions of that money to encourage the recipient to make socially optimal, rather than individually optimal, decisions.
1.3.1.3.3.5. Pro: According to the vast majority of experts, [replacing current welfare programs with UBI is a bad idea](http://www.igmchicago.org/surveys/universal-basic-income).
1.3.1.3.3.5.1. Con: This is a very biased survey, proposing to replace several essential services and valuable subsidies with a very low UBI. Many people who support UBI as a concept would vote against this particular proposal.
1.3.1.3.3.5.1.1. Con: The "very low UBI" in the proposal was roughly $13,000 per person. Across the entire United States \(assuming roughly 250M eligible recipients\), this would cost over $3,000,000,000,000 \($3 trillion\). 
If $13,000 is too low, it does not seem that anything "sufficient" would be affordable, as discussed by this [super-con.](https://www.kialo.com/argument/1634.1473)
1.3.1.3.3.5.2. Con: Policy effectiveness is a not a matter of voting. We rely on similar examples and previous studies to gauge the potential effects of new policies.
1.3.1.3.3.5.3. Con: This statement is misleading. A more accurate statement would read "A handful of experts don't agree that this particular version of a UBI would be successful."
1.3.1.3.3.5.4. Con: The thesis proposed in the survey tied UBI with elimination of basic public health insurance in the US. Health insurance is not a welfare program.
1.3.1.3.3.5.5. Con: This links to a relatively small panel of economists, not a sampling of all relevant experts across disciplines, making the wording of this claim highly misleading.
1.3.1.3.3.6. Pro: Many targeted welfare programs provide certain necessary goods \(food, medicines, education supplies\), unlike a UBI that will provide only money and will increase the number of things poor people need to worry about.
1.3.1.3.3.6.1. Con: Many welfare programs also offer cash that the recipients need to worry about distributing.
1.3.1.3.3.6.2. Pro: Poor people might experience anxiety from the necessity of managing money by themselves, as they were used to have a government providing goods for them.
1.3.1.3.3.6.2.1. Con: This is the same situation they'll be in when paid a wage.
1.3.1.3.3.6.2.1.1. Con: Even when being paid a wage the people may still be receiving some government benefits \(e.g. medicare\) that take some of the burden of budgetary planning off of the poor people's shoulders.
1.3.1.3.3.6.2.1.2. Con: Someone who is being paid a wage can be expected on average to be more fiscally/personally responsible than someone who is unable or \(perhaps temporarily\) unwilling to get a job. For example, someone may not be able to get a wage because they suffer from mental health issues—mental health issues which also may prevent them from budgeting well.
1.3.1.3.3.6.3. Con: Families know better than the state in which areas they need to spend, and in which they need to spend less.
1.3.1.3.3.6.3.1. Con: This assumes that individuals will generally act rationality, but this is a flawed assumption. Given this, "free range" money reasonably may not always lead to better provision of needs than traditional welfare.
1.3.1.3.3.6.3.1.1. Pro: Gambling is generally an irrational activity: it is empirically and theoretically known to \(occasionally\) be addictive, financially destructive, and mathematically illogical. Yet people still engage in this activity.
1.3.1.3.3.6.3.1.2. Pro: All people exhibit some degree of cognitive biases and other basic "thinking mistakes." One of the most notable is that of planning failure, as exemplified through procrastination. Another common example is that of the [gambler's fallacy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gambler%27s_fallacy), which causes some people to inaccurately assess the probability of crucial outcomes. Other important examples include [egotistical and confirmation biases](https://www.inc.com/sam-mcroberts/7-cognitive-biases-that-are-holding-you-back.html), such as where we choose to believe something simply because we want it to be true.
1.3.1.3.3.6.3.1.3. Pro: Hard drug usage \(e.g. meth, heroine, crack\) is generally considered to be an irrational activity, sometimes brought on by misleading environmental information. For example, if someone is misled by friends or the general culture he lives in that the drugs are not harmful—or that the habit is sustainable and the pleasure outweighs the harm—then they may irrationally turn to such drug usage.
1.3.1.3.3.6.3.2. Con: The parent claim does not offer any empirical studies to support what it is claiming.
1.3.1.3.3.7. Pro: Traditional welfare programs allow governments to target specific issues in specific communities, a benefit that is not available with a UBI.
1.3.1.3.3.7.1. Con: UBI can [target poverty](https://www.huffpost.com/entry/universal-basic-income-is_b_10251176), whatever the cause. If specific communities are poor, they will be implicitly targeted by a UBI; but targeting them explicitly generates ill-feeling toward those communities.
1.3.1.3.3.7.2. Con: UBI allows for the government to be able to cover almost everyone's needs using only one system. Subsequent welfare programs that are smaller in size can then be established to help certain individuals.
1.3.1.3.3.7.3. Con: Politicians focusing intervention on specific groups fosters corruption, as well as making those groups beholden to the politicians that keep them under the focus of welfare.
1.3.1.3.3.7.3.1. Pro: Out of a pool of equally needy people, politicians have incentive to enter into a cooperative relationship with the most organized, most politically active groups: this would basically amount to people who can otherwise not get enough money being forced to vote for whoever promises to keep them on welfare, as well as leave out people who might be needy, but are not a part of one of these client groups.
1.3.1.3.3.7.3.2. Pro: Welfare programs are there to support low-income people. Low-income people are the most vulnerable to be controlled through a program grant. Giving the individuals the possibility of deciding how to apply their income would prevent polítical or sectorial interests from controlling those people.
1.3.1.3.3.7.4. Pro: There are groups in society who require more support from the state than others. For example, those with illnesses and disabilities who require specialised care cannot live off a small amount of money but have no ability to work to increase their income.
1.3.1.3.3.7.4.1. Con: In every case except for those who are not physically healthy or able, the UBI will be sufficient for living expenses because that is how the amount is determined.
1.3.1.3.3.7.4.1.1. Con: Cost of living differs drastically by region. For example, if a UBI in the United States is set at the poverty line, those who live in Manhattan will not be able to afford basic living expenses.
1.3.1.3.3.7.4.1.1.1. Con: Initiatives like the [London Living Wage](https://www.london.gov.uk/what-we-do/business-and-economy/london-living-wage) calculate how much more money people living in an expensive city need in comparison to other places. Although this is a voluntary approach for businesses, a similar compulsory measure could be included in a UBI.
1.3.1.3.3.7.4.1.1.1.1. Con: This would result in uneven wages, giving people incentive to move into cities. This would compound itself over time, making it more expensive to operate and more difficult to fund.
1.3.1.3.3.7.4.2. Con: The medical treatment of illnesses and disabilities is covered by health insurance. A UBI enables individuals to pay for theirs.
1.3.1.3.3.7.4.2.1. Con: If an individual is allotted $10k a year worth of UBI payments, it would be not enough to cover health care costs in addition to daily living expenditures.
1.3.1.3.3.7.4.2.1.1. Con: It would be $10,000 in addition to their employment income, which would be enough for people to afford most health care services.
1.3.1.3.3.7.4.3. Pro: Groups with special needs, such as the severely ill or disabled, are able to get greater levels of assistance under targeted welfare programs than what a UBI would offer.
1.3.1.3.3.7.4.3.1. Pro: Across [the many different welfare systems](https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2015/apr/15/which-best-countries-live-unemployed-disabled-benefits), disability payments either exceed or top-up existing unemployment/welfare payments.
1.3.1.3.3.7.4.4. Pro: The loss of those extra support resources from targeted welfare schemes could lead to dire health problems for the chronically ill, who may no longer be able to afford adequate healthcare under private or increasingly privatized systems.
1.3.1.3.3.7.4.5. Con: It is right to defend a system where the poor are no longer forced into government endorsed establishments. Most benefits are in-kind: only 24% of US government benefits are direct cash assistance where governments have the authority to dictate what kinds of products the poor buy and who they buy those products from \([Brenkman, p. 125](http://bien2016.org/wp-content/uploads/2016/02/BIEN2016_Sess4_Brenkman_en.pdf)\).
1.3.1.3.3.7.4.6. Con: In systems with no public universal healthcare, the fact that the UBI helps the poor pay for their health will create demand for clinics that offer rates cheap enough for low income earners to afford.
1.3.1.3.3.7.4.6.1. Con: This requires doing so to be profitable, and it is not clear that even with access to a UBI, the poor in countries without universal healthcare - like America - would be able to pay enough to make opening low-cost clinics worthwhile.
1.3.1.3.3.7.4.6.2. Con: -> See 1.3.1.3.3.7.4.2.1.
1.3.1.3.3.7.4.7. Con: Under a system of in-kind welfare, governments are responsible for responding to the needs of constituents where they often inaccurately underestimate the needs of those with chronic illnesses. Market incentives provide competition and choice to people who are diverse with differential needs.
1.3.1.3.3.7.4.7.1. Con: The market does not care about those who cannot afford their products.
1.3.1.3.3.7.5. Con: Overcoming shortcomings in certain areas, like infrastructure, is still a responsibility of the government regardless of whether a UBI is implemented.
1.3.1.3.4. Pro: A UBI would fix the threshold and [poverty trap](http://www.investopedia.com/terms/p/poverty-trap.asp) effects induced by the current means-tested schemes.
1.3.1.3.4.1. Pro: Since it is universal, a person is free to raise their incomes without having to worry about losing their benefits. This fixes the perverse incentive that exists with traditional welfare programs.
1.3.1.3.4.1.1. Pro: Welfare creates a "perverse incentive" not to work as, especially with low wage jobs, individuals can find themselves worse off. The UBI does not create this perverse incentive.
1.3.1.3.4.1.2. Con: The level of taxation that UBIs require would create an even larger perverse incentive not to work.
1.3.1.3.4.1.2.1. Con: With a UBI, tax brackets can be adjusted, in order to produce incentives to perform work in the lower brackets.
1.3.1.3.4.1.2.2. Con: With an UBI in place, taxation can be applied more heavily to activities which are no longer performed by humans due to automation and AI proliferation, creating incentives for new and innovative human activities which are virtually tax free.
1.3.1.3.4.1.2.3. Con: -> See 1.2.7.8.5.5.4.1.
1.3.1.3.4.2. Pro: Most existing means-tested anti-poverty schemes exclude people because of their complexity, or because people don’t know how to apply or whether they qualify. With a UBI, people currently excluded from benefit allowances will automatically have their rights guaranteed.
1.3.1.3.4.3. Pro: [The Speenhamland system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speenhamland_system) was a form of outdoor relief intended to mitigate rural poverty in England at the end of the 18th century and during the early 19th century. It was the first UBI experiment and it was at the time very successful.
1.3.1.3.4.3.1. Con: The speenhamland system \(Berkshire Bread Act\) was a welfare system, not a UBI. Poor families were given extra money by the parish to top up wages enough to buy bread.
1.3.1.3.4.4. Pro: Welfare is a system that is built to merely keep people on sustenance, and never provides them with the resources to pull themselves out of poverty.
1.3.1.3.4.4.1. Con: The alternative to a UBI is not just status quo welfare but rather using all of the money that would go towards a UBI to massively expand welfare. This means welfare could include things such as education, which would allow people to escape poverty.
1.3.1.3.4.4.2. Pro: Benefits received from the Supplemental Nutrition Assistance Program \(SNAP\) are [not enough](http://www.npr.org/sections/thesalt/2016/03/03/468955099/the-snap-gap-benefits-arent-enough-to-keep-many-recipients-fed) to keep most beneficiaries fed. Many families are still forced to visit a food pantry each month.
1.3.2. Pro: The reliability of a UBI, relative to other forms of welfare, allows the poor the ability to better plan and budget their spending.
1.3.2.1. Pro: A UBI can be spent in productive ways \(e.g. investing into a business\) that traditional welfare cannot. This creates social mobility.
1.3.3. Pro: A UBI helps reduce governmental, bureaucratic, and regulatory burdens.
1.3.3.1. Pro: UBI reduce administrative control on citizen life, by making help unconditional, hence removing administrative power through arbitrary decisions
1.3.3.2. Pro: UBI is possibly the most cost effective solution for addressing homelessness, poverty, and crime as the overall cost is less than the cost of policing, medical care and lack of opportunities induced by poverty.
1.3.3.2.1. Pro: A UBI is a proactive approach to preventing abject poverty in the population, and proactive approaches are typically superior to reactive approaches. Studies have demonstrated [significant anti-poverty benefits](http://bassiounigroup.com/is-universal-basic-income-an-effective-way-to-eliminate-poverty%EF%BC%9F/) from the implementation of a UBI.
1.3.3.3. Con: Reducing government bureaucracy has a cost. Moving to a UBI system would put thousands of government employees, currently working in the welfare system, out of work. The transition will be difficult, and needs to be handled delicately.
1.3.3.3.1. Con: Maintaining inefficient programs to preserve jobs will lead to a very inefficient society in the long run. The cost of a delicate transition period is a one time cost, whereas the cost of keeping people at work to support inefficient welfare programs is a continuous cost.
1.3.3.3.2. Con: The transition would be softened considerably by the new UBI program.
1.3.3.3.3. Con: At least some existing government employees would be needed to handle the logistics of the new UBI system.
1.3.3.4. Con: UBI is a centralized system therefore it is subject to the [single point of failure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Single_point_of_failure) problem.
1.3.3.5. Pro: A process applied to many people equally is a lot simpler than many independent agencies and laws coming into play for fewer people.
1.3.3.5.1. Con: It may be simpler, but some people do not deserve or need a UBI \(wealthy individuals, for example\). Filtering out fewer people - who are in actual need - allows them to get more of the available resources.
1.3.3.5.2. Pro: A UBI is simpler to administer and is therefore less prone to denying deserving people coverage because of bureaucratic inefficiencies \(delays, miscategorisation of people, and other barriers to access\).
1.3.3.5.2.1. Pro: Many people are unaware of the fact that they are eligible for government programs; for example the US [has more than $300 million in unclaimed pension benefits](http://money.cnn.com/2013/01/24/pf/unclaimed-money/index.html). This lack of information often prevents them from accessing their entitlements.
1.3.3.5.2.2. Pro: With a one-size-fits all income transfer there is less need for governments to waste resources tracking down issues such as welfare fraud or when people cross into higher income thresholds.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1. Con: A UBI is subject to fraud.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.1. Pro: In the US, [federal agencies](https://www.downsizinggovernment.org/fraud-and-abuse) are often sloppy in their administration of programs, and Congress provides little serious oversight.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.1.1. Con: A UBI is much easier to administer than other welfare programs — all it requires is that the government send a check. This has little margin for error or exploitation.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.1.2. Con: By the same argument, the military has documented waste in the billions, year after year. Yet it is tolerated in the name of the greater good.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.1.2.1. Con: It is not well tolerated by some; there are plentiful criticisms of the American military, both inside and outside America.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.1.2.2. Con: This claim aides that the central government presiding over the military will have difficulties preventing UBI fraud if the same government wastes billions on other programs.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.1.3. Con: This is just as much an argument for no UBI as it is for a UBI. If there was UBI in the US it would be administered by the same flawed and inefficient system as current programs, which means that the same challenges would persist.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.1.4. Con: This is a systemic issue of congress and federal agencies, and shouldn't relate to UBI specifically. This is an issue which should be addressed regardless of subject matter.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.1.4.1. Con: If we accept that congress is sloppy, etc. then to say that we should implement this policy without first fixing said issue seems ill advised.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.1.4.1.1. Con: Using this argument we should stop building schools because some construction companies are corrupt.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.1.4.2. Con: We are discussing UBI specifically. The systemic issue existing does not detract from its affects on UBI and fraud.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.1.5. Con: Then the argument should be towards an improvement of program implementation, not the elimination of programs that benefit the people.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.1.5.1. Con: This discussion is about a UBI; however, and in the US, a UBI doesn't yet exist. So no elimination of programs is being considered. At this point it's more of a matter of the US shouldn't implement this program within a broken system.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2. Pro: Fraudulent use of the funds could include people claiming the UBI payments of care-dependent or deceased relatives.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.1. Con: Deceased people can easily be removed from eligibility lists when they are registered as dead.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.2. Con: Care-dependent people still merit a UBI payment. If those providing their care are collecting it and spending it on their behalf - with their permission - it is unclear why this is a problem.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.2.1. Con: The problem is that the family members may not actually be spending it on behalf of the care-dependent; they may just be taking it for themselves.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.2.1.1. Con: This would constitute financial abuse, which already happens with present-day pensions and disability and care payments. It is a separate issue, not an argument specifically against the UBI.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.2.1.1.1. Con: It is an argument specifically against the UBI because prevention methods would be less effective with UBI. That is to say, even if stronger laws/enforcement against this kind of financial abuse are enacted, the introduction of a UBI would increase the amount of financial abuse by making it far easier and widespread/applicable.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3. Pro: A UBI will incentivize certain people to have children irresponsibly so they can receive more money "for them".
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.1. Con: Deliberately claiming another's money and using it against their best interests would constitute domestic abuse, which would be penalized and thus disincentivized.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.1.1. Con: This would mean that a parent who buys food for their child \(paid for partly by the child's UBI\) would be commiting domestic abuse, which seems absurd. In practice, a UBI would probably allocate a children's income to their parents until they are 18 or not living at home.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.1.2. Con: Irresponsible use of children's UBI payments by parents is too hard to police properly.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.1.2.1. Con: Only if you give the child's UBI to the underage child or to their parents, instead of having it held in trust until they're old enough to make decisions about it.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.1.3. Con: Parents as the legal guardians of their children will have the right to administer their UBI payments until the children are of legal age.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.1.3.1. Pro: And because this amount is unconditionally paid to everybody, also to children, a UBI creates an inherent incentive to give birth to many children. As legal guardians, parents can freely dispose of the children's UBI.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.2. Con: This same incentive also exists under traditional welfare programs, most of which provide additional benefits to dependents.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.2.1. Pro: People across Europe are massively abusing children's allowance systems like the [German one](https://www.thelocal.de/20180321/germany-paying-350-million-in-child-benefits-to-children-abroad).
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.3. Con: This is nothing but supposition.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.4. Con: A UBI is generally considered to be an amount that is paid to everybody, and everybody gets the same amount. It is not depended on individual circumstances.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.4.1. Con: -> See 1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.1.3.1.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.5. Con: A UBI incentivises individuals to pursue their own individual aspirations by eliminating the barriers of poverty. Those who no longer envision their lives as being stuck in a multigenerational cycle of poverty would be less inclined to limit their options through an accidental pregnancy and less inclined to see the benefit of having a child when they could pursue their individual goals instead.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.6. Con: The distribution for having children could be based upon the marginal cost of an extra child as determined by the poverty guidelines. This would greatly diminish this effect.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.7. Con: This is assuming that UBI payments would be sent to underage citizens in a way that allows their parents to abuse it, as opposed to \(for example\) being held in trust and issued as a lump sum upon majority.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.8. Con: A UBI encourages more people to have children, solving the low birth rate problem that exists in many Western countries.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.8.1. Con: Countries with relatively high Human Development Index \(HDI\) ratings experience a [demographic transition](http://www.economist.com/node/14164483): fertility \(defined as the number of children born by a woman over her lifetime\) drops from around eight to near one and a half. As a UBI would further increase the HDI rating of a country \(in particular the income per person aspect\) this trend is likely to intensify.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.8.1.1. Con: One explanation of this phenomenon is the extensive entering of women into the [the workplace](http://www.economist.com/node/14164483). A UBI would mitigate the need for a job and enable women to focus on their motherhood, if wished. This could raise fertility rates again.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.8.1.1.1. Con: This assumes women enter the workforce because they need to, rather than because they want to.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.8.1.1.1.1. Con: Exactly how many people enter the workforce because they want to? Since when is joining the capitalist system vs being a marginalized "poor" outcast, a choice? People who "choose" to work are already either enjoying some kind of unearned income, or have lowered their quality of life to conform to what little they may legally get for free in the system.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.8.1.1.2. Con: The financial independence would allow women to pursue individual enrichment, such as gaining education, a desired career, an artistic pursuit, or travel opportunities. This freedom will let them put off motherhood until they are financially secure; thereby reducing birth rates
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.8.1.1.2.1. Con: Motherhood can be considered among the many forms of individual enrichment a woman might pursue.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.8.1.1.2.2. Con: That is not the case if having more children would increase the amount of money they would receive.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.8.1.2. Pro: If rich countries generally have [lower](http://www.independent.co.uk/news/world/europe/population-paradox-europes-time-bomb-888030.html) fertility rates, childcare costs are not the only [factor](https://evolution-institute.org/article/why-do-modern-people-have-so-few-children/) decreasing fertility and thus a monetary increase like a UBI is unlikely to boost it.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.8.1.3. Con: This claim indirectly implies we should avoid developing countries \(or reducing poverty\) in order to increase the birth rates. This is barbaric.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.8.2. Pro: The lack of [financial resources](http://www.rawstory.com/2016/08/the-real-reason-were-not-having-kids/) is an important reason why many individuals hold off on having children. A UBI mitigate this issue and helps the affected individuals to fulfil their desire to have children.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.8.2.1. Pro: The [average child](http://www.rawstory.com/2016/08/the-real-reason-were-not-having-kids/) in the U.S. represents an investment of $304,500 from the moment of birth to the child's 18th birthday. This is a significant amount.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.8.2.2. Pro: In the USA [child-care costs](https://www.washingtonpost.com/news/wonk/wp/2015/10/06/the-staggering-cost-of-daycare-when-you-make-only-the-minimum-wage/?tid=a_inl&utm_term=.f500cb489981) account for at least 30% of a minimum-wage worker’s earnings. In New York and Massachusetts the cost rises to as much as 80%.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.8.2.3. Pro: Surveys have shown that [European](http://www.independent.co.uk/news/world/europe/population-paradox-europes-time-bomb-888030.html) women want to have two or more children \(2.36 on average\). While countries in north-west Europe approach that number, in southern Europe they do not. Government action in the former, through child benefits, tax breaks, maternity leave or nursery provisions, may be a key factor.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.8.2.4. Con: UBI contributes to the planet's biggest future problem, that of over-population. By removing the responsibility of being able to afford having children, it will lead to a vicious downward spiral of starvation and violence as more people compete for less and less life-sustaining resources such as food and water.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.8.2.4.1. Con: Birth rates are increased in poverty, not in abundance. Hans Rosling [spoke](https://youtu.be/2LyzBoHo5EI?t=898) about this topic.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.8.2.4.2. Con: Over population should be considered a serious problem but using financial positions to keep people from reproducing is perhaps an irresponsible approach. If we need to be careful with our population then that issue should be addressed directly and honestly and people should be informed of it rather than relying on an unrelated aspect of society to steer some people away.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.8.2.4.3. Con: Overpopulation is now being shown to be a[non-issue](https://www.un.org/development/desa/en/news/population/world-population-prospects-2017.html), people are very aware of it and have reacted without being prompted. There have been fewer births recently than in a long time.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.8.2.5. Con: That claim is very much contrary to the evidence of the people that have more children are generally low income.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.8.2.6. Con: It may not be a good idea for people who do not have moneymaking know-how or a sense of responsibility to raise children who would grow up to have similar traits.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.8.2.6.1. Con: In a society under UBI, "responsibility" would become far more diverse than simply "providing for yourself" and "not living beyond your means".
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.8.3. Con: The birth rate [isn't too bad](https://www.google.com/search?q=birth+rate+in+us&rlz=1C1CHBF_enUS750US750&oq=birth+rate&aqs=chrome.1.69i57j0l5.13972j0j7&sourceid=chrome&ie=UTF-8). This assumes that finances are the main reason people don't have more kids.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.8.4. Con: There is no reason to assume low birth rates would be a problem.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.8.4.1. Pro: A birth rate below the replacement rate reduces human population \(without harming\), which is arguably desirable in this current state of overpopulation.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.8.4.2. Con: A low birth rate in one area or cultural segment will lead that segment to disappear in the long term, hence being taken over by other ways of thinking or living.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.8.4.2.1. Con: The disappearance of certain cultural segments is not necessarily a bad thing.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.8.4.2.1.1. Pro: The existence of certain cultural segments is not objectively good or bad for the society, therefore it is hard to argue with certainty that their disappearance will be bad.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.8.4.2.1.2. Pro: Many cultural segments have disappeared along human history and it is hard to quantify whether this fact was detrimental to humanity as a whole in terms other than intuition.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.8.4.2.1.3. Pro: The disappearance of certain cultural segments and the appearance of new ones is one of the factors that enables cultural evolution.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.8.5. Con: -> See 1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.2.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.8.6. Con: The World population \(and US population\) are climbing rapidly. China, India and Sub-Saharan Africa are currently overpopulated, requiring significant food imports to sustain the population.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.8.7. Con: Lower birth rates might be a better way of conserving resources especially among those with greater difficulty with job retention strengths.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.8.8. Con: The [annual cost](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cost_of_raising_a_child#United_States) of raising a child is likely more than a base income of, for example, ten thousand dollars. Welfare policies give benefits to parents, but that happens only because governments acknowledge the immense cost that goes with it.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.8.8.1. Con: -> See 1.2.7.6.3.14.1.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.8.8.2. Con: A UBI is not meant to cover all familiar needs, but gives an incentive to parents that otherwise could not afford raising a child only with their jobs.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.8.8.3. Con: No welfare policy is given solely out of recognising immense costs. The government would not be financially incentivising something it does not want to encourage.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.8.8.3.1. Con: Medicare and other provisions of healthcare are given out because the government recognizes the immense costs of healthcare for the elderly and disabled.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.8.8.4. Con: Children would likely be included in a UBI. An extra income would cover many of the expenses related to a child.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.9. Con: -> See 1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.8.8.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.4. Pro: The existing high rates of [benefit fraud](https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-57091551) are an indicatation of how easy it could be to abuse a system like UBI.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.3. Pro: Fraudulent use could include people creating false documentation in order to get paid more than once.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.3.1. Con: In order to avoid overspending on fraudulent persons, there is an incentive to monitor for fraud more carefully.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.3.1.1. Con: An incentive to monitor for fraud more carefully means a need for more bureaucracy, which is contrary to the claim that a UBI would reduce bureaucracy.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.3.2. Con: Similar kinds of fraud can exist in the current system; the parent does not explain why a UBI system would be more subject to fraud.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.3.2.1. Pro: The incentives for identity theft/fraud are already strong in developed economies. It is unclear that extracting traceable regular, relatively small UBI payments will be more attractive than e.g untraceable one-off, high value credit card fraud.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.4. Con: UBI reduces the types of fraud possible to carry out identity fraud. This makes it much simpler and easier to fight than many types of fraud.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.4.1. Pro: Most countries have a national ID number, and UBI could certainly be implemented alongside a stronger national identification database.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.4.2. Con: It's easier to fake an identity, than to fake an identity and make it look qualified to whichever entitlement program is being defrauded.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.5. Con: UBI is difficult to defraud due to being universal and unconditional.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.5.1. Pro: Whereas conditional income assistance can be exploited by taking the money without a genuine effort to move out of the program, a universal system has no such conditions and thus there is less room for exploitation.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.5.1.1. Pro: [In Kenya,](http://basicincome.org/news/2016/11/kenya-unconditional-cash-transfers-towards-unconditional-basic-income-randomized-controlled-trial-come/) the move from conditional to unconditional payouts is underway, and the effects are mostly positive, with little abuse of the system.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.5.1.1.1. Pro: A 12 year basic income experiment in [Kenya](http://nordic.businessinsider.com/kenya-village-disproving-biggest-myth-about-basic-income-2017-12) shows that people typically spend this money on necessities like education, medicine, and food.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.5.2. Pro: Because a UBI is unconditional, benefit fraud will likely decrease. The need to forge documents or misrepresent the truth in order to obtain benefits will become obsolete.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.5.2.1. Pro: Because everyone knows exactly how much and how often they should be receiving their payments, there is almost no room for corruption.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.5.2.2. Con: Not obsolete - the room for fraud/corruption would be almost entirely limited to attempting to claim for another person - alive, dead, or fictional.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.5.2.2.1. Con: Which is likely still more easily uncovered than most welfare fraud committed today.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.5.2.3. Pro: UBI registration will encourage a more accurate population count, thus removing the need for an expensive census. The stringency of registration rules would affect the subsequent rate of fraud within the system.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.5.2.3.1. Con: The census data is richer than that collected via a UBI.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.5.3. Pro: Controlling one universal income which applies to everyone with no distinction is easier than multiple sources of financial help that depend on the person's characteristics.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.5.4. Con: All government benefits are subject to fraud, including a UBI. Therefore, no government benefit program should exist, including UBI.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.5.4.1. Con: Ignoring the plight of large numbers of poor people can lead to rioting and other disruptions of peace.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.5.4.2. Con: While it is impossible to eliminate fraud from government benefit programs, the problems that would arise form eliminating them are much worse than the cost of keeping them.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.5.5. Con: The universal availability and equal distribution would actually make it more susceptible to fraud, because there's less scrutiny so it's easier to just invent a fake identity and collect multiple payments. With the current entitlement system, in addition to creating the fake identity, you have to make that fake identity look eligible for the entitlement.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.6. Con: -> See 1.1.3.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.7. Con: [Blockchain](https://futurism.com/the-first-blockchain-based-universal-basic-income-experiment-just-launched/) can be used to set up a transparent, immutable UBI system, that can be verified by anyone. [Grantcoin](http://www.grantcoin.org/) is an example of an UBI project that runs on the blockchain. It has been running for more than a year.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.7.1. Pro: Part of the answer to the problem of income inequality and meeting basic human needs will come from technology. Block chains could be a cost effective platform to create better mechanisms for managing public welfare spending.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.7.2. Con: Implementing UBI on a blockchain would need to go together with major social education and implementation of matching payment methods.
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.7.3. Con: Blockchain contracts are also vulnerable, and can fail in spectacular ways. [A hacker stole $31 million of Ether.](https://medium.freecodecamp.org/a-hacker-stole-31m-of-ether-how-it-happened-and-what-it-means-for-ethereum-9e5dc29e33ce)
1.3.3.5.2.2.1.8. Pro: UBI replaces many social welfare programs with central administration. This process streamlines transfer payments, it also creates an slush fund for demagogues. A diffuse & complicated system is difficult to suborn. A centralized source of patronage payments creates an irresistible and attainable slush fund for machine-politicians.
1.3.3.5.2.3. Pro: Means-tested benefits often [exclude many](https://www.theguardian.com/social-care-network/2013/jan/14/means-testing-benefits-not-efficient-fair) who are in need of support. Since a UBI is universal, those who need the money most would receive it.
1.3.3.5.2.4. Con: That ease comes at the cost of inefficiency of expenditure. The best system would efficiently spend money \(allocate it where it is most needed\) and assume it gets there \(eliminate bureaucratic efficiencies\), but logistical errors in a system do not warrant an entire systemic overhaul.
1.3.3.5.2.4.1. Pro: Allowing individual \(distributed\) decision-making provides for the money to flow where it is most needed \(in the recipient's informed opinion\), since the recipients of the UBI stipend will decide how to spend the money.  If the money is most needed in adjacent communities/sectors from those where it was initially disbursed, market forces will eventually drive the UBI money in that direction.
1.3.3.5.2.5. Con: Assuming that even a minimal level of currently existing welfare programs need to remain, the existence of a UBI disincentivizes the state to quickly reduce bureaucratic inefficiencies.
1.3.3.5.2.5.1. Con: Having fewer welfare programs allows the state to focus on making them efficient instead of having to spread its attention across all of the currently existing programs.
1.3.3.5.2.5.2. Pro: The presence of a UBI will demand government attention directed to implementing it. This means that less attention will go towards making other social welfare programs more efficient.
1.3.3.5.3. Pro: A UBI is cheaper to administer than existing welfare systems and thus frees would-be bureaucrats to contribute to the economy and thus wastes less money.
1.3.3.6. Pro: Current welfare systems are [complex](https://www.researchgate.net/publication/268176421_Complexity_in_welfare_systems_governance_and_the_changing_borders_of_the_third_sector_researching_two_local_multi-sectoral_partnerships) and fragmented between a range of issues and services. This necessitates \(and has resulted in\) a significant [bureaucracy](http://www.dailykos.com/story/2010/9/6/899569/-) which is highly cost ineffective and reduces the proportion of the welfare budget that is actually distributed.
1.3.3.6.1. Pro: In Nijmegen, in the Netherlands, roughly [90 million euros is given in welfare, but it costs 16 million](https://www.theguardian.com/world/2015/dec/26/dutch-city-utrecht-basic-income-uk-greens) euros to run the bureaucracy.
1.3.3.6.2. Pro: If the UBI replaces all other monetary welfare payments, it cuts related bureaucracy to zero, as it is transferred completely automatically to everyone every month.
1.3.3.6.3. Con: Even if welfare systems are overly complex, it does not necessitate UBI, which may come with unknown issues, as a solution.
1.3.3.6.3.1. Con: While UBI might not be the only solution, it does have one big advantage: simplicity. It would apply to everyone, so no need for huge administrations handling huge amounts of paperwork, and people wouldn't have to 1-know that they can apply for it \(not always obvious, especially for isolated people\), 2-prepare complicated applications \(which once again can exclude the people who need the most assistance\), 3-wait for said applications to come through \(even though their need might be urgent\).
1.3.3.6.4. Con: -> See 1.3.1.2.
1.3.3.6.5. Con: A UBI could be rife with weaknesses compared to traditional welfare programs—or problems of its own—requiring a whole new \(i.e. unfamiliar\) bureaucracy. As a result, the supposed efficiency increases might be nonexistent or likely minimal at best.
1.3.3.6.5.1. Pro: -> See 1.3.3.5.2.2.1.
1.3.3.6.5.2. Con: In convention welfare programs, the primary function of bureaucracy is to verify means testing on a case by case basis. In the absence of means-testing, it is difficult to imagine any function for the hypothesized bureaucracy. Hence the most reasonable conclusion is that such bureaucracy would not need to exist.
1.3.3.6.5.3. Con: It 'could' be rife with weaknesses but need not be. For example, if you have a universal basic state pension scheme that kicks in at a certain age \(66 ish and rising in the UK \), then the only mechanism needed would be to reduce the age threshold.
1.3.3.6.5.4. Pro: A blanket dividend such as UBI struggles to account for geographical disparities in the cost of living. In some regions, recipients might receive proportionally too much or too little as compared to other recipients in other areas.
1.3.3.6.5.4.1. Con: People can choose where they want to live, balancing tradeoffs such as cost of living vs services available and lifestyle. A UBI would prevent government from implicitly subsidizing certain geographic areas.
1.3.3.6.5.4.1.1. Pro: UBI that offers a consistent federal income, rather than heavily location-based benefits, makes moving away from overcrowded and expensive cities more appealing to low-income people.
1.3.3.6.5.4.1.2. Con: The cost to move is finite. Unless a newly implemented UBI accounts for this cost, some people in high cost of living areas will be trapped.
1.3.3.6.5.4.1.2.1. Con: It would be up to an individual to work a job to earn the funds to make a move to a city with lower living costs where the UBI will carry more financial weight. Undeveloped regions would begin to grow as a result of this incentive.
1.3.3.6.5.4.2. Con: The amount of UBI individuals receive could be tied to the cost of living of the areas they live in.
1.3.3.6.5.4.2.1. Con: There is no support that a central regulating body would be capable of making such complex analyses; on the contrary, it seems unlikely that such regulating bodies would be capable of efficiently/accurately doing this.
1.3.3.6.5.4.2.2. Pro: Cost of living is easily and regularly calculated by the Bureau of Labor Statistics. [bls.gov](https://www.bls.gov/cpi/)
1.3.3.6.5.4.3. Con: A UBI could account for geographic disparities in cost of living \(COL\) which would have the advantage of enabling people to move to cheaper areas with far less risk and allowing for income to be injected into areas with no current job market. UBI would therefore help alleviate COL disparities and create many new markets.
1.3.3.6.5.4.4. Con: UBIs would encourage people who can work remotely to move to areas with low costs of living. This could lead to a repopulation of neglected rural areas and take housing pressure off cities.
1.3.3.6.5.4.4.1. Con: People who work remotely already receive at very least a modest salary, therefore the incentive would already be there and not increased by UBI.
1.3.3.6.5.4.4.1.1. Pro: Without the UBI on top of their incomes, remotely working individuals have a higher incentive to move in low-cost areas.
1.3.3.6.6. Con: Current welfare systems may or may not be complex/fragmented and require more bureaucracy, but these extra costs should be weighed against the UBI's extra costs: money which is paid to the 100 million \(or so\) people who would already be well off without an income boost. These costs could easily be as high as $500 billion or more per year \(just assuming a mere $420/month payment\).
1.3.3.6.7. Pro: Navigating bureaucracy takes time that a person can better spend seeking employment, caring for others, in leisure or in the community.
1.3.3.6.8. Con: While UBI may simplify many forms of welfare, a system to provide additional means-tested benefits is needed. To be fair the UBI sufficient for a single home-owning person will not meet the needs of a single person with children having to pay rent. Not all the bureaucracy disappears.
1.3.3.6.8.1. Pro: UBI could replace the basic unemployment benefit but systems would still be required to provide other welfare benefits such as disability, child care, etc. The admin savings claimed may not be as great as claimed.
1.3.3.6.8.2. Con: Children would also receive a UBI.
1.3.3.6.8.3. Con: It would not have to eliminate other assistance.
1.3.3.6.9. Pro: Income redistribution agencies absorb about two-thirds to three-quarters of each dollar budgeted to them in overhead costs. Using government data, Robert L. Woodson \(1989, p. 63\) calculated that, on average, 70 cents of each dollar budgeted for government assistance goes not to the poor, but to the members of the welfare bureaucracy and others serving the poor. Michael Tanner \(1996, p. 136 n. 18\) cites regional studies supporting this 70/30 split.
1.3.4. Con: There are alternative policies that are superior and more efficient to a UBI.
1.3.4.1. Con: The existence of alternatives, even more effective ones, does not make a UBI a bad idea.
1.3.4.1.1. Con: Choosing less effective and inferior solutions can be seen as a bad idea.
1.3.4.1.2. Con: If there are better alternatives, countries should provide those, not UBI. \(We are not discussing whether UBI is a good or bad idea, we are discussing whether it should or shouldn't be implemented.\)
1.3.4.1.3. Pro: Many suggest that UBI would actually be [more successful](https://www.vox.com/future-perfect/2019/10/18/20919322/basic-income-freedom-dividend-andrew-yang-automation) if accompanied by other social programs.
1.3.4.1.3.1. Con: The UBI, when combined with other social programs, would result in even higher costs, including all of the negative consequences thereof.
1.3.4.2. Pro: A [negative income tax](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Negative_income_tax) \(NIT\) refund is equivalent to a taxed UBI, but more cost-effective.
1.3.4.2.1. Con: Even though the negative income tax would be more cost-effective, it has other major disadvantages.
1.3.4.2.1.1. Pro: If implemented in a country where tax returns are awarded once a year, a negative income tax will provide this source of welfare in one lump sum once a year.
1.3.4.2.1.1.1. Pro: Relying of single lump sum payment to provide support for an entire year results in a difficult budgeting challenge for people who may already have difficulties in that area.
1.3.4.2.1.1.2. Pro: A welfare system paid in a lump sum may not adequately help an individual who experiences a sudden change in their financial situation.
1.3.4.2.1.2. Pro: A negative income tax only helps lower-income members of society, whereas a UBI helps more of the middle class.
1.3.4.2.1.2.1. Con: A negative income tax can in theory be designed to have very high thresholds for when income taxation begins. For example, such a policy may not even begin taxation until $100,000.
1.3.4.2.2. Con: As the labour market in society is becoming increasingly automated, the lack of jobs would make a negative income tax ineffective.
1.3.4.2.2.1. Con: The lack of jobs has little impact on the effectiveness of negative income taxes: negative income taxes [do not](https://smartasset.com/taxes/negative-income-tax-explained#:~:text=In%20order%20to%20qualify%20for,it%2C%20regardless%20of%20employment%20status.) \(necessarily\) require someone to be employed to receive benefits.
1.3.4.2.2.2. Pro: Automation and the increased use of technology [affects](http://www.mckinsey.com/business-functions/digital-mckinsey/our-insights/four-fundamentals-of-workplace-automation) the whole socio-economic spectrum from low to higher-income professions.
1.3.4.2.2.2.1. Pro: As many as [45%](http://www.mckinsey.com/business-functions/digital-mckinsey/our-insights/four-fundamentals-of-workplace-automation) of the activities individuals are paid to perform can already be automated by adapting current technologies.
1.3.4.2.2.2.1.1. Pro: It has been estimated that [47% of US jobs are at risk of automation](http://www.oxfordmartin.ox.ac.uk/downloads/academic/The_Future_of_Employment.pdf) by 2040. There is no way for the law of creative destruction to catch up. The great depression hit its height at 30%, and UBIs are the only feasible solution to keep money flowing through the economy while we adapt.
1.3.4.2.2.2.1.1.1. Pro: In 2016, 45% of [McDonalds](https://www.thestreet.com/story/13511123/4/mcdonald-s-rolling-out-self-ordering-kiosks-in-europe-will-u-s-be-next.html) restaurants in Europe rolled out in-store kiosks to automate the ordering process. While convenient, the kiosks take the place of jobs with raising minimum wage costs.
1.3.4.2.2.2.1.1.2. Pro: Even creative domains are at risk of being automated.
1.3.4.2.2.2.1.1.2.1. Pro: There is [software](https://www.wired.com/2015/10/this-news-writing-bot-is-now-free-for-everyone/) that generates texts and stories and it is already used by the Associated Press to cover [sports](https://www.poynter.org/2016/the-associated-press-will-use-automated-writing-to-cover-the-minor-leagues/419489/).
1.3.4.2.2.2.1.1.2.2. Pro: A supercomputer successfully created a totally AI based [movie trailer](https://phys.org/news/2016-09-supercomputer-world-ai-created-trailer.html).
1.3.4.2.2.2.1.1.2.3. Con: The current methods for creating art using an AI are based on pattern and rule recognition followed by recombination of preexisting data. Artificial Intelligence currently has no idea of context and cannot create art without specific human direction.
1.3.4.2.2.2.1.1.3. Pro: Between 2000 and 2010 the US lost 5.6 million [manufacturing jobs](https://www.ft.com/content/dec677c0-b7e6-11e6-ba85-95d1533d9a62) and 85% of these losses can be attributable to technological change \(mainly automation\).
1.3.4.2.2.2.1.1.4. Pro: Fast spreading new [technologies](https://www.ft.com/content/dec677c0-b7e6-11e6-ba85-95d1533d9a62) like advanced robotics or 3D printing will obviate millions of low-skilled jobs.
1.3.4.2.2.2.1.1.5. Pro: In the [medical sector](http://www.economist.com/news/special-report/21700758-will-smarter-machines-cause-mass-unemployment-automation-and-anxiety) deep-learning systems are already more accurate than humans in classifying malignant tumors from CT scans, or wrist fractures from X-ray scans.
1.3.4.2.2.2.1.1.6. Pro: A new software by JPMorgan \([COIN](https://www.bloomberg.com/news/articles/2017-02-28/jpmorgan-marshals-an-army-of-developers-to-automate-high-finance?cmpid=socialflow-facebook-business&utm_content=business&utm_campaign=socialflow-organic&utm_source=facebook&utm_medium=social)\) for Contract Intelligence is able to replace whole teams of lawyers and loan officers, interpreting commercial-loan agreements faster and with fewer errors.
1.3.4.2.2.2.1.1.7. Con: [Very few occupations—less than 5 percent—are candidates for full automation today.](https://www.mckinsey.com/~/media/McKinsey/Global%20Themes/Digital%20Disruption/Harnessing%20automation%20for%20a%20future%20that%20works/A-future-that-works-Full-report-MGI-January-2017.ashx) A surplus of human labor is much less likely to occur than a deficit of human labor, unless automation is deployed widely.
1.3.4.2.2.2.1.1.8. Con: The Frey and Osborne paper ["is a set of guesses with lots of padding to increase the appearance of 'scientific precision'.”](https://robotenomics.com/2016/07/28/new-research-fears-of-technological-change-destroying-jobs-may-be-overstated/) Researchers at the OECD analyzed the Frey and Osborne study and conducted their own research on tasks and jobs and concluded that: “automation was unlikely to destroy large numbers of jobs.”
1.3.4.2.2.2.1.1.9. Pro: It is estimated that [4 million jobs](http://www.marketwatch.com/story/hello-self-driving-cars-goodbye-41-million-jobs-2016-09-15) in the US will be replaced by self-driving cars over the next decade.
1.3.4.2.2.2.1.1.10. Pro: ATMs are substituting bank employees worldwide, the same as Home-banking is: nearly all done via technology.
1.3.4.2.2.2.1.1.11. Con: That prediction of replacement is based on current day jobs and the probability of automation based on the predicted abilities of automatons, not other factors such as labor costs, automaton costs, market demand trends or future industries. Schumpeter's [entire creative destruction concept](https://www.zdnet.com/pictures/the-evolution-of-office-technology-from-the-typewriter-to-the-tablet/) is one that explains exactly why such predictions and fears never measure up to actual real world outcomes.
1.3.4.2.2.2.1.2. Con: Fewer than [5%](http://www.mckinsey.com/business-functions/digital-mckinsey/our-insights/four-fundamentals-of-workplace-automation) of occupations can be entirely automated using current technology. Thus, it is likely that positions will not entirely disappear, only the tasks and activities humans perform will change.
1.3.4.2.2.2.1.2.1. Con: Technological progress occurs rapidly and what is not possible today could very well be possible in a few years.
1.3.4.2.2.2.1.2.2. Con: As human-performed activities will be reduced, jobs will transform from full-time to part-time. This will result in lower wages and fewer benefits.
1.3.4.2.2.2.1.2.2.1. Pro: About 60% of all occupations could see 30% or more of their constituent [activities automated](http://www.mckinsey.com/business-functions/digital-mckinsey/our-insights/where-machines-could-replace-humans-and-where-they-cant-yet) with today's technologies.
1.3.4.2.2.2.1.2.3. Con: Occupations do not have to be entirely automatable to result in significant job losses. An occupation that is, for example, 60% automatable, is likely to result in employers reducing their staff in that occupation by 60% and reallocating the unautomatable work accordingly.
1.3.4.2.2.2.2. Pro: UBI is necessary to combat the fact that AI will be able to do any task better than humans, physical and cognitive. Unlike the previous industrial revolution, both physical and mental jobs will be replaced.
1.3.4.2.2.2.2.1. Pro: Society will reach a point where general artificial intelligences will be able to learn at a faster rate than humans, thus rendering human labor inefficient and economically impractical.
1.3.4.2.2.2.2.1.1. Pro: This can already been seen with IBM's artificial intelligence system, [Watson](http://time.com/4960778/computers-smarter-than-humans/). It has the increasing ability to learn and to solve problems that are too complex to maintain in the human mind.
1.3.4.2.2.2.2.2. Con: The technological singularity is not a 'fact' at all. It is a hypothesised possibility of a future we might live in.
1.3.4.2.2.2.2.2.1. Con: You don't require singularity to have most jobs replaced by highly specialized AIs.
1.3.4.2.2.2.2.3. Pro: As AI increases, more and more jobs will become obsolete. UBI will only make sense at this point since the majority of people will not be able to make an 'honest' living anymore.
1.3.4.2.2.2.2.4. Pro: A UBI would enable AI to do work that no longer should be done by humans - binary, repetitive, programmable. This frees humans to do work which is creative, exploratory, self fulling, voluntary, for remuneration of not is their choice as they have a basic income to fulfill basic needs.
1.3.4.2.2.2.2.4.1. Con: This is pro is contingent on having efficient and useful AI tools to achieve a rational savings enough to create a universal income. Current machine learning allows us to have high level of sales development tools and makerting web software. While the advancements of ecommerce is a popular idea to suggest the removal of work forces and thus rationalize a UBI, there could be more jobs available for people to work that as a result of AI.
1.3.4.2.2.2.2.5. Pro: This claim does not necessarily rely on the singularity but on the fact that as technology increases the productive capacity of each individual, there will come a time where two trends intersect- first a relatively small number of workers will be able to produce sufficient goods for society and second that there will be a significant number of people who are incapable of developing the skills needed to be economically viable. A UBI is needed if 20% of the population is not economically useful.
1.3.4.2.2.2.2.6. Pro: Consumers are also workers, so if there are no workers, there are no consumers. A UBI provides money to consumers to keep the economy going, when all the work is automated. The economy exists to provide goods and services to consumers, they are therefore the primary source of money in the economic cycle. [Elon Musk: Automation Will Force Universal Basic Income](https://www.geek.com/tech-science-3/elon-musk-automation-will-force-universal-basic-income-1701217/)
1.3.4.2.2.2.2.7. Pro: Google's Deepmind [won](https://www.vox.com/future-perfect/2019/2/15/18226493/deepmind-alphafold-artificial-intelligence-protein-folding) by a huge margin against all researchers in the annual protein folding prediction competition \([CASP13](http://predictioncenter.org/casp13)\).
1.3.4.2.2.2.3. Pro: UBI will become increasingly necessary as advances in technology render larger and larger portions of the population to be unemployable.  UBI would help us retain societal order, rather than mass unemployment causing chaos. [Humans Need Not Apply - CGP Grey](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=7Pq-S557XQU)
1.3.4.2.2.2.4. Con: Policy acceptance is more dependent on perceived vulnerability than actual vulnerability. Insofar as most higher-income professionals do not view themselves at risk, they are therefore still unlikely to support a policy that they see as irrelevant to their interests.
1.3.4.2.2.2.5. Con: Executive-level jobs tend to rely on more transferable general management and administrative skills. It may be true that single-industry workers are most at risk of obsolescence if their factory closes down whereas professionals, managers, executives and technical occupations have comparatively less trouble transferring to a laterally-related field.
1.3.4.2.3. Pro: A negative income tax achieves one of the goals of a UBI by cutting down the costs of welfare administration.
1.3.4.2.4. Con: Whether or not a negative income tax can be more cost-effective depends to a large extent on the individual countries and their administrative systems.
1.3.4.2.4.1. Pro: The negative taxation mechanism would not be 'cheaper' in the UK: We do not have 100% interaction with the Inland Revenue. The majority of people are taxed though their workplace \(PAYE\) and payroll deductions. Some, such as the self employed, do have direct interaction \(self assessment process, similar to the US \) . The unemployed have no contact at all. [www.economicshelp.org](https://www.economicshelp.org/macroeconomics/inequality/tax_uk/) and [www.gov.uk](https://www.gov.uk/self-assessment-tax-returns) .
1.3.4.2.4.2. Pro: In the UK the cheaper way would be as though an 'early onset' pension. The 'channel' for paying it should be the pension department \(DWP \) that \(by virtue of a National Insurance link\) does have near-universal interaction with all people, unlike tax department \(HMRC\).
1.3.4.2.4.3. Con: Many countries have existing [revenue services](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Revenue_service), and an NIT uses that existing infrastructure.
1.3.4.2.5. Pro: A UBI wastes resources by distributing funds to the already wealthy. A negative income tax does not have this problem.
1.3.4.2.5.1. Con: Under many methods of funding a UBI, the wealthy end up partially paying for it in the first place.
1.3.4.2.5.1.1. Pro: Wealthy citizens will pay more in tax than they receive from the UBI.
1.3.4.2.5.1.1.1. Con: This tax would have been paid anyway, so should not be considered when deciding between a welfare/UBI system.
1.3.4.2.5.1.1.1.1. Con: If the UBI comes with new taxes implemented specifically to help pay for it, then those taxes would not have been paid anyway.
1.3.4.2.5.1.1.1.1.1. Con: If the new taxes primarily target wealthy people \(as opposed to being an across-the-board raise\) then the program is just a negative income tax in disguise.
1.3.4.2.5.1.1.1.1.2. Con: Introducing new taxes involves its own set of costs that would need to be evaluated and balanced against the benefits of having a UBI.
1.3.4.2.5.1.1.1.1.2.1. Pro: Higher taxes can lead taxpayers to try to more actively [minimize their tax burden](https://www.investopedia.com/financial-edge/1211/how-taxes-affect-the-economy.aspx), resulting in stagnant or lower tax revenues.
1.3.4.2.6. Pro: Negative income taxes distort wealthy people's incentive to work less than a UBI does. Under a UBI, wealthy people are taxed at a percent of their income and then a flat amount is returned to them under the UBI. This makes their marginal tax rate higher than if they were simply subject to a NIT.
1.3.4.3. Pro: A [work guarantee](https://www.kialo.com/the-us-government-should-guarantee-every-citizen-a-job-24455.1) is preferable to a UBI.
1.3.4.3.1. Pro: A work guarantee will help workers [retain](https://www.socialeurope.eu/job-guarantee-programs-careful-what-you-wish-for) their job skills they would otherwise lose if unemployed for long periods of time.
1.3.4.3.2. Con: A work guarantee would negate the ideal perspective of each individual determining their own meaning in life.
1.3.4.3.2.1. Pro: People have different preferences in life, and given the vast amount of opportunities for different careers, it's principally wrong to deny them these opportunities. Some may want to be philosophers, while others might want to become biohackers or DNA-explorers.
1.3.4.3.3. Pro: A UBI is not able to provide individuals with purpose, self-worth, or social contact. A work guarantee can do so.
1.3.4.3.3.1. Con: A UBI is preferable to a work guarantee because it allows people to define for themselves what they see as a sense of purpose, meaning in life, identity etc. whether that be work or something else which is most likely still useful.
1.3.4.3.3.1.1. Pro: Currently unpaid roles such as parenting and caring for loved ones/family, would continue to be unvalued with a work guarantee. A UBI would give carers this sense of purpose, self-worth and social contact that they deserve.
1.3.4.3.3.1.1.1. Con: A UBI would not directly change the social status or recognition of unpaid family roles.
1.3.4.3.3.1.2. Pro: People will work harder and be more motivated when working towards goals they personally understand, identify with and/or see the value in.
1.3.4.3.3.2. Con: Looking at [Bullshit Jobs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bullshit_Jobs), it becomes very questionable how a work guarantee is supposed to do better in providing purpose and self-worth to individuals.
1.3.4.3.3.2.1. Pro: [A 2015 UK survey](https://www.newyorker.com/books/under-review/the-bullshit-job-boom) found that 37% said no and 13% were not sure - when asked if they think their work makes a significant contribution to the world.
1.3.4.3.3.2.2. Pro: [A poll in the Netherlands](https://www.newyorker.com/books/under-review/the-bullshit-job-boom) concluded that 40% of respondents believed their jobs had no reason to exist.
1.3.4.3.4. Con: A work guarantee will create distortions in the economy by crowding out private-sector activity, which can reduce economic growth.
1.3.4.3.4.1. Con: The job markets are becoming smarter and are being based on big data. This will help find the 'right' job for everyone in the private sector.
1.3.4.3.4.2. Con: Many of the world largest employers are state-owned. The potential distortions created will be minor.
1.3.4.3.4.2.1. Con: The fact that government employers already have a huge impact on the economy through government monopolies in various sectors is no argument that even further government incursion into the job market will produce only minor distortions.
1.3.4.3.4.2.2. Pro: The world's number one employer is the [United States Department of Defense](https://www.msn.com/en-in/money/photos/the-worlds-30-largest-employers-will-surprise-you/ss-BBKxFrN#image=31) \(2.87 million employees\).
1.3.4.3.4.2.3. Pro: [China's armed forces](https://www.msn.com/en-in/money/photos/the-worlds-30-largest-employers-will-surprise-you/ss-BBKxFrN#image=30) are the second-largest employer \(2.35 million employees\).
1.3.4.3.4.3. Pro: Jobs provided by the government will destroy the purpose and efficiency of a free market.
1.3.4.3.4.3.1. Pro: If governments create many jobs for people that would otherwise do unpaid care work, there will be A LOT of care workers, many more than actually needed \(inefficient\) and private care institutions become kind of obsolete.
1.3.4.3.4.3.2. Con: There is no such thing as a purely 'free' market. National, federal, and local governments interfere with employment by subsidies, enticement, and taxation.
1.3.4.3.4.3.2.1. Pro: Governments are [lobbied](https://www.itif.org/publications/2018/03/26/how-ensure-americas-life-sciences-sector-remains-globally-competitive) to support sectors , [often with success.](https://www.gov.uk/government/topical-events/the-uks-industrial-strategy)Therefore the conceptual free market does not exist. [US example](https://thewordenreport-governmentandmarkets.blogspot.com/2016/12/modern-day-mercantilism-donald-trump.html)
1.3.4.3.5. Con: A work guarantee does not help people who cannot work because they are disabled, ill, or a caregiver for a family member who is disabled or ill.
1.3.4.4. Pro: -> See 1.3.1.3.3.
1.3.4.5. Pro: A [participation income](https://www.involve.org.uk/resources/blog/opinion/what-about-participation-income) would be better than UBI.
1.3.4.5.1. Pro: If there is some requirement to contribute to society in some way \(work, volunteer, go to school, create art, start a business, teach, mentor people, join the military, etc.\), the government could use the program to encourage people to contribute to society in specific ways.
1.3.4.5.1.1. Con: If work is required for a UBI, it goes against the premise of a UBI which is provide a living wage for all without work needed. It therefore becomes a “fast track” of sorts to get a job. However, we currently have more positions available for work than unemployed. If the person wanted a job, the opportunities are available right now and a UBI wouldn’t be needed.
1.3.4.5.1.1.1. Con: Lack of social contribution is one of the biggest hindrances to a UBI. A Participation Income would prioritize self worth and self improvement while providing a support model.
1.3.4.5.1.1.2. Con: In Australia, the ratio of unemployed workers to jobs can get as high as [18:1](http://unemployedworkersunion.com/job-seekers-v-job-vacancy-data/).
1.3.4.5.2. Con: The government should not have the ability to determine whether someone is contributing to society.
1.3.5. Pro: A UBI can help tackle different types of inequality.
1.3.5.1. Con: Studies have shown that a UBI does very little to decrease inequality \([p.1](https://www.brookings.edu/wp-content/uploads/2019/08/UBI-ESG-Memo-082319.pdf)\). If implemented and inequality still remains, there is likely to be political unrest on both sides.
1.3.5.2. Con: Universal Basic Income would not completely address economic inequality because it would not change the fact that at least some higher end services and goods would be too expensive for somebody on such income to afford, such as private health insurance.
1.3.5.2.1. Con: A UBI may not eliminate inequality \(and total equality may not even be desirable\) but it will help to prevent inequality from reaching levels which create suffering, and create a safety net for those on the "short end" of the "inequality stick".
1.3.5.2.2. Con: The desire to have access to luxury products and higher end services are still good incentives for work.
1.3.5.2.3. Con: Universal health care would not require a health insurance for basic health services.
1.3.5.2.4. Con: The aim of the UBI isn't so that we can afford anything we wanted to, but that we would have every opportunity necessary to survive and succeed in life.
1.3.5.3. Con: People don't want to create financial equality for all \(as a UBI would provide\) but instead demand financial fairness, [where their efforts are rewarded and opportunities are accessible](https://www.theatlantic.com/science/archive/2015/10/people-dont-actually-want-equality/411784/).
1.3.5.3.1. Con: UBI would not create financial equality for all, as those who worked for pay or sold desired goods, ideas, or services could earn money above and beyond the provision of the UBI. This would also allow for efforts to be rewarded and opportunities to be accessible.
1.3.5.4. Con: Without other policies, a UBI alone would not be able to address the structural causes of inequality.
1.3.5.4.1. Pro: Having a UBI could reduce the political will needed to stop other welfare programs from shrinking or being discontinued. However, these other programs that would be necessary alongside a UBI.
1.3.5.4.1.1. Pro: The all-encompassing and comprehensive nature of UBI is unlike any other form of welfare \(which is much more targeted and small scale\). Given this, a UBI is likely to be seen as a complete solution to poverty and a replacement for current welfare policy.
1.3.5.4.1.2. Pro: Since instituting welfare programs requires political capital and public goodwill, a majority of which will be used instituting a UBI, it is unlikely that the general public will be sympathetic towards additional welfare programs if a UBI is implemented.
1.3.5.4.1.2.1. Pro: For those in the middle and lower classes, there is unlikely to be buy-in towards a system that also distributes money to wealthy individuals who are not in financial need.
1.3.5.4.1.2.1.1. Pro: Wealthy people are able to meet their basic needs already. A UBI is not necessary for them to achieve adequate housing, medical care, education, or proper nutrition as it is with middle and lower class individuals.
1.3.5.4.1.2.1.2. Con: The money paid out to the wealthy through a UBI will be offset through increases in progressive taxes.
1.3.5.4.1.2.1.2.1. Pro: A UBI can be combined with shifting taxing schemes from income-based taxes towards capital-based taxes, which would increase the liquidity of money and ensure that capital has a reasonable return rate while also forcing those who own capital to invest it in value-creating activities instead of just "sitting on it".
1.3.5.4.1.2.2. Pro: The public is reluctant to fund welfare because our society has deeply ingrained the capitalist idea of rewarding individuals for effort and working hard. Hard work is seen as a virtue, and something deserving of reward, and those with wealth they have not themselves earned \(for example, the rich children of billionaires\) are vilified.
1.3.5.4.1.2.2.1. Pro: Rich people are likely to believe that those in poverty are receiving money for nothing. Even now, with welfare usually contingent on demonstrating that one is at least looking for employment, stigma against those who "leech off welfare" without contributing is rampant.
1.3.5.4.1.2.2.2. Con: It tends to be poor people who do not work that are discriminated against, while rich people, including [inheritors](http://www.executivestyle.com.au/heir-style-the-rich-kids-who-have-it-all-2xbt0) and rich children, can become idols.
1.3.5.4.1.2.3. Pro: In Switzerland, the concept of a UBI was rejected [by close to a 3-1 margin](https://www.google.com/url?q=http://www.bbc.com/news/world-europe-36454060&sa=D&ust=1479913397736000&usg=AFQjCNGl2VcGG-v8Z_Qa1w8vKXvWgh59Cw), reflecting how politically unpopular a UBI is, and how much it is likely to squander political capital towards welfare if instituted.
1.3.5.4.1.2.3.1. Con: While Switzerland may have rejected the vote, 64% of Europeans in general were in favor of a UBI according to [a 2016 poll](https://daliaresearch.com/two-thirds-of-europeans-for-basic-income-dalia-ceo-presents-surprising-results-at-future-of-work-conference-in-zurich/).
1.3.5.4.1.2.3.2. Con: [A recent survey](https://futurism.com/survey-reveals-nearly-50-of-americans-favor-a-universal-basic-income/) in the US, a country known to favour free-markets and oppose government policy that could be construed as socialist, found that nearly half of Americans would support a UBI.
1.3.5.4.1.2.3.3. Con: As Switzerland already has the second [highest](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_\(nominal\)_per_capita) GDP per capita, its interest in implementing a UBI is obviously lower than that of comparatively poorer countries.
1.3.5.4.1.2.3.4. Con: Switzerland is an economically conservative country with a relatively small welfare system. More truly liberal countries such as Finland and the Netherlands are currently piloting the program with little backlash.
1.3.5.4.1.2.3.4.1. Con: One concern brought to light by the [Finnish experiment](https://www.jacobinmag.com/2017/01/ubi-finland-centre-party-unemployment-jobs/) is that a person's allotment of the UBI replaces their state benefits, which are controlled by labour unions. A person may be loth to accept UBI and be blocked out of his/her union as a result.
1.3.5.4.1.2.3.4.1.1. Con: This a situation entirely unique to Finland.
1.3.5.4.1.2.3.4.2. Pro: At the outset of the Finnish UBI experiment, [over half the population was in favour](http://yle.fi/uutiset/osasto/news/study_over_half_the_population_supports_basic_income_scheme/8583547) of the scheme.
1.3.5.4.1.2.3.4.3. Con: In the case of Finland, this is potentially due to the fact that their pilot program is only a 'partial basic income', available only to [unemployed](http://99percentinvisible.org/episode/the-finnish-experiment/) participants, who would likely not oppose an influx of free money.
1.3.5.4.1.2.4. Con: Numerous additional welfare programs will ideally not be required under a UBI system. This model is intended to replace the fragmented and often inconsistent programs that states have in place currently.
1.3.5.4.1.2.4.1. Con: Effective use of welfare monies requires that individuals actually spend income on measures that improve their welfare. A UBI cannot guarantee this - which in the long run is more likely to perpetuate the social problem in question, compared to means testing or in-kind assistance.
1.3.5.4.1.2.5. Con: A UBI appeals [across the spectrum](https://www.theatlantic.com/politics/archive/2014/08/why-arent-reformicons-pushing-a-guaranteed-basic-income/375600/) - for example, to liberals who see a duty to help those in poverty without putting unduly restrictive conditions on that help, and to fiscally concerned conservatives who object to the inefficiency and bureaucracy of current welfare systems \(Milton Friedman [supported a form of UBI](http://fivethirtyeight.com/features/universal-basic-income/)\). Therefore, a UBI is unlikely to undermine sympathy for other welfare programs.
1.3.5.4.1.2.5.1. Con: Even though a UBI appeals to both liberals and conservatives, their reasons are mutually exclusive. Because conservatives would see a UBI as a strategy to replace most of the existing welfare state, it would likely undermine their support for other welfare programs, whereas for liberals, a UBI would supplement existing safety-net programs \(e.g. childhood education subsidies, comprehensive health insurance etc\).
1.3.5.4.1.2.5.2. Con: It is likely that the implementation of a UBI would be fraught with division over where the funds would come from. For example, conservatives may not want increased taxes and liberals likely would, at least compared to cuts to public services.
1.3.5.4.1.2.5.2.1. Con: That is also true for any budget or tax reform discussion in any given government, not something specific to a UBI.
1.3.5.4.1.2.5.3. Con: While many may claim to support a UBI in principle, when it comes to implementing such a policy and very real sacrifices have to be made in the budget, it is likely that these opinions will change. Decreases in funding towards institutions like education, or the military could prove very unpopular.
1.3.5.4.1.2.6. Con: Whilst it is true that there will be limited demand for most welfare programs, some programs without the stigma of poverty will likely still have sufficient political capital: e.g. funding for children with disabilities.
1.3.5.4.1.2.6.1. Pro: -> See 1.3.1.3.3.7.2.
1.3.5.4.1.2.7. Con: Given that everyone receives something under a UBI, once normalised, it may actually increase the balance of political capital for welfare. Extreme cases would likely still have public support on top of the standard of living that a UBI guarantees for everyone.
1.3.5.4.1.2.7.1. Con: This argument is premised on the notion that individuals' utility from the UBI outweighs the loss of utility from a higher tax rate and perceived infringement on their quality of life. Middle-class individuals might receive more UBI back from the state than they have to pay in taxes, but the perception that their hard-earned monies are being taken away does resonate with voters squeezed by the changing economy.
1.3.5.4.1.2.7.1.1. Con: If a policy would financially benefit people, the government should work to fix people's perception of the policy instead of giving up on it.
1.3.5.4.1.2.7.2. Con: UBI neglects political reality because wealthy citizens receive payments regardless of whether the system is in the self-interest of any beneficiary; whether it be bureaucrats, special interest groups, or voters. All political incentive is driven by the pressure of beneficiaries. Since there is no beneficiary, there cannot be any political incentive and thus a UBI will not be ratified.
1.3.5.4.1.2.7.2.1. Con: There are people who will vote for a political entity who proposes to implement a UBI, whether because of personal self-interest, moral and ethical reasons, or other reasons entirely. The idea that people act purely out of self-interest is patently incorrect in practice.
1.3.5.4.1.2.7.2.1.1. Con: Politicking is inherently immoral. Politicking does not include the dissemination of policies, so movements such as the abolishionist movement are exempt from this issue.
