UBI, however, does not fall into that category. Political voting is at least amoral. Therefore, there being a sufficient motivation among citizens for UBI on moral grounds is unlikely.
1.3.5.4.1.2.7.2.1.1.1. Con: The moralities of the policies proposed by political parties and candidates do have an effect on which ones get voted for by citizens, so they are relevant.
1.3.5.4.1.2.7.3. Pro: -> See 1.3.1.3.3.7.2.
1.3.5.4.1.3. Con: Political will can be renewed and expanded rather than permanently depleted.
1.3.5.4.1.3.1. Pro: It is the role of the Citizens to demand from their government those services that ensure equity. A person with a severe disability requires extra services to ensure individual equity. Political will stems from the voice of the citizens who choose their representatives. Political will for a social welfare safety net will not be found in those who would seek to place the fate of citizens in the hands of the corporate class.
1.3.5.4.1.3.2. Pro: One a UBI has been passed the political will needed to do so will quickly renew \(as the policy becomes normalised\) and can then be used to support other policies.
1.3.5.4.1.3.3. Pro: If there continue to be deficits in the public economy that need addressing, such as daycare, or basic nutrition, or housing, then there will still be demand for targeted programs to support those societal needs.
1.3.5.4.1.3.4. Pro: Political events such as economic crises or right-leaning governments are inevitable and mean that the levels of welfare provided often follow a feast/famine cycle. Given this, a UBI provides a circuit-breaker solution that shores up some minimum level of support even as political will for welfare flucates over time.
1.3.5.4.1.3.4.1. Pro: When existing welfare programs are cut or restructured, having a UBI in place will help tide over those affected until the new programs are implemented.
1.3.5.4.1.3.4.2. Con: A UBI can also be dismantled or reduced. This is especially problematic because a UBI would be, by far, the biggest welfare program.
1.3.5.4.1.3.4.3. Con: Once established, a UBI changes the structure of the economy and societal psychology, and therefore is a commitment. If then, at any point, some serious crisis pushes the government to cut the UBI, it may be a deadly twist for the economy and people.
1.3.5.4.1.3.4.3.1. Con: This is true, but at the same time the changes to the economy and societal psychology could equally push people to care more about each other's welfare, be more interconnected, and respond better and with a more united front to any given crisis.
1.3.5.4.1.3.4.3.2. Con: This argument applies to many other systems, like schools, firemen, police, social security, etc. That did not stop previous generations from creating those systems. There are cases around the world where these systems have indeed collapsed, with disastrous consequences; but the people recognize they are necessary and try really hard to re-establish them. A UBI would probably enter in that category if it is created.
1.3.5.4.1.3.4.4. Con: Unlike most welfare programs, UBI is easily reduced, altered, or removed entirely. All it would take to slash benefits for millions is altering a few numbers in a law code.
1.3.5.4.1.4. Con: The amount of welfare might decrease, but given that there is often a welfare gap that systematically excludes certain individuals even in the best versions of welfare, a UBI is a more equitable distribution of resources which maximizes overall welfare.
1.3.5.4.1.4.1. Pro: Even if it has more monetary weight, a UBI cuts bureaucracy costs and removes the disincentives to work that afflict many welfare systems.
1.3.5.4.1.4.1.1. Con: -> See 1.3.1.2.
1.3.5.4.2. Pro: A UBI would not address educational inequality, which is one of the [main drivers](https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2018/may/03/choice-inequality-education-system-social-segregation) of inequality overall.
1.3.5.4.2.1. Pro: Those with less overall income would be still be less able to afford expensive high quality schools.
1.3.5.4.3. Pro: A UBI would not challenge the current oligopolistic structure of our economy, which would continue to create an unequal society.
1.3.5.4.3.1. Con: If automation is not a threat, but rather the way things are, then we should treat the symptoms of this economic system. A UBI is a way to do this.
1.3.5.4.3.2. Pro: A UBI would exacerbate the structural forces that drive inequality, by making a larger portion of the population unemployed and dependent on the government.
1.3.5.4.4. Pro: Despite the Seneca Nation paying [$8000 annually](https://ifstudies.org/blog/what-can-we-learn-from-native-americans-about-a-universal-basic-income) to each tribe member, unemployment, poverty and theft continue to rise.
1.3.5.4.4.1. Con: There are other factors involved in the underprivileged situations endured by many indigenous people, such as poor education and cultural integration.
1.3.5.5. Con: A UBI does not share wealth because it gives a portion of funds to those who are already wealthy. This does nothing to resolve financial inequality.
1.3.5.5.1. Con: While a UBI does not correct income inequality, it can create equality of opportunity.
1.3.5.5.1.1. Con: A UBI is not enough to create equal opportunities for both the poor and wealthy.
1.3.5.5.1.1.1. Con: There is a difference between equality of opportunity and equality of economic income. Equality of economic income would never be reached and would potentially lead to an economic paralysis, while equality of opportunity would be the incentive to make everybody participate in the work market.
1.3.5.5.1.1.2. Pro: The wealthy have educational advantages since they can pay for prestigious university tuition and tutors.
1.3.5.5.1.1.3. Pro: Members of wealthy families often have the opportunity to gather capital very easily in order to found a business or make investments.
1.3.5.5.1.1.4. Pro: Wealthy individuals often have networks with peers and good connections that make it easier for them to find jobs.
1.3.5.5.1.1.5. Pro: If only wealthy countries offer UBIs, then only wealthy countries will benefit, leaving poorer countries at an even greater disadvantage.
1.3.5.5.1.2. Pro: A UBI would provide people with a sufficient amount of money to allow everybody to have access to food, health, education and housing.
1.3.5.5.1.2.1. Con: Food, health & education - yes, maybe. But housing is a mostly fixed supply - regardless of how much money you give people, everyone cannot have access to housing unless there is more supply than demand. This isn't usually the case due to the inherent difficulty of creating new housing units.
1.3.5.5.1.2.1.1. Con: There are currently houses and units left unoccupied for economic reasons - and/or because the landowner prefers the loss of no tenant rather than the risk to their investment - in at least some countries.
1.3.5.5.2. Con: The rebate offered to the wealthy by a UBI is not of consequence given that those people pay more tax than they receive in government services. It is just effectively a slight change in their tax rate.
1.3.5.5.3. Con: The implementation of a UBI will most likely require tax rates to become more progressive overall, and thus levels of financial inequality will decrease.
1.3.5.5.4. Con: Because a UBI is a flat rate, the portion it gives to the poor could double their current income. To the wealthy it would only be a marginal increase. In relative terms this is a huge decrease in inequality.
1.3.5.5.4.1. Pro: This is referred to as the [diminishing marginal utility of income](https://www.economicshelp.org/blog/12309/concepts/diminishing-marginal-utility-of-income-and-wealth/).
1.3.5.5.4.2. Pro: A person making $1000 and receiving a UBI of $500 would see their income increase by 50%;  a person making $10,000 and receiving a UBI of $500 would only see an income increase of 5%.
1.3.5.5.5. Pro: Giving everyone welfare, including the uber wealthy, is not the solution to problems with the way mean-tested schemes of welfare are calculated. The solution needs to be to change the way welfare is calculated.
1.3.5.5.5.1. Con: Removing the concept of "welfare" will go a long way to solving perception issues such as this. The whole idea of a universal income is that it is earned by one's place in society and is not a 'handout'.
1.3.5.5.6. Pro: A UBI would further increase the economic gap because the needy would use it for necessities and the rich would bank/invest theirs.
1.3.5.5.6.1. Con: A UBI would not be a significant increase to the finances of highly wealthy individuals, but would be a significant improvement to less well-off people - both those who would use it to eat and those who would use it to invest in owning their own home.
1.3.5.5.7. Pro: This means that minorities are still in the same socio-economic position they were before relative to the privileged in society.
1.3.5.5.7.1. Con: -> See 1.3.5.5.4.
1.3.5.5.7.2. Con: This is an over simplification. When you have not enough money to make it through the month, your position is much more desperate that when you don't have money for leisure. To deny this basic comfort on the base of "relative wealth" is to forget that there is more similarity of experiences between poor people everywhere in the world than within the "steps" of a single social pyramid.
1.3.5.5.7.3. Con: Minorities are much better off when they have economic security.
1.3.5.5.7.3.1. Pro: Even groups who lack the social cohesion, self identification and social recognition of minorities will benefit from this. It will effectively surpass current efforts to increase the quality of life in the current narrative and support structure.
1.3.5.5.7.3.2. Pro: The idea of a UBI is that even the poorest would have enough money to cover basic expenses.
1.3.5.5.7.3.2.1. Pro: By choosing to work, they can earn additional income, which would be disposable income. This would never be possible without a UBI since all of their money would go towards making ends meet.
1.3.5.5.7.4. Pro: Minorities continue to experience a wage gap in the workplace. A UBI does not solve this discrimination.
1.3.5.5.7.4.1. Con: [Some feminists](http://basicincome.org/bien/pdf/munich2012/katada.pdf) see a UBI as an “emancipation fee”, and criticize the welfare state because they argue that it is largely based on a traditional model of male breadwinner and female housekeeper. They conclude that a UBI can be useful to overcome this form of discrimination.
1.3.5.5.7.4.1.1. Pro: A UBI is a way of reevaluating the economic value of the work women have primarily handled in the past, while also promoting the economic independence of women. UBIs are an “Emancipation Fee” paid to promote women’s liberation \([Katada, p. 3](http://basicincome.org/bien/pdf/munich2012/katada.pdf)\)
1.3.5.5.7.4.1.1.1. Con: UBI payments go to men as well as women, and regardless of contributions to housework, childcare, eldercare or other traditionally unpaid female labour.
1.3.5.5.7.4.1.2. Con: A UBI returns women to the home, and contributes to a backlash that runs counter to women’s liberation and independence.
1.3.5.5.7.4.2. Con: A UBI was never designed to — or claimed to — eliminate workplace discrimination.
1.3.5.5.7.4.3. Pro: [Gay](https://ecf.cand.uscourts.gov/cand/09cv2292/evidence/PX0605.pdf) government employees in the US earn 8% to 29% less than their heterosexual counterparts.
1.3.5.5.7.4.4. Pro: [Transgender workers](http://www.transequality.org/sites/default/files/docs/resources/NTDS_Exec_Summary.pdf) experience rates of unemployment at double the rate of the general population due to firing, wage discrimination, or harassment. This has a negative impact on their income.
1.3.5.5.7.4.5. Pro: The [racial wage gap](http://time.com/4390212/race-wage-gap-pew-analysis/) in the US shows that Black men earn only 73% of white men's hourly earning and Hispanic men earn just 69%. These numbers have not significantly changed in 35 years. Its unlikely that UBI will change this.
1.3.5.5.7.4.5.1. Con: Improved social mobility due to reduced paycheck-to-paycheck budgeting allows them to find better and less discriminatory opportunities, and will help close this gap.
1.3.5.5.7.4.6. Con: If someone is not dependent on a certain job, they can choose more freely to walk away from workplaces where they are not treated fairly with less risk of endangering their means for subsistence.
1.3.5.5.7.4.6.1. Pro: The fact that labor would no longer be forced into sub-poverty conditions by an employer or else face starvation and social chastisement should they "fail to keep a job" will change the job market drastically and empower everyone.
1.3.5.5.7.4.6.2. Con: This wage gap disparity is widespread. The likelihood of a minority finding a job with fair wages in comparison to those not considered a minority is slim.
1.3.5.5.7.4.6.2.1. Pro: Despite some forward progress, [white men](https://www.pewresearch.org/fact-tank/2016/07/01/racial-gender-wage-gaps-persist-in-u-s-despite-some-progress/) still tend to out-earn minority groups such as Black and Hispanic men, and all women.
1.3.5.5.7.4.6.2.2. Pro: Minorities often have [more](https://www.forbes.com/sites/hbsworkingknowledge/2017/05/17/minorities-who-whiten-resumes-get-more-job-interviews/#7d06c1ad7b74) obstacles to hiring, such as excluding any references to their race on their resume.
1.3.5.5.7.4.6.2.3. Con: Minorities will be empowered to become entrepreneurs and their representation in companies will increase as a consequence of UBI. This will assist in the correction of the indicated problem.
1.3.5.5.7.5. Pro: Minorities will still be locked out of certain employment opportunities for prejudicial reasons.
1.3.5.5.7.5.1. Pro: There are [many accounts](https://www.revealnews.org/article/when-companies-hire-temp-workers-by-race-black-applicants-lose-out/) of temp agencies hiring out with direct orders not to hire Black Americans, despite equal opportunity employment policies.
1.3.5.5.7.5.2. Pro: [Hiring practices](http://thelasource.com/en/2011/10/31/university-study-finds-prejudice-in-hiring-practices/) in Montreal, Vancouver, and Toronto favored applicants with English sounding names over Asian sounding names.
1.3.5.5.7.5.3. Con: Whether or not this is true, this is no reason not to offer a UBI. If this is true it means we should be looking for ways to reduce this prejudice. It does not mean we should give up on other ways to help a given minority, i.e. it is no reason not to offer a UBI.
1.3.5.5.8. Con: -> See 1.2.7.8.5.
1.3.5.5.9. Con: Only by making UBI universal will the wealthier support the provision of such an income rise for the poorer. As the wealthier seem happy to pocket universal benefits too \(even when claiming to disagree with them\).
1.3.5.5.10. Pro: Wealth can be equally shared by effective taxation and welfare programs.
1.3.5.5.11. Pro: According to the [relative income hypothesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Relative_income_hypothesis), consumers make purchases and save money depending on their percentile position within the income distribution. A UBI may increase spending, but does not improve the income gap.
1.3.5.5.11.1. Con: This does not account for the fact that a UBI is a redistribution program. The money that goes to those who are poor comes from those who have more, so there is a difference in relative income.
1.3.5.5.11.2. Con: These two points are unrelated. Whether or not UBI will increase spending \(unclear\) has no bearing on the income gap, which it does address.
1.3.5.6. Pro: Most advocates of a UBI emphasize equality of opportunity, saying that “each person should have equal opportunity to pursue a conception of the good life” \([Allahyar, p. 8](https://www.academia.edu/8447710/Universal_Basic_Income)\).
1.3.5.6.1. Pro: It is difficult for the poor to improve their position without a basic safety net that provides reliable access to education and income. A UBI will provide this.
1.3.5.6.2. Pro: Experiments with UBIs [showed](http://qz.com/765902/ubi-wouldnt-mean-everyone-quits-working/) that teenagers put off getting part-time jobs to focus on school, leading to noticeable improvements in high school performance.
1.3.5.6.2.1. Pro: Dropout rates [fell](https://qz.com/765902/ubi-wouldnt-mean-everyone-quits-working/) in Dauphin, Canada after their UBI experiment.
1.3.5.6.2.2. Pro: [UBI experiments](https://qz.com/765902/ubi-wouldnt-mean-everyone-quits-working/) led to double-digit increases in high school completion among participating families in New Jersey, Seattle and Denver.
1.3.5.6.2.3. Con: -> See 1.1.5.3.1.2.2.1.
1.3.5.6.2.4. Con: [Youth allowance](https://www.humanservices.gov.au/customer/services/centrelink/youth-allowance) fixes this same problem in a more targeted and efficient way.
1.3.5.6.3. Pro: Equality of opportunity is recognized as a [human right](https://www.un.org/en/universal-declaration-human-rights/).
1.3.5.6.3.1. Con: No benefit can be guaranteed unconditionally to some without enforcing an unconditional obligation on others. Before distributing money as a UBI, all the money must first be collected, plus the overhead required to complete the redistribution process. When the cost of enforcing the transfer is included, UBI is negative sum and constitutes Legal Plunder, as defined by French economist Frederic Bastiat in his 1850 work, [The Law](https://mises.org/library/law).
1.3.5.6.3.1.1. Con: This assumes that all the production comes from human activities. This is already changing with automation and artificial intelligence, which would push most of the income toward activities which are not performed by humans and therefore removing one side of the equation defined by Bastiat.
1.3.5.6.3.1.2. Con: The idea that the UBI is always a negative sum does not take into account the fact that people with less money spend most if it, therefore it goes back into the economy. In contrast, much of the money from the top 1% is not put back into the economy since it is saved offshore, used to speculate in stock markets \(not natural market forces\) and to unfairly influence governments.
1.3.5.7. Pro: Implementing a UBI would reduce income inequality.
1.3.5.7.1. Pro: A hypothetical $12k UBI if implemented in the US would be [enough to reduce its Gini index](http://www.scottsantens.com/does-basic-income-reduce-income-inequality-gini) \(a measure of income inequality\) from 0.6 to 0.46; bringing it on par with that of the Nordic states.
1.3.5.7.1.1. Pro: A $12k per year UBI would cover a large portion of my survival expenses freeing up the time I presently use working to meet my basic needs, to do other more effective and self-actualizing things.
1.3.5.7.2. Con: Income inequality is not inherently bad because it provides good economic incentives. The promise that you can make it to the top if you work hard makes it more likely that people advance their economic position.
1.3.5.7.2.1. Con: These are the twin myths of meritocracy and trickle down economics, neither of which have ever been proven in a real world setting.
1.3.5.7.2.2. Con: If that were true, we would have miners and road workers as presidents.
1.3.5.7.2.3. Con: This asks people who know that they work hard long hours to accept that the only reason they are still in poverty is that they aren't working hard enough. Anyone who does not see the problem here is encouraged to go out, and try doing this directly.
1.3.5.7.2.4. Con: This argument asks us to ignore the sloth, and underhandedness characteristic of many, if not all, members of the upper class.
1.3.5.7.2.5. Con: The average person should ask if the reason they are not rich is due to a lack of trying, and evaluate the above claim based on their conclusion.
1.3.5.7.2.5.1. Pro: Investment is easier when one has greater access to starting capital and/or loans.
1.3.5.7.2.5.2. Pro: People who are already among the wealthy \(or who belong to families which are\) have access to information and strategies which are not commonly taught or provided to the lower classes. Even if they can eventually gain access to this information \(through courses, etc.\), it is not a routine part of their formative years and education.
1.3.5.7.2.5.3. Pro: The old adage of "it's not what you know, it's who you know" often proves true; growing up and/or living in wealthy circles makes one more likely to receive opportunities for further wealth.
1.3.5.7.2.6. Con: If this promise is a false promise, as there is reason to believe, it effectively amounts to deception at best and slavery by deception at worst.
1.3.5.7.2.7. Con: Income inequality is an inherently bad thing, as it leads to stress, relationship breakdown and crime amongst other things.
1.3.5.7.2.8. Con: The [great gatsby curve](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_Gatsby_curve) shows that the greater inequality is, the lower social mobility is - so that even though there might be greater incentives, its actually much harder to get to them.
1.3.5.7.2.9. Con: People like all animals do not need to "better themselves". We need to have needs met such as food, water, community, etc.
1.3.5.7.3. Pro: Capitalism has a tendency to lead to the accumulation of capital into the hands of a few \([Piketty, 2013](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Capital_in_the_Twenty-First_Century)\) leading to an inevitable stratification of income levels and the collapse of the middle class. A UBI is a way to redistribute wealth, stimulating consumer demand and businesses.
1.3.5.7.3.1. Con: UBIs tamp down support for more fundamental reforms like land redistribution, or like reformulating the economic system to reward something other than immediate self-interest. Previous welfarist proposals have been lauded by reactionaries as "[saving capitalism](https://www.hoover.org/research/how-fdr-saved-capitalism)". UBIs work the exact same way.
1.3.5.7.3.2. Con: Capital accumulation is not reduced by a UBI. By increasing [reservation wages](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reservation_wage) and reducing the desirability of hiring workers, capitalists gain a justification to leave workers out of the market.
1.3.5.7.3.2.1. Con: A major redistribution between the population translates into a reduction of capital.
1.3.5.7.3.2.2. Con: By increasing the lowest wage which workers would accept, employers will expect workers to be highly competent and rigorous, stimulating work efficiency.
1.3.5.7.3.2.3. Con: Workers still need to be hired to fuel capitalism, so they cannot be left out of the market.
1.3.5.7.3.3. Con: Inequality can be reduced in less disruptive ways, such as by encouraging [inclusive growth](http://siteresources.worldbank.org/INTDEBTDEPT/Resources/468980-1218567884549/WhatIsInclusiveGrowth20081230.pdf) and economic participation.
1.3.5.7.3.3.1. Con: Income inequality being reduced in less disruptive ways does not solve for the root cause of inequality, but it rather runs the risk to lead to worse conditions where individuals can still use income inequality to oppress individuals that have a lower standard of living.
1.3.5.7.3.4. Con: The effect of stimulating consumer demand will be mitigated by the extent to which prices are raised by businesses.
1.3.5.7.3.5. Pro: Consumer demand isn't stimulated when individuals do not have the autonomy to spend how they wish. With a UBI, individuals can finally choose what to invest in.
1.3.5.7.3.6. Pro: When robots take over the jobs, people will still need money to buy the products robots are making. A basic income is a great idea because people have more free time to start a business or become an artist or what ever and start earning more money with the things they like to do in life, this way more money will be created for the government by paying taxes on the extra income they create!
1.3.5.7.3.7. Pro: The profit employers make by effectivization tends to go to people with an already high standard of living rather than employees which are in more in need of the profit - which could be implanted through fewer working hours with sustained monthly wage. The free market's inability to deal with this calls for wealth-distribution measures, such as a UBI.
1.3.5.7.3.8. Pro: UBI allows those who previously had no capital to participate in capitalist endeavours of enterprise. This is preferable than being excluded from it and forced to rely on others because of their lack of opportunity.
1.3.5.7.4. Pro: Studies show that Alaska \(the only state with UBI at present\) is also the state with the lowest financial inequality. In [2015 Alaska had the 2nd lowest income inequality, but today it has the least.](https://motherboard.vice.com/en_us/article/jp5wdb/only-state-free-money-alaska)
1.3.5.7.4.1. Con: Alaska may offer a poor case test. It’s geographical and demographic markers, may make the state too anamolous — too much of an outlier — to properly transfer to the US mainland.
1.3.5.8. Pro: Introducing a UBI would help reduce wealth inequality.
1.3.5.8.1. Pro: -> See 1.3.5.7.3.
1.4. Con: A UBI might erode the personal and societal incentives for financial responsibility, self-improvement, and hard work; at least for some people.
1.4.1. Con: Due to [modern technologies](https://www.entrepreneur.com/article/332468) and high productivity, not every member of society must participate in the workforce in order to maintain decent living standards.
1.4.1.1. Pro: With current levels of technology, it does not take one person's work to feed another.
1.4.1.1.1. Pro: Consider a combine harvester reaping a field. It takes only one person to drive it, and a specialist to maintain it \(who also maintains other machines\). However, this field may produce enough grain to feed hundreds or even thousands. And while other work such as irrigation is also required to reach this output, that work is equally enhanced by technology, such as massive irrigation arrays.
1.4.2. Con: Once implemented, a UBI can be seen as a kind of inheritance we receive from society. Receiving an inheritance can not be seen necessarily as a disincentive for financial responsibility, self-improvement and hard work.
1.4.2.1. Pro: If this inheritance pays only for an individual's basic needs, they still might pursue financial responsibility, and should keep hard-working for reaching their desires, dreams, and aspirations.
1.4.3. Pro: When a basic standard of living is available to all, some people will not [contribute](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Benefit_fraud_in_the_United_Kingdom) to its upkeep. This creates [resentment](https://books.google.com.au/books?id=QORW1i6XDKgC&pg=PA192&lpg=PA192&dq=welfare+resentment+surveys&source=bl&ots=CfzLza5DRM&sig=FgGV1gEjyqMN_IWkZonVnSGO82s&hl=en&sa=X&ved=0ahUKEwjw9ZanoefWAhWJTrwKHfwVAyoQ6AEIPDAF#v=onepage&q&f=false) within those who do contribute; an aspect of human nature that a UBI plays out on a much larger scale than alternative redistribution schemes.
1.4.3.1. Con: While this is a valid concern, one of the considerations when structuring a UBI would be to make the stipend [considerable enough](https://www.npr.org/2019/10/17/770962701/a-game-changer-andrew-yang-explains-how-he-d-give-every-american-1-000-per-month?live=1) to make an economic difference but not enough to be work replacement. It's not meant to be a livable wage.
1.4.3.1.1. Con: Not every job contributes to the funding of the UBI, e.g., low-wage or part-time jobs are hardly taxable.
1.4.3.2. Con: Current implementations are certainly lacking for our observation, but the implementation in Alaska seems to suggest the opposite. It is wildly popular even though Alaska is incredibly conservative. [The APFC has been in place since 1980.](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alaska_Permanent_Fund) [A recent paper](http://humcap.uchicago.edu/RePEc/hka/wpaper/Jones_Marinescu_2019_labor-market-impacts-universal-transfers.pdf) found that the dividend has had no effect on employment rates and actually increased part-time work by 17%.
1.4.3.3. Con: If everyone receives a UBI, people overall will feel less resentment for those who receive UBI without contributing to society.
1.4.3.4. Con: Following this logic we should get rid of disabled people instead of trying to fit them in to society.
1.4.3.5. Con: People who have a good life do not have as much incentive to resent others, as they have less stress and thus less reason to seek a place for blame.
1.4.3.5.1. Con: Resentment, stemming from jealousy, is a common human emotion which isn't eliminated by higher socioeconomic status. Crucially, the prominence of higher opportunity at a lower rate of investment fundamentally disregards the meritocracy of the traditional 'good life', which generates an exceptionally higher incentive for jealousy.
1.4.3.6. Con: The success of a policy should not be measured by what people think about those who benefit from it, but rather the benefit it provides to society. Given a UBI is an economic policy it's success should be measured by its economic benefit.
1.4.3.6.1. Con: It is also a humanitarian and welfare policy. This makes it a moral issue in a way most economic policies are not. It is a responsible government's duty to look out for the best interests of all its populace, whether they are wealthy and economically powerful or not. A UBI could be argued to be a way of doing so.
1.4.3.6.2. Con: A policy that creates widespread resentment could result in a net negative for society.
1.4.4. Pro: A UBI grows people's sense of entitlement and makes it harder for them to see the value of hard work.
1.4.4.1. Pro: Hard work can be intrinsically valuable because when one works hard, one builds self-esteem.
1.4.4.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.2.7.6.3.14.4.
1.4.4.2. Con: Not everybody considers hard work as a virtuous value. For some, it is a sad necessity to be minimized.
1.4.4.2.1. Pro: The puritan work ethic of modern capitalism propagates a harmful ideology that equates one's self-worth with their productivity and economic achievements.
1.4.4.2.1.1. Pro: John Stuart Mill was an early advocate against the 'gospel of work'; that work should be considered just only if instrumental to some greater object; [not as an end unto itself.](http://abolishwork.com/2014/01/15/some-anti-work-comments-from-john-stuart-mill/)
1.4.4.2.1.1.1. Pro: If we had UBI we would be a lot more productive and creative as we would not have to worry about where our next meal was coming from nor would we be tied up in menial, soul-destroying labour simply to make others richer.
1.4.4.2.1.2. Con: One's worth to society is shown through how much they contribute to society. This is fair because the more one contributes to society, the more they get out of society.
1.4.4.2.1.2.1. Con: What is here said, and what is perceived are very rarely the same thing, even if it is not the intent to communicate these harmful ideas, the harm still occurs.
1.4.4.2.1.2.2. Con: There are innumerable contributions made to society that are not recognized monetarily.
1.4.4.2.1.2.2.1. Pro: Raising the next generation of children is often considered one of the [hardest jobs](https://www.motherhoodandmore.com/9-reasons-why-parenting-is-worlds/)(Being a parent comes with more responsibility and stress than any occupation, but there’s no paycheck, no seasonal bonuses, no monetary compensation of any kind. And generally speaking, the more time you spend parenting, the less money you make. There’s also no paid leave from parenting. In fact, much of the time, you have to pay someone else to watch your kids so you can have “time off.”), yet it is not paid.
1.4.4.2.1.2.2.1.1. Con: In a super crowded world, having children is not a valuable contribution, it just creates another person to consume our limited resources. It shouldn't be endorsed.
1.4.4.2.1.2.2.2. Pro: Many individuals act as [unpaid carers](https://forwardcarers.org.uk/info-advice/are-you-a-carer/). In doing so they provide support and assistance to friends and family with physical or mental health conditions that would otherwise need to be paid for by the [taxpayer](https://www.carersuk.org/news-and-campaigns/press-releases/facts-and-figures)(Carers save the economy £132 billion per year, an average of £19,336 per carer.).
1.4.4.2.1.2.3. Con: By that same logic we ought not care for the elderly and those unable to work, or tie down children with a social debt they have to pay off upon reaching adult hood.
1.4.4.2.2. Pro: The richest value [working smarter](https://www.huffingtonpost.com/anthony-w-orlando/the-rich-arent-rich-becau_b_4791626.html), not harder. They leverage their time and skills in order to provide the highest output with less time, resulting in more time for enjoyable activities, or work if they choose.
1.4.4.3. Con: One's being rich achieves the same effect. Since we accept and encourage wealth in society, this would not be a reason to protest the implementation of a UBI.
1.4.4.3.1. Con: Wealth is accrued as a function of financial responsibility, self-improvement, and hard work. Therefore, it can be assumed that those attributes have already been learned by the wealthy.
1.4.4.3.1.1. Con: Many people are more wealthy than others due to their position of birth, not their efforts or abilities.
1.4.4.3.1.1.1. Pro: As of 2010, between [55-65%](https://www.washingtonpost.com/us-policy/2019/02/06/people-like-estate-tax-whole-lot-more-when-they-learn-how-wealth-is-distributed/) of wealth in the US was inherited.
1.4.4.3.2. Con: We do not accept and encourage wealth in society per se. Accruing wealth through theft, for example, is not encouraged. Rather, we encourage value provision, and use money as a tool to incentivize the provision of value.
1.4.4.3.2.1. Pro: If \(net\) value is provided to individuals then, definitionally, value is added to the society that individual inhabits.
1.4.4.3.3. Pro: Having wealth is not the same as earning wealth. One example where the two diverge is inherited wealth.
1.4.4.3.3.1. Pro: It appears many people and countries feel fine about inheritances, which only incentivize "self-improvement and hard work" in the first dying party that built the wealth, not in subsequent recipients.
1.4.4.3.3.1.1. Pro: According to a [UK poll](https://www.gransnet.com/online-surveys-product-tests/inheritance-tax-survey), 42% of respondents believe that inheritance tax should be abolished immediately and 73% consider it a form of double taxation and therefore unfair.
1.4.4.4. Con: People are entitled to these things, because we should [own a share of the resources of the earth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Left-libertarianism#Steiner%E2%80%93Vallentyne_school) that is ours in common.
1.4.4.4.1. Pro: The wealth of a nation is predicated on common knowledge acquired over generations and is the proper inheritance of everyone.
1.4.4.5. Con: The UBI is an additional payment within a capitalist economic system. It does not change either the prevalent economic system or the incentive scheme.
1.4.4.5.1. Pro: Existing studies suggest that one's incentive to work is not meaningfully reduced for recipients of a UBI \([p. 3](https://www.nber.org/papers/w24312.pdf)\).
1.4.4.6. Pro: UBI enforces the idea that people owe others just because they are the citizens of the same country.
1.4.4.6.1. Con: A nation cannot perform all of its necessary functions unless it requires its citizens to contribute money and/or labor to benefit some or all of its other citizens.
1.4.5. Pro: While a government introducing UBI seems to help the poor in the short term, in the long term it can harm them by removing the poor's incentive for them to work and develop their self-sufficient skills needed to provide for themselves in the long-term.
1.4.5.1. Con: The extra income a UBI provides is likely to encourage and potentially make it easier for individuals to improve their lives.
1.4.5.1.1. Pro: Hiring a financial planner can cost between [$1,000-$2,000](https://www.moneyunder30.com/when-is-it-time-to-hire-a-financial-advisor) to create a comprehensive financial plan. A UBI can help fund this investment, which can have a positive affect on one's overall finances.
1.4.5.1.2. Pro: People are often forced to make [short-term decisions](https://medium.com/\@megha0111/how-scarcity-affects-the-decision-making-c891df0fd560) when their resources are scant. The extra income may allow them to plan for their future better.
1.4.5.1.3. Pro: People will have more time for self-improvement and education if they are not forced to seek money from employers to survive.
1.4.5.1.3.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.5.3.1.1.
1.4.5.1.3.2. Con: Although the extra time "could" be used for productive activities such as self-improvement and education, not all people are willing to spend their free time for those.
1.4.5.1.3.3. Pro: The virtues of financial responsibility, self-improvement, and hard work apply to the goal of getting 100% employment in capitalism that is human-dependent. However, if the goal changes to 0% employment and complete automation, these virtues fail and new virtues would need to take their place to allow humans to still contribute or at least have a purpose.
1.4.5.1.3.3.1. Con: While "financial responsibility" and "hard work" may not be as useful in the future as they are now, it would be foolish to abandon them before we actually have the automated infrastructure to safely let them go as virtues.
1.4.5.1.3.3.1.1. Con: Yes, it would be foolish to let go of such virtues, but this could be tacked by, educating the masses on how to cope with the major shift and how to focus more on self development, now that they are free to do so.
1.4.5.1.3.3.2. Pro: UBI creates the space for "hard word" and "financial stability" to be replaced with "creative output" and "network solutions". Rather than amassing more things, we can strive towards a society that is highly resilient and self-sustainable. The churn of automation would be less of a threat, because more people would be safe to pursue self-employment.
1.4.5.1.3.3.3. Con: UBI is simply a mechanism to maintain the rich/poor divide and not an attempt to bridge it. UBI will only prolong a dependence on a financial social ranking. Society has surpassed the need to rank individuals by wealth however we/the world still rely on this outdated system.
1.4.5.1.3.3.3.1. Con: Not having money is maintaining the rich/poor divide. Ubi wouldn't change it completely, but it would at least give the people a chance to move up.
1.4.5.1.3.3.4. Pro: Hard work is not an intrinsic utilitarian or moral goal. For example, we embrace labor multiplying technologies such as tractors, dishwashing machines, laundromats, automobiles, and other tools and social systems that facilitate radical reductions in labor without a corresponding perception of moral failure or laziness.
1.4.5.1.3.4. Pro: Many people are interested in being productive or good at something for the [enjoyment of it](https://www.elitedaily.com/wellness/blood-sweat-tears-happiness/1259555).
1.4.5.1.3.4.1. Con: The things that people wish to be productive and good at are often different from those that pay well and contribute to tax revenue, which is why many people prefer their personal projects to those of work.
1.4.5.2. Con: A UBI would benefit those who otherwise struggle to finance themselves to work towards a greater goal.
1.4.5.2.1. Pro: A UBI would be beneficial for those who fight for the environment's protection.
1.4.5.2.1.1. Pro: A UBI would help NGOs that dedicate themselves to environmental protection, as they can save activists' salaries and thus have more available capital.
1.4.5.2.1.1.1. Pro: More people will be able to donate their time instead of just money.
1.4.5.2.1.2. Pro: Citizens who are concerned about the environment will be able to donate larger amounts with the help of a UBI.
1.4.5.2.1.2.1. Con: Considering much of the redistributed income will go towards the poor, as the wealthy citizens' gain is marginal, it seems unlikely they will donate higher amounts to the environment. It is more likely they will spend the money on education, food, health care, and housing.
1.4.5.2.1.2.1.1. Con: Lower Incomes donate a higher percentage of income  than any other income bracket. The IRS’s 2011 Statistics of Income file on individuals who itemize on their tax return shows a relationship between total adjusted gross income and charitable giving as a percentage of AGI. Those with income between $100,000 and $200,000 contribute, on average, 2.6 percent of their income, which is lower compared to those with income either below $100,000 \(3.6 percent\).
1.4.5.2.1.3. Con: Many of the wealthy who donated or invested much of their income to the protection of the environment will no longer be able to do so because of the increase in taxes.
1.4.5.2.1.3.1. Con: If charity [donations](https://www.gov.uk/donating-to-charity/overview) are fiscally [deductible](https://www.irs.gov/charities-non-profits/charitable-organizations/charitable-contribution-deductions), a lot of wealthy individuals will still donate.
1.4.5.2.1.4. Con: Since a UBI enables a person to pursue basically any cause they want, it doesn't provide any special advantage to environmental activism.
1.4.5.2.1.5. Pro: A US study suggests that race, gender, and class can have an impact on environmental activism \([p. 40-41](https://www.fs.fed.us/pnw/pubs/gtr534.pdf)\).
1.4.5.2.1.6. Con: Environmentalists may be more concerned with how [increased consumerism](https://www.fastcompany.com/40463533/a-universal-basic-income-would-do-wonders-for-the-u-s-economy) brought about by a UBI will negatively impact the environment.
1.4.5.2.1.6.1. Pro: Increases in consumerism can have [a negative influence](https://grist.org/living/consumerism-plays-a-huge-role-in-climate-change/) on climate change. Implementing a UBI may increase this influence.
1.4.5.2.1.7. Pro: Social workers and NGO workers, who are helping a lot of people, are paid very poorly for their meaningful work. This deters many people from entering the non-profit sector.
1.4.5.2.1.7.1. Pro: Governments are [investing less and less](https://books.google.it/books?id=JcPiBwAAQBAJ&pg=PA298&lpg=PA298&dq=crisis+government+cut+social+aid&source=bl&ots=PUj8DMEcpk&sig=OEDtkapMpf1Efhh8BW8cXng47S0&hl=it&sa=X&ved=0ahUKEwiJoLnh563RAhVEVhoKHUnwApoQ6AEIWzAI#v=onepage&q=crisis%20government%20cut%20social%20aid&f=false) money for social aids, leaving to NGOs the important role to replace this obligation. A UBI would reward their work with governmental funds.
1.4.5.2.1.7.2. Con: A UBI does not make this work relatively more appealing, as the bonus income will equally apply to all jobs.
1.4.5.2.2. Pro: Activism for social causes is often harmed by a lack of income among their supporters.
1.4.5.2.2.1. Pro: Many fear that if they get arrested at a protest, they will not be able to afford to [pay for a lawyer](https://www.marketplace.org/2020/06/10/what-it-can-cost-to-get-arrested-at-a-protest/). With basic income, more will be able to afford these costs.
1.4.5.2.2.2. Pro: Many [fear getting fired](https://www.cnbc.com/2020/06/05/can-you-get-fired-for-attending-a-protest.html) from their jobs for participating in a protest. That fear would be less acute if people had a solid base of income to rely on.
1.4.5.3. Con: Forcing people to work under threat of starvation, homelessness, or death is barbaric.
1.4.5.3.1. Pro: Once the state claims the rights over lands, water, and any useful resource, and prevent people to use those to survive, the minimum that the state could do is to provide the basic needs to its population.
1.4.5.3.1.1. Con: The basic needs of people differ on an individual basis. For example some people have need for medical treatment that is not needed or deemed as a basic need by most.
1.4.5.3.1.1.1. Con: This does not mean that the state should not provide things which are basic needs for all. Specific programs can also be put into place to address specific needs.
1.4.5.3.1.2. Con: The state does not claim rights over land, water or any useful resource. Individuals buy these resources and trade it among themselves, if the government truly owned these resources like they do in communist countries, than yes it would be the responsibility of the state to distribute these resources to their citizens
1.4.5.3.1.2.1. Con: In most countries, government legislation permits and manages the claiming, purchase, and sale of such resources.
1.4.5.3.2. Con: Starvation, homelessness, and death all happen in nature if one does not work towards obtaining resources for their own survival. Therefore, not providing resources for free isn't barbaric.
1.4.5.3.2.1. Con: Nature is barbaric - the point of a society is to do better than that.
1.4.5.3.3. Con: If people do not work for the food or land they are consuming, than they are actively consuming the goods produced by someone else without compensating that person. The state is not forcing the individual to work to eat food the state creates, the state is protecting the individuals right to sell the fruit of their labor to their neighbor and receive compensation.
1.4.5.3.4. Con: Forcing someone to work for food and then giving half of what they worked for to someone who does nothing is barbaric. Requiring that people function as adults and do their part to earn their own food is called participating in society.
1.4.5.4. Con: People are motivated to be productive by more than just money. After meeting basic needs, almost everybody seeks a sense of purpose, and of social value. Money alone is a poor way to achieve these.
1.4.5.4.1. Con: There has never been a time in human history where all basic needs have been met without having additional responsibility. Purpose has always arisen out of responsibility. Even before money was invented, purpose and societal value was found in cultivation and trade.
1.4.5.5. Con: Poverty does not provide an incentive to work and be productive; it is a barrier to it.
1.4.5.5.1. Pro: [The analysis](https://www.developmentpathways.co.uk/publications/india-basic-income-experiment/) of a UBI experiment in India indicates that a UBI in the long term increases the productivity and motivation of the recipients.
1.4.5.6. Pro: Humans are too complex to suggest that a UBI, untethered from providing value to society, would be beneficial to all. Some would benefit and find their own way to/continue to contribute and some would quickly devolve into hedonism and/or nihilism. This will include the lazy and unmotivated and also those whose upbringing and/or culture did not instill altruism or accomplishment as important values. It will create a self-perpetuating underclass.
1.4.5.7. Pro: A UBI will promote the false idea that there is such thing as a free lunch because people will constantly receive something for doing nothing. The renowned economist Milton Friedman wrote a book about how there is no such thing as a free lunch-[amazon.com](https://www.amazon.com/Theres-Such-Thing-Free-Lunch/dp/087548297X). A UBI appears to be ‘free’ but it will in reality be very costly, to one group or another.
1.4.5.8. Pro: A UBI promotes stagnation and irresponsibility among the poor. After welfare reform in the 90's limiting who could receive welfare, and making it more of a temporary thing poverty rates and unemployment rates fell. [Poverty & Unemployment Data](https://fee.org/articles/undeniable-welfare-reform-increased-work-and-reduced-poverty/)
1.4.5.9. Con: People in poor and uneducated communities already have a [low understanding](https://motivationgrid.com/rich-mindset-vs-poor-mindset/) of, and limited incentive or ability to implement, these factors into their lives, in which a UBI cannot erode any further than the level where they already are at.
1.4.6. Con: In the hypothetical case of [half the population](http://www.businessinsider.com/over-half-of-jobs-in-the-eu-at-risk-2014-7) or in some cases [two thirds of the population](https://futurism.com/un-report-robots-will-replace-two-thirds-of-all-workers-in-the-developing-world/) being at risk of job loss, a UBI reverses consequences that weren't linked to either good or bad choices. In such a scenario, even many of the productive and responsible would benefit from a safety net.
1.4.7. Con: Some people might be highly internally motivated by psychological and/or sociological factors, rather than purely due to external factors. Therefore, a UBI is less likely to substantially change their behavior.
1.4.7.1. Pro: People have other incentives for self-improvement, such as obtaining a sense of accomplishment or mastery.
1.4.7.1.1. Pro: A UBI would allow us to think about other options than financial incentives like personal growth and benefits for society.
1.4.7.2. Pro: The thesis presupposes that human beings are primarily motivated by their own consumption. However, humans are primarily motivated by an existential calling. After all, one would not be interested in sustaining their life if it did not have [meaning](https://www.psychologytoday.com/intl/blog/the-meaningful-life/201807/search-meaning-the-basic-human-motivation) for them.
1.4.7.2.1. Pro: Most people do not consider the meaning or purpose of their life to be to accrue wealth, and instead accrue wealth in service of other goals.
1.4.7.2.2. Pro: Many people undertake volunteer work.
1.4.7.3. Pro: One's productivity is not [dependent](https://www.theatlantic.com/business/archive/2015/02/why-the-gap-between-worker-pay-and-productivity-is-so-problematic/385931/) on money, which a UBI provides, but rather [happiness](https://inside.6q.io/5-great-reasons-why-happiness-increases-productivity/) and [supply and demand](http://www.economicsdiscussion.net/price/factor-of-production-demand-and-supply/3889). Thus, a UBI wouldn't necessarily lead people being less productive.
1.5. Con: Providing a UBI may create harmful political consequences.
1.5.1. Pro: A UBI increases the risk of a [tyranny](https://quillette.com/2017/10/09/universal-basic-income-threat-tyranny/) of a diminishing minority responsible for wealth creation, while the majority will have no leverage to mitigate this minority.
1.5.1.1. Con: Since [people don't stop working any significant amount under a UBI](https://www.vox.com/future-perfect/2020/2/19/21112570/universal-basic-income-ubi-map), this wouldn't happen.
1.5.2. Pro: -> See 1.3.5.4.1.
1.5.3. Pro: UBIs could be used by political elites to avoid addressing structural injustices.
1.5.3.1. Pro: A UBI replacing the myriad number of welfare programs means that welfare as a political issue will only have a single dimension: at what rate should the UBI be set. This can distract from other policy options that could more strategically target injustice and welfare needs.
1.5.3.1.1. Pro: Passing a UBI may mean that welfare and poverty will be seen as a 'solved' issue, which requires no further help from the State.
1.5.3.1.1.1. Con: This is an argument against literally any attempt to fight poverty. This risk does not outweigh the policy's tangible benefits for the poor.
1.5.3.1.1.2. Pro: -> See 1.3.5.4.1.1.
1.5.3.1.1.3. Con: If society believes that welfare and poverty is a "solved" issue, then it is a fault of the society, not the UBI.
1.5.3.1.1.3.1. Pro: UBI can help improve faulty perceptions and beliefs around poverty.
1.5.3.1.1.4. Con: Just because an UBI exists, does not mean that people are not in poverty. This poverty will still be visible to the people and the state.
1.5.3.1.1.5. Pro: Equality of opportunity, which can be achieved through affirmative action policies, is unlikely to be popular when huge portions of the budget are already being spent on a UBI.
1.5.3.1.1.5.1. Con: Affirmative action policies rarely cost money to implement. It is unclear why the cost of a UBI would influence whether they are implemented.
1.5.3.1.1.5.2. Con: -> See 1.3.5.6.1.
1.5.3.1.1.6. Pro: A bigger issue concerning inequality relates to distribution of wealth. There is a danger that UBI will act as a distraction, and disguise the bigger issue. This is especially the case in an era of accelerating technology, when profits to GDP have been hovering around an all-time high and we seem to be seeing a winner takes all society evolve, with the creation of natural monopolies in technology verticals, such as search, social media, room bookings, car sharing, etc.
1.5.3.1.2. Pro: Because it is universally accessible, a UBI does nothing to address specific sociocultural issues of minorities. If anything the 'equality' it provides erases differences and unfairly redirects blame for poverty back onto minorities.
1.5.3.1.2.1. Con: Many socio-cultural issues exist because of the economic disadvantaged state of minorities. A UBI alleviates this economic discrepancy.
1.5.3.1.2.2. Pro: Poverty is an endemic issue for minority groups; but one whose root cause is systemic discrimination in the past and present. These underlying issues are overlooked, meaning a UBI is easily characterised as a 'universal handout' that will fix the issues of poverty in minority communities.
1.5.3.1.2.2.1. Con: Just because it will fix the poverty of everyone does not mean that it will not also fix poverty in minority groups.
1.5.3.1.2.2.2. Con: Poverty, whatever the cause, is primarily a financial condition. Any measure that eases that condition does so regardless of social divisions and other disadvantages. Systemic bigotry against minorities that may hinder further advancement of members of a given group is a separate issue that requires unrelated counter measures.
1.5.3.1.2.2.2.1. Con: Dealing with the symptoms of an ailment often helps expedite recovery.
1.5.3.1.2.3. Con: Because if people take the free living wage provided by the government and squander it on something unnecessary, it really is their fault if they are out of their living wage.
1.5.3.1.2.3.1. Con: UBI will actually help fix the spending habits of the poor. Studies show that bad spending habits[come from a mindset of the scarcity of now](http://articles.latimes.com/2012/nov/03/science/la-sci-poor-people-decisions-20121103), and when people are given enough resources to think about the future those habits disappear.
1.5.3.1.2.3.1.1. Pro: The fact is, most poor people [do not spend](http://journals.plos.org/plosone/article?id=10.1371/journal.pone.0083428) in the way we assume and peer reviewed studies say that policies that contain food price inflation, improve decent-paying job opportunities for the urban poor are likely to reduce the use of negative coping strategies.
1.5.3.1.2.4. Con: A nation's choice of redistribution schemes is not the only tool by which to address specific differences in equality and so UBIs should not be dismissed as a means to provide economic relief to all individuals.
1.5.3.1.2.5. Con: The fact that, while working to alleviate a particular immediate problem, a UBI does not fix every other issue is not an argument against a UBI.
1.5.3.1.2.6. Con: A law that ameliorates a symptom instead of a root cause can still have value. For example, distributing food or money to combat hunger does nothing to aleviate the root causes of hunger, but still adds tremendous value to humanity.
1.5.3.1.2.6.1. Pro: Many diseases are treated with "supportive care" to make things easier for the body in other ways, allowing it to focus its "efforts" and energy on the primary illness.
1.5.3.1.2.7. Con: A UBI is not the solution to discrimination. A UBI is the solution to automation. Discrimination must be fixed separately. If discrimination were fixed, we would still need a UBI. Therefore, misuse of a UBI for the sake of discrimination is not a reason to oppose a UBI.
1.5.3.1.3. Con: It is possible for a UBI to co-exist with a bare minimum degree of social welfare programs. These welfare programs could address other injustices.
1.5.3.1.4. Pro: More important than a flexible income are the necessities of life: access to clean food, air, and water, a doctor when you're sick, a roof over your head and a good education. We should fight for universal access to these goods first.
1.5.3.1.4.1. Pro: This fight should take place at an individual and community level. A UBI gives these communities more to work with.
1.5.3.1.4.1.1. Pro: A UBI would help people to find the time and means to engage in activism.
1.5.3.1.4.2. Con: A UBI should provide that instead.
1.5.3.1.5. Con: When people are free from concerns about their own destitution they will be empowered to mobilize around the plights of their fellow citizens. A UBI makes sincere prioritization of other's needs possible in a way that is currently impossible for most citizens.
1.5.3.1.6. Con: "Strategic" often translates to more complex, and complexity is more difficult to fight through democratic means. A UBI would simplify political discussion to a level that can be impacted by votes.
1.5.3.1.7. Pro: If a country were to hit a rough patch economically, putting UBI on a hold would throw those dependent on it back into poverty. UBI addresses the symptoms, not the problem.
1.5.3.1.7.1. Pro: Once the welfare system is focused into one monetary sum, it will become a political target subject to erosion at each election. Over time, politicians will drive the payments down to win votes, leading to increased poverty. The poor today have multiple options to address income, housing and healthcare. Rolling those into one payment subject to political whim is a high risk strategy for the poor.
1.5.3.1.7.1.1. Con: The poor are already under this kind of attack. A UBI might actually mitigate this as it would reduce desire for "incentive" and "work requirement" schemes. It would be simpler to implement as well.
1.5.3.1.7.1.2. Con: With it all being in one sum, it would be harder for politicians to skim some money off the top as they do now. Ie. Take 2% off of $20 in 5 different values and nobody will notice, but take 2% off of $100 once and it becomes more pronounced.
1.5.3.1.8. Pro: UBI is unsustainable in a democracy because over time because organized voting blocs will lobby and vote for increasingly larger payments out of the public treasury. Enough politicians will be incentivized to dispense them in exchange for consolidating their political power.
1.5.3.1.8.1. Pro: Ultimately the level of UBI would become distorted by politicians looking to curry favor from the population receiving them. Such biased inputs would ultimately lead to unsustainable redistribution, as people are unlikely to turn down increasing amounts of UBI.
1.5.3.2. Pro: A UBI is used as a way of merely making [techno-capitalism](https://www.theguardian.com/technology/2016/may/16/universal-basic-income-equality-tech-silicon-valley) — a system that fuels inequalities \([Suarez-Villa, p. 162](https://books.google.es/books?id=UjMHDAAAQBAJ&pg=PA162&lpg=PA162&dq=technocapitalism+and+inequality&source=bl&ots=Iej_CriTK6&sig=eB_Qycqzfe2b7lS6RswdkJyr7FA&hl=it&sa=X&ved=0ahUKEwiL7-DeuojUAhWJjCwKHVhaAcYQ6AEIKTAA#v=onepage&q=technocapitalism%20and%20inequality&f=false)\) — more tolerable for people, instead of addressing the root causes of exploitation and economic inequality.
1.5.3.2.1. Pro: Silicon Valley, the epitome of techno-capitalism, [strongly supports](https://www.theguardian.com/technology/2016/jun/22/silicon-valley-universal-basic-income-y-combinator) a UBI; therefore, the companies expect that they will benefit.
1.5.3.2.1.1. Pro: [Silicon Valley's](https://www.wired.com/2017/03/silicon-valley-lawmakers-1-trillion-plan-save-trump-country/) congressional representative Ro Khanna has proposed a 1 trillion dollar earned income tax credit expansion as a form of basic income, doubling the income going to low-income families.
1.5.3.2.1.2. Con: Appeal to authority is logical fallacy. Silicon Valley's support doesn't provide justification, as they are neither political, nor economic experts.
1.5.3.2.1.2.1. Pro: A motive for Silicon Valley to support UBI can simply be explained by the notion that Silicon Valley just wants everyone to be able to buy their products.
1.5.3.2.1.2.2. Pro: The motive does not gel either. By pushing for a UBI, they are also pushing for a higher general tax rate on themselves.
1.5.3.2.1.2.3. Con: If one takes a free market approach to equality and growth then one must accept that Silicon Valley \(representing a huge market share of automation\) must be a source of good 'knowledge' on such a topic in a free market.
1.5.3.2.1.3. Con: The fact that Silicon Valley supports UBI is a given since they are the main drivers behind the techo-capitalism and would thus, reasonably, support policies supporting its continued growth.
1.5.3.2.1.4. Con: This is conformation bias. A number of local governments and organisations strongly support UBI and have no interest in techno-capitalism.
1.5.3.2.2. Con: If techno-capitalism provides the impetus to implement an effective universal welfare system, that would help address economic inequality directly.
1.5.3.2.2.1. Con: Actually, income inequality might increase with a UBI because there would be no more societal pressure to create meaningless jobs. Which means there will be an automation boom and significantly fewer jobs; meaning income inequality would actually increase. So, while covering the basic needs of the majority of the population would certainly pacify it, the political and relative economic power of the vast majority would be greatly reduced.
1.5.3.2.2.1.1. Pro: This would divide society into two camps, those solely reliant on UBI and those working or owning along with a UBI. That is, the powerful and the weak whom face an uphill battle maintaining rights and power balance in the long term. Specifically, jobs become scarcer and capital is more concentrated due to growing tech monopolies and automation. On such a trajectory any safe guards fall by the wayside where convenient for elites.
1.5.3.2.2.1.1.1. Con: We already have those classes, and the problems that come with them, it's just right now the weaker class gets nothing.
1.5.3.2.3. Con: The fact that a UBI is not a universal panacea is not an argument against a UBI.
1.5.3.2.3.1. Con: But it's not just that it's not a cure all, it actually creates new problems. The newest problem is that affordability will not be fixed, and the rich will still get richer by simply hiking up the prices of goods.
1.5.3.2.4. Con: While there may be structural inequalities in varying degrees \(depends on the country\), the fact is people are not equally skilled and success \(as measured in whatever way\) tends to accumulate to a small set of individuals. In this sense you can't take away all the causes of inequalities no matter what you do.
1.5.3.2.4.1. Con: Inequality is not the only problem a UBI is intended to address; absolute poverty and related illnesses and fatalities are also a critical issue a UBI would go a great way towards addressing.
1.5.3.2.5. Con: The emergence of the internet has turned the average citizen into a source of income for many corporations that essentially sell your identity to advertisers. A UBI would be a reasonable and necessary redistributive structure in light of our current economic framework.
1.5.3.2.5.1. Con: The general refusal to pay for Internet services resulting in the monetization of user data is not justification for mandating that the services make less money.
1.5.3.3. Con: A UBI inherently levels the playing field to remove structural injustices.
1.5.3.3.1. Con: A UBI incapacitates citizens as it creates a financial dependency on the state.
1.5.3.3.1.1. Con: Every job existing before the implementation of a UBI would presumably still exist afterwards and can still be applied for. At lower wage levels many jobs are already supported by the government anyway. In the UK, each [supermarket](https://www.citizensuk.org/taxpayer) is subsidized, so they can underpay their workers and pay their shareholders.
1.5.3.3.1.2. Pro: Citizens will become reliant on the UBI once they start using it to support ongoing business or personal projects. This creates a dependency and thus a bargaining chip for governments.
1.5.3.3.1.3. Con: Modern social welfare structures currently exist, with strings attached — you can't be homeless and get housing assistance, you need to be in college to get pell grants, and you must be working to get the earned income tax credit etc. By equalizing the payments and removing the strings attached to social programs, the influence of politicians and bureaucrats is lessened relative to the status quo.
1.5.3.3.1.4. Con: Public subsidies for business \(like arts programs\) are currently used as bargaining chips by governments. Restructuring those into a UBI \(welfare for people instead of corporations\) would remove bargaining chips from the hand of governments.
1.5.3.3.1.4.1. Con: Social programs addressing the needs of individuals are also used as bargaining chips by governments.
1.5.3.3.1.4.1.1. Con: As a UBI is universal and unconditional, it could not be used as such.
1.5.3.3.1.5. Con: Every single entity within a society is dependent on the state through the social contract.
1.5.3.3.1.5.1. Con: The degree of the dependency varies strongly.
1.5.3.3.1.5.1.1. Con: Even the wealthiest of people are at the mercy of chance, nature, and crime without the state making sure they are not victims and/or helping them recover fro such events \(e.g. rebuilding disaster zones, policing, firefighting etc\). People tend to underestimate these guarantees given by the state and take them for granted thinking they are not dependent on them when in fact everyone is just as dependent. If anything the dependence of the wealthy could be even greater as they have more to lose.
1.5.3.3.1.6. Pro: The poor will be more reliant on the state if the only method they have for reducing inequality is through state provision. This creates a perverse incentive to not challenge the state or demand social change.
1.5.3.3.1.6.1. Con: A UBI would not disincentivize the poor to challenge the state, rather they might have new challenges that do not concern economics anymore, but rather social or political issues.
1.5.3.3.1.6.2. Con: People retain all the mechanisms to enact social change that already exist; this policy does not limit their abilities.
1.5.3.3.1.6.3. Con: The poor are already reliant on the state, and usually have to "earn" their welfare through makework, such as seeking and applying for jobs that they have no chance of getting. Removing this requirement makes them less dependent on the state, because they can take chances on jobs or enterprises without facing destitution if they fail.
1.5.3.3.1.7. Pro: Multiple generations of UBI could lead to dependence on a delicate system that requires a lot of social cooperation to sustain. If this system ever collapses because subsequent generations take it for granted, the effects could be disastrous.
1.5.3.3.1.7.1. Con: -> See 1.3.5.4.1.3.4.3.2.
1.5.3.3.1.8. Pro: A UBI creates a fragile core centered around global government in a high-tension time in history. This core could collapse at any moment and create catastrophe.
1.5.3.3.1.9. Pro: A UBI would disallows an individual to attain a higher standard of life by trapping them within a limited income and lifestyle. By issuing a limited income, people will come to rely on that income solely. Assuming that welfare would remain in many countries, there would be even less incentive to improve ones life and lifestyle. Many poor and uneducated are content to keep status quo, but the ones that would "Dream" of something better, would ultimately lose.
1.5.3.3.1.9.1. Con: Individuals desiring a higher quality of life or additional goods and services could always still earn money through paid employment.
1.5.3.3.1.9.1.1. Pro: This would be actively advertised and encouraged by businesses seeking employees, who must now compete for labour as people are not beholden to them merely for their living expenses.
1.5.3.3.1.10. Pro: It would be harmful for citizens to become solely reliant on the government to provide sustenance. As the population grows, the system would become unaffordable, leaving a low skilled workforce with no means to support themselves.
1.5.3.3.2. Pro: People with "invisible", poorly documented or otherwise hard to prove disabilities can receive a UBI without tolerating questioning or having to produce proof.
1.5.3.3.3. Pro: UBI is a good starting point for establishing much broader systemic economic reform. The establishment of a UBI standard that is matched with a variety of other economic factors would allow our whole society to be enriched by innovation and cultural development. It seems over simplified to suggest that it would stifle self determination; If done with, integrity it may  allow individuals to focus on their true passions and become innovators as well foster a stronger democratic-capitalist system.
1.5.3.4. Pro: Cultivating dependence on the state through UBI reduces the bargaining power of the governed. Only when the state depends on its population for their labour, and when the people have the ability to withhold it, do the people have any power over the state save for that of violent revolution.
1.5.3.4.1. Con: In a democratic society the power lies in the vote. The elected officials would gain power if they could convince their constituents they would support and/or increase the UBI.
1.5.3.4.2. Con: Most persons in mainstream western society are dependent on private employers, which could be argued to be worse and less empowering than being dependent on the state.
1.5.3.4.3. Pro: If the income provided by a UBI does not offer more than basic subsistence, or at least a significant improvement relative to low wages currently offered by employers, it could leave people desperate and on the edge of poverty and insecurity, reinforcing [poverty traps](https://economictimes.indiatimes.com/definition/poverty-trap).
1.5.3.4.3.1. Con: Even just reducing the stress of the poorest strata of society will lead to positive shifts in society and their lifestyle.
1.5.3.4.4. Pro: A UBI would create political inequality, as people would become afraid to bite the hand that feeds them.
1.5.3.4.4.1. Pro: A UBI would become a political tool that, like welfare, is used by politicians to gain or hold power. As with welfare programs, UBI recipients would be held hostage by corrupt politicians and processes that divide those who receive a UBI from those who pay the taxes to provide UBI.
1.5.3.4.4.1.1. Con: If the UBI is a net positive for enough families say more than 50%, it would be very difficult politically to hold it hostage. Like countries with National Health Care, it is almost impossible to roll it back.
1.5.3.4.5. Pro: A UBI can be used to placate the general populace in order to lessen opposition to the government or calls for more fundamental reforms.
1.5.3.4.5.1. Pro: The [Saudi Arabian](https://www.forbes.com/sites/jeffreydorfman/2016/10/03/saudi-government-budget-cuts-prove-basic-income-is-no-solution-to-slow-economic-growth/#1376eff54bbe) government provides its citizens with a form of UBI in order to keep them happy and thus cement government power and the prevailing societal conditions.
1.5.3.4.5.1.1. Con: The Saudi system is not a UBI but rather large-scale public employment and spending that systematically excludes women and religious minorities.
1.5.3.4.5.1.1.1. Con: The difference between a UBI and such an employment program is negligible.
1.5.3.4.5.1.1.1.1. Con: The difference is not negligible if you are a woman or religious minority; it is the difference between receiving a healthy living wage and between receiving nothing.
1.5.3.4.5.1.1.2. Pro: It cannot even be called an UBI if it is not universal.
1.5.3.4.5.1.1.3. Pro: Saudi Arabia has [guaranteed jobs programs](https://www.businessinsider.de/saudi-arabia-giving-citizens-13-billion-hand-out-2018-1?r=US&IR=T); they have abandoned their UBI. If you cannot find a job, the government will give you one and pay you monthly till they find you private sector work. As of right now the boom going on there all the people that took advantage of the UBI and got educated without having to work all make six figures now. So before they changed the program, it allowed for the creation of millionaires from the slums.
1.5.3.4.5.1.2. Con: There is scant evidence that the UBI prevents social critique of the regime in Saudi Arabia. Causality is hard to determine, but pressure on the Kingdom seems to have increased since transfer payments from Oil wealth were introduced.
1.5.3.4.5.1.3. Con: Saudi Arabia, where people are being oppressed into compliance, is one of the states with the [worst human rights policies](https://wayback.archive-it.org/all/20110131191445/http://www.freedomhouse.org/uploads/special_report/88.pdf) of the world. That the people of Saudi Arabia don't call for a change might have little to do with a UBI.
1.5.3.4.5.1.4. Con: Providing the public with a stable existence isn't a form of a control, it is a basic function of government.
1.5.3.4.5.2. Con: If everyone is "too happy" to care about bad social conditions, then what exactly is it that is so "bad" about them? By that logic any other kind of progress that makes people better off than before should be opposed too on the same grounds.
1.5.3.4.5.2.1. Con: While the logic itself is sound, the key element is that it cements "prevailing societal conditions", such as restricting the rights of their female citizens. The argument is akin to saying "The Colonial Slaves had food, shelter, and protection provided to them by their masters, why should they complain?"
1.5.3.4.5.2.2. Con: Privilege can blind people to the suffering and states of those not so privileged, such as first-world peoples having only a theoretical understanding of the suffering of those most disadvantaged in the third world.
1.5.3.4.5.3. Pro: For example, the oil-rich [rentier states](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rentier_state) of the Middle East grant generous allowances to their subjects, allowing demands for rights to be ignored as there is no consequence to the state for doing so.
1.5.3.4.5.3.1. Con: It is up to voters to ensure that they do not trade basic comforts for basic rights. If a goverment says 'allow torture or you can't have security' voters can \(and have\) said 'we will not allow torture, you are still being paid to provide and responsible for security'. If they do not then any basic comfort or need can hold them hostage.
1.5.3.4.5.4. Con: Saying that the UBI is used only to cement government power is misleading. You can say the same for every form of welfare. Maybe the government could act only in an egoistical way but this is still something that makes people's life better. You don't need any proof, if the government gives money to the people it can't be considered something bad.
1.5.3.4.5.5. Pro: A UBI is a way of legitimising the capitalist system, by 'paying off' the electorate, disadvantaging future generations and nature.
1.5.3.4.5.6. Pro: A UBI can be used to lure voters or group of voters. Political parties can use this as a campaign tool promising unjustified and economically non-viable income to poor voters. This could not just affect economy but also undermines principle of free and fair voting.
1.5.3.4.6. Con: People currently depend on the state for physical security \(police, military\), environmental protection \(clean water\), power, etc. The state cannot withhold universal protections to assert power, they can only withhold limited exceptions.
1.5.3.5. Con: Any program that promotes social equality could be used by elites to avoid addressing structural issues. However, such programs in general weaken the influence of elites by empowering the disadvantaged.
1.5.3.5.1. Pro: A UBI will empower the politically marginalized by freeing them from the shackles of wage slavery. When people are free to think and organize without fear of destitution they will be able to take control of their democracy and enact their political might on structural injustices.
1.5.3.5.1.1. Pro: The ability to organize one's workplace without the threat of losing access to basic needs will be a great democratizing force.
1.5.3.5.1.2. Con: If the UBI is being provided by the government, organizing against the government would create a fear of destitution.
1.5.3.5.1.3. Con: By financing UBI through this system it would gain legitimacy. The structural injustices would be seen as a necessary evil in order to provide for the basic needs of everyone.
1.5.3.5.2. Con: A UBI together with automation will strengthen the position of high tax payers, by making the state dependent on their taxes \(to pay for UBI\).
1.5.3.5.2.1. Pro: If wealthy people already feel as though they are contributing towards the UBI, they are less likely to support other important initiatives.
1.5.3.5.2.1.1. Con: What the wealthy want and desire becomes less relevant in a world where massively more people are enfranchised.
1.5.3.5.2.1.2. Con: This same logic could also be applied to all of the social welfare programs, yet many wealthy individuals still distinguish between their own self interest and what is best for people broadly.
1.5.3.5.2.2. Con: Those high tax payers already control the state in the US, and elsewhere. This is a general problem of money in politics, not of welfare policies, UBI or otherwise.
1.5.3.5.2.2.1. Pro: The wealthiest actually pay less tax than anyone else, as a percentage of income, currently, allowing them more to spend influencing politics.
1.5.3.5.2.3. Pro: Assuming a UBI is financed through high marginal tax rates, this would make democracies \(e.g. US/UK\) even more extraordinarily protective of their most profitable industries, some of which may be globally exploitative, such as investment banking and resource extraction.
1.5.3.5.2.4. Con: A UBI will afford those with lower incomes more flexibility, which can allow them to organize politically. You could consider it a "strike fund" that allows labor and citizens to advocate for themselves.
1.5.3.5.2.4.1. Pro: A UBI might provide people with time to rally and create movements to solve inequality. The security provided by a basic income could allow people to become politically more vocal since they'd perhaps be less cautious about publicly holding a belief/opinion opposed to that of their bosses.
1.5.3.5.2.5. Con: A UBI does not necessarily have to be funded through taxes, or can be funded through taxes which are not dependent on an individual's income.
1.5.3.5.2.6. Pro: Historically and currently, in any government the more a population pays more in taxes, the more it is likely to be listened to by representatives or the state. Those on a UBI are not taxed, and will be beholden to the increasing control of those who will be taxed and who pay for the UBI. Systemically this will concentrate into fewer hands over time, leading to serfdom.
1.5.3.5.3. Pro: A UBI allows would-be political activists more time to engage in such activities without feeling as much financial pressure to engage in paid work instead.
1.5.3.6. Pro: A UBI may [create fiscal incentives for a country to reduce immigration](https://www.pbs.org/newshour/economy/making-sense/how-a-basic-income-in-the-u-s-could-increase-global-poverty).
1.5.3.6.1. Con: The idea that "reducing immigration is harmful" is a political and social value judgement. It is not widely shared enough to use as a reason for instituting an unrelated policy.
1.5.3.6.2. Con: When there is more economic stability among less wealthy people there will be less anger and anxiety directed towards immigrants and those with different cultures.
1.5.3.6.2.1. Con: On the contrary, people might just as well  become more resentful of the hand that feeds them, as they are completely dependant on it.
1.5.3.6.3. Pro: A UBI requires wealthy nations to maintain strict immigration policies to restrict access to citizenship from people from less wealthy nations.
