It would continue to incentivize the upward distribution of wealth \(specifically resources that provide wealth\) from poor nations to wealthy nations.
1.5.3.6.4. Pro: If every citizen is entitled to a UBI, that creates political incentives to discourage immigration. The biggest economic inequalities [are been rich and poor countries](http://timharford.com/2014/11/a-passport-to-privilege/), not within rich countries, and anything that could create big barriers to migration from poor to rich countries is counterproductive to global economic justice.
1.5.3.6.4.1. Pro: Immigration is a force for [reducing global inequality](https://www.brookings.edu/blog/future-development/2015/01/28/migration-as-a-way-to-reduce-global-inequality/). Reduced immigration is therefore a net harm because it harms the welfare of humanity as a whole and makes borders more rigid in general.
1.5.3.6.4.1.1. Pro: Migration and remittances increases the welfare of families who remain in their countries of origin. In [Guatemala](http://journals.plos.org/plosone/article?id=10.1371/journal.pone.0152089), having a migrant relative is associated with improved child nutrition.
1.5.3.6.4.1.2. Pro: Migration increases job opportunities and access to productive inputs to improve the effectiveness of labour. Both of these factors increase income for migrants in destination countries. Therefore, reduced migration increases global labour market inequalities.
1.5.3.6.4.1.3. Con: This is dependent on the actual reduction of migration.
1.5.3.6.4.1.3.1. Con: The United States would need 4 trillion a year to pay every citizen 15,000 a year. Adding 4 trillion a year to the national deficit would create inevitable desires to reduce immigration.
1.5.3.7. Con: The possibility that a system could be misused by malicious actors does not constitute evidence that the system would not be beneficial if used in good faith. If the system implemented faithfully would provide benefit, then the question of how to prevent abuse is a secondary \(if still important\) issue.
1.5.3.7.1. Con: Plenty of government policies if "implemented faithfully", would be beneficial, but it must be acknowledged that they aren't. A prime example includes regulatory capture, but in theory even a dictatorship, if benevolent, would likely be beneficial yet we recognize we should not support such a kind of government.
1.5.3.7.1.1. Con: It is possible to assess the consequences by performing experiments at a small scale. The fear itself of it failing cannot work as a deterrent, it is necessary to assess whether those fears are real or imagined. In the example of a dictatorship, it has been tried before over and over, however, most of the time it is not beneficial. It is not possible to say the same about an UBI unless experiments are completed.
1.5.3.7.1.1.1. Con: While it may be a good idea to test UBIs \(or not; it may not be practical to "test" UBIs on a "small scale"\), no examples of concluded, positive tests have been offered. Simply put, we cannot yet use the "possibility" of tests as support for the thesis.
1.5.3.7.2. Pro: Corruption is an evil in our society and is not affected by economic policies.
1.5.3.7.2.1. Con: That doesn't mean that economic policies aren't affected, or even directed by such corruption, to better benefit certain groups or individuals.
1.5.3.8. Pro: It is difficult to create political sympathy for the poor when they are already by far one of the largest spending items on a government budget \([e.g. US War on Poverty](https://www.cbpp.org/research/federal-budget/policy-basics-where-do-our-federal-tax-dollars-go)\). Therefore it is difficult to politically motivate for a structural reform while implementing this policy.
1.5.3.8.1. Con: The [largest spending item](http://www.businessinsider.com/2012-us-defense-budget-largest-since-world-war-ii-2011-7) on the US budget in 2012 was defense, not spending on the poor, and there was very little to no loss of political support for the US military.
1.5.3.8.2. Con: A UBI does not just go to the poor. It goes to everyone, including the middle class. Therefore, it should have more support than most welfare programs.
1.5.3.8.3. Con: The lack of political sympathy assumption is false. By this reasoning, it should be difficult to create political sympathy for the elderly because the vast majority of health care money is spent on them \(and this does not happen\).
1.5.3.8.3.1. Con: This comparison is flawed. Contrary to poverty, aging is a process almost every member of society will experience. Therefore political sympathy for the elderly is in the self-interest of \(almost\) all citizens.
1.5.4. Con: A UBI achieves both the progressive goal of providing a safety net for workers and the conservative goal of downsizing government - hence it can unify otherwise disparate political aspects of society to create a more bipartisan condition.
1.5.4.1. Con: A UBI doesn't ultimately achieve conservative goals.
1.5.4.1.1. Con: Conservative goals often reduce safety, stability, health, quality of life, agency, and freedom for the underprivileged and less fortunate.
1.5.4.1.2. Pro: A UBI would not necessarily downsize the government and so would not unify otherwise disparate political aspects of society.
1.5.4.1.2.1. Con: UBI could, at least partially, replace some government assistance programs like [SNAP](https://www.fns.usda.gov/snap/supplemental-nutrition-assistance-program) \(formerly food stamps\) which have the same aim but are far more bureaucratic.
1.5.4.1.2.1.1. Pro: Unlike cash transfers, assistance programs like food stamps are prone to [distributional leakages](https://economics.mit.edu/files/12488).
1.5.4.1.2.1.2. Pro: -> See 1.3.1.3.3.4.2.2.2.
1.5.4.1.3. Pro: A UBI hands even more power to a centralized structure through the non-voluntary confiscation of resources \(higher taxes\) eliminating individual agency and freedom. The power to choose how one spends their own resources largely disappears.
1.5.4.1.3.1. Pro: Conservatives aren't in favor of higher taxes for most anything — more authority over our collective resources increases the power of governments rather than decreases it.
1.5.4.2. Pro: Andrew Yang is gaining support for his 2020 Presidential campaign that centers around his "[Freedom Dividend](https://www.yang2020.com/policies/the-freedom-dividend/)" a UBI proposal that would guarantee $1000 a month to US citizens 18 & older.
1.5.5. Pro: Recipients may vote to raise their UBI even if it would create negative economic consequences.
1.5.5.1. Con: Some UBI recipients may have an incentive to expand the program, but net payers have an incentive to try and abolish or reduce the UBI because it is a drain on their incomes.
1.5.5.2. Pro: UBI creates well defined groups of recipients and net payers. It's easier to be a recipient, so there might be a tendency for the group of recipients to enlarge. The recipients are motivated to vote for more financial equality so inevitably enlarge the group of recipients now with even more democratic votes.
1.5.5.2.1. Con: This may not be an issue if the UBI rate is set to be low.
1.5.5.2.2. Con: In a representative democracy, politicians can resist pressure from recipients and refuse to pass the laws for more financial equality.
1.5.5.2.3. Con: Citizens can see this threat in advance and, at some point, will prefer the greater good over their own interests.
1.5.5.2.4. Con: Although the threat of a slippery slope seemingly exists for any welfare programme, welfare states do not actually experience this issue.
1.5.5.2.4.1. Con: Current welfare programs are not universal, so the groups of receivers have some limit to growth.
1.5.5.3. Pro: People in absolute poverty must care about themselves first. Any economic recession would increase their interest in redistribution and lower their responsibility to the whole society.
1.5.5.4. Con: Voters balance interests, whether tactical or strategic, for them or others. A UBI is not unique in creating something to balance out - Almost every government intervention does that.
1.5.5.5. Con: Welfare, changes in taxes, workfare, increased money supply - every intervention causes electoral conflict. If we were to instruct the government to avoid conflict of interests within the electorate, nothing would get done.
1.6. Pro: A UBI would improve the lives of many people.
1.6.1. Con: A UBI may increase the risk of political unrest.
1.6.1.1. Con: Increased wealth inequality has [led](https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2020/jan/24/most-political-unrest-has-one-big-root-cause-soaring-inequality) to a number of protests throughout the world. UBIs decrease inequality and so too the risk of political unrest.
1.6.1.1.1. Pro: Protests across Latin America have been [triggered](https://www.weforum.org/agenda/2020/01/inequality-triggered-protests-latin-america-2019/) by extreme inequality.
1.6.1.1.2. Con: A UBI might be enacted at a time when it's not developed enough for mass use and people are too desperate for money, which could cause it to fail, due to people overpowering an incapable system \(like when the coronavirus generated mass unemployment to where people rioted and led to a lack of a [2nd stimulus check](https://www.shieldwallnetwork.com/2020/06/10/black-lives-matter-protests-mean-no-2nd-stimulus-check/) in the end\). Its failure only leads to more [inequality](https://www.dhakatribune.com/business/economy/2020/06/09/unplanned-stimulus-delivery-to-increase-inequality-instability) and unrest than before.
1.6.1.1.2.1. Con: A UBI can be adjusted to avoid this pitfall. Thus, it is not a reason to avoid implementing it outright.
1.6.1.1.2.1.1. Con: Due to an emergency situation or strong public pressure, politicians may be forced to rush to implement the UBI.
1.6.1.2. Pro: The [majority](https://healthpolicy.usc.edu/evidence-base/what-do-americans-think-about-universal-basic-income/) of Americans oppose a UBI. Going against the majority risks creating political tensions and unrest.
1.6.1.2.1. Con: This can be said for any new controversial policy and does not only apply to UBIs.
1.6.1.2.2. Con: One study is not conclusive. Given the economic crisis caused by the novel coronavirus pandemic, it might well be that more Americans support a UBI in the future.
1.6.2. Pro: A UBI promotes [social justice](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Social_justice) by improving the distribution of wealth, opportunities, and privileges within a society.
1.6.2.1. Con: The distribution of societal resources should be targeted [at those most in need](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Rawls) of opportunities and support.
1.6.2.1.1. Con: It is unfair that those who are successful in life are treated unequally and excluded from governmental grants.
1.6.2.1.1.1. Con: Those who are successful do not need these government grants to continue to be successful. Those who are struggling do. In either case, the passion and drive to better or add to the world should be the motivating factor, not the money. Thus, a UBI lessens the barrier to entry for such individuals that they may contend with those who are already successful. If they are truly successful on their own merits, it would indeed be fair on the grounds of keeping them honest.
1.6.2.1.2. Pro: Giving a UBI to wealthy citizens is a waste of money and resources. The money is way better used to help those in hardship.
1.6.2.1.2.1. Con: The assumption that wealthy citizens will "waste" the money given to them is not implicit. On the contrary, it is possible that, on average, wealthy citizens will contribute more per "dollar" received than citizens in need.
1.6.2.1.2.1.1. Con: [That wealth individuals waste their money by saving it](http://www.nber.org/papers/w7906) [is not an assumption.](http://factmyth.com/factoids/people-with-more-money-save-more/)
1.6.2.1.2.2. Con: -> See 1.3.4.2.5.1.1.
1.6.2.1.2.3. Pro: As rich people save more money relative to their income than poor people and savings are macroeconomically bad UBI is inefficient.
1.6.2.1.2.3.1. Con: The saved funds are not simply put under the mattress but become reinvested in order to achieve a profitable return. Therefore, the saved proportion is available as loanable funds adding fresh capital stocks to the markets and as a result, increase the markets' [productive capacity](https://mnmeconomics.wordpress.com/2011/07/17/why-the-saving-rate-is-important/).
1.6.2.1.2.3.1.1. Con: Reinvestment is not as good for the economy as actually spending money is. An estimate of [the consumption multiplier is somewhere above 1.36](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fiscal_multiplier#United_States_of_America), while [the savings multiplier, even if we assume the highest interest rate on a savings account is undervalued by a factor of 20, is 1.26.](http://money.cnn.com/2013/10/01/pf/savings-account-yields/index.html)
1.6.2.1.2.3.1.2. Pro: Money in savings accounts is used by banks as collateral in their loaning and investment activities. In this way it doesn't 'sit there' but rather enables other productive uses.
1.6.2.1.2.3.1.2.1. Con: -> See 1.6.2.1.2.3.1.1.
1.6.2.1.3. Con: Because almost all tax systems are progressive, those who are vulnerable and in need already receive more benefits than those who are better off. If anything a UBI makes this distribution even more progressive.
1.6.2.1.4. Pro: Some people will need more support then others, some will need less; a flat UBI check is probably going to run into a similar problems as flat taxes, and similarly simplistic tax codes. The lack of discretion will hurt those that need more help.
1.6.2.1.4.1. Pro: UBI doesn't account for differences of need between people from disability or illness.
1.6.2.1.5. Con: Targeted benefits inevitably have arbitrary cutoff points for eligibility, excluding some people who have essentially the same need for support as others, but fall just beyond the cutoff, or who have other extenuating circumstances that aren't addressed by eligibility criteria.
1.6.2.1.5.1. Con: Cutoffs could be easily removed by using a sliding scale, where applicants can get a variable amount depending on their needs, instead of being eligible for all or nothing depending on a cutoff point.
1.6.2.1.5.2. Con: This can be addressed by taking care over the criteria, and making sure they are appropriate. In theory the cutoffs should be a good thing, preventing those not in need taking resources which could go to those in need. If this isn't the case in practice, then the criteria simply need revisiting.
1.6.2.1.6. Con: Implementing a UBI does not exclude the possibility of providing extra benefits to those who may need them.
1.6.2.1.7. Pro: A UBI 'takes' money from those who need it to 'give' to those who do not. Those who need money most are given proportionately less than under other welfare distribution systems.
1.6.2.1.8. Con: Providing the same benefit to every citizen—even wealthy ones—makes it more likely that the UBI will remain dependable. Everyone who receives the UBI has a stake in keeping it going, so the more people who receive it, the more people will vote for officials who support the UBI policy. Social Security uses this strategy, and it's proven impossible for any politician to cut or even reduce benefits.
1.6.2.1.9. Con: This compromises democracy and encourages a client-patron relationship between groups and the politicians that secure their official "group in need the government must help" status.
1.6.2.1.10. Pro: Treating people who are unequal equally is not egalitarian and will not address current inequalities or produce a more equitable distribution of capital and income wealth as an outcome.
1.6.2.1.10.1. Pro: Universal access to a basic living wage above the poverty line that uses a simple and automatically-administered mean test would offer all the benefits claimed by a UBI without the cost burden of payments to people who currently don't need any financial assistance.
1.6.2.2. Pro: The UBI financially supports citizens who are engaged in meaningful but low or unpaid activities, such as: domestic labor, art, volunteering, engaging with politics, and pursuing higher education.
1.6.2.2.1. Pro: Many forms of creative expression, such as art or writing, are not pursued because only the most successful artists or writers can generate a self-sustaining income.
1.6.2.2.1.1. Pro: Even the pursuit of creative activities as a way to achieve a financial reward may in many cases lead to those activities [being enjoyed less and pursued less effectively](https://www.gnu.org/philosophy/motivation.html).
1.6.2.2.1.2. Pro: Wikipedia and many other open-source community creations are an example of unpaid work that has great societal value. A UBI would help reward and sustain these valuable resources.
1.6.2.2.1.3. Con: Producing quality "creative expression" is a hard work. Assuming that introduction of unconditional income will increase the total amount of high quality works of art is unfounded and naive.
1.6.2.2.1.3.1. Con: "Practice makes perfect", or at least has a positive relationship with quality. Additional time permitted for artistic endeavour \(such as by a UBI reducing the stress of providing for basics\) will increase the quality of artistic production.
1.6.2.2.1.3.2. Pro: Simple probability statistics disagree with this thesis. A greater number of artistic creators, even using the Infinite Monkey Theorem as a productivity yardstick, will invariably increase the number of quality artistic works being created.  More artists means more art means more good art. While the average quality of UBI-funded art may be less, but more great art will invariably get produced if there are more artists making art.
1.6.2.2.1.4. Pro: An UBI enables artists to live without the need to commodify their art so that it can be sold.
1.6.2.2.1.4.1. Pro: Artists, who have always struggled to find funding adequate enough to develop their oeuvre, will benefit greatly from a reliable income.
1.6.2.2.1.4.2. Con: Artists struggling to earn enough money to make a living is a price signal meaning that, for people, that art has more costs than benefits. In order to allow that art to be created, people would have to give up things that are more important.
1.6.2.2.1.4.3. Pro: Many contemporary artists [receive pressure](https://books.google.se/books?id=aXCHJ9CBQLEC&pg=PA46&lpg=PA46&dq=pressure+on+artists+to+mainstream&source=bl&ots=zDsQLQ7nBa&sig=ACfU3U2i6qhIUlarF9MMvybsqcx5f7u63g&hl=en&sa=X&ved=2ahUKEwj4sdjk073lAhVC2aQKHU_XASEQ6AEwCXoECAkQAQ#v=onepage&q=pressure%20on%20artists%20to%20mainstream&f=false) from producers to alter their work to better appeal to a mainstream audience, against their own desires or creative vision.
1.6.2.2.1.5. Pro: A UBI will enable artists to do what they do best rather than having to scrape by, spending most of their time working minimum wage jobs. The benefit to society is that we have an infusion of arts and culture.
1.6.2.2.1.6. Pro: A UBI will help to subsidize independent and critical thought and thus help bring new ideas into fruition.
1.6.2.2.1.7. Con: -> See 1.1.5.3.1.1.5.
1.6.2.2.2. Pro: -> See 1.4.5.2.1.7.
1.6.2.2.3. Con: If a contribution is valuable to society, then market forces will already define a fair price for such a contribution.
1.6.2.2.3.1. Con: "Meaningful contribution to society" is a vague and ill defined concept. To the extent economic policies incentivise certain behaviours they should do so in a targeted fashion to ensure the outcomes are positive and progress is measured relative to costs.
1.6.2.2.3.1.1. Con: Yes, it's a vague concept, but that's kind of the point. A number of activities that contribute to society, not only in wellbeing/community, but in solid economic gains, are undervalued. Childcare and aged care for example; or the arts. In contrast the current system, seems to incentivise only property barons, mining magnates, and telemarketers — often groups that actively detract from public good.
1.6.2.2.3.1.2. Con: The previous claim provided a number of examples that helped to clarify the broad category of "meaningful to society."
1.6.2.2.3.1.3. Con: There is much labor that is important — child care, art, charity, self-education and improvement — but is not compensated under the present form of capitalism. UBI would allow persons who raise children, create beauty, and do good works to sustain themselves without being forced to spend the majority of their time doing other jobs just to support themselves.
1.6.2.2.3.1.3.1. Con: The first beneficiary of self-education is oneself. If it is beneficial for also for a second party it can make a decision to sponsor the hopeful student. This ensures that the studies are progressing, as it costs funds. With unconditional UBI for all, this reality check won't work.
1.6.2.2.3.1.3.1.1. Con: Nobody and nothing exists in a vacuum - self-improvement and self-education will inevitably benefit one's neighbours as well, through processes as simple and basic as information and knowledge spreading through conversation, as well as whatever active uses the person puts their education, improvement and knowledge towards.
1.6.2.2.3.1.4. Con: A UBI will distribute the decision of what is meaningful work. Instead of having politicians decide what is "meaningful" and design policies that actually achieve the objective, you have lots of people with the ability to spend time/money on what they think is important. In that context it's not vague and ill defined, it's the average of the population's well-defined preferences.
1.6.2.2.3.2. Con: In the case of America the market system specifically does not define a fair price for such a contribution due to skewed distribution of wealth and price influence.
1.6.2.2.3.2.1. Con: Further skewing the market by use of involuntary wealth transfers would exaggerate this problem rather than fixing it.
1.6.2.2.3.3. Con: If you solve a problem for a rich person "society" rewards you much more than if you solve a problem for a poor person. Even if your contribution toward the poor person had a greater impact on their life. For instance, tutoring a poor kid or tutoring a rich kid provides different rewards for the same contribution. \(tried to place the tutoring example as a pro, to separate things\)
1.6.2.2.3.3.1. Con: Directing resources to the already well resourced can be the most efficient investment. For example, if an expensive worker is delayed by one hour in getting to work, the lost production is much greater than if an inexpensive worker is delayed by the same time. Allocating a private car to the expensive worker may be justifiable even though allocating a private car to the less expensive worker would not be.
1.6.2.2.3.3.2. Con: The above claim falsely assumes that wealth is always unearned and concludes it unfair that a rich person can outbid a poor person. However, wealth is often earned, therefore the ability to outbid more for a contested resource must sometimes be acknowledged to be a reward for earlier effort.
1.6.2.2.3.4. Con: Market forces stop making sense when people stop being able to pursue the best value deals. When people are no longer players due to lack of funds, they are unable to reward contributions made available towards them.
1.6.2.2.3.5. Con: Many market failures exist. For example, a family member caring for an elderly person is not rewarded by the market, while paying a stranger to do so is.
1.6.2.2.3.6. Pro: Individuals differ in their valuation of contributions. The market economy is the only means by which we may discover those valuations.  A "contribution to society" is meaningful and valuable only insofar as the members of that society are willing to pay for it.
1.6.2.2.3.6.1. Con: Willingness to pay for something does not mean that a financial transaction has to take place. The opportunity cost \(for example of a missed salary\) is also information about the value of something to a member of society.
1.6.2.2.3.6.2. Con: Roads are pretty valuable, yet society doesn't pay for them as part of a market economy.
1.6.2.2.3.6.3. Con: Police in general are pretty valuable, yet police that individuals pay for are called protection rackets, and are a determent to society.
1.6.2.2.3.6.3.1. Con: Actually, they're called "security firms" and are widely engaged. Protection racketeering is more along the lines of blackmailing victims with property damage and extorting money out of them on these grounds.
1.6.2.2.3.6.3.1.1. Con: Security firms work within the established framework of the law, have tons of oversight, and limited power —  they are basically the deputies of the goverment-run police and can't really be evaluated as an independent agency
1.6.2.2.3.6.4. Con: By the reasoning provided, intellectual property is not valuable. It would be better not to encourage the monopoly for any length of time. While some question the extent of our monopolies, most people see a little value in them.
1.6.2.2.3.6.5. Con: This statement highly values bribable officials. [Society](https://globalcompliancenews.com/anti-corruption/anti-corruption-laws-around-the-world/) tends not to overly care for them.
1.6.2.2.3.6.6. Con: Market exchange does not account for 'positive externalities' and 'negative externalities'.
1.6.2.2.3.7. Pro: A UBI removes the incentive to deliver precisely those goods and services most highly valued by individuals. The value of a good or service to an individual is only measurable by what that individual is willing to forego to attain it, namely, what they are willing to pay. By flattening the price disparities between different activities, a UBI will dilute the information contained in prices and lead to inefficient allocation of resources.
1.6.2.2.3.7.1. Con: The UBI is not paid to the product, but the person.
1.6.2.2.3.8. Con: Market forces value the jobs that are harder to fill, not the jobs with the biggest contribution to society.
1.6.2.2.3.9. Con: In capitalist societies there are many instances where jobs that provide the least use value are rewarded with higher wages than those that provide intrinsic value.
1.6.2.2.3.9.1. Pro: Use value is a good or service that is intrinsically valuable, or at least an instrumental good not far removed from a good in itself. Occupations that deal in exchange values are those which mediate goods and services, but themselves are not productive; advertising etc.
1.6.2.2.3.10. Con: Many jobs exist where people perform them out of a sense of duty or vocation - these jobs are frequently poorly paid, because people are willing to endure low wages in order to work in their chosen field, and because it is felt that a better wage might encourage people to seek the job for the money rather than a "purer" reason. In some cases, such as teaching or nursing, people then leave the field in order to pursue better paid jobs, because they can no longer afford to follow their vocation.
1.6.2.2.3.11. Con: Hard work and reward do not correlate.
1.6.2.2.3.11.1. Con: Hard work is not limited to physically hard work. Achieving "large returns" on investments consistently is very hard work, although not physical.
1.6.2.2.3.11.2. Con: “Hard work” is the wrong term. Work which has higher societal benefits would be more accurate. Athletes \(professional game players\) produce no material goods, but they are paid millions because people enjoy watching them. Investment banking isn’t physically hard work but is an important engine in growing the domestic economy.
1.6.2.2.3.11.2.1. Con: Investmant bankers spurred the 2008 financial crisis. Collateralized debt obligations and preditory lending allowed bankers to leverage their risk onto the pockets of tax payers in pursuit of personal profit. Their actions did not help American society. Skilled trades people help grow society.
1.6.2.2.3.11.3. Con: "Hard work" is an ambiguous term. It can mean physically taxing work - this indeed does not correlate with pay. But "hard" can also mean "hard to learn/acquire necessary skills" - in this meaning hardness does correlate with pay.
1.6.2.2.3.11.3.1. Con: This form of hard work should correspond with pay. Manual labor causes many health problems, both immediately and over time \(ex.: spinal misalignment, hernia\). There's general some risk of direct injury or loss of life involved with labor jobs, such as shelving collapses, forklift accidents, loading dock accidents, and others.
1.6.2.2.3.11.3.1.1. Pro: No one was ever been killed from learning calculus. The proposed 'thinking' form of hard work does not imply danger.
1.6.2.2.3.11.4. Con: If individuals are resentful of the current circumstance for reasons like low-return labor, then reducting that resentment by eliminating those positions with a UBI does not contribute to decreased productivity and societal incentive. The threat to societal incentive pertains to increased opportunity space.
1.6.2.2.4. Con: The state could sufficiently support people for meaningful and/or social contributions by paying people specifically for these contributions rather than giving everyone an unconditional income.
1.6.2.2.4.1. Con: State actors rarely do well with picking which activities are useful or productive. Better to keep the state out of it and let the laws of basic economics and market forces guide such decisions.
1.6.2.2.4.1.1. Con: -> See 1.2.8.4.1.
1.6.2.2.4.2. Con: There are many accidents that result in valuable contributions. Allowing anyone to be able to try allows more accidents to happen \(not to mention the intentional benefits\). If we don't support people trying and failing, we won't have enough trials to make any significant progress.
1.6.2.2.4.2.1. Con: There was no such support US during 19th or 20th centuries but the rate of both economic growth and innovation there was huge during most this centuries. There are plenty of other countries that enjoyed the high growth without having special support for failed entrepreneurs.
1.6.2.2.4.2.2. Pro: In many areas, "practice makes perfect" or at least has a positive relationship with quality of work produced. Permitting people to practice and indulge in areas of interest to them, even if it does not initially produce valuable goods, may permit valuable work to be produced in the future, or provide inspiration that may lead to societally beneficial innovation.
1.6.2.2.4.3. Pro: A targeted welfare system is more able to account for this.
1.6.2.2.4.3.1. Pro: Specific benefits might be paid to single parents, or people working as caretakers for dependent family members.
1.6.2.2.4.4. Con: There is likely to be a high amount of bureaucracy and fraud that would be involved in tracking every hour of care, cooking, helping out neighbours and then rewarding these actions.
1.6.2.2.4.5. Con: It is impossible to track how many hours of reading and research and leisure, sometimes spread over decades, went into the making of one invention.
1.6.2.2.4.6. Con: If someone would be otherwise engaged in criminal activity, doing nothing is in itself an improvement. UBI may well make those who would otherwise engage in crimes less desperate.
1.6.2.2.4.7. Pro: Even if the UBI rewards persons who currently perform unpaid \(but valuable\) contributions, such as artists or stay at home parents, it rewards everyone equally. Thus it is not a fair form of pay that recognises certain unpaid contributions are more valuable and laborious than others.
1.6.2.2.4.8. Pro: If society currently under-appreciates the contributions of important sources of unpaid labour, such as stay at home parents, a UBI may provide them some financial compensation but its universal nature denies them any form of specific recognition of their labour.
1.6.2.2.5. Pro: Domestic labour currently receives poor support in society. Instituting a UBI would better support [domestic labourers](http://interactions.eldis.org).
1.6.2.2.5.1. Pro: A UBI can decrease the necessity to work long hours, thus providing working partners more time and energy for domestic labor efforts. This essentially helps both individuals.
1.6.2.2.5.2. Pro: The value of unpaid care in the US is estimated at [$691 billion](http://www.washington.edu/news/2015/06/16/study-reveals-surprising-truths-about-caregivers/); over 4% of GDP.
1.6.2.2.5.2.1. Con: Unpaid care could and should be addressed by targeted programs, rather than a UBI which just indiscriminately provides everyone with money.
1.6.2.2.5.3. Con: Historically, recompense for domestic laborers came in the form of having their basic needs provided for by the partner that worked, therefore UBI isn't necessary.
1.6.2.2.5.3.1. Con: The traditional model has many disadvantages, in particular for the unpaid domestic laborer.
1.6.2.2.5.3.1.1. Pro: The traditional model leaves the unpaid worker vulnerable to abuse and otherwise at the mercy of the provider, who can provide exactly as much or as little as they please, unless the unpaid domestic worker has effective legal recourse.
1.6.2.2.5.3.1.1.1. Pro: An unpaid domestic worker is/was unlikely to have effective legal recourse due to lack of resources under the traditional model.
1.6.2.2.5.4. Con: There are more direct and effective ways to compensate for child/aged care.
1.6.2.2.5.4.1. Pro: In an experiment in an old-age home in Holland, [free housing](http://www.universityherald.com/articles/53852/20161207/dutch-university-students-free-housing-exchange-valuable-service-elderly.htm) was given to students in exchange for time they spend with elderly residents.
1.6.2.2.5.4.2. Pro: Access to healthcare for children and aged populations would be a more effective way at compensating for child/age care as it more specifically addresses the target groups.
1.6.2.2.5.4.3. Pro: Tax deductions or credits for having or adopting children, would be a specific compensation for childcare. This is not only more effective, but also signals that the state values those who take care of their children.
1.6.2.2.5.4.4. Con: Those methods are not mutually exclusive with a UBI; moreover they only compensate for some aspects of raising a child \(healthcare, or 9-5 care\) rather than the entire endeavour.
1.6.2.2.5.5. Con: Unlike employment, a UBI would support all domestic labourers, including the ones that were neglectful of their work.
1.6.2.2.5.5.1. Pro: As domestic labourers receive pay in the form of UBI regardless of how well they work, the incentive to do a good job is lower.
1.6.2.2.5.5.1.1. Con: As the UBI isn't tied to the work of domestic labourers, it doesn't have any effect on their incentives.
1.6.2.2.5.5.1.1.1. Con: The satisfaction of receiving a disposable income could replace the desire to seek satisfaction in other ways, such as through doing a good job as a domestic labourer.
1.6.2.2.5.5.1.2. Pro: -> See 1.6.2.2.5.5.1.1.1.
1.6.2.2.5.5.2. Pro: Domestic labourers shouldn't be rewarded for doing a bad job. However, receiving a disposable income every month would make them feel like they're rewarded for the things they do, regardless of how poorly.
1.6.2.2.5.5.3. Con: The majority of parents are good; to punish them for the actions of the bad ones is unfair.
1.6.2.2.5.5.4. Con: There are plenty of discriminatory systems that can co-exist with a UBI, such as criminal sanctions, child services interventions, and the withdrawal of non-UBI benefits or privileges.
1.6.2.2.5.6. Con: The fact that their valuation of such services is not as high as some would like represents a valid difference in personal priorities and is not a structural imbalance in need of reform.
1.6.2.2.5.6.1. Pro: If this work were of high value to parents, the caretakers of the aged, and the aged themselves, they would be willing to pay for it in proportion to that high value.
1.6.2.2.5.6.2. Con: People cannot be willing to pay money they do not have, as in the case of stay at home parents who either are not provided a fair portion of the family's income, or are simply in a lower-income socioeconomic bracket.
1.6.2.2.5.6.3. Con: Historically, caretaking roles have been undervalued for a number of reasons, including the expectation for women to perform them for free, merely in return for housing and basic necessities \(and often with a side of abuse\). This historical impact has not yet entirely faded.
1.6.2.2.5.6.4. Con: This claim assumes most people are enlightened capitalists able and willing to think in such terms. This is not true. Most people do not make rational purchasing decisions based purely on capitalistic logic and ideals.
1.6.2.2.5.7. Pro: A UBI benefits caregivers and gives a decent life to people with disabilities.
1.6.2.2.5.7.1. Con: -> See 1.6.2.2.5.2.1.
1.6.2.2.5.8. Pro: Unpaid labour tends to be highly gendered with the bulk of work expected to be fulfilled by women. In this context it is important to implement systems that support women in these roles and provide greater financial autonomy.
1.6.2.2.5.8.1. Pro: -> See 1.3.5.5.7.4.1.1.
1.6.2.2.5.8.2. Pro: A UBI alleviates the stress of insufficient [maternity leave](http://www.forbes.com/sites/ritarubin/2016/04/06/united-states-lags-behind-all-other-developed-countries-when-it-comes-to-paid-maternity-leave/#20f54bd05ada) for expectant mothers and their families.
1.6.2.2.5.8.2.1. Con: The problem of insufficient maternity leave \(which the article notes is a major problem in America\) could be addressed by increasing maternity leave support, rather than providing everyone with unconditional money regardless of whether they likely need it more or less than the average person.
1.6.2.2.6. Pro: With basic needs guaranteed people will be more able to engage with politics and the democratic process.
1.6.2.2.6.1. Pro: If sustainable, a UBI helps keep people invested in the society that is supporting them, leading to less likely chance of revolutionary action.
1.6.2.2.6.2. Pro: A UBI would strengthen democracy. On the one hand, people would not feel as forgotten, but rather as a part of society. On the other hand since there would more free time - it would be a real oppurtunity to take part in democratic processes.
1.6.2.2.6.2.1. Pro: Citizens would have more free time to become politically active/involved.
1.6.2.2.6.2.1.1. Pro: Engaged and involved citizens hold the government accountable and ensure that it is working in the people's best interests.
1.6.2.2.6.2.1.2. Con: Even if it'd be so most of them would rather be involved in quite passive ways - by reading the press, attending meetings with politicians etc. which could lead to an even further expansion of goverment control over them.
1.6.2.2.6.2.1.3. Pro: A UBI would mitigate the current personal financial costs to core democratic activities, such as Jury Duty.
1.6.2.2.6.2.2. Pro: -> See 1.5.3.1.4.1.1.
1.6.2.2.6.2.3. Pro: The disenfranchised might feel as though somebody actually cares about them, which might even encourage some of the more troubled people to seek help.
1.6.2.2.6.3. Pro: Taking fewer shifts at work may allow people to vote or attend a town hall or a school board meeting. This allows lower income employees time to be more politically engaged in their communities so that they are better able to represent their own interests in government. [\(Berry 2012\)](https://politicsandculture.org/2012/05/02/leisure-the-sacred-gesture-and-human-dignity-thorstein-veblen-and-josef-piepers-understandings-of-leisure/)
1.6.2.2.6.3.1. Pro: Increased political engagement is good because it allows political forces to represent the people more easily. Increased political engagement will also help increase trust in government, leading to a better civil service.
1.6.2.2.6.3.2. Con: Lower income employees lack political representation due to the lobbying power that being wealthy brings, not because they lack the time to be politically engaged.
1.6.2.2.6.3.2.1. Con: Lobbying power is definitely not the only reason. [Turnout inequality](https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2005/may/14/election2005.constitution) is a reality in most countries, thus regardless of the reasons \(though money and consequently time are probably among the factors\) the poor vote less and are less politically engaged.
1.6.2.2.6.3.2.2. Pro: Universal basic income will allow more people to lobby.
1.6.2.2.6.3.3. Con: Supplementing the income of higher-paid employees with a UBI \(since they would have received zero welfare beforehand\) gives them a greater ability to work less and increases their representation in government, which may work against the interests of lower income persons.
1.6.2.2.6.3.3.1. Con: High-paid employees usually have [high](https://www.forbes.com/sites/moneywisewomen/2012/03/21/average-america-vs-the-one-percent/#75150af22395) levels of [spending](https://www.theguardian.com/business/2015/dec/25/wealthy-americans-living-paycheck-to-paycheck-income-paying-bills), therefore a UBI that is above the poverty line is unlikely to cover their lifestyle if they work less.
1.6.2.2.6.3.3.2. Con: -> See 1.3.5.4.1.2.1.2.
1.6.2.2.6.3.3.3. Con: The relative value of the basic income depends on the recipient. For someone who lacks political and economic power, a supplement of $4000 might change their behaviour. Giving the same supplement to someone who earns more than $100k a year won't buy them any representation they don't already have.
1.6.2.2.6.4. Pro: A UBI ensures that no citizen is forced to choose between earning and income and exercising their democratic right to vote in an election.
1.6.2.3. Con: Welfare redistribution schemes, in general, are unjust. Therefore, a UBI would be the same.
1.6.2.3.1. Con: If taxation is equivalent to, and enforced as a crime, no modern state would be able to function.
1.6.2.3.1.1. Con: A state that sustains itself by violating the rights and freedoms it was built to defend undermines its purpose.
1.6.2.3.1.1.1. Con: This assumes "defending people's rights and freedoms" is the purpose of a given state. If taxation is indeed theft, then such a state is a paradox - a criminal organisation whose purpose is to protect people from crime.
1.6.2.3.1.2. Con: Read the claim carefully, taxes for an involuntary wealth transfer are equivalent to crime, not all taxes.
1.6.2.3.1.2.1. Con: All taxes are involuntary, because one can't refuse to pay them without threat of governmental violence.
1.6.2.3.2. Con: Given taxation and redistribution policies are widespread, there is no reason to hold a UBI in particular to a different standard.
1.6.2.3.2.1. Con: Prevalence is no proof of legitimacy.
1.6.2.3.2.2. Con: This discussion is about a UBI though. No different standard is held, all wealth redistribution programs are immoral and equivalent to theft.
1.6.2.3.2.3. Con: Not all taxes are immoral. Defense is a service rendered to all people, and it would be near impossible for the military to selectively defend people. Therefore all people should have to pay for that service. Infrastructure, like roads, are a good that all people benefit from, therefore all people should have to pay for that good. Taxes for these purposes are not immoral. but taxes for a wealth redistribution scheme like UBI, welfare, etc. are. UBI isn't being held to a different standard.
1.6.2.3.3. Con: Taxes aren't crimes. They are simply citizens paying for the services their goverment provides.
1.6.2.3.3.1. Con: Any service by definition is something you voluntarily choose to hire and fire at will.
1.6.2.3.3.2. Con: Taxes are the involuntary apropriation of a part of an individual's wealth. A theft is the involuntary apropriation of a part of an individual's wealth. Taxes equal theft, and if society legitimates taxes, it is legitimating theft.
1.6.2.3.3.2.1. Con: Taxes are the legitimate exercise of one of a government's legitimate functions, which is to provide public services from public funds for the maintenance of society. Members within a society exist in that society, not in isolation, and benefit from their position within that society. It is reasonable and ethical for individuals to contribute, and be expected to contribute, towards maintenance of the society they are a part of and benefit from.
1.6.2.3.3.3. Pro: Governments defend people's right to private property, which could otherwise be breached in any number of ways \(theft, financially motivated murder...\) without consequence. In order to provide this protection, it is necessary for governments to source funding somehow. This is the principle behind taxes, which are not immoral in this light.
1.6.2.3.3.3.1. Con: But you've created a false equivalency and a straw man. The claim is that "Taxation for a wealth redistribution scheme is equivalent to crime". Not that all taxes are equivalent to crime. You've chosen a specific tax that actually provides a service to the person paying the tax. In the case of a UBI, the person paying the tax receives no good or service in return.
1.6.2.3.3.4. Con: Note the claim states that taxation specifically for involuntary wealth transfers are EQUIVALENT to crime, in that case specifically theft. More specifically, even if theft wasn't considered a crime for some odd reason\(anarchic society\), it would still be immoral.
1.6.2.3.3.4.1. Con: This claim makes a claim about anarchy that makes no sense. Anarchy doesn't recognize private property, not theft.
1.6.2.3.4. Con: The nature of political power in democratic society is that it involves individuals giving up power over one aspect of your life in order to gain benefits in the other.
1.6.2.3.4.1. Con: Individuals don't give up their power voluntarily, even if they are immensely better off with a democratic society. This is unethical and immoral.
1.6.2.3.4.2. Con: Not all wealthy countries are democratic.
1.6.2.3.4.3. Con: No benefit is gained by the person paying the tax for the UBI. Nor any tax based wealth redistribution program for that matter.
1.6.2.3.5. Con: Taxation is not theft given democracy defines the rule of law. Our law defines what is criminal and what isn't, meaning that legal taxation cannot be seen as a crime.
1.6.2.3.5.1. Pro: The costs and benefits of taxation can hardly be crimes if they exist by the assent of the majority of the voting population.
1.6.2.3.5.1.1. Con: This assumes that democracy and criminality are equivalent to morality. Democracies can and do act in immoral and criminal ways.
1.6.2.3.5.1.2. Con: The majority should not be able to decide what to do with an individual's resources. That's immoral, and demotivational. The US founding fathers recognized that they had to do something to protect minorities, that's why the senate exists.
1.6.2.3.5.2. Con: The claim you're attempting to refute states it is equivalent to crime, not that it is crime. It also doesn't state that all taxation is equivalent to crime, but that wealth redistribution schemes are equivalent to crime.
1.6.2.3.6. Pro: A UBI is a "means to an end" policy, where stealing is justified on the basis of knowing better and only the possibility, but no guarantee, of achieving an ends.
1.6.2.3.6.1. Con: From a [consequentialist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Consequentialism) standpoint, taking actions in order to achieve good ends even though those ends are not guaranteed describes all moral action outside of hypothetical situations where perfect knowledge is assumed.
1.6.2.3.7. Con: It could equally be argued that leaving people to suffer and die in poverty is equivalent to a collective societal crime.
1.6.2.3.7.1. Con: This is not a necessary consequence of forgoing a tax-based welfare program. These kinds of programs interfere in the voluntary non-profit/philanthropic sector, as well as the commercial market sector. There is no telling what altruistic and possibly more effective uses a person would have had for that money had it not been taken away from them via taxation.
1.6.2.3.7.1.1. Con: The private market and industries cannot be relied on to ensure the provision of basic human rights and reasonable quality of life.
1.6.2.3.7.2. Con: Rights, responsibilities, and things like crimes can only truly be applied to individuals, not societies. Societies are made up of individuals, but are not the same thing.
1.6.2.3.7.3. Con: Currently speaking, theft is on the books as a crime, not being charitable isn't a crime.
1.6.2.3.8. Pro: Wealth redistribution schemes are unjust because they violate the moral right individuals have over their private property.
1.6.2.3.8.1. Con: Property rights and capital ownership are community assets. Individuals with no property or capital need to be compensated by those who are hoarding these assets.
1.6.2.3.8.1.1. Pro: UBI mediates between the natural right of every human to a part of the earth and its natural resources and the liberal concept of individual ownership of those goods.
1.6.2.3.8.1.1.1. Pro: A UBI is a small step towards remedying the exceedingly imbalanced distribution of wealth generated with Earth's resources.
1.6.2.3.8.1.2. Con: Property rights are community assets but capital and property itself are not community assets. Control of natural resources \(e.g. land, water, spectrum and air\) belongs to the entire society which then sells temporary rights for exclusive use to individuals. Private property is a natural, inalienable right that is not granted by society. Everyone has at least some property; their body. We trade our labor to acquire more property. Our property is not a community asset unless we are slaves.
1.6.2.3.8.1.3. Pro: A UBI is not the redistribution of income from those who earned it to those who did not. A UBI is the redistribution of accumulated wealth from those whose ancestors hoarded it to those whose ancestors have provided the labor and intellectual capital which has created that wealth seance the end of the stone age. A UBI is not a handout, it is inheritance from all of your ancestors for 10,000 years.
1.6.2.3.8.1.4. Con: There is nothing communal about property rights. Only individuals can own things. Even corporations and the government are reduced to individuals when presented as legal entities.
1.6.2.3.8.1.4.1. Con: While it's true that only individuals can own things, property rights are not "things" by any common definition of the word. For example, an individual might hold a copyright on a book but it's a temporary right granted for a limited time by society. Nobody can actually own the story because an idea is not a "thing". Likewise real estate rights are not things. They are a temporary right granted by society. If there is no community, then there are no property rights, there's just property.
1.6.2.3.8.2. Pro: Anybody has the moral right to keep what they have worked for and legally obtained. A UBI robs people of their labor and private property \(i.e. money\) and gives it to those who haven't earned it.
1.6.2.3.8.2.1. Pro: UBI requires redistribution of fairly earned income from those who require "assistance" to those who do not.
1.6.2.3.8.2.1.1. Con: Not if it is financed from sources other than income taxes \(machine tax, carbon tax, funds from social security programs that would become obsolete, taxes on financial speculation, stimulus packages that are supposed to "trickle down" to the middle class\).
1.6.2.3.8.2.1.2. Con: This statement effectively says that those with income are the ones that need assistance. The fundamental tenet of civil society, and ultimately its purpose, is that we help each other survive. We do that by giving of ourselves when we can to others that need it. Meaning we take from those that can earn income and give it to those who can't. This is either poorly written or idealogically incongruent with the goal of living together.
1.6.2.3.8.2.2. Con: This argument is based on a vision of human nature that is not further elaborated upon. Instead of advocating for a greedy nature, I would plea for a social nature that is core to human being. In the end, people do like to share what they have both with their beloved ones and strangers. A UBI would not kill productivity or rob the laborer, but instead answer to a key feature of human nature.
1.6.2.3.8.2.3. Con: In nature, only the strongest person has that right — they can take anything from anyone else. Societies exist because of the collective recognition that such a state of anarchy is unjust and unproductive; and so they construct a legal system that protects the vulnerable from random acts of violence. The laws that enable these protections are the only 'rights' that exist.
1.6.2.3.8.2.3.1. Con: Capitalism is not a zero-sum game. Everybody gets something from an exchange. Even those you would call "weak."
1.6.2.3.8.2.3.1.1. Con: Assuming rational actors, both only benefit if the opposite party has no control over what you can put on the negotiating table. This restriction is something we have written into the law, the courts, and the application of that law.
1.6.2.3.8.2.3.1.1.1. Pro: The fact that exchanges within the system is better for everyone, says nothing about the system as a whole. 
Capitalism removes access to most property from most people by allowing individual ownership of all things. So while two people trading to get what they want, more than what they already have, makes both better off, the property system that dictates the distribution of property makes most people worse off prior to the trade.
1.6.2.3.8.2.3.1.2. Con: Trades or market transactions, are both not unique to capitalism. Capitalism is the result of our property laws.
1.6.2.3.8.2.3.2. Con: If society as a whole decides what constitutes a law, then we cannot critique any of the rights it creates — anything that the collective implements is thus tautologically good.
1.6.2.3.8.2.3.2.1. Con: This is a non sequitur. If there is a Constitutional majority that decides on what is a "right", it becomes one. The original claim was about what was A right, not what was right.
1.6.2.3.8.2.3.2.2. Con: Society's laws are based on authorities such as divinity, or reason, and through those authorities it can be attacked, but these systems don't argue for unchangeable property rights to support people to the determent of all.
1.6.2.3.8.2.3.2.3. Con: Society may be persuaded to change its mind. Most societies permit discussing forbidden acts  — at least up to a point.
1.6.2.3.8.2.3.3. Con: You don't have to construct a "legal system" which upholds "rights" either. At least you don't have to use that language. There can be a group of people who organize a self-defense squad to protect each other and their property.
1.6.2.3.8.2.4. Con: This argument can be equally applied to all forms of taxes. Yet taxes exist in nearly every society because their collective value overrides the individual harms.
1.6.2.3.8.2.4.1. Con: The question is, however, whether the problem lies in the argument, or in the assumptions made on the necessity of those taxes.
1.6.2.3.8.2.4.1.1. Con: Few people would argue for the removal of the military, the police, public road, or the sewer, and most of those that act upon this belief quickly die as a result of their lack of these facilities. Maintaining those takes money, and that money has to come from somewhere.
1.6.2.3.8.2.4.1.1.1. Con: Many services have historically been provided through collective action. These kind of systems could return or be rejuvenated.
1.6.2.3.8.2.4.1.1.1.1. Pro: There have been historical cases of polycentric legal systems, where even the writing and administering of law was in essence [privatized](http://www.tomwbell.com/writings/JurisPoly.html).
1.6.2.3.8.2.4.1.1.1.1.1. Con: These laws always existed in addition to, not instead of, actual law.
1.6.2.3.8.2.4.1.1.1.1.2. Con: Commercial examples are effectively contracts, and as such require consideration not only for a person to be entered into the contract, but for the existing members of the contract to grant outsiders the protections of this contract, if these are not granted, abuse will happen.
1.6.2.3.8.2.4.1.1.1.1.3. Con: Mutual respect doesn't keep criminals from robbing one's house, murdering you, or just about anything else, it has no enforcement mechanism for failure cases, and relying solely on it would be a disaster.
1.6.2.3.8.2.4.1.1.1.1.3.1. Con: There are many thieves, but few murderers. Clearly there is at least some aspect of mutual respect common to humanity that means we rarely cause each other grievous harm.
1.6.2.3.8.2.4.1.1.1.2. Con: The warlords of Japan and Europe might have repelled foreign invaders, but they definitely killed, and took advantage of those they 'protected'.
1.6.2.3.8.2.4.1.1.1.2.1. Con: If warlords did not draw some profit from those they protect, they could not afford to offer that protection.
1.6.2.3.8.2.4.1.1.1.3. Con: There has also been someone that [tried to privitize public parking](https://www.npr.org/templates/transcript/transcript.php?storyId=412240643). Just because a thing can be privatized does not at all mean it is best for the public.
1.6.2.3.8.2.4.1.1.1.4. Pro: Roads have often been built or maintained by collective action, often in the form of toll roads.
1.6.2.3.8.2.4.1.1.1.4.1. Con: Toll roads are often disliked by the public.
1.6.2.3.8.2.4.1.1.1.4.1.1. Pro: Observation shows that putting a toll on a road reduces usage. See, for example: [Lindsey R. "Congestion Relief: Assessing the Case for Road Tolls in Canada." C.D. Howe Institute](https://cdhowe.org/sites/default/files/attachments/research_papers/mixed//commentary_248.pdf)
1.6.2.3.8.2.4.1.1.1.4.1.2. Pro: Protests against tolls are not uncommon. For example, in the first month of 2018, the following protests were reported: [Lekki Toll Hike Area Boys smash phones and cameras as Lagosians protest new fares](http://www.pulse.ng/news/local/area-boys-disrupt-lekki-toll-protests-in-lagos-id7959535.html), [Gurugram residents take to streets to protest against toll plaza](http://www.timesnownews.com/mirror-now/civic-issues/article/gurugram-residents-take-to-streets-to-protest-against-toll-plaza/190828), and[Bayside Group To Protest Gov. Cuomo's Congestion Pricing Plan](https://patch.com/new-york/bayside/bayside-group-protest-gov-cuomos-congestion-pricing-plan).
1.6.2.3.8.2.4.1.1.1.4.1.3. Pro: Political parties often try to win votes by promising to reduce \(or subsidise\) tolls. For example, in order to win support in Western Sydney seats, the NSW Labor Party, in Australia, committed the State to providing a cashback on tolls that has, so far, cost over $1.5 billion AUD.[Taxpayer bill for cashback scheme on Sydney toll roads hits $1.5 billion - and climbing](http://www.smh.com.au/nsw/taxpayer-bill-for-cashback-scheme-on-sydney-toll-roads-hits-15-billion--and-climbing-20161207-gt6hiq.html)
1.6.2.3.8.2.4.1.1.1.4.1.4. Con: When costs are concentrated and benefits are spread thin and wide, those carrying the cost are usually louder than those who enjoy the benefit, which distorts perceptions.
1.6.2.3.8.2.4.1.1.1.5. Pro: Power companies - like Edison's electricity company - have often been private, even when building substantial public infrastructure.
1.6.2.3.8.2.4.1.1.1.5.1. Con: Power companies such as Edison were dangerous monopolies that ignored public safety to the point of giving [the Dodgers their name](https://gizmodo.com/the-la-dodgers-got-their-name-from-brooklyns-deadly-str-1687077696), and [suffocating innovation](http://jawadonweb.com/?page_id=900), all while using public resources.
1.6.2.3.8.2.4.1.1.1.5.2. Con: The claim that "power companies have often been private" \(true though it is\) does not support its parent claim \("many service has been provided through collective action"\)
1.6.2.3.8.2.4.1.1.2. Con: The value of money is determined by the demand for it. A UBI lowers the people's demand for money and increases the government's demand for it, thus making people's money more valuable to the government via taxes. The less purchasing power the money has, the less valuable it is. Therefore, the government and businesses will be expected to provide more and better services with a UBI for the same price. A high marginal tax ensures a continuous supply of money for UBI disbursements.
1.6.2.3.8.2.4.1.2. Con: Yes but your con is being applied to the argument that taxes are unjust in a general sense, and does not take into account the context of the tax \(e.g., to pay for UBI\). Your claim is infact a pro to the above claim, not a con.
1.6.2.3.8.2.4.2. Pro: It could be equally claimed to be illegitimate to take income from A to help pay for B's security; for the courts used to enforce their rights; or for the use of other public goods that are available to all but are used disproportionately by some.
1.6.2.3.8.2.4.2.1. Con: Paying for someone's \(B\) security is legitimate only when B can't work for a living. Disproportional redistribution is the price we have to pay to know that we can count on help should we need it. It is like an insurance we pay for our comfort.
1.6.2.3.8.2.5. Con: In almost every UBI proposal, laborers stand to gain the most, and those looking for labor are provided freedom to seek it.
1.6.2.3.8.2.5.1. Con: This generalizes labor. Some laborers stand to 'lose' \(with higher marginal tax rates\) and some laborers stand to 'win' \(those who are at dead-end jobs to make ends meet\).
1.6.2.3.8.2.5.1.1. Con: Not many people are too concerned about slightly inconveniencing rich people, even if they are working rich people.
1.6.2.3.8.2.6. Pro: UBI may sound nice in theory, but any political theory which is developed without putting human nature into consideration can never successfully be employed. Given people's inherent selfishness, the better idea is to let people keep what they've earned as much as possible.
1.6.2.3.8.2.6.1. Con: Human nature includes both competitive and cooperative aspects as outlined by [game theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Game_theory). So while this statement considers the compete nature of humanity it fails to consider the cooperate nature of humanity as well.
1.6.2.3.8.2.6.2. Con: The notion of "letting people keep what they've earned as much as possible" has led to the macroeconomic situation for which we propose the remedy of UBI in the first place.
1.6.2.3.8.2.6.3. Con: Progressive taxation for the purpose of redistribution is being employed successfully nearly everywhere in the world.
1.6.2.3.8.2.6.4. Con: It may not be possible for people to earn much in the first place, even if they put in a lot of effort and time towards such an earning, due to random chance \(like getting hit by a car in winter or cancer\). A UBI helps soften these blows from random chance by providing at least some form of security to the populace.
1.6.2.3.8.2.6.5. Con: Part of "human nature" is the ability to reason, think rationally and change one's own mind; a capacity for improvement which could be used to accept and embrace a UBI, if indeed one wasn't part of the many who would already welcome and embrace such a thing.
1.6.2.3.8.2.6.6. Pro: As nature does not guarantee human beings subsistence without labor, a UBI is a violation of natural law and fosters complacency. This leads to social disorder.
1.6.2.3.8.2.6.6.1. Con: Nature supplies water and food without labor, it isn't a necessity in nature so UBI wouldn't violate any law. Theres no reason to think UBI would lead to social disorder behavior if those peoples life is provided by that same society.
1.6.2.3.8.2.6.6.2. Con: Humans already employ a wide variety of "unnatural" technologies and techniques in their day to day lives. There is no reason for humans \(or human societies\) to deliberately restrict themselves on the grounds of what is "\(un\)natural".
1.6.2.3.8.2.7. Pro: A UBI, like other welfare programs, involves many taking money from a few. The many have no right to take from the few just because they want it.
1.6.2.3.8.2.7.1. Con: The few don't have the right to take the amount of money they do from the many, that doesn't stop them.
1.6.2.3.8.2.7.2. Con: Capitalism is based upon expoitation of workers, as exploitation is the only way that a firm can make a profit \(see Marx\). Under our current laws, the few have the "right" to take from the many, but this is far removed from what is ethical.
1.6.2.3.8.2.7.3. Con: This assumes 'rights' are god-given, and not enshrined under law \(51% of people\) or Constitution \(67% of people\).
1.6.2.3.8.2.7.4. Con: There are many ethical and philosophical systems which believe the greatest good for the most people should be served as much as possible.
1.6.2.3.8.2.7.5. Con: 'Just because they want it' is not the principle upon which UBI rests. Rather, it rests on the principle that all economic surplus was created in part with some externalized costs \(unfairly\) borne by the populace as a whole. Thus, some of all wealth earned should be redistributed across the population in order to balance this inequality. UBI is a direct means to do this.
1.6.2.3.8.2.8. Con: In a world in which labor is replaced by capital \(machine, robots, etc.\) in production processes, an ever greater share of income is generated by machines. These can be taxed heavily without robbing people of their labor.
1.6.2.3.8.2.9. Con: If 'worked for' is a precondition to income, then inheritances should be outlawed. Earnings from dividends are also called into question, since it's the labor of others utilizing your capital that is creating the surplus - not your own labor. You would have to eliminate these forms of income \(and others\) to use this argument.
1.6.2.3.8.2.9.1. Pro: Investment and speculation falls into a similar "unearned" category, by this logic.
1.6.2.3.8.2.9.1.1. Con: Liberal ideology would suggest that investment returns are a result of risk managing.
1.6.2.3.8.2.10. Con: Workers are robbed of the fruits of their labor each time a profit is earned by a boss.
1.6.2.3.8.2.10.1. Con: Workers sell their time. By definition, no-one would buy that time, unless they can make a profit, so employment is most commonly a win-win exchange, not a win-lose exchange. This doesn't mean that employment cannot be exploitative, but profit is better understood as one party's share of benefits jointly earned. Even if that share is larger than would be fair, that still falls short of being theft.
1.6.2.3.8.2.11. Con: Society affords its members certain benefits. Taxes, to a degree, can be considered payment for these favorable circumstances provided to the individual.
1.6.2.3.8.2.12. Con: "A UBI robs people of their labor and gives it to those who haven't earned it" 
That's capitalism. The current winners are capitalists. Under UBI, the winners will be, on the whole, closer to 'everyone.'
1.6.2.3.8.2.13. Pro: If our economic system is inherently unjust, we should work on ways to improve how our economy rewards good behavior and punishes bad behaviour; rather than seeking to force a minimum standard of living in a way that decouples economic reward from productivity.
1.6.2.3.8.2.13.1. Con: The only way to have control over what our society deems worthy is through state intervention. This would lead to less freedom not more.
1.6.2.3.8.2.13.1.1. Pro: But if you believe that human freedom is a core ideal, than oneself should be the ultimate decider on what behavior contributes to society. A UBI would provide precisely that freedom to people.
1.6.2.3.8.2.13.2. Con: Seeking a minimum standard of living for everybody is required to comply with human rights and has nothing to do with economy.
1.6.2.3.8.2.13.3. Con: Not everyone will agree on what constitutes "good behaviour" and "bad behaviour", and to impose such standards would often lead to violations of freedom.
1.6.2.3.8.2.14. Con: Suggesting that people are only entitled to what they have worked for only produces a reasonable outcome in a society with no involuntary unemployment.
1.6.2.3.8.2.15. Con: This implies that the collective from which you receive benefit and a multiplier effect from has no right to charge for use of collective property. The property which allowed you to earn that income in the first place.
1.6.2.3.8.3. Con: The right to basic living conditions is a more important than the right to private property.
1.6.2.3.8.4. Con: We already do pay taxes to help support those who can't support themselves. This is just a different method of distributing that help.
1.6.2.3.8.5. Con: If a UBI was funded through taxation, it would be a legitimate government service.
1.6.2.3.9. Con: Permitting suffering and death due to poverty is cruel and immoral too. It should be weighed out against the cons of a UBI to see if it's worthwhile to have.
1.6.2.3.9.1. Con: UBI is economic suicide and will put more people in poverty when it quickly becomes unaffordable and the country defaults on its debt.
1.6.2.3.9.2. Con: Not all systems of ethics consider "allowing suffering" \(not helping\) to be immoral. [For more information see this article on supererogation](https://plato.stanford.edu/entries/supererogation/).
1.6.2.3.9.3. Con: Some systems of ethics consider "allowing suffering" \(not helping\) as less immoral then "confiscating goods" \(harming\). See for example the Doctrine of Doing and Allowing harm \(DDA\) [plato.stanford.edu](https://plato.stanford.edu/entries/doing-allowing/)
1.6.2.3.9.4. Pro: Under [consequentialist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Consequentialism) systems of ethics, permitting a death to occur through inaction may be considered morally equivalent to killing a person, because the consequences are the same.
1.6.2.3.10. Pro: -> See discussion #7734: Taxation is theft
1.6.2.3.11. Pro: Forced redistribution is in conflict with the right to the pursuit of happiness. If people are equal in this right, they cannot be forced to spend their time \(money\) for others just because they are rich or successful \(unless they do any harm to someone\). If the rich are forced to give money to the poor, then their pursuit of happiness \(they demonstratively choose wealth to achieve happiness\) is sacrificed for someone else's comfort.
1.6.2.4. Pro: A UBI reduces the stigma of social welfare, because the payments are distributed to everyone in society.
1.6.2.4.1. Con: Stigma is caused by a certain thing's association with low status individuals or groups: in a society with a mature UBI tradition, and perhaps in one where UBI is new as well, being known to rely on UBI would be just as shameful as relying on welfare is in the real world.
1.6.2.4.1.1. Con: Money is inherently fungible, so the source is readily obscured where necessary, while foodstamps are obviously foodstamps.
1.6.2.4.2. Con: A UBI will entrench stigmas against several social groups rather than remove them.
1.6.2.4.2.1. Pro: Stigma likely [arises](https://books.google.com.sg/books?id=SOPSL7y4cRYC&pg=RA1-PT121&lpg=RA1-PT121&dq=welfare+stigma+taxpayer+resentment&source=bl&ots=LKEKmQKU_Z&sig=1hRl3JJ3nS6Mv5H6GOjzM96EEDQ&hl=en&sa=X&ved=0ahUKEwiXrMzPosDQAhUOR48KHYepDzMQ6AEIRjAG#v=onepage&q=welfare%20stigma%20taxpayer%20resentment&f=false) from individuals being unable to work or being perceived as taking more from the collective resource pool than they give.
1.6.2.4.2.1.1. Pro: This perception is getting traction in states experiencing an electoral backlash against liberal values.
1.6.2.4.2.2. Pro: A UBI might add to anti-immigration sentiments, as citizens will likely be concerned with immigrants taking advantage of this system. This makes the lives of immigrants and perceived immigrants worse.
1.6.2.4.2.2.1. Pro: UBI guaranteed to any citizen creates greater economical inequality between citizens and non-citizens, be it foreign students, refuges or other migrants
1.6.2.4.2.2.2. Pro: The cost of financing incomes for migrants is difficult to justify politically. This is likely to cause migrants to be treated like second class citizens who will be further neglected by the lack of social institutions.
1.6.2.4.2.2.2.1. Con: UBI is a scalable system. It is scalable as long as the taxes paying for it balances out with the increase in people receiving it. Enough immigrants contribute to innovation and companies to pay the immigrants that don't.
1.6.2.4.2.2.2.2. Con: A large percentage of immigrants would not be economically dependent on the UBI due to the realities of most immigration systems, which give preferences to migrants with wealth, high levels of education, etc.
1.6.2.4.2.2.2.2.1. Con: Economically dependent or not - once accepted as citizens those immigrants would receive a UBI as well.
1.6.2.4.2.2.2.3. Con: Even where some immigrants are dependent on the UBI \(refugees, asylum seekers\) the overwhelming majority of all UBI payments are directed to local citizens rather than incoming immigrants.
1.6.2.4.2.2.2.4. Con: This perception leads countries to not accept immigrants when they are needed to provide labor. Not only is this bad for immigrants, but also the countries that need them.
1.6.2.4.2.2.2.4.1. Con: If someone comes to a new country to work, they will inevitably pay tax, therefore supporting the overall system, including a UBI. If that work finishes and they choose to remain, they should be  entitled to receive UBI themselves.
1.6.2.4.2.2.2.4.2. Pro: UBI on a national level would create a very exclusive nationhood, and could cause strong differences between citizens and non-citizens living in a country. Freedom of movement could suffer as xenophobia for social envy would arise.
1.6.2.4.2.2.3. Pro: -> See 1.5.3.6.4.1.
1.6.2.4.2.2.4. Con: A UBI is not likely to lead to anti-immigrant sentiment because it ensures that opportunities exist for all individuals, not just immigrants.
1.6.2.4.2.2.4.1. Pro: Xenophobia [is often a response](http://digitalcommons.unl.edu/cgi/viewcontent.cgi?article=1089&context=edpsychpapers) to a lack of economic resources and opportunity; thus a UBI will increase social cohesion.
1.6.2.4.2.2.4.1.1. Pro: Many pilot schemes have found that UBI [increases social cohesion](http://www.huffingtonpost.com/scott-santens/universal-basic-income-wi_b_8354072.html) through community empowerment and mobilisation.
1.6.2.4.2.2.4.1.1.1. Pro: Alaska's long-running UBI scheme has contributed to it having the highest rate of well-being of any state across indicators that [include social and community well-being](http://cdn2.hubspot.net/hub/162029/file-2513997715-pdf/Well-Being_Index/2014_Data/Gallup-Healthways_State_of_American_Well-Being_2014_State_Rankings.pdf?t=1437575309454).
1.6.2.4.2.2.4.1.1.2. Con: A UBI may only exacerbate existing trends in social cohesion. In relatively communal countries, it may build ties; [but in individualistic ones, it may actually increase the level of individualism](https://www.opendemocracy.net/transformation/max-harris/will-universal-basic-income-make-us-lonely).
1.6.2.4.2.2.4.1.1.3. Con: Providing all citizens with the means to be self-sufficient decreases the need for individuals to engage with the community through work.
1.6.2.4.2.2.4.1.1.3.1. Con: Even if citizens do not engage with the community through work, there are many other ways: local associations, volunteering, leisure activities, sport, local festivities, etc.
1.6.2.4.2.2.4.1.1.4. Con: Redirecting welfare spending from community services \(such as [soup kitchens](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soup_kitchen) or free healthcare clinics\) towards individual income supplements would erode much community infrastructure that builds ties between strangers.
1.6.2.4.2.2.4.1.1.4.1. Con: When every citizen has the means to pay for food, education or healthcare, some of these services are no longer necessary.
1.6.2.4.2.2.4.1.1.5. Pro: -> See 1.5.3.5.2.4.
1.6.2.4.2.2.4.1.1.6. Con: Pilots are not universal. Basic income works well for small populations that benefit from the 'hard' economy in the rest of the world, and in developing countries where income is very low.
1.6.2.4.2.2.4.1.1.7. Con: [The article](https://www.huffpost.com/entry/universal-basic-income-wi_b_8354072) is not about UBI schemes, it is about giving money to the poor people - with expectable result. Discussion about cost side of the problem is missing completely. People who are forced to give away their money might reduce social cohesion.
1.6.2.4.2.2.4.1.2. Con: Inequality is also supported by the [demonization](https://www.theguardian.com/books/2011/jun/08/chavs-demonization-owen-jones-review) and mischaracterisation of the poor by the middle and upper-classes. Those who do not lack opportunity and resources employ that tactic to maintain their superiority.
1.6.2.4.2.2.5. Con: Anti-immigrant sentiment will not occur because in order to gain the status of having a UBI, one needs to be registered either as a permanent resident or citizen of that government. Immigrants who do not pay taxes would not receive government benefits such as the UBI.
1.6.2.4.2.2.5.1. Con: Even if this is true, most immigrants strive to eventually achieve legal status, and subsequent citizenship benefits. This backlash still hurts them a lot.
1.6.2.4.2.2.5.1.1. Pro: For example, it likely harms efforts to grant visas and/or amnesty to illegal immigrants.
1.6.2.4.2.2.5.1.1.1. Pro: A UBI replacing current forms of welfare would make it harder for immigrants to become naturalized citizens.
1.6.2.4.2.2.6. Con: The economic benefits that migrants give by spending money in the system lead to far more benefits than their consumption of government expenditure costs.
1.6.2.4.2.2.6.1. Pro: Many undocumented workers pay tax despite not being eligible for many government assistance programs. On net they [contributed $12 billion to the US economy in 2010](https://www.ssa.gov/oact/NOTES/pdf_notes/note151.pdf).
1.6.2.4.2.2.6.2. Pro: Immigrants "[grease the wheels of the labor market by flowing into industries and areas where there is a relative need for workers](http://www.bushcenter.org/catalyst/north-american-century/benefits-of-immigration-outweigh-costs.html)" and generate rises in income for both migrants and citizens.
1.6.2.4.2.2.7. Con: The claim that immigrants rely on the system is contingent on the fact that these immigrants contribute nothing to the economy, which is false.
1.6.2.4.2.2.7.1. Pro: Once migrants have the chance to work, they would pay taxes as everybody else. In this sense, nobody could claim that they don't deserve a UBI.
1.6.2.4.2.2.7.1.1. Con: This perception leads countries to not accept immigrants when they are needed to provide labor. Not only is this bad for immigrants, but also the countries that need them.
1.6.2.4.2.2.7.2. Pro: The majority of [immigrants](http://www.migrationpolicy.org/article/what-immigrants-say-about-life-united-states) have and value a 'strong work ethic', which often means they work long hours and many jobs. Even with a UBI, this work ethic is likely to continue.
1.6.2.4.2.2.7.3. Pro: -> See 1.6.2.4.2.2.2.4.
1.6.2.4.2.2.8. Con: Most anti-immigrant sentiment comes from people who feel that their own economic security is threatened. If they did not have to worry about their own livelihood anymore, they would likely perceive other people living a decent life in their proximity as less of a threat.
1.6.2.4.2.3. Pro: Middle class individuals who imagine themselves to be hardworking will feel resentment at having a vastly increased tax rate to fund UBI payments for discriminated minorities.
1.6.2.4.3. Con: A UBI assigns a specific and clear monetary value to welfare, which feeds into classist rhetoric, making easier for some people to complain about wastefulness.
1.6.2.4.4. Pro: Those most in need are spared the degrading experience of pleading with welfare officers on a regular basis to demonstrate that they fit into specific categories.
1.6.2.4.4.1. Con: Given the harmful effects of poverty in general, having to justify your need \(even in a degrading way\) is far from the biggest issue facing the poor.
1.6.2.4.4.2. Con: It is often quite simple to prove that one is entitled to a welfare payout by providing proof of unemployment, or showing an insufficient income.
