1.6.2.4.4.3. Con: Communities are organized geographically based on socio-economic conditions and it is often an entire neighborhood that is on welfare. Proving one is part of a specific category is less degrading, because perceptively it is seen as a common experience, rather than a uniquely degrading one.
1.6.2.4.4.4. Con: We can likely tailor the current system to make admittance into a welfare program a more private affair done electronically or by mail.
1.6.2.4.5. Pro: Targeted welfare often entrenches Darwinian narratives about those in poverty, particularly where ethnic minorities are overwhelmingly overrepresented in those statistics. This increases discrimination in social interactions and also reduces political capital for useful measures.
1.6.2.4.5.1. Con: It is more realistic that targeted welfare entrenches the narrative that there are specific groups of people who are in special need of assistance, and that it is the state's obligation to assist these people, not that this assistance is a result of some kind of evolutionary deficit.
1.6.2.4.5.2. Con: Seeing which minority groups correlate with the type of, and need for, welfare, provides us with valuable data about how much inequality still exists in society. A UBI limits our ability to gain and use this data to fight said inequality.
1.6.2.4.5.2.1. Con: Government studies and surveys about income distribution across the population are still a powerful tool to determine inequality. Those studies can still be conducted with a UBI.
1.6.2.4.5.2.2. Con: A UBI may limit data that demonstrates inequality, but only to the extent that it actually reduces inequality.
1.6.2.4.6. Pro: Middle class voters are more likely to accept a tax hike for a UBI because they will actually see some return from this policy.
1.6.2.4.6.1. Con: If the tax hike is higher than the UBI payout, middle class voters are paying more than they are receiving, and will know that their money is going to people who may not even be working. This in turn reinforces stigmas against the poor.
1.6.2.5. Pro: A UBI would compensate people for the injustice created by the institution of private property.
1.6.2.5.1. Pro: -> See 1.6.2.3.8.1.
1.6.2.5.2. Pro: According to [Marxist thought](https://www.greenleft.org.au/content/capitalism-and-unemployment-%C3%A2%C2%80%C2%94-marxist-view), capitalism is predatory by nature and requires poverty. One cannot be on top if there is no one at the bottom.
1.6.2.5.2.1. Pro: Marxist economics suggests that unemployment is a [permanent feature of capitalism](https://www.greenleft.org.au/content/capitalism-and-unemployment-%C3%A2%C2%80%C2%94-marxist-view), as capitalism requires a permanent reserve army of labour. Unemployment is one of the [most common causes](https://www.borgenmagazine.com/unemployment-creates-poverty/) of poverty.
1.6.2.6. Pro: A UBI enhances the freedom and dignity of individuals.
1.6.2.6.1. Pro: Only a UBI can guarantee that every person is provided the basic amount of resources needed to survive.
1.6.2.6.1.1. Con: Alternatively, basic resources could be provided in-kind \(government-issued food, health care, shelter\). A UBI is not the only way to accomplish that aim.
1.6.2.6.1.1.1. Con: To be provided in-kind by the government would require a much higher cost due to the increased bureaucracy than simply giving people the money to do this themselves.
1.6.2.6.1.1.2. Con: This would limit the services able to be accessed by lower income people to the government supplied and would remove the free market advantages and personal choice
1.6.2.6.1.1.3. Con: This would provide a similar stigma to current government programs such as 'food stamps'
1.6.2.6.1.2. Con: Each person's basic amount of resources needed can vary, especially when it comes to healthcare. A person may still be precluded from required, costly medical treatments if the UBI is not high enough.
1.6.2.6.1.2.1. Pro: Funds from a UBI are not likely to be enough to cover the rising costs of healthcare or emergency visits.
1.6.2.6.1.2.1.1. Con: In developed countries people on low-incomes do not typically pay for their own health care. Even in the US there are medicaid provisions and the [costs of emergency visits are passed on](https://insight.kellogg.northwestern.edu/article/who-bears-the-cost-of-the-uninsured-nonprofit-hospitals).
1.6.2.6.1.2.1.2. Con: A UBI could increase in line with an index that tracks the cost of living \(including healthcare\).
1.6.2.6.1.2.1.3. Pro: Emergency visits in the US on average cost [$1,233](http://blog.bcbsnc.com/2014/04/5-emergency-room-myths-busted/). This would take a substantial amount from the monthly UBI payments to cover.
1.6.2.6.1.2.1.3.1. Con: The state is not covering the cost of this care for the vast majority of people under the status quo anyway.
1.6.2.6.1.2.2. Con: In many developed countries, healthcare is free at the point of need. In others, UBI would enable more people to afford health insurance. Even though UBI will not solve every problem, it may still be much more effective than current systems.
1.6.2.6.1.2.3. Con: The UBI money would still provide part of the healthcare cost, thereby making the rest more affordable. It would be much better than nothing.
1.6.2.6.1.3. Con: Supplying basic resources does not guarantee happiness or contentment.
1.6.2.6.1.3.1. Con: True, but a lack of basic resources guarantees ill health and intense stress that prevents someone from persuing the endeavours that do make happiness and contentment more likely.  See [Maslow's heirarchy of needs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maslow%27s_hierarchy_of_needs#Hierarchy).
1.6.2.6.1.4. Pro: Universal Basic Income would allow everyone the opportunity to survive regardless of their ability to work and/or skills, training, etc.
1.6.2.6.1.4.1. Pro: Without work for income, one must resort to begging or theft to survive. If one is unable to even do those things, they would surely perish.
1.6.2.6.1.5. Con: A UBI may only guarantee that [the citizens](https://basicincome.org/news/2016/09/reconciling-ubi-immigration-concerns/) of a country get the resources necessary for survival. The aim of welfare should be to provide every resident, including non-citizens, with the means to fulfill their basic needs.
1.6.2.6.1.5.1. Con: -> See 1.6.2.4.2.2.
1.6.2.6.1.6. Pro: UBI frees individuals from the constant worry of keeping their heads above water. This liberation from survival-mode encourages positive economic education, familial stability and increased joy. The psychic costs of a lifetime's survival  must be accounted for, and remedied.
1.6.2.6.1.7. Pro: A UBI will give the most vulnerable and disadvantaged people in society a little bit of housing security, which many have never had.
1.6.2.6.1.7.1. Con: This is more an argument for voluntary charity or means-tested social programs as it is for UBI, because extreme cases of poverty are relatively rare, and could be better dealt with programs aimed at that specific situation.
1.6.2.6.1.7.1.1. Pro: In the US, the number of chronically homeless individuals in 2018 was about [97k](https://endhomelessness.org/homelessness-in-america/homelessness-statistics/state-of-homelessness-report/). That's roughly 0,03% of the total population of [329 million](http://worldpopulationreview.com/countries/united-states-population/).
1.6.2.6.1.7.2. Pro: We are in "winner takes all" societies. Top performers capture a disproportionate share of the value created. In many professions, such as those related to arts, sports, or entertainment, there is a long tail of people which can't afford basic needs such as housing.
1.6.2.6.2. Con: A UBI would further erode individual liberty and responsibility, leading to more government control over citizens.
1.6.2.6.2.1. Pro: -> See 1.5.3.4.5.1.
1.6.2.6.2.2. Pro: -> See 1.5.3.3.1.
1.6.2.6.2.3. Pro: The incentives that a UBI provides to having children may lead to government-mandated breeding restrictions or forceful sterilizations to prevent certain groups from having children; a move that would harshly curtail [people's reproductive liberty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reproductive_rights).
1.6.2.6.2.3.1. Con: Even when security is provided the [birthrate stabilizes below the replacement rate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Income_and_fertility#Fertility_J-curve) independently of culture.
1.6.2.6.2.3.2. Con: Given existing welfare schemes often result in parents being criticised for having children \(and thus receiving extra assistance payouts\) a UBI is likely to dramatically increase this effect and potentially lead to greater disincentives to having children.
1.6.2.6.2.3.2.1. Con: -> See 1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.8.1.
1.6.2.6.2.3.2.2. Pro: A UBI could lead to population control, as there can't be even the most basic income for everyone if the population gets big enough.
1.6.2.6.2.3.2.3. Con: Planet Earth is overpopulated. A reduction in \(unsustainable\) birth rate in the mid-term could be considered an advantage of UBI, and could also allow room for population redistribution from more overpopulated regions.
1.6.2.6.2.3.2.3.1. Pro: Later on, birth rates may increase closer to the replacement rate again when Earth and humans are under less resource strain, and the need for more people is more apparent, as opposed to there being a general knowledge of the overpopulated state of the world among educated people.
1.6.2.6.2.3.2.3.1.1. Con: That there is "a general knowledge of the overpopulated state of the world among educated people" is a baseless assertion.
1.6.2.6.2.3.2.3.2. Con: Depopulating one country so that people from another country can spread into the depopulated area is a policy that's been tried before, and is usually not good for the receiving country - empires throughout history have done this, but the most memorable policy to have this aspect as a focus in particular was the German concept of [Lebensraum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lebensraum).
1.6.2.6.2.3.2.4. Con: UBI may incentivise population growth to an unsustainable level since it removes the need to provide for the basic necesities of children.
1.6.2.6.2.3.2.5. Con: Even if people would be highly criticised for having children if a UBI would be functional - it would rather lead to an increase in population, as extra money from the government for having children now leads to.
1.6.2.6.2.3.2.6. Pro: A UBI is likely to increase births. Having more children [increases](http://green.blogs.nytimes.com//2009/08/07/having-children-brings-high-carbon-impact/) a family's carbon footprint, particularly in developed Western countries. Thus any policies that encourage this should be avoided for the preservation and health of the planet.
1.6.2.6.2.3.2.6.1. Con: -> See 1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.8.1.
1.6.2.6.2.3.2.6.2. Pro: An uncontrolled fertility rate has already been the reason for governmental intervention, e.g. [China's One Child Policy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/One-child_policy), to contain the negative environmental and socioeconomic consequences.
1.6.2.6.2.3.2.6.2.1. Con: A fertility increment - which in many countries is necessary to insure the rate of natural increase \([RNI](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rate_of_natural_increase)\) - is not an “uncontrolled fertility rate”. Moreover, the attempts to control birth rates by forced and brutal methods such the policies implemented in China have had [disastrous](http://www.theaustralian.com.au/arts/review/chinas-onechild-policy-a-demographic-disaster/news-story/5a71127133138acec2644df76f01d9b0) demographical consequences.
1.6.2.6.2.3.2.6.3. Pro: -> See 1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.8.2.
1.6.2.6.2.3.2.6.4. Con: US birth rate versus income refutes this claim entirely. Well-off people [have fewer kids](https://www.statista.com/statistics/241530/birth-rate-by-family-income-in-the-us/) so UBI will reduce birth rates.
1.6.2.6.2.3.2.6.5. Con: While the claim that poverty is correlated, casually or not, with lower birthrates is dubious at best, even if the point is conceded, the solution to overpopulation surely cannot be opposition to proposals which aim to reduce poverty.
1.6.2.6.2.3.2.6.5.1. Pro: Increasing poverty - or deliberately not reducing it where one could - as a solution to overpopulation is not far removed from a "kill the poor" solution.
1.6.2.6.2.3.2.6.6. Con: This argument implies that any amount of human suffering is acceptable if it reduces the birth rate.
1.6.2.6.2.3.3. Pro: -> See 1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.
1.6.2.6.2.3.4. Pro: Since people can breed far beyond their means to acquire the resources of others, this may create resentment and a population beyond current capacities, leading to resentment and fights over resources. Since people are enabled to breed beyond their means they will then become a target for restrictions due to resource limitations or disruptions to quality of life from a surge of new children.
1.6.2.6.2.3.4.1. Con: -> See 1.3.3.5.2.2.1.2.3.1.
1.6.2.6.2.4. Con: A restructuring of social programming into simple cash transfers allow people to decide what the money is spent on, rather than bureaucrats deciding for them. This enhances individual liberty and autonomy.
1.6.2.6.2.4.1. Con: Aside from a minarchist system there is no form of governance where no negative rights are infringed. There can be no individual liberty when people are forced by the government to transfer money to others \(coercive redistribution\).
1.6.2.6.2.4.1.1. Con: Individual liberty must be protected by some organization at some expense, as there are many individuals who would otherwise be willing to infringe others' rights for their own benefit. Therefore, some form of government is necessary and essential for protecting individuals' rights.
1.6.2.6.2.5. Con: -> See 1.6.2.2.6.2.1.
1.6.2.6.2.6. Pro: There may be rules implemented in which an individual loses their right to their UBI based on certain circumstances or actions, making a tool to control behavior.
1.6.2.6.2.6.1. Con: Current social programs already do that. Current private contracts do that, as well.
1.6.2.6.2.6.1.1. Con: Just because other programs do this does not mean other programs ought to do it.
1.6.2.6.2.6.1.1.1. Con: It is probably wrong for existing programs, and individuals to exploit people, but this isn't a reason not to have a new program that might exploit people when all the existing ones definitely do.
1.6.2.6.2.6.1.2. Con: A UBI would be applied to a wider group, therefore it would affect more people and, by that lead to a greater erosion of liberty.
1.6.2.6.2.6.2. Pro: It is hardly a violation of due process for the government to regulate that of which is subsidises, i.e. the mandatory drug tests now [mandatory for those on welfare in Australia](http://www.abc.net.au/news/2017-08-22/welfare-recipients-could-face-drug-tests-as-early-as-january/8827288). The same draconian laws could come to apply to all under a UBI.
1.6.2.6.2.6.3. Con: That wouldn't be a Universal Basic Income, then, as it would lose the Universal aspect.
1.6.2.6.2.6.4. Con: The conditions imposed upon current welfare recipients \(drugs testing; forced budgeting\) would be unpalatable if extended outside the lower classes. Thus they are unlikely to ever be conditions of a universal benefit.
1.6.2.6.2.6.5. Con: In that case, it cannot be really called an UBI, it would just be another welfare program. UBI as a program is only relevant if it is truly universal. Even people in the prison system should be entitled to an UBI, even if they cannot use it while they are in jail. This would provide them with either a way to help their families during incarceration or small savings for when they leave the prison, reducing recidivism.
1.6.2.6.2.7. Pro: The UBI would extend the reach of a government to the economic affairs of citizens.
1.6.2.6.2.7.1. Pro: -> See 1.5.3.3.1.
1.6.2.6.2.7.2. Con: -> See 1.6.2.2.6.2.1.
1.6.2.6.2.7.3. Con: Having welfare programs actually puts the government in control of the citizens' lives, as welfare promotes doing nothing because people who work more, get less. With a UBI, people who work more, get more.
1.6.2.6.2.7.3.1. Con: It is not strictly true to say that people who work more get less under welfare programs. There are many jobs which pay more than welfare programs.
1.6.2.6.2.7.3.2. Pro: Welfare programs are designed to apply only to individuals with a specific income or less, which means that effectively, if a person on welfare is able to improve its income by only a couple of dollars above the cut, they will no longer qualify for welfare, eliminating a large portion of their income. This is an incentive for people to never to reach this point, effectively converting welfare into a way to limit the desire of people to progress.
1.6.2.6.2.7.4. Con: Citizens would continue to have the exact same power they have now: elect a new government that may or may not make changes to the UBI program.
1.6.2.6.2.8. Con: A UBI cannot erode personal liberty as it affords choice where none before existed. A UBI does not erode responsibility as it compels those previously without means to be responsible for utilising this new resource within their life.
1.6.2.6.2.8.1. Con: A UBI would be given to everybody, so it would also target those, who now have responsibility of earning at least a minimum of money to survive and erode that responsibility.
1.6.2.6.3. Pro: A UBI will reduce discrimination by empowering groups that traditionally lack influence due to economic barriers.
1.6.2.6.3.1. Pro: With a UBI, these groups now have the capital to receive an education and pursue high-skilled jobs. This will help remedy the lack of skilled professionals in minority communities.
1.6.2.6.3.1.1. Pro: Access to education and the ability to lift oneself from one class to another are more easily achieved when one has basic needs accounted for.
1.6.2.6.3.1.2. Pro: Minority doctors are more likely [to return to their communities](http://commonhealth.legacy.wbur.org/2012/02/minority-doctors-diversity) and service those who traditionally do not have access to medical care.
1.6.2.6.3.2. Pro: A UBI would improve the economic freedom of women.
1.6.2.6.3.2.1. Pro: Women would no longer have to [stay in abusive relationships](https://www.independent.co.uk/voices/domestic-violence-abuse-bill-theresa-may-financial-independence-a8260736.html) due to a lack of income.
1.6.2.6.3.2.2. Pro: UBI would allow for more equal responsibility in parenting as both parents would be able to live off UBI and so not be limited by the constraints of [2-week paternity leave](https://www.moneyadviceservice.org.uk/en/articles/paternity-leave-and-pay#:~:text=If%20you're%20an%20employee,to%20your%20normal%20holiday%20allowance) \(UK\).
1.6.2.6.3.2.3. Pro: A UBI can make a critical contribution to women's autonomy, security, and flexibility \([p. 14](http://www.basicincome.org/bien/pdf/montreal2014/BIEN2014_Regehr.pdf)(A basic income could make a critical contribution to increasing adequacy, autonomy, security and flexibility for women, men and families in order to improve their wellbeing, close many of the cracks through which people fall into poverty initially and enable them to rebound from setbacks. It can reduce risk for everyone and provide more options to women to balance the varied demands of their lives and help close gender gaps in income, work time and access to learning)\).
1.6.2.6.3.2.3.1. Pro: -> See 1.3.1.3.3.1.8.1.2.2.1.
1.6.2.6.3.2.3.2. Pro: The guarantee of a UBI reduces the risks from part time work, thus helping females enter and leave the work force as they need or want.
1.6.2.6.3.2.3.3. Pro: Unpaid care work, such as supporting younger and older family members, is often a [burden disproportionately affecting females](https://www.oecd.org/dev/development-gender/Unpaid_care_work.pdf).
1.6.2.6.3.2.3.4. Con: This can only be achieved by reducing the autonomy, security and flexibility of other women \(via increases their taxes\). To justify helping some women by harming other women is hypocritical.
1.6.2.6.3.2.3.4.1. Con: Given men earn, on average, more than woman the redistributive effects UBI would actually be a net benefit to women at the cost of men.
1.6.2.6.3.2.3.4.2. Con: Assuming proportionate taxation, the effect on taxpaying women will be far less significant than the effect on women who depend on or gain significant benefit from receiving a UBI.
1.6.2.6.3.2.3.4.3. Con: Taxes besides income tax can be used to fund a UBI, as can non-taxation methods of increasing government revenue.
1.6.2.6.3.3. Pro: Allowing people to take advantage of opportunities when they arise without being limited by the current rules and restrictions of a benefit that often has restrictive criteria, allows people to be socially mobile when the oportunity arises.
1.6.2.6.3.4. Con: Economic "empowerment" to the bare minimum of basic needs does not allow marginalized voices to be heard because those individuals are still at the bottom of the heap, where there is a lot of noise. A UBI does not eliminate poverty, it simply redefines it.
1.6.2.6.3.4.1. Con: By providing money for basic needs, a UBI would allow marginalized individuals and groups to spend more time and possibly even money on making themselves heard.
1.6.2.6.3.5. Pro: A UBI would give individuals the agency to invest in their own communities.
1.6.2.6.3.5.1. Con: The decision to invest in a community is a personal choice. A UBI does not guarantee the extra funds will be used towards renewing the community.
1.6.2.6.3.5.1.1. Pro: It is plausible an individual in a poorer community will use the extra funds from their UBI payments to move into a better quality community rather than to stay and make improvements.
1.6.2.6.3.5.1.2. Pro: For many individuals in poorer areas, they are surviving with minimal necessities. Funds from the UBI are more likely to go towards more urgent needs like food, shelter, and healthcare than the community at large.
1.6.2.6.3.5.1.2.1. Con: The fact that each poor individual is able to afford better food, better housing conditions or better healthcare will necessarily have a positive impact in the whole community.
1.6.2.6.3.5.2. Con: It would be more effective to invest in those communities directly.
1.6.2.6.3.5.2.1. Pro: This way, it is easier to know the money invested is going towards directly improving the quality of life within these communities. With the UBI an individual can choose to use the extra income for other personal interests unrelated to improving the community.
1.6.2.6.3.5.2.1.1. Pro: This investment can go towards better housing, educational programs, and community initiatives that help to uplift the residents as a whole.
1.6.2.6.3.5.2.2. Con: Hierarchical investment in communities driven by people that don't actually live with the consequences of those investment decisions are less likely to result in community changes that the people in those communities would actually value for themselves.
1.6.2.6.3.5.3. Pro: Increased amounts of free time will better allow individuals to invest their human capital in improving the community and helping other community members.
1.6.2.6.3.5.3.1. Con: It would be more effective simply to invest in those communities directly.
1.6.2.6.3.5.3.1.1. Con: Top down and hierarchical investment in communities, often driven by people that do not actually understand specific necessities, are less likely to result in valuable changes for the communities.
1.6.2.6.3.5.4. Con: A UBI would equally give people more time to engage in anti-social behavior, which is also supported by a UBI.
1.6.2.6.3.5.4.1. Con: This claim implies there is some inherent moral failing that applies to the economically disadvantaged and not to all other people with wealth. This is socially irresponsible.
1.6.2.6.3.5.4.2. Con: There is no evidence that suggests people are more likely to engage in anti-social behavior than they are in positive community building efforts.
1.6.2.6.3.5.5. Pro: People who receive a UBI are more capable of contributing value, through voluntary work for example, than those who do not have an income above the poverty line.
1.6.2.6.3.5.6. Pro: Having to rely on government investment in local communities is problematic.
1.6.2.6.3.5.6.1. Pro: Governments are often less capable of understanding where aid is most needed.
1.6.2.6.3.5.6.1.1. Pro: Diverting welfare funds to a UBI helps these people buy the goods that they actually need.
1.6.2.6.3.5.6.1.2. Pro: Governments often do not include members of these communities.
1.6.2.6.3.5.6.1.2.1. Pro: The impact of this is that even if politicians then wanted to help members of these communities, their attempts to do so would be inefficient at best, and actively harmful at worst, as they do not understand the needs and concerns of the people living in these areas.
1.6.2.6.3.5.6.2. Con: [The free rider problem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Free_rider_problem) makes individual investment in the community harder. The central, coercive, coordinating mechanism of the government is necessary to make community projects work.
1.6.2.6.3.5.6.2.1. Con: Free riding is vastly outweighed because people feel the urgency to change the unequal systems that currently exist.
1.6.2.6.3.5.6.3. Pro: People in these communities lack the resources to lobby their local officials for change.
1.6.2.6.3.5.6.3.1. Pro: They don't have the time or the money to engage in the political process.
1.6.2.6.3.5.6.3.2. Pro: Given that many of the poor are disenfranchised, the government ignores their situation and has no incentive to invest in impoverished communities.
1.6.2.6.3.5.7. Pro: With a UBI, investment within communities would better enable a process of organic change.
1.6.2.6.3.5.7.1. Pro: Individuals that live and work in these communities best understand the needs of these communities. As such, they are able to prioritize which areas to invest in first and how to go about investing in the community.
1.6.2.6.3.5.7.2. Pro: UBI policies shift the provision of welfare from a national level to a local level, where individuals are more capable of investing in their communities.
1.6.2.6.3.5.7.2.1. Pro: People are more responsible for investments they believe serve their own interests.
1.6.2.6.3.5.7.2.2. Pro: With money from a UBI, individuals within at-risk communities have the ability to invest in fixing urgent problems in areas the government has not helped \(e.g. [Flint, Michigan water crisis](http://time.com/4269300/flint-task-force-report-water-crisis/)\).
1.6.2.6.3.5.7.2.3. Pro: It is easier for individuals to petition local institutions and local leaders for change.
1.6.2.6.3.5.7.2.3.1. Pro: Local community leaders are closer and more reachable to people than national politicians.
1.6.2.6.3.5.7.2.3.2. Pro: Local leaders are heavily involved in the community, which makes them accountable not only to their community but also to themselves.
1.6.2.6.3.5.7.2.4. Pro: Leadership in local institutions is based on real abilities to manage money, instead of connections and nepotism \(which is usual at higher levels\). This happens because citizens have an interest in ensuring their community's success.
1.6.2.6.3.5.7.2.4.1. Con: Leadership of local government is often deeply unaccountable, due to the low stakes of the powers that they have, and the inability of media organisations to individually scrutinise each local government. As a result, corruption can flourish at a local level even when it would quickly be stamped out at a national level.
1.6.2.6.3.5.7.2.4.2. Con: Citizens have an interest in ensuring the success of their country, state, province or city, too. It is unclear why this would be stronger for very local government than for more powerful and higher-ranking government bodies.
1.6.2.6.3.5.8. Pro: The UBI provides individuals with more money to buy goods from local businesses. This leads to more money circulating in these communities and contributes to their economic revitalization.
1.6.2.6.3.5.8.1. Con: A UBI does not guarantee that individuals will use this additional money within their own communities. It is plausible that an individual will continue to shop online or outside of the community for certain goods and services.
1.6.2.6.3.5.8.2. Pro: Welfare policy often forces consumers to buy goods from non-local private companies that the government associates with.
1.6.2.6.3.5.9. Pro: When individuals invest more in their communities and buy goods from local businesses, they flourish and private investors are more willing to invest in them [\(Greenup 2015\)](http://www.geektime.com/2015/12/17/how-a-universal-basic-income-could-fuel-entrepreneurship/).
1.6.2.6.3.5.10. Pro: A UBI can be a great method to facilitate full expression of individual potentials especially in communities where there is a large disparity of control and ownership of commodity resources among the members.
1.6.2.6.3.5.11. Pro: Altruistic individuals would be more able to put forward charity or other altruistic forms of work without having to worry about their own maintenance or profitability.
1.6.2.6.3.6. Con: Empowering marginalised groups is contingent on providing them meaningful amounts of wealth. An income just above the poverty line is neither meaningful wealth nor economic inclusion.
1.6.2.6.3.6.1. Con: A UBI provides as a baseline an income below the poverty line; increase your income above poverty line would then be relatively easy to achieve — so long as you work to supplement your UBI. Basic income schemes are characterized by a low clawback rate, such that it would be challenging to have even a part-time minimum-wage job and still sit below the poverty line.
1.6.2.6.3.6.2. Con: A UBI ensures one's most important needs are accounted for in a stable manner. This is still incredibly meaningful for disenfranchised groups and enables far more political engagement.
1.6.2.6.3.7. Con: There could be an increased dependence of cheaper "non-citizens" to provide the labour force. Eventually leading to an elite/subordinate citizen/ non-citizen segregated society.
1.6.2.6.3.7.1. Con: Our moral values are strongly against such things, at least in the West. So it's unlikely to happen at least in the current Western nations.
1.6.2.6.3.7.2. Con: "Non-citizens" who provide labour could become citizens and therefore recipients of a UBI.
1.6.2.6.3.7.3. Con: A valid point but something that could easily be avoided by providing UBI to anyone living in said country. Today, in most industrialised countries there are welfare programs taking care of workers, earning too little to make a living, anyway. So this would be a different distribution pattern.
1.6.2.6.3.8. Con: Minorities will still be at a socioeconomic disadvantage.
1.6.2.6.3.8.1. Con: Although minorities might remain relatively disadvantaged, their socioeconomic position would definitely improve in absolute terms.
1.6.2.6.3.8.2. Con: If the government's actions are oppressing minorities, then those in power should not be allowed to decide how social spending dollars are allocated. A UBI can only remove decision-making power from any bigots in public office and allocate it to the downtrodden instead. If the structure is oppressive, then removing power from the structure helps the oppressed.
1.6.2.6.3.8.3. Con: If a UBI does not worsen the socioeconomic disadvantages of minorities, this is not a reason not to implement a UBI, as it can lead to other improvements.
1.6.2.6.3.8.4. Pro: -> See 1.3.5.5.7.4.
1.6.2.6.3.9. Pro: A UBI would improve the lives of the poor.
1.6.2.6.3.9.1. Pro: Low-income government-[subsidized housing](http://www.urbancure.org/washington/article.asp?id=563&t=Government-housing-is-a-bad-idea) often leaves at-risks families no choice but to reside in crime-stricken 'pre-existing ghettos', which puts their safety and livelihood at higher risk. With a UBI, families will have a choice of where they choose to reside.
1.6.2.6.3.9.1.1. Con: A UBI would most likely not be high enough for people to afford an opportunity to relocate to any chosen housing.
1.6.2.6.3.9.1.1.1. Pro: Even if it were, then if all residents in such a community receive UBI, then all have equal opportunity to leave. Because the demand to relocate increases, so does the cost, and the opportunity to relocate decreases.
1.6.2.6.3.9.2. Pro: Giving the poor a higher baseline income allows them to better participate in price signalling \(relative to the middle/upper classes\) and [thus better drive demand for inexpensive but high-quality goods](http://ordinary-gentlemen.com/2016/04/22/the-unconditional-basic-income-and-the-hayekian-price-system/) that improve their lives.
1.6.2.6.3.9.2.1. Pro: Conditional grants \(such as food vouchers\) are a mechanism that force consumer choice towards particular goods and services, rather than a market-driven aggregation of individual preferences. Allowing consumers more agency over how they acquire goods more efficiently directs the market to provide the goods and services that individuals actually value.
1.6.2.6.3.9.2.2. Pro: A UBI would facilitate a more democratic capitalist society by providing all citizens with sufficient funds to 'vote' for the companies they want to exist through the purchasing of products and/or services. This informed consumption would provide a greater flow of capital to companies that meet the wants of the greater population forcing companies to adapt or be replaced. This is in contrast to a wealthy elite defining the direction and ethos of companies and thus the economy.
1.6.2.6.3.9.3. Pro: A UBI will provide basic human needs and enable higher intelligence in the lower class. When people have time to think about other things than surviving the day, they can better think, evaluate their choices, and make long term decisions. Hunger, stress and insufficient safety result in a preoccupied mind.
1.6.2.6.3.9.4. Pro: Wealth is also a significant predictor of [civic engagement](http://www.people-press.org/2015/01/08/the-politics-of-financial-insecurity-a-democratic-tilt-undercut-by-low-participation/). A UBI will provide people from less advantaged backgrounds the agency and status necessary to push for the types of political change that would not be possible otherwise due to financial discrimination.
1.6.2.6.3.9.4.1. Con: A UBI ensures that our most important needs are accounted for in a stable manner. This is incredibly meaningful and comparatively leads to far more political engagement, even if not all recipients become dedicated activists.
1.6.2.6.3.9.4.2. Pro: Those on the poverty line have little energy to invest in the political process - outside of voting purely for financial reasons - due to work and life demands. By giving workers wage security \(and thus freeing decision-making processes such as voting from that economic incentive\) a UBI provides individuals room for potentially more thoughtful debate.
1.6.2.6.3.9.4.3. Pro: A UBI changes incentives for politicians and pressures them to more actively target or incorporate traditionally politically excluded and disenfranchised groups.
1.6.2.6.3.9.4.4. Con: They still only have a finite degree of political capital when engaging with elites. If wealthy people already feel as though they are contributing towards the UBI they are less likely to support these kinds of initiatives.
1.6.2.6.3.9.4.4.1. Con: The majority decides what's best for the society, not just the wealthy.
1.6.2.6.3.9.4.5. Con: NGOs and other special interest groups \(such as the NAACP\) are able to take on the burden of and fulfill this form of engagement.
1.6.2.6.3.9.4.5.1. Con: These groups cannot \(and do not claim to\) speak to all of the diverse interests and desires of their own constituency. To claim that NGO's and hierarchical organizations can by themselves compensate for the political voices of millions is naive and dangerous.
1.6.2.6.3.9.4.5.2. Con: These NGOs and advocacy groups are much stronger and greater political forces in a world where their constituents have more access to capital.
1.6.2.6.4. Con: One of the [psychological benefits](https://www.researchgate.net/profile/Peter_Warr3/publication/16154562_Psychological_aspects_of_employment_and_unemployment/links/564a2f8108ae295f644fbc44.pdf?inViewer=0&pdfJsDownload=0&origin=publication_detail) of employment is that it "provides a personal identity and status within social institutions. The employed person occupies a role, with associated rights and obligations which are not possessed by those out of work."
1.6.2.6.4.1. Con: This logic seems at face value like it would apply just the same to slavery. Slavery is obviously not desirable.
1.6.2.6.4.2. Con: The benefits of employment do not require that it be paid work. A UBI can enable people to do voluntary work for institutions or people who cannot afford paid staff, giving the workers the benefits of employment and providing value to society through their labour. Someone who cannot do voluntary work without risking losing their benefits is unable to provide value to society and gain the benefits of employment by volunteering.
1.6.2.6.5. Con: The fact that a system benefits some people is a weak argument in favour of it's morality. For example, if a person were to murder someone and take all their money, that would be objectively beneficial to that person, but it would ignore the effect on the other.
1.6.2.6.5.1. Con: Leaving people to suffer and die in poverty is also not moral behaviour, and taxing the mega wealthy likely wouldn't even change their personal lifestyles, merely affect their investments.
1.6.2.6.5.2. Con: Dignity and freedom are highly valuable and by UBI would be achived by taking away less important ones, therefore UBI is exchange from low value to high value, while mentioned example works in opposite direction.
1.6.2.6.5.2.1. Pro: UBI would be most probably achieved by higher taxation or decreasing spends on some other goods provided by goverment. Therefore it is exchange of possibility to keep all money you earn now \(by making this sum lower\) to freedom dignity etc. for those who are in greatest need of such values.
1.6.2.6.6. Pro: There are [societies which highly respect the elderly](https://www.huffingtonpost.com/2014/02/25/what-other-cultures-can-teach_n_4834228.html) even though they do not actively earn money and frequently may cause costs.
1.6.2.6.6.1. Con: People care for the elderly because the elderly have cared for and paid the expenses for current generations. One doesn't respect one's elders because they appeared out of nothing, it is because elders have paved the way.
1.6.2.6.7. Pro: A UBI can have a positive impact on societal practices and culture by acknowledging the inherent humanity of all citizens.
1.6.2.6.7.1. Pro: A UBI provides unconditional help to all people, while existing welfare systems have limited range.
1.6.2.6.7.1.1. Pro: Providing a guaranteed payment to all citizens promotes the idea that human life is intrinsically valuable and should be supported.
1.6.2.6.7.1.2. Pro: The current economic systems is extremely unfair, often because of corrupt management and lack of workplaces.
1.6.2.6.7.1.3. Pro: Placing conditions on the receipt of social benefits saps political support from the idea as it divides society into recipients and contributors. For example, Medicare in the US receives broad support because it is so broadly applied.
1.6.2.6.7.2. Con: The idea that you are owed money simply for existing is toxic. Your humanity guarantees you protection from your fellow humans and the state - negative freedoms. It does not permit you to force your fellow citizen to serve you.
1.6.2.6.7.2.1. Con: There is no such thing as a pure "negative right". All rights require enforcement, and all enforcement requires labor. Even a right to be left alone is generally considered to require the labor of an enforcing police presence.
1.6.2.6.7.2.1.1. Con: A "negative right" is defined by no one having to do anything in order to respect it, as opposed to a "positive right" that requires action in order to not violate it. Enforcement is an entirely different matter, as it only becomes relevant to guard against and punish violations of rights.
1.6.2.6.7.2.2. Con: This individualism is simplistic. Actions by groups of fellow humans and the state negatively affect humans inadvertently - most humans, for instance, cannot simply leave organized society and subsist off the land, and thereby are inevitably reliant on the state/society and other humans for their subsistence, due to past and present actions of the state and humanity as a collective. This arguably creates a duty from the state to humans in general.
1.6.2.6.7.3. Pro: A UBI makes money less important and puts the value in making life wonderful. We tend to translate everything into economic terms; distracting our attention to what its important. When one feels secure with UBI its less probable that people will get distracted by concerns of financial security. One can focus on doing things, even for free, and let life's rewards come without fear.
1.6.2.6.7.4. Pro: A UBI eliminates mankind's existential angst. Waking up every morning with no worries about how to maintain your life unleashes the best in people.
1.6.2.6.7.4.1. Pro: If the only thing ensuring initiative would be the drive to provide oneself's sustenance, then there would be no volunteer organisations.
1.6.2.6.7.4.1.1. Con: If the point implied above were valid, organizing a UBI through voluntary contributions to a non-government organization would be the superior solution.
1.6.2.6.7.4.1.1.1. Con: The point above showed a valid counterpoint, saying it's not strong enough to support a whole economic policy does not nullify it.
1.6.2.6.7.4.2. Con: One of Ghandi's 7 sins is [wealth without work](http://www.mkgandhi.org/mgmnt.htm). The reason for this is simple: that our lives are enriched by purpose and accomplishment. Helping someone over a hump is one thing but paying charity over time reduces the quality of life which is inhuman.
1.6.2.6.7.4.2.1. Con: -> See 1.2.9.4.4.4.
1.6.2.6.7.4.2.2. Con: This presupposes that those are virtues. However, these are merely tools that allow people to improve their living conditions or accomplish a goal when needed.
1.6.2.6.7.4.2.2.1. Pro: A UBI instead frees people to find other ways to self-improve by removing the forced correlation between being able to eat and submitting to a job.
1.6.2.6.7.4.2.2.1.1. Con: Too many people would instead take the opportunity to explore what they consider their undiscovered artistic genius. Combining a UBI with turnkey self-publishing could create a nation full of subpar poets and writers, indistinguishable from simple layabouts.
1.6.2.6.7.4.2.2.1.1.1. Con: Artistic works contribute to society by allowing not only enjoyment, but new ideas to spread. The inspiration thus created could lead to many other positive results.
1.6.2.6.7.4.2.2.2. Con: Different cultures have different perspectives on what is virtuous, so it is difficult to bring virtue into an objective argument.
1.6.2.6.7.4.2.3. Con: This argument is deeply entrenched in the virtue theory of morality, to the point of ignoring any of the other ethical frameworks. [Social contract theories](http://www.iep.utm.edu/soc-cont/), for example, suggest that it's none of your business what other people do as long as it doesn't effect you.
1.6.2.6.7.4.2.4. Con: Given Ghandi was a [deeply socialist political actor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gandhian_economics), the levels of 'wealth' he is referring here probably refers to the resources of the rich; not a guarantee by the state that everyone is able to live just above the poverty line.
1.6.2.6.7.4.2.4.1. Con: This is not an exclusive matter; "wealth without work" can easily refer to both scenarios.
1.6.2.6.7.4.2.4.1.1. Con: It seems much more plausible that Ghandi did not think of the state providing basic resources as a 'sin' given that was one of the policies he implemented and maintained.
1.6.2.6.7.4.3. Pro: A UBI will enable people to think about life's important questions more. The Earth and our human societies find themselves in the dire situations today, because we haven't given proper importance to the individual to answer these questions for themselves. Questioning life is what gets us out of this way of thinking, and is the beginning to a philosophical and spiritual education.
1.6.2.6.7.4.3.1. Con: Arguably, having an uninspiring and demotivating job makes us ponder these questions more than otherwise.
1.6.2.6.7.4.3.1.1. Con: Not for the majority of the population who live with minimum income to make their basic needs, they are more focused on surviving; hence not able to afford energy to improve society.
1.6.2.6.7.4.3.2. Con: There is no evidence that people will spend more time in contemplation of spiritual questions with a UBI. If a UBI is enabling recipients to partake in jobs and ventures that feel more meaningful for them, they may have less unstructured time.
1.6.2.6.7.4.3.3. Pro: For anyone whose primary concern is their own immediate physical and financial security for themselves and family, such personal need will eclipse any beneficial concern for community and habitat. UBI enhances the likelihood that most people will consider the common good.
1.6.2.6.7.4.3.3.1. Pro: Many people who work may wish to do more meaningful or charitable labour, but are aware there is less or no money in it in most cases.
1.6.2.6.7.4.3.4. Pro: UBI allows people to make more ethical decisions based on want and fewer coerced decisions based on need. Legally and socially criminal behavior for some is a choice but for many it is the only viable choice they feel they have to survive.
1.6.2.6.7.4.4. Con: UBI reduces worries about basic needs \(i.e shelter, food etc.\), but does not by itself deliver incentives to stretch for self-actualization \(i.e. following your passion\) rather than solely giving in to short-term driven urges \(e.g. playing video games for the sake of instant gratification\).
1.6.2.6.7.4.4.1. Con: An individual whose basic needs are met doesn't have to pursue self actualization, it is a personal choice. However if a UBI cannot make that individual seek self-actualization, neither can a job. So if nothing can truly make the individual want to pursue what's good for them, we can only reduce what prevents an individual from doing so.
1.6.2.6.7.4.5. Con: Existential Angst can also be a feeling of emptiness, or lack of purpose. If we accept [Maslow's Pyramid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maslow%27s_hierarchy_of_needs) of needs to be somewhat true, we can fathom that even if basic human needs are met, there will always be other needs. A UBI doesn't really extinguish that angst.
1.6.2.6.7.4.5.1. Con: Accepting some of Maslow's hierarchical conception of needs entails recognizing that some needs are "higher" than others. Higher needs that follow from the fulfillment of "lower" needs are not subject to feelings of emptiness or angst, as fulfillment of needs leads to climbing the pyramid toward self-actualization.
1.6.2.6.7.4.6. Con: No amount of money will eliminate existential angst. But, feeling worried about your economic future is an incentive to seek work and growth. In this way a UBI reduces the marginal utility of producing.
1.6.2.6.7.4.6.1. Con: Work does not necessarily lead to growth. It can simply lead to stress, exhaustion, frustration, desperation, hatred, etc.. Poor job fits — and people feeling like they have little other choice but to remain in them — can even lead to depression and suicide.
1.6.2.6.7.4.6.2. Con: If you pay attention you will see that one thing that every great past thinker had in common is that none of them was worried about surviving. That said each person has their own spectrum of what is "essential to survival."
1.6.2.6.7.5. Pro: A UBI sends a message of trust from government to citizens. Trust is usually responded to by accepting responsibility and willing to be trustworthy.
1.6.2.6.7.5.1. Con: Welfare programs also send a message of trust from governments to citizens, as they have to trust that their benefits will be dispersed monthly, the same as UBI has been proposed.
1.6.2.6.7.6. Pro: Current welfare systems incentivize the poor to value working over their physical and mental health. A UBI affords individuals more freedom to enjoy a greater quality of life.
1.6.2.6.7.6.1. Pro: A UBI will allow individuals the opportunity to take fewer work shifts, giving them more time to pursue other interests they hold valuable.
1.6.2.6.7.6.1.1. Pro: A UBI reduces the risks of working part-time or on a seasonal basis, allowing workers to better choose a schedule that suits their lifestyle.
1.6.2.6.7.6.1.1.1. Pro: Our time is the most important commodity we each possess; allowing us to better allocate it is important to self-determination and happiness.
1.6.2.6.7.6.1.1.2. Pro: The rise of [freelancing](http://www.afr.com/business/small-business/future-of-work-how-the-freelance-revolution-is-changing-the-meaning-of-a-day-job-20140508-jyw7q) and [casualisation](https://www.theguardian.com/business/grogonomics/2014/oct/17/the-casualisation-of-australias-workforce-is-nothing-to-panic-about) of the workforce is eroding the stability and financial security provided by full-time employment.
1.6.2.6.7.6.1.1.2.1. Con: Freelancing and casualization are indicators that people as a whole are now more capable of providing for themselves in the market than they were previously.
1.6.2.6.7.6.1.1.3. Pro: People's living expenses are relatively constant costs — rent, food, etc are all must be paid for on a regular basis. When people do not work consistently the need for 'income smoothing' becomes burdensome and risky as people must carefully budget for how future costs can be met with irregular incomes.
1.6.2.6.7.6.1.2. Pro: Fewer shifts allows more time for children, family, and ensuring proper health outcomes. This leads to the development of healthy and meaningful relationships that better both a person's physical and mental health.
1.6.2.6.7.6.1.2.1. Pro: Supportive healthy [relationships](http://www.health.harvard.edu/newsletter_article/the-health-benefits-of-strong-relationships) have shown to reduce harmful levels of stress that negatively affect the immune system.
1.6.2.6.7.6.1.2.2. Pro: A [75-year long study](https://www.inc.com/melanie-curtin/want-a-life-of-fulfillment-a-75-year-harvard-study-says-to-prioritize-this-one-t.html) came to the conclusion that good relationships are the most important factor to create sustainable and fulfilling happiness.
1.6.2.6.7.6.1.2.3. Con: These precise outcomes can be achieved through something like an earned income tax credit for low-income individuals, without necessitating a UBI.
1.6.2.6.7.6.1.2.3.1. Pro: An earned income tax credit is also a lot more economically feasible, as it [costs less than a high UBI.](https://www.vox.com/2016/7/16/11948800/robert-greenstein-basic-income)
1.6.2.6.7.6.1.2.4. Pro: Additional time spend with children can reduce mental health problems of future generations.
1.6.2.6.7.6.1.2.5. Con: Work could be done in family businesses or at least with help of children in many cases, which would not only be good for relation, but also for the economy.
1.6.2.6.7.6.1.3. Con: Incentives to work long shifts exist even under a UBI, and employers can design the system to their advantage. For example, employers might hand out promotions only to employees who work long shifts. Thus, low-income individuals are pressured into taking long hours in any case.
1.6.2.6.7.6.1.3.1. Con: Under a UBI, the vast majority of people will have the option to not work 12 hours a day, even when bonuses for long hours are in place. Thus, a UBI offers more individual choice, which is likely to be used by many workers, not just those with low-income.
1.6.2.6.7.6.1.4. Pro: Reduced working hours allow more time for educational pursuits like earning a degree or taking classes in a subject of interest.
1.6.2.6.7.6.1.4.1. Pro: With a UBI, people will have more income to easily afford more enjoyable educational experiences, like studying subjects within their interests or learning through world exploration.
1.6.2.6.7.6.1.4.1.1. Pro: People are more likely to have better lives if they have better access to education and enriching experiences than if they spend their whole lives trying to maximize their productivity in a capitalistic sense.
1.6.2.6.7.6.1.4.2. Pro: High and specialized education becomes increasingly important as more and more jobs are being replaced by machines, especially the lower-skilled jobs.
1.6.2.6.7.6.1.4.2.1. Pro: -> See 1.3.4.2.2.2.1.1.3.
1.6.2.6.7.6.1.4.2.2. Con: Moral-panic around increased mechanisation of the labour force has existed since before the industrial revolution, yet never actually come to fruition.
1.6.2.6.7.6.1.4.2.2.1. Pro: Overwhelmingly, technological advancements lead to job creation in other fields, due to increased net demand as a product of economic growth.
1.6.2.6.7.6.1.4.2.2.2. Con: This is not correct. [Current trends](http://www3.weforum.org/docs/WEF_FOJ_Executive_Summary_Jobs.pdf) could lead to a net employment impact of more than 5.1 million jobs lost to disruptive labour market changes over the period 2015–2020, with a total loss of 7.1 million jobs.
1.6.2.6.7.6.1.4.2.2.2.1. Con: The very same study suggests that these losses can be offset by a ['talent revolution'](http://www3.weforum.org/docs/WEF_FOJ_Executive_Summary_Jobs.pdf) whereby strong leadership from government and business elites leads to job creation in new fields.
1.6.2.6.7.6.2. Pro: Society and government undervalue health and family life and instead [overvalue working](https://politicsandculture.org/2012/05/02/leisure-the-sacred-gesture-and-human-dignity-thorstein-veblen-and-josef-piepers-understandings-of-leisure/). We begin to value the quality of our own lives when we are not working solely out of necessity.
1.6.2.6.7.6.2.1. Pro: Under capitalism, the meaning of [leisure](https://politicsandculture.org/2012/05/02/leisure-the-sacred-gesture-and-human-dignity-thorstein-veblen-and-josef-piepers-understandings-of-leisure/) has been undermined by what is considered its opposite: work. Leisure is not merely free or unoccupied time, but rather time spent thoughtfully at your own discretion.
1.6.2.6.7.6.3. Con: If low-income people start substantially reducing the amounts they work, this is likely to trigger substantial political backlash to the UBI \(as low-income individuals are perceived to be leeching off of society\), which will result in reductions in the UBI and increased stigma against low-income workers.
1.6.2.6.7.6.3.1. Con: It requires a lot more political capital to reduce UBI payments \(because that affects everyone\) than to cut welfare entitlements.
1.6.2.6.7.7. Pro: United Nations' [Declaration of Human Rights](http://www.un.org/en/universal-declaration-human-rights/) Article 25 states that "Everyone has the right to a standard of living adequate for the health and well-being of himself and of his family ..." In a world of increasing job scarcity and rising job specialization, requiring fewer workers, UBI could be the only sustenance for hundreds of millions of people.
1.6.2.6.7.7.1. Con: Recognising the 'right' to something doesn't mean that everyone has to be provided with it.
1.6.2.6.7.7.1.1. Pro: In some situations it's impossible to answer even most crucial needs.
1.6.2.6.7.7.1.1.1. Pro: There are places where high standard medical care is unavailable.
1.6.2.6.7.7.1.1.1.1. Con: A UBI will help to fund the purchase of medical services in such areas, thus incentivizing their provision and sale.
1.6.2.6.7.7.1.1.2. Pro: There are places where access to food and water is highly limited.
1.6.2.6.7.7.1.1.2.1. Con: A UBI will help to fund the transport and sale of food and water to such locales.
1.6.2.6.7.7.1.1.3. Con: The occurrence of such situations should be reduced, and a UBI is one possible vehicle to do so.
1.6.2.6.7.8. Pro: UBI is fair because those who work also get help, not only the ones who don't contribute to society.
1.6.2.6.7.8.1. Con: A UBI would help only those, who earn less than average, because it would be financed from taxes, so those who earn more would pay more for UBI to exist, than they recieve from it.
1.6.2.6.7.8.1.1. Con: This threshold to receive a net benefit would likely be much higher than just the average income level, given progressive taxation means that the most wealthy continue a disproportionate amount of tax revenue.
1.6.2.6.7.8.1.1.1. Con: Such tax would have to be highly progressive to function properly, because rich people have lawyers, who help them to minimise the amount of tax they pay.
1.6.2.6.7.8.1.1.1.1. Pro: Some of most richest would decide to move or at least pay taxes somewhere else.
1.6.2.6.7.8.1.1.2. Con: A highly progresive tax, which would be needed to support such option, would hurt the economy significantly by reducing the motivation to earn \(and by that produce\) more of those who provide huge part of GDP.
1.6.2.6.7.8.2. Con: A UBI would hurt richest, who provide significant input into economic development.
1.6.2.6.7.8.2.1. Con: Wealthy individuals are not the only source of input for economic development; collective projects and large-scale voluntary donations can also finance development, as can governments.
1.6.2.6.7.8.2.2. Con: Many wealthy people act against the common good, creating more damage than improvement.
1.6.2.6.7.9. Pro: The economic consequences of wealth redistribution must be seen in philosophical and material terms. UBI is a humanist approach: supporting people because they exist. Against this is a materialist approach dehumanizing people to be mere earners and not able to appreciate life's demands to advance oneself.
1.6.2.6.7.10. Pro: A UBI at the least should be considered a human right, as there are not near enough job opportunities to earn more than a basic amount of income.
1.6.2.6.8. Pro: A UBI provides people the [real freedom](https://www.jstor.org/stable/2265291?seq=1#page_scan_tab_contents) to pursue the realization of one's conception of the good life.
1.6.2.6.8.1. Con: A person who wants to laze on a beach can only be fed by taking from some other source of income. This is not enough to justify a UBI as a way to redistribute revenue.
1.6.2.6.8.2. Pro: The adoption of a UBI could help to change the narrative that working full-time is the only way to lead a fulfilling life, which will become important as that becomes increasingly unnecessary and/or inefficient.
1.6.2.6.8.2.1. Con: It is false to equivocate acceptance of technological advances with acceptance of a post-work world. As new industries unearth new market needs and generate new wants, new jobs in industries arise to replace those lost to obsolescence.
1.6.2.6.8.3. Pro: With a UBI, people will not be forced to settle for a job that only pays the bills, but will be free to explore career paths until they find a meaningful and fulfilling occupation.
1.6.2.6.8.3.1. Pro: A UBI gives the individual more freedom. It allows people to decide how to take part in society. There are no strings attached. People can decide whether they want to work or not and they would be urged to do the things they like and that would create happiness.
1.6.2.6.8.4. Pro: A UBI would allow people to focus on jobs that they are passionate about rather than simply monetary gain, and thus would improve mental health, job and life satisfaction, self-value and contribution to society.
1.6.2.6.8.4.1. Pro: This would also improve their effectiveness and productivity at work, due to increased effort and motivation.
1.6.2.6.8.4.2. Pro: An unconditional UBI separates work from income, so that you can do whatever you are best in - and not, what you have to do because somebody is willing to pay for it.
1.6.2.6.8.4.3. Con: If the contribution to society is one of value, then the associated job comes with an equally valuable salary. If the contribution to society isn't valuable, then society shouldn't subsidize it.
1.6.2.6.8.5. Pro: -> See 1.1.5.3.1.3.6.4.2.4.
1.6.2.6.8.6. Pro: A UBI would give the hardworking person a chance to work for something the person really believes in, rather than being a slave to someone else's project. This leads to a more satisfied world.
1.6.2.6.8.7. Pro: People who prefer to laze on beaches should not be compelled to work in order to feed themselves. The same goes for people who would opt for a part-time job, or take up volunteering, or start up a new business. A UBI [could provide everyone](https://www.jstor.org/stable/2265291?seq=1&Search=yes&searchText=fed&list=hide&searchUri=%2Fbetasearch%2F%3Facc%3Don%26Query%3Dwhy%2Bsurfers%2Bshould%2Bbe%2Bfed%26fc%3Doff%26wc%3Don&prevSearch=&resultsServiceName=null&loggedin=true#page_scan_tab_contents) with a minimal sum that would support them in living the life they want to live.
1.6.2.6.8.7.1. Con: Most people prefer engaging in recreational activities to doing work. However, if all of these people chose not to work, society would not have enough productive labor to function.
1.6.2.6.8.7.2. Con: Freedom means choosing a path without being coerced by others, and accepting the consequences of one's actions. People who laze on beaches do not produce anything, yet a UBI would allow them to avoid the natural consequences: not being able to consume.
1.6.2.6.8.7.2.1. Con: Not entirely true: they would have less resources to consume than those who choose to engage in labour in order to fund additional consumption. "Lazing on a beach" does not in fact consume resources — it is an economically neutral activity.
1.6.2.6.8.8. Pro: A UBI can help individuals self-actualize and express themselves creatively. Being able to do so is a basic human right.
1.6.2.6.8.8.1. Pro: As set forth in many articles of the [Universal Declaration of Human Rights](http://www.un.org/en/universal-declaration-human-rights/), every human being has a right to certain freedoms, including leisure, expression, human dignity, and development of personality. A UBI makes this more obtainable for everyone.
1.6.2.6.8.8.2. Pro: Working with an exclusive materialistic intention is sad as it makes humans subject to alienation.
1.6.2.6.8.8.3. Pro: The philosophy that guides a UBI is that a life free of poverty is a universal right, not a privilege earned through working.
1.6.2.7. Con: A UBI does not do enough to address the individualism which is at the core of class disparity. As long as capitalism exists so does profit, and profit creates power dynamics which allow for the perpetuation of individualistic ideologies.
1.6.2.7.1. Con: Capitalism, while far from perfect, has a lot going for it in terms of encouraging production and innovation. While it might be possible to replace it once we move into a post-scarcity society, at present the most efficient economic systems available are at least partially capitalistic.
1.6.2.7.1.1. Con: in many cases, scarcity, [is manufactured](https://eand.co/why-artificial-scarcity-is-killing-you-d19ff364c812).
1.6.2.7.1.1.1. Con: The term "post-scarcity economy" refers to the distinction in economics between scarce and nonscarce goods; artificial scarcity that increases the price of a good that *already has a price* is a different sort of scarcity, and it is disingenuous to conflate them.
1.6.2.7.2. Con: From an individualist perspective, the fact that a UBI does not "address" individualism is a good thing, and a policy that did so would just be trampling on individuals' rights and liberties in the well-worn name of "the greater good", like so many anti-capitalists [have done before](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mass_killings_under_communist_regimes).
1.6.2.8. Con: Providing work is a better vehicle to promote social justice than providing a UBI.
1.6.2.8.1. Con: Even if this is true, it may be much harder to provide work for all. On balance, therefore, a UBI is preferrable to help all people.
1.6.2.8.2. Pro: UBI does not give people meaning in their lives the way that work does.
1.6.2.8.2.1. Con: There are plenty of people whose jobs do not give their lives meaning.
1.6.3. Pro: A UBI will benefit the middle class, by increasing median household income among the middle class.
1.6.3.1. Con: Resource distribution in society will always be unequal. A UBI or any other welfare scheme does not alter this fundamental reality.
1.6.3.1.1. Con: -> See 1.3.5.2.1.
1.6.3.2. Pro: Government-funded research and education is a key driver of innovation, yet its returns mostly accrue to the owners of capital. By helping to circulate wealth from the rich to the lower and middle classes, a UBI helps recapture this public investment for the public itself.
1.6.3.2.1. Con: Those who are wealthy already benefit from technological advancement as they are the ones who own the means of production. The government does not also need to provide them with a UBI, that is just double dipping.
1.6.3.2.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.6.2.1.2.
1.6.3.2.1.2. Con: -> See 1.3.5.4.1.2.1.2.
1.6.3.3. Pro: [Alaska, the only state with a UBI](https://qz.com/1205591/a-universal-basic-income-experiment-in-alaska-shows-employment-didnt-drop/), has the [highest median household income for middle class](https://www.cnbc.com/2017/03/24/heres-how-much-money-middle-class-families-earn-in-every-us-state.html). Alaska's middle class make $80,000 per year, while middle class in all other states make less — up to $10,000 less.
1.6.3.4. Pro: Unlike current welfare schemes which only benefit the most disadvantaged in society, a UBI will benefit almost everyone by taxing those who have accumulated large amounts of wealth over a long period of time.
1.6.3.4.1. Pro: The income gap continues to increase between the wealthiest 1% and the rest of the population. A UBI would not only help close the gap, but would also all but eliminate the constant debate of a liveable minimum wage we've been having for over a decade.
1.6.4. Pro: A UBI would improve working capabilities and conditions.
1.6.4.1. Con: -> See 1.2.
1.6.4.2. Pro: Employers will no longer be forced to play the role of social security guarantors. Therefore, employing people will be [easier, cheaper](https://www.businessinsider.com/companies-dont-like-having-full-time-employees-2016-6?r=US&IR=T) and therefore more accessible.
1.6.4.3. Con: UBI [relatively disadvantages](https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/16255446) the [working segment of a society](https://dictionary.cambridge.org/dictionary/english/working-class), since it will be payed out from their taxes.
1.6.4.3.1. Pro: Freedom can be seen as the absence of enforcement. A UBI requires the enforcement of taxation, which by definition, impairs freedom.
1.6.4.3.1.1. Pro: The additional purchasing power that net beneficiaries gain from UBI has to be taken from those who are net payers. The latter group's freedom is reduced because they have less control over the fruits of their labor.
1.6.4.3.1.1.1. Con: For most state's considering implementing a UBI, a social welfare system in already in place. If those systems are replaced with a UBI, the net payer's group freedom is not reduced any more than it already is. And the beneficiaries gain the freedom to choose how their UBI is spent.
1.6.4.3.1.1.1.1. Con: This is only true if taxation stayed the same as without UBI. However, most UBI models require substantial increase in spending compared to the current welfare state, and thus would require more taxation.
1.6.4.3.1.1.1.1.1. Pro: Tax reforms to support a UBI would need to be highly progressive and focus on taxing luxury items.
1.6.4.3.1.1.1.1.1.1. Con: This could be argued to be a desirable and effective way for the tax system to operate, as it would tax the wealthy who could afford it to support those who are in need for essentials.
1.6.4.3.1.1.2. Con: There is no such thing as the isolated fruits of ones labour. Is buying shares on the stock market not a labour comparable to someone making minimum wage on 60 hours a week cleaning toilets? Society is contract, not a mass of individual interest.
1.6.4.3.1.1.3. Con: These individuals often rely on a market of consumers. The UBI would act as an economic stimulant that would benefit them too, in the long run.
1.6.4.3.1.1.4. Con: Net tax payers still owe society money in exchange for having an educated population that consumes their goods/services.
1.6.4.3.1.1.5. Con: This argument assumes that 100% of the production comes from human activities. This has already changed with massive automation and will change further with more and more AIs in charge of production. In that case, the "net payers" would not be humans, but automated systems and therefore there would be no loss of freedom for human actors.
1.6.4.3.1.2. Con: Not being able to afford a decent quality of life, often due to life circumstances not reasonably within your control, is not a dignified way for a human to exist.
1.6.4.3.1.3. Con: Spending the majority of your time merely trying to survive in an unfair \(and stressful\) financial situation does not offer many opportunities for freedom.
1.6.4.3.1.4. Pro: The only way to guarantee one person an income is to take from another through coercive force. Government is the only legitimate user of physical force. It should only be used to protect, not to take. To believe it is okay is to not believe in moral absolutes. In other words, anything is okay that one deems so within their conscience based on their subjective views, rather than the objective facts of reality.
1.6.4.3.1.4.1. Con: Deontological rules such as "Government is the only legitimate user of physical force" are subjective views about how morality works, not objective facts of reality.
1.6.4.3.1.4.1.1. Con: While true, by that logic no moral argument \(or very few anyway\) is valid. Just looking at Deontological, Consequentialist, and Virtue ethics theories, it will be nearly impossible to get all three views to agree on any issue. Therefore since you can't convince them all, you shouldn't try.
1.6.4.3.1.4.1.1.1. Con: That you should try is something most moral systems can agree on: It is virtuous to try to convince people to be virtuous, convincing people to do good things is a good thing, and convincing people to maximise utility helps to maximise utility.
1.6.4.3.1.5. Pro: The State is unethical, because it's existence is based on the illegitimate monopoly of coercion, and its source of income is based on theft \(taxes\).
1.6.4.3.1.5.1. Con: Most people prefer living in a stable and orderly society to anarchy where force or coercion may be used by any individual against any individual and individual rights do not exist or are not enforced.
1.6.4.3.1.6. Con: Defining 'freedom' as the 'absence of enforcement' is intrinsically problematic. This is, essentially, an anarchistic look at freedom. Lack of enforcement only works if everyone can be relied upon not to take advantage of the lack of enforcement, which in turn rewards those who DO take advantage of the lack of enforcement.
1.6.4.3.2. Pro: It is immoral to legislate that citizens who work hard for their pay, forfeit a portion of it to other citizens who didn't.
1.6.4.3.2.1. Con: -> See 1.6.2.3.8.4.
1.6.4.3.2.2. Con: Not helping the poor is immoral. A UBI helps the poor and does it more efficiently and more gracefully so than welfare schemes.
1.6.4.3.2.2.1. Con: There are ways to help the poor without a UBI or welfare scheme, for example free-will charity. The poor are helped, no one is forced to give. Trading one immorality \(not helping the poor\) for another \(UBI\) when a solution exists that is completely moral \(charity\), is immoral in itself.
1.6.4.3.2.2.1.1. Con: Having free-will charity help the poor on the scale that UBI would is not, in fact, a solution that exists. It's impossible to make anyone voluntarily give to charity, because that's a contradiction in terms; no-one is personally rich enough to solve poverty by their philanthropy; and just hoping people will give to charity sufficient that UBI will be unnecessary is wishing you had better moral luck than you, in fact, have, not offering a solution.
1.6.4.3.2.2.1.1.1. Con: It's not been established that a UBI actually is necessary to begin with, so currently charity wouldn't have to meet that level.
1.6.4.3.2.2.1.2. Con: Charity has not, to date, managed to solve the poverty problem.
1.6.4.3.2.2.1.3. Con: [Charity is a power play](https://fee.org/articles/when-charity-is-actually-a-power-play/); the UBI gives the needy the power to help themselves.
1.6.4.3.3. Con: -> See 1.6.2.3.8.4.
1.6.4.4. Pro: With the insurance of a UBI acting as a safety net, workers can challenge their employers if they find their working conditions unfair or degrading.
1.6.4.4.1. Pro: This serves as a check against the leverage typically held by employers. They now have a greater economic reason to ensure that their workers are satisfied.
1.6.4.4.1.1. Pro: UBI gives workers more power at the negotiating table. The choice is "work or don't have extra benefits" instead of "work or starve."
1.6.4.4.1.1.1. Pro: Currently many workers hesitate to speak up for fear of losing their jobs and their family's livelihoods.
1.6.4.4.1.1.2. Pro: UBI allows less skilled workers to negotiate pay and working conditions with employers fairly, rather than from a position of desperation.
1.6.4.4.1.2. Pro: -> See 1.6.4.4.1.1.2.
1.6.4.4.1.3. Con: Everyone but the most poor have adapted to living beyond the meagre means offered by a UBI. This means that the income that an employer offers still affords them a large amount of power over employees.
1.6.4.4.1.3.1. Con: They can adapt to living on less just as much as they've adapted to living on more.
1.6.4.4.1.3.2. Con: Even so, the threat of losing your luxuries is not as grave as the risk of losing provision for your basic needs, or having to rely on welfare system that are not always certain or reliable, and often stressful or stigmatizing.
1.6.4.4.1.4. Pro: Many workers stay in jobs with unhealthy working conditions due to a tight job market. A UBI can alleviate this stress.
1.6.4.4.1.4.1. Pro: When workers are more able to leave bad jobs it leads to bad employers having persistently high turnover rates. This creates more effective pressure on the employer to deal with their problematic power dynamics and mitigate stressful working conditions.
1.6.4.4.2. Con: New ways of maintaining the power imbalance between the employer and the employee will be found.
1.6.4.4.2.1. Pro: The imbalance of power serves to increase productivity, as hierarchical management serves to guide and govern workers with a centralized system of rules and goals.
1.6.4.4.2.1.1. Con: It is morally abhorrent to use the threat of making someone jobless as a negotiating tool.
1.6.4.4.2.1.2. Con: The imbalance of power could also create antagonistic relations between employee and employer, which diminish productivity.
1.6.4.4.2.1.3. Pro: [Proper management techniques](http://www.forbes.com/sites/work-in-progress/2014/01/20/8-common-causes-of-workplace-demotivation/2/#390e499cf401) like rewarding success and addressing low performance increases overall motivation and productivity.
1.6.4.4.2.1.4. Con: This argument sounds very similar to the one used to justify slavery. Slavery is wrong.
1.6.4.4.2.2. Con: The power inbalance would be shifted towards the worker with a UBI, any countereffect on behalf of the employers is a seperate issue that could be argued in chicken-egg like fashion as the countereffect to the countereffect takes place.
1.6.4.4.2.3. Con: It would still increase the difficulty for the employer in maintaining said power imbalance, which is beneficial for the employees.
1.6.4.4.3. Pro: UBI could help make employing and being employed more voluntary. If employment is driven by grave need, it becomes voluntary only in name.
1.6.4.4.3.1. Pro: There cannot be a free labour market without a UBI. When labour sellers are forced under pain of starvation to sell their labour to any available labour buyer there is no free choice and no free market. The labour buyers have no such urgency.
1.6.4.4.3.1.1. Con: This ignores the various government safety nets that already exist, such as means-tested welfare \(e.g. unemployment benefits\). In short, UBI is not the only option for enabling greater job freedom.
1.6.4.4.4. Pro: A UBI would make wages depend less on bargaining power, and more on skill requirements and effort.
1.6.4.4.5. Con: -> See 1.6.4.4.3.1.1.
1.6.4.4.6. Con: Walking away is often too little, too late. For example, an unsafe workplace is often only exposed through serious accident. Proactive government intervention is better than retroactive employee action.
1.6.4.4.6.1. Con: Proactive government intervention can still continue even if a UBI is implemented.
1.6.4.5. Pro: A UBI would allow people to take the time to find a better job.
1.6.4.5.1. Pro: Having this time to find suitable work will stop the "revolving door" effect, where people on benefits are forced to take a job, hate it, and quit shortly thereafter.
1.6.4.5.2. Con: The additional time that people take to look for jobs would result in reduced time spent performing productive labor.
1.6.4.5.2.1. Con: In the US, only 30% of workers feel engaged in their jobs; [leading to productivity losses of around $500 billion a year](http://screenwerk.com/2013/06/25/gallup-70-of-workforce-not-engaged-costs-billions-in-lost-productivity/).
1.6.4.5.2.1.1. Pro: Productivity tends to [increase](https://www.fastcompany.com/3048751/the-future-of-work/happy-employees-are-12-more-productive-at-work) when people enjoy the work they do. Allowing them to do so will therefore benefit the economy.
1.6.4.5.3. Pro: More time gives citizens a greater chance to find a job close to their current home and social environment \(family, friends\), which has a positive effect on their quality of life.
1.6.4.5.4. Con: A UBI will make citizens very picky and demanding concerning their job choices. Some positions and jobs would be very hard or next to impossible to staff.
1.6.4.5.4.1. Con: It is the companies' duty to create incentives in order to staff their positions.
1.6.4.5.5. Con: UBIs threaten corporations that depend on salaried employees that earn all of their income from them.
1.6.4.5.5.1. Pro: A UBI could lead to increased employee turnover if employees are able to quit due to small dissatisfactions. This hurts companies, and in the long run, the economy.
1.6.4.5.5.2. Con: If a business can't survive paying a fair rate to its employees, it shouldn't exist in the first place.
1.6.4.5.6. Pro: Taking renumeration to make ends meet out of the equation will also allow people to only pursue what is of interest to them. Counter-intuitively, the biggest impact will be in the job market, as [workplace efficiency was studied and known](https://www.emergenetics.com/blog/workplace-appreciation-gratitude/) to be proportional to the psychological effects of workplace appreciation & gratitude.
1.6.4.5.7. Con: This is not a feature unique to a UBI. It is something that targeted unemployment benefits manage to achieve quite well under the status quo.
1.6.4.5.7.1. Pro: -> See 1.3.1.3.3.2.
1.6.4.5.7.2. Con: While unemployment benefits help people seeking full time employment, they generally provide a disincentive to finding part time work, as reporting this work means losing part or all of their benefits for that period.
1.6.4.6. Pro: A UBI will help the labor market adapt to inevitable disruptions caused by [advancements](https://www.theatlantic.com/business/archive/2016/09/the-next-industrial-revolution/498779/) in automation and artificial intelligence.
